The Overs

by timely_thunderstorm

Summary

In the summer of 1960 Franklin, Tennessee, enigmatic physician Dr. Michael Robinavitch saves small-town farmhand Dennis Whitaker from heat exhaustion, beginning the most turbulent period of his life.

Notes

Please do not post this story on any other platform, nor in any other format, for any reason.

Chapter 1: What Is There To Say

Chapter Summary

Enigmatic local physician Dr. Michael "Robby" Robinavitch saves a new resident of small town Franklin, Tennessee from a fit of heat exhaustion.

Chapter Notes

Welcome to "The Overs" fic! I am not a doctor, but I am trying my very best to do enough research to make some of the... medical... scenes accurate. Each chapter of this story is named after and reflected by a song:

Chapter 1's song is "What Is There To Say" by Chris Connor on Nina Simone's 1959 album, "Nina Simone and Her Friends"

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Running from home could only ever be romanticized so diligently before its appeal dulled, and Dennis Whitaker worried he'd suddenly passed far beyond that point. His home and his family in Nebraska, though a painfully complicated bridge he'd already burned now, began to appear almost like a dream worth scurrying back to, and the kid had only been in Tennessee for a month.

Or perhaps it was just the suffocating, wet summer heat inspiring such second thoughts.

“How long you been workin’ here now, son?” 

Dennis did not immediately respond to his boss. He stared at the gruff old man, unsure what the question had to do with his current predicament: a splitting headache and warped, blurred vision in his peripheral.

The little room inside the barn was stuffy, and in the hot summer heat, the short-haired, pale boy with tired eyes could feel sweat stifling the space between his itchy farmer overalls and his meatless thighs. As hard as the kid worked, he really couldn’t afford to eat well; he was rather small and parts of his body were kissed pink by the beading, merciless sun. 

“About a month,” he swallowed, then hastily added a, “sir,” for polite measure. 

Southern hospitality. Dennis Whitaker could muster enough of it to pass around to anyone, though it didn't seem to do him much good in Franklin. 

“Right. This farm requires a level of…” the old man searched for the right words in his apparently limited vocabulary, “work ethic,” he settled like the words were snake fangs. “A level o’ work ethic that I hope you still got. ‘Cause I don't like my farmers come cryin' to me about feeling ill when they're standin' up on two good feet. Whining about needin’ to go to a— a doctor. That kind of talk is for pansies and yankees.” He paused for a moment, gave Dennis a quick scan, then leaned into his desk, big belly pressing into the edge as he declared in a whisper: "And I don't hire fags for farmers, boy."

Dennis' sweaty eyebrow twitched.

“Course not, sir. I—“ the boy rubbed at his dirty forehead vigorously, trying to bury the pain of intense throbbing in his skull, “it ain't serious, you're right. I was just misrememberin' if the job came with any benefits? To cover a short doctor visit after work, or—” 

The old man belly-laughed. There was a distinct wheeze in his chest as he did, wet and gravelly. 

“Benefits,” he hummed, humored. “Boy, what 'benefits' did you think came with good ole' Tennessee farm-work?” 

It was a satirical question. Dennis knew that. And yet he still willed himself to answer guilelessly:

“I don’t quite remember, sir.”

The boss sighed, resting his hands on the desk in front of him and saying "We don't got no benefits out here. Best I can tell you is to sleep it off tonight. I'll even cut you some slack comin' in a little late tomorrow mornin', hm? How 'bout it?” 

Dennis Whitaker would take what he could get.

"Yes sir, Mr. Howell."

"Go on now. Those fields ain't gonna work 'emselves, boy."

Dennis obeyed without so much as a second glance. The boy walked back out into the relentless summer heat, farm boots kicking up dust as he hustled back to his side of the land and mentally prepared himself to spend the next six hours in extreme physical labor while fighting off an admittedly intense and worsening delirium. But that was the power of a pushover; Dennis Whitaker had a kind of special and unspoken willpower. He’d get anything done he set his mind to if it meant pleasing someone. Likeliest was that he was something of a coward, but equally, of course, a pushover, and as many of the men around him would argue: a pansy. 

“You gonna make it, Whitaker?” 

A rough, dirty, almost antagonizing hand patted the boy’s back two firm times and he flinched, whipping his head up to see his farmhand, Randall, looming over him. The shadow he cast upon Dennis was bad news, because he now remembered what it felt like to be out of the direct assault of the sun; that coolness made him realize just how much he wasn’t going to make it to the end of his shift without probably dying. 

“Mm,” Dennis nodded vigorously through the delirium. “Yeah— yes. Just one of those days,” he lied, pointing up at the offending sun in the sky.

Randall squatted next to Dennis, watching idly as he struggled to work. The heat of the sun now back to beaming on the back of his poor, reddened neck sent him straight back into his stupor. 

“Whitaker,” Randall tried with a groan, stopping the boy’s right hand with his own firm grasp. “You look just ’bout keeled over, friend. Go sit under that oak tree you like and daydream about fairies n' flowers and whatnot, hm? Shift's only another hour longer anyway. I can manage alone.”

Randall’s small kindness was a life vest for Dennis, especially given the man's general unkindness. He was a bit of an ass. In truth, too, Dennis wouldn’t have taken an opportunity to rest without someone else practically forcing him. Even if it’d killed him. Perhaps Randall's demanding nature was the only kind that'd save the boy today.

Dennis nodded, wiping a bead of sweat from his brow but dirtying it with the back of his muddy hand. 

“Okay,” he said meekly. “Okay. Thank you.”

As he stood up, the toned, rugged farmhand took his place and went straight to work.

“Don’t get used to this, though. You owe me!” Randall hollered as Dennis began hobbling away. “Susan’s birthday’s comin’ up next month and I need us pullin’ our weight, ‘cause I ain’t missin’ it!”

Dennis dragged himself for ten straight minutes all the way across the vast expanse of farmland, nearing the edge of the green forests surrounding it. A single oak tree stood firm in the open area, and as he walked under the shade cast by the leaves, Dennis shuddered. A moment of silence and coolness; it was the moment he truly processed how far gone he was. His pulse thrummed in his ears, leaving flashes of light pulsing in his peripheral vision as he swallowed. 

If it was time to die, this wasn’t a terrible spot to lay.  

The boy tried leaning his weight onto the trunk of the tree, but his legs gave out, and he crumbled to the ground, rolling onto his back which lay awkwardly on the thick roots protruding from the dirt. A groan escaped Dennis' dry lips, and his eyes traced the flickering, shimmering light of the sun which danced between the fluttering oak leaves. He could hardly discern that light from the flashes surrounding his vision anymore.

"Oh..." he resigned in a hoarse whisper, allowing himself to be swallowed whole by the darkness under his eyelids.

A fitting end for the black sheep of the Whitaker family, he concluded.

 

 

Orange light. Warm, grounding light swam through the room from the ceiling like a soft evening sun. Dennis was still on his back. But no longer was he laying on uncomfortable oak tree roots. He was now near-completely submerged in a porcelain tub of cold water. His arms hung out on either side of the tub. His head lay back, hooked on the top edge. Frankly, he was a bit afraid to open his eyes. Had he been kidnapped and harvested for organs, he'd rather not have seen it... better to die in the dark behind his eyelids than die screaming about the holes in his stomach.

Dennis would have stayed that way, too. Would have sat in the cold tub with closed eyes until he bled out, if not for the warm hand that suddenly pressed against his forehead.

He flinched, torn from his thoughts, eyes widening open as he scanned his surroundings.

"Hey, hey, hey... calm down," the man cooed, grasping at both of Dennis' thin biceps to ground him.

"Where am I?"

"You're in my home above the Robinavitch Health Center. I've got you in an ice bath to bring down your temperature. You suffered some pretty nasty heat exhaustion, kid," the man spoke fast and clear, not a single stutter as he yammered on as though he was reciting from a page.

"Who are—" 

"I'm Dr. Robinavitch, but you can call me Robby."

Robby allowed a brief silence for Dennis to respond, and they locked eyes. Dennis scanned the man's face. He had scruffy but mostly well-kept facial hair, worn eyes, a distinct nose and short, unkempt brown hair. Likely late thirties. He was a handsome man. There was a glint in his eyes that calmed the kid inexplicably. The man was - embarrassingly, quite immediately - like a beacon in the fog of his mind.

Dennis nodded, reaching his hand out of the water to wipe his eyes.

"I'm gonna," Robby began as he reached over to the sink and grabbed a clipboard, scanning it and continuing, "ask you some simple questions to make sure the sun didn't totally fry you, alright?"

Without skipping a beat, the doctor fired away: "What's your name?"

"Dennis Whitaker," the farmer croaked out.

"How old are you, Mr. Whitaker?"

"Twenty-three since April."

Robby's eyes lit up, "No kidding? I'm an April baby, too. Thirty-nine this year."

Before Dennis could acknowledge that, the doctor continued: "City of residence?"

"Franklin— I can't afford this, sir," Dennis admitted abruptly, interrupting himself.

Robby stopped reciting, looking up and eyeing Dennis in a lull of silence.

"You expect me to have you pay for an ice bath?" 

The pale farm boy looked down into the water. He was stripped down to only his white briefs. Tiny cubes of ice bobbed around the surface, but the water had been warming for a while, he'd guess. His cheeks flushed red realizing he was on - nearly - full display.

"Yes, this one," the doctor commented sarcastically, tapping his finger on the edge of the tub.

Robby was personable. That made Dennis feel a little less uncomfortable about the whole situation. 

"Well..." Dennis hesitated, fearful of sounding stupid. "I wasn't— I'm not sure how this all works. I just didn't want to accept care I couldn't pay back."

"You're not accepting care you can't pay back. And I don't want your money, Mr. Whitaker," Robby assured kindly. "I hear you're a hardworking kid, hm? Your friend brought you here from Howell Farm. Said he saw you half-dead in the field. Rushed you here as fast as he could."

"Randall?" Dennis inquired.

Robby shrugged. "Dunno. My receptionist - who you'll probably meet before you get out of here - mentioned he dropped you off then said he had to bolt straight back to work."

It was probably Randall.

"Miss Evans said you were redder than a cooked lobster, and... I don't have a functional tub downstairs, so we brought you upstairs to my home."

Dennis looked around. It was certainly a regular bathroom. Sink, mirror, toilet, fuzzy brown rug on the otherwise white-tiled floor. The door behind Robby was creaked open, and he could see a bed in the other room. It was a mess.

"Am I gonna be okay?" Dennis asked before his eyes returned to Robby's face.

Robby chuckled at the irrationality of the question, placing the clipboard on the floor and taking a proper seat next to the tub: backside on the floor, knees huddled to his chest.

"You're gonna be alright, Mr. Whitaker, yes. But you..." he contemplated if his next words were appropriate. "You need to take more care of yourself."

Dennis took those words in, pausing in silence for a moment and studying the concern on Robby's face.

"That's a little difficult with bills to pay," he admitted.

Robby knew what Dennis meant, but he still urged: "Can't pay the bills if you're dead."

"Difficult paradox, huh?" Dennis whispered, and Robby's eyes darted to his lips in the most subtle glance possible. Then, Dennis shifted in the tub, saying, "I don't have much money, sir. And I ain't got any family out here. One screw-up would probably cost me everything."

Robby brainstormed in silence for a moment, allowing himself to study Dennis' tired face, gnawing at his own lip as he reached for whatever solutions he could find to help the stranger in his tub.

Here we go, he thought to himself. Here we go again.

Robby had a peculiar habit. He saw a stranger's problems and oftentimes he'd totally absorb them. He'd become fixated on solving them like they were his own. It wasn't a sustainable habit, and it certainly was difficult for the man to control.

"You're twenty-three, yes?"

Dennis nodded.

"This world..." Robby tried, grabbing the edge of the tub as he sighed. "It's gonna swallow you up and spit you out a hundred more times before you know how to really live in it. But you've got time. Don't push yourself past a finish line that doesn't belong to you."

Dennis was intensely focused on Robby's words, eyes darting back and forth between the man's chapped lips and his brown eyes.

"That is to say, take these things slow. Take them one at a time. Start with yourself."

Robby continued searching for solutions, searching for words. After a moment of tense quiet between the two men, he came up with a question he, admittedly, already knew the answer to:

"For example: do you drink much water at work?"

Dennis shook his head.

Robby nodded, then stood up and left the bathroom.

Hollering somewhat theatrically from another room, he said, "I've got something very simple that'll help you with that, Mr. Whitaker! A single, small step toward grabbing the reigns and taking control of your life!"

When he came back, Robby held in his right hand a massive red thermos, the kind that Dennis couldn't dream to afford. 

The thought of equating a thermos to 'the first small step toward taking control of your life' made Dennis want to chuckle, but he didn't want to take away from the moment. It was, after all, an immensely kind gesture.

"Simple solutions to big problems, hm? This-" Robby pointed at the thermos as he leaned back onto his knees at the edge of the tub "-will carry a lot of water. And keep it cold, too."

"Sir—"

"You bring this to work with you on hot days like today and I promise you'll be less inclined to drop dead under an oak tree."

The comment finally drew that suspended chuckle from Dennis. That damned chuckle that he'd tried holding down because it didn't feel appropriate. Making light of anything happening. Having been taken care of in a doctor's own home. Having been saved by Randall. Having been given a moment to rest after four long weeks of endless work which had now nearly killed him.

"Sir, I shouldn't take—"

"I'm not letting you leave until you do, to be frank with you, Mr. Whitaker."

They stared at one another in an odd, almost intimate sort of stalemate. Dennis' lip twitched. He didn't know what to say. Robby clearly always knew what to say, it seemed. He was good at being personable, but that likely just came with the territory of dealing with patients so very often.

Dennis knew vaguely of the Robinavitch Health Center. Randall had mentioned it in passing once or twice at work, saying it was a miracle for the people of Franklin because its doctor was 'urban educated,' and the place was a lot less formal than a true hospital—a lot more affordable. 

"I can't have you being carried back in here cooked medium-well knowing I could've helped to prevent it," Robby continued. 

Dennis was quiet.

Robby placed the thermos on the tiled floor next to the tub and reached over, placing his hand over Dennis' forehead. He brushed a strand of the farmer's curled, dirty hair away, flattening his palm and feeling for temperature.

"I'm inclined to discharge you by the time we close this evening," Robby whispered, moving his palm closer to Dennis' hairline, leaning further into his space. "But you got beaten pretty bad by the heat, so I'd rest easier to know you have someone at home to keep an eye on you. Just for a day or two."

"I do," Dennis lied immediately, nodding his head.

Robby's face became... unreadable. Dennis wasn't entirely sure the lie landed.

"Say, Mr. Whitaker," Robby began with a cocked eyebrow, "your folks any good at making meals at home?"

The whole while, Robby couldn't help but notice how thin Dennis was for his age. He suspected the kid hadn't been eating much, at least for a few weeks.

"I don't live with my folks," Dennis corrected softly, "just with a friend. And, yeah," he lied through his teeth, "we make do."

"Got a favorite?"

"What?"

"Food. Got a favorite food?"

Dennis' brows furrowed. "Uh..." he said and looked away from Robby, "can't go wrong with a peanut butter sandwich. Or a pot pie on a good day, I guess."

Skeptical, Robby gnawed at his inner lip for a moment, but nodded. "...I see."

"Why?"

Robby shrugged. "Just making sure you're eating," he spoke honestly, then with a sharp inhale, he took his hand back from Dennis' forehead and stood to his feet.

"I, uh..." he stammered. Dennis noted that it was the first time he didn't seem to have a set of words neatly prepared. "I've gotta go check on Miss Evans downstairs and see what my patients are getting up to."

Dennis chewed on his inner cheek, nodding.

"If the tub gets too cold, step out and dry off," Robby pointed to a neatly folded towel he'd laid for Dennis on the toilet cover. "I've got your clothes in my dryer right now."

Dennis nodded again, quiet as a mouse.

"I'll come back up and check on you as soon as I can."

Robby left Dennis to himself in the silence, but the boy could hear as the doctor stomped down the creaking stairs back to the first floor. Back to the one-story, house-shaped makeshift hospital. He wondered if it was busy down there. Wondered if Robby had gone out of his way to help him or if he was just another patient.

'Just another patient,' he mocked himself, sinking further into the water. He thought it was a stupid thing to dwell on.

It was only as he sunk further that he realized he was getting quite cold. The doctor had instructed him to get out if he got 'too' cold, but what was 'too' cold? Was shuddering and goosebump-riddled skin enough? He grazed his fingertips along the hairs at the back of his neck to check if they stood; they did. Was standing hair of the nape cold enough to warrant getting out of the tub? Or should he stay longer to force a regulated body temperature?

Suddenly he wished he'd asked Robby more questions instead of sitting half-naked in awkward silence the whole time. But more intensely, he wished he just knew these things himself. That would've really simplified things.

 

 

"Robby, we got a nice lady and her son in room C waiting on you," Dana Evans called out as Robby walked past the front desk.

He spun around to face her but continued walking backward as he nodded.

"Been waitin' for nearin' thirty minutes!" She scolded softly.

Robby offered a flippant smirk and nodded again. "I know Dana, I know. Had to get our lobster boy acquainted with my bathroom."

"How's he doin', anyway?"

Robby stopped at the edge of the desk and scanned the appointment log. It was always mostly empty; though his health center was advertised as a general health establishment, he frequently took on emergency and trauma patients, which quickly gave him a reputation in Franklin as... something of a low-cost miracle-worker.

"He's awake. He's..." Robby contemplated his next words with a tight, strained smile: "sweet kid."

Dana offered a humored but confused expression.

"Didn't think I'd see you again before I left, fruitcake," an older woman in her wheelchair rasped from behind Robby.

He sighed and, without turning or diverting his focus from the appointment log, offered: "Miss Myrna... I think we've seen enough of each other for one day, don't you?"

Dana swallowed a laugh that threatened to come out, ducking under the front desk to pretend being busy.

The older woman shrugged and unwrapped the mint in her hand, popping it in her mouth before saying, "I dunno, we could fit in one more round 'fore the ole husband rolls around."

"Jesus, Myrna." Robby pinched the bridge of his nose.

A singular, stifled laugh came from behind the front desk.

Robby turned to face Myrna, leaning his weight on the edge of the front desk and giving her a once-over. The bruise on her right temple was calming down.

He offered, "I'm the biggest advocate of 'em all that you find yourself a nice, kind man, but I'm spoken for!"

Myrna chuckled dryly and shot back, "You? Spoken for?"

Robby shrugged and defended half-heartedly: "Anything's possible."

The bruised woman rolled her eyes and wheeled herself toward the front door as she snorted, "Uh-huh."

Robby scurried over and opened the door for her, and she thanked him by muttering, "cocksucker," on her way out.

"Have a good one, Myrna," he offered and let the door shut behind him as Dana peered back up over the front desk.

They exchanged a look, and she pointed down the hall behind her toward room C.

"Go, go, go, champ."

"I'm going, Dana."

As he passed her, Dana handed the doctor a clipboard and sheet with the patient's information on it.

"And while you do that, I'm takin' advantage of the lull," she spoke and pulled a fresh pack of Winston cigarettes from her windbreaker. "I'll be back inside in five!"

"Alright," Robby called out before stopping in front of room C and closing his eyes.

He prayed it wasn't a hassle. Turning the doorknob, he pushed the door open and stepped inside, letting it close softly behind him as he studied the woman and boy sitting at the opposite end of the room.

One quick glance at his sheet, then Robby offered: "Miss Saunders?"

"Theresa is fine," she nodded softly, then looked at her son. "This is David."

"Hello, David," Robby tried, but the teen only gave a single uninterested glance before going back to picking at his nails.

Disrupting the ensuing awkward silence, Robby tapped his pen on the clipboard and spoke: "So, we're concerned about a potential melancholia situation?"

 

 

Robby's bedroom wasn't as messy as it had looked from the limited view of the tub. Dennis had dried himself off and willed himself to get up and look around, but only because it had now been nearly an hour since the doctor went downstairs. It sounded busy down there, too.

Against his better judgement, Dennis had wrapped the towel around himself and snooped around the room, landing on a neat array of thick books sitting atop a work desk next to the bed. He'd made himself cozy in the rickety wooden chair and picked a random yellow one, opening to the front page and skimming.

"Principles of Internal Medicine," he muttered, reading aloud.

It was a medical book from 1953. As seconds turned into handfuls of minutes, Dennis drowned himself in the massive walls of text with each turned page, drinking in the kind of knowledge he never imagined he'd be able to afford.

He could read, which was a bit of a step-up in his family, but he certainly couldn't afford many books, let alone the kinds that looked like they were passed around by well-made scholars and doctors and the likes. To anyone else, the book may have been a bore. To Dennis Whitaker, however, it was a treat.

More than a treat, it was a spark. The beginning of a fascination. It was a window through which he could see a better version of himself. Helping people.

His eyes remained fixated mostly on the benefits section for each surgery he read about. What was the surgery for? Who did it help? What was the potential for improved livelihood with those who underwent each procedure? Lots of questions were answered, and Dennis was nearly halfway through the thick book when the sound of creaking stairs ripped him from his focus.

Looking up, he saw that sunlight no longer flooded the windows; it was nighttime. He had no clue how long he'd been reading in Robby's bedroom, but the footsteps coming up the stairs alerted him enough to snap out of it and scurry back to the bathroom.

He kept the towel firmly wrapped around his waist, then sat on the floor just next to the tub, catching his breath and stabling himself from the woozy feeling creeping up his skull.

"Mr. Whitaker?" Robby called.

"In the restroom."

"Do you need privacy?"

"No, I'm decent."

Robby carefully pulled the door open, eyes lighting up ever so subtly when he saw Dennis looking... far better than he had looked several hours prior.

"You look thoroughly cooled off," he leaned on the doorframe, crossing his arms.

Robby looked tired. He'd been running around the house all day.

"I feel a lot better," Dennis nodded, offering a meek smile.

"That's good." A moment of awkward silence. "I'm sorry I kept you waiting so long."

The younger man shook his head, eyebrows raising. "No, it's fine! I didn't mind the quiet. The—well, y'know... the farm is... there's always something going on. It was nice to have a break."

"You don't get breaks when you go home? All swaddled in soft sheets and cool air?"

"My house isn't nice like yours," Dennis chuckled.

Robby nodded sheepishly, scratching the back of his head and pursing his lips.

"Yes, this is a nice home," he agreed awkwardly before pausing. "It's a bit odd, though. Working where you sleep, I mean."

Dennis flinched, staggering to his feet as he realized he should probably leave.

"I shouldn't keep you held up in your own home."

Robby looked confused for a short second. Like he was pondering if something he'd said gave the impression he wanted the younger man out of his house.

"I would've argued you should stay the night at the clinic so we can make sure you're ready to be back on your own two feet," Robby said convincingly.

Dennis shook his head, crossing his arms around his bare chest.

"You've done more than enough for me, sir—doctor. I couldn't ask for more."

All he did was drag the kid into an ice bath to keep him away from the grasp of - what could have become - a heatstroke. It didn't seem like all that much, but perhaps, to Dennis Whitaker, it was.

"Well," he sighed, "I can't force you to stay," Robby resigned, clearing the doorway for Dennis to pass by. "Your clothes are dried and folded. Hanging on the banister next to the stairs."

"Thank you," Dennis offered genuinely.

"I'll uh, stay right here. You get yourself dressed. Take your time, go easy."

When Dennis disappeared around the corner beyond, Robby stepped back out into his bedroom, slowly creeping over to his work desk.

Robby huffed with amusement at the sight before him.

On the desk, the fat, old cover of 'Principles of Internal Medicine' was opened to page 36. Robby wasn't sure if Dennis had actually thoroughly read all 36 pages, but the thought of him sneaking around the bedroom and stuffing his nose in these most monotonous of texts was very intriguing. Perhaps the kid had an interest in the medical world. Or, perhaps he was only bored—but 36 pages? Robby's interest was piqued.

"Mr. Whitaker?" Robby called out, listening to the movement of fabric as Dennis dressed himself out of view.

"Yes sir?"

"Would you like to pick out a few of these books to take home with you?"

There was an awkward silence, so Robby added:

"Could give you something to pass the time when you're not cooking like a lobster in the sun."

Dennis' small head peered over the corner, and he stepped into view as he finished the last button on his plaid shirt, cheeks red with embarrassment.

"I'm sorry."

"For what?" Robby's brows knitted in confusion as he studied the younger, shorter man.

"I mean— I'm sorry for snooping around. It ain't my house, I—"

"Pick out some books, would you?" Robby interrupted.

The tiniest pause, then Dennis jumped to action. "Yes sir."

Dennis walked past the doctor, feeling a firm pat on his shoulder.

"There you go," Robby praised.

 

 

"How'd you escape Robby, kid?"

Dana's voice came from behind Dennis just before he stepped onto the dirt road. She was leaned back against the front wall of the clinic, cigarette butt between her fingers, a fresh billow of smoke zipping past her lips.

"How do you mean, ma'am?"

"I was aware you'd be stayin' at the clinic overnight for monitoring, is all," she elaborated in that raspy, nurturing voice of hers.

Dennis paused for a moment, realizing he'd lied to Robby and said he had someone at home who'd care for him. It's probably the reason the doctor didn't actually force him to stay.

With a faint smile, he explained: "I've got someone at home to make sure I don't... y'know."

"Oh, really?" Dana asked with a sarcastic tone. "What's her name?"

A twitch of Dennis' lips. His brow furrowed, if only just slightly.

The silence lasted a beat too long, and Dana was the one who broke that silence:

"Can't fool a mother, silly."

Dennis resigned his posture. Exhaled the breath that had steeped in his lungs.

"Plus, your friend told me your situation while Robby was haulin' your sunburnt keister up to his house. Ran away from home, livin' alone all the way out here in Franklin. God only knows why you settled on this shithole of all places," she spoke while looking up at the stars, blowing another batch of smoke from her mouth.

"There are worse places to be than here, I think," Dennis offered like a timid mouse.

Dana gave a singular chuckle. "Don't I know it."

"You not from around here either?"

"No, Mr. Whitaker, I'm not." She sighed, closing her eyes and rubbing the bridge of her nose with the cigarette still perched between her fingers. "My husband moved us down here for work a couple years back. Met doctor Robby that same year. We both rolled into town around the same time."

"So where's he from? How'd he end up here?" Dennis overstepped, but he was too curious. "Doctor Robinavitch, I mean."

Dana shrugged.

"That isn't my story to tell."

"You don't know?" The question was genuine, but if it had come from any mouth other than Dennis', Dana would've heard it as condescending.

Judging by the look on her face, though, perhaps Dana didn't know all that much about it.

"I can tell you he came from a rich little family up north," Dana snorted, "folk around here joke and call him a carpetbagger for that. So he keeps it to himself. But... he cares about people. A lot. Too much. That's all you need to know about Robby, really."

Dana looked down from the stars and pointed at the three books crammed between Dennis' waist and his right arm. "You doin' some reading?"

He flinched, then looked down at the books and back up at Dana.

"Oh! Well—yes. I—doctor Robinavitch insisted that I take them. They're from his..." Dennis gestured to the second floor of the clinic, "house. Upstairs."

An unreadable expression settled onto Dana's face, and she chuckled softly to herself, shaking her head.

"Ma'am? Could I ask a favor?"

Dana brushed a stray bang from her face, tucking it behind her ear as she shrugged.

"Depends, I'm not gettin' in trouble, am I?"

"Well... could I trouble you not to tell the doctor about my— my living situation? Being alone, and all. I just... he's done a lot for me already, and I can't afford to pay him back for more care anyway."

The woman looked visibly conflicted for the first time. Like she wanted to argue with him. Like she wanted to lecture him of the importance of listening to a doctor and taking care of oneself. But it took only a handful of seconds before she resigned once more.

"That's your secret. Not mine, kid," she reassured in an oddly caring way. It was a foreign thing for Dennis.

"Thank you," he nodded and offered a final glance before turning awkwardly, prepared to head down the road and make the trek back home in the dark.

"Mr. Whitaker," Dana said one last time, and the boy looked back at her over his shoulder. She stared at him with an odd kind of fondness. 

"Take care of yourself out there."

Chapter 2: The Boxer

Chapter Summary

Dennis struggles to keep his life together in the farm fields as he navigates new interests. Robby and Dana take an unexpected trauma patient late one autumn night.

Chapter Notes

Chapter 2's song is "The Boxer" by Simon & Garfunkel on their 1970 album, "Bridge Over Troubled Water"

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Autumn wind fluttered through the open barn door, tickling Dennis' short, dirty hair as he turned the crisp page of his big, red medical book. Randall chowed away at his lunch, but food in mouth did not stop the man from talking:

"I'm thinkin'... a nice pink cake. Whipped frosting, sprinkles, the likes. She's always been a sugar monster," he chuckled, scratching the beard at his neck, kicking a stray rock on the ground.

Dennis was not paying attention. A distracted "Mhm," was all he offered.

Randall looked over at the pale farmhand whose nose lay buried in the book. That sight had become common the last few weeks. Not that - for a scrawny, dorky looking fellow like Dennis - it was entirely surprising that he would read, but Randall could no longer keep track of how often he'd been doing it now.

"What'm I talkin' about, Whitaker?" Randall tested.

"Daughter's birthday," he mumbled back.

"What's my daughter's name?"

A pause from Dennis.

"Cindy—"

"Susan, ya' prick!"

He tossed his dirty shoe at Dennis' feet, and the boy flinched, finally breaking his eyes away from the pages.

Dennis forced himself to laugh with Randall, yelling "Sorry! Sorry," and grabbing the shoe, lazily lugging it back in the other man's direction.

Randall joked, "No party invite for you now, ya' turd."

"Dammit," Dennis joked as he marked his page and closed the book.

Lunch break was almost over. He picked up the big thermos Robby had given him and unscrewed the top, taking a greedy swig of cool water. That little gift had gone a long way—it gave him the means to survive the remainder of a hot, humid Tennessee summer when he'd otherwise have melted to a puddle. 

"One day you're gonna get a daughter of yer' own and it'll flip your whole world downside up, Whitaker." Randall jabbed a finger up at the roof of the barn as he emphasized another, "Downside up!"

Not that Randall would ever know, but Dennis knew he probably wouldn't have children. Why, he never liked to dwell on for too long. The 'why' always scared him; it scared him nearly as shitless as having conversations about family. Conversations about spouses. Conversations about a domestic life he knew deep down he'd never be afforded the luxury of living. Not in the way he'd like.

"I'm sure it will," Dennis lied, then stood up and brushed dirt from the ass of his overalls.

"But— if I can have a moment of honesty between men?" Randall asked for permission to speak.

Dennis looked over, then nodded once. He begged to God the conversation was almost over.

"You stay stuck in those books with your cock wettin' the pages, it's gonna be real hard findin' a woman who'll let you wet her pages." He stood to his feet, packing his lunchbox back in his sack and tossing it in the corner of the barn. With a hoot, Randall added: "Let alone knock 'er up."

"I don't suppose I'm reading any more than I usually do?"

"Whitaker, you read more words on pages in the last few weeks than I've read in my whole life."

"You know how to read?" Dennis asked.

"Kiss my ass."

Dennis asked with genuine curiosity - but it came off sarcastic.

Fall had taken over Franklin, and the surrounding forests of the farm had ignited with vivid, warm color. Crimson reds bled unto sunset oranges; even sour yellow treetops teased the autumn palette as gracefully as Mother Nature willed. Dennis loved studying the color as he worked. Tennessee was still very new to him; it was his first fall in the state. It was - to Dennis - quite similar to Nebraska... if Nebraska were riddled solely with thick oak forests rather than naked, vast expanses of grassland, rocky hills, and haunting memories.

For having been mostly absent, nose buried in the book during lunch break, Dennis compensated with conversation: "Your wife helpin' any with Susan's party?"

"Ain't got a wife," Randall snorted. "Knocked 'er up, then she let me go. Didn't like how I 'handled myself' sometimes."

"How do you mean?"

"Said I was 'too angry too often.' See, that's the thing about a woman, Whitaker." Randall wagged his finger toward Dennis, not unlike a teacher preparing a valuable statement for his student. "She wants you 'till she gots you. Couldn't handle seein' me stressed. Couldn't understand the shit a man's gotta go through, always workin' to keep the lights on at home. I mean... how could she?"

An awkward pause.

"Sorry," Dennis offered meekly. "Didn't know you had all that goin' on."

Randall slapped his palm reassuringly onto the back of Dennis' neck and said, "It ain't a thing. Honestly I'm happy we're talkin' like this. Gives me a chance to give you the one-two on women. How to wrangle 'em in and keep 'em when you got 'em."

"Huh," Dennis huffed dryly.

"Y'know, I think I like teachin' you this stuff," Randall mused.

Dennis didn't respond to that.

He wasn't fond of Randall's perspective, but it made for decent conversation to kill time on the farm. And since the autumn set in to knock away the heat of summer, his job had become far more tolerable anyway. Working in the farm... Dennis was quick in learning to romanticize it. If nothing else, it was still a survivable escape from the life he'd fled in Nebraska. And the farm had only become more pleasant as the weeks went by - the weeks after he'd met Michael Robinavitch.

Every day he'd come into work with one of his three large medical books, his fancy red thermos, and his thin brown jacket. He'd read, clock in, work, ramble with Randall, read for lunch, work, clock out, and head home. Once he was home, he'd keep reading. It was something of a benefit to him, too; he read, so he didn't pay much attention to the growling of his stomach when dinner wasn't on his table. Every night. It helped maintain the facade. It helped maintain Dennis' ability to romanticize it all.

One night in particular, he'd kept his nightstand light on for too long. Nose buried in the red book, eyes strained from reading pages in near-darkness, neck stiff as his back pressed against the wall at the head of his mattress.

"Simple interrupted suture," he muttered as his eyes tracked the words. "Suture must begin at end point of incision..."

Dennis stayed in that position, entirely losing track of time, reading the page over and over. Fixated on the procedure as though it were a spell of magic, whose contents required undivided attention, unwavering devotion. To learn such a magic required that he stay up until the absurd hours of the morning.

It wasn't until the heavy red book slammed onto the floor next to his bed that he awoke again with a violent jump.

Dennis looked around, eyes straining to open against the bright light of late afternoon.

"Oh..."

He looked over at his clock. It was nearly 4 PM.

"Oh..." he moaned again with utter dread, ripping the sheets from his body and jumping out of bed. "No, no, no."

The pale boy sprinted out the front door of his house with one shoe on, the other snagged and hanging awkwardly from his foot. His overalls were unwashed, and the top button of his jacket just wouldn't lock, despite the relentless effort of his fidgeting hands as he hopped and scurried down the street.

Dust kicked up the cold dirt road with every racing step Dennis Whitaker took toward the farm.

 

 

"Incoming, Robby! Twenty-eight year old male with a puncture wound to the right foot," Dana looked down and huffed before turning back to yell, "kitchen knife!"

Robby walked hastily out into the hallway, assessing the man in the wheelchair.

"The food finally fights back," he quipped, taking the clipboard from Dana and reading: "Mr. Stevens, how long has this been in your foot?"

The man winced, grabbing at his ankle with gritted teeth before seething: "Twenty? No, thirty minutes? Give or take. God—It really fuckin' hurts."

"I bet," Robby nodded at Dana and stepped behind the wheelchair, hauling the poor man into Room B and parking the chair in the center of the room, closing the door behind him.

"Do you wanna tell me what happened?" He tried as he knelt down, examining the puncture wound carefully with a fresh pair of gloves.

The man winced at the touch, but spoke anyway.

"My girlfriend's daughter... it's her fifth birthday. I wanted to help take the workload off her for the party because the father didn't even show up, but I got carried away and dropped the knife..."

"Where's your girlfriend and the daughter?"

"Told 'em it wasn't a big deal. Told 'em I could get over here, get patched up, and get back to the party before sundown."

"Where were your shoes?"

"Wasn't wearing any."

"That might've saved you a trip to my clinic at all," Robby chuckled.

"Well it ain't a big deal, right? Your place is the best in Franklin, I heard."

"Flattered," Robby spoke as he stood to his feet, opening a cabinet and pulling out medical cloth and a small spray bottle. "Unfortunately, though," he began, kneeling back down and sprayed directly on the wound, listening as the patient groaned at the stinging sensation. "This could be a bit of an issue for you, Mr. Stevens."

"How so?" He spoke with an audible sense of dread.

Robby squinted, spraying more on the wound, then looking up at the man. "Well, y'know... it depends. This is a metal puncture wound."

A pause.

"How's your tetanus shot status, Mr. Stevens?"

The man's lip curled downward, fear flooding his eyes as he groaned, "Oh, fuck me."

"Right," Robby nodded, assessing the situation further. "I can work a hell of a miracle, but I'm fresh out of tetanus immunizations."

"Am I gonna die?"

"I'd wager not," Robby assured, patting the man's left calf as he wrapped his right hand around the handle of the knife. "But only if we get you to a proper hospital that can help with the tricker part of this wound." He paused, then needlessly reiterated: "The tetanus part."

Robby raised the lever of the wheelchair, forcing the foot support outward so the man's foot was elevated.

"Do most people survive a wound like this?" The man still sounded panicked.

"Well... funny enough, there's a statistic floating around in my field. It claims men and women in the south of the United States die more often thanks to complications of tetanus. Can you guess why that is, Mr. Stevens?"

The man shook his head.

Robby pulled the knife out of his foot, drawing a guttural growl from Mr. Stevens as he clutched the handles of the wheelchair with white knuckles.

"It's because men and women in the south don't tend to value the power of a vaccine."

Wasting no time at all, Robby applied intense pressure on the wound of the man's foot, tightly wrapping white cloth around it over and over.

"When a child isn't administered a tetanus shot, they grow up like a sitting duck just waiting for a single metal puncture wound. Then begins the complication."

"Fuck..." the man groaned, wincing and trying to stabilize his composure despite the pain in his foot.

"Mr. Stevens," Robby demanded his attention.

The man looked down at Robby.

"Consider talking to your lady about scheduling a vaccine appointment for her daughter, would you?"

 

 

"Dana," Robby called out as he rolled Mr. Stevens back into the front lobby.

"Robby," she responded.

"I'm taking Mr. Stevens here to Williamson since we're in a lull. If it gets busy, follow protocol. Emergencies get referred to Williamson. General care—"

"—General care to wait here and fill out appointment forms, I got it. I got it. I know this rodeo, cowboy." Dana smirked, leaning into the front desk.

Robby nodded.

"Don't be gone long, though! It'll be dark soon and I ain't runnin' the graveyard shift alone! Got too many ghost stories in my purse to fit another!"

"I'll be back before you know I'm gone, Dana," Robby assured, then hustled Mr. Stevens with his freshly bandaged foot out the front door.

Dana watched until they were out of view, then busied herself with checking off appointment logs and verifying the Tuesday schedule. She fit appointments between free time to account for potential trauma patients, but luckily in Franklin, there were never more than one or two of them any given day. Franklin was a small town, and though it wasn't true of this particular small town that everyone knew one another, Dana was confident she could name most residents.

"Where'd my fruitcake go runnin' off to?" Myrna sighed, wheeling herself into the main lobby from the lounge area.

"Haulin' off a poor guy with a knife in his foot. Drivin' him to Williamson for aftercare." Dana groaned. "And hello to you, too, Myrna."

"Better be back soon," Myrna sounded gravelly as usual. "I like his grumpy self."

"Myrna, baby. We discharged you hours ago," Dana spoke and double-checked the log to make sure.

The older woman shrugged. "It's home-y here. Think I've grown to like this place." She looked around as she spoke.

"Wonderful! Can't wait for you to start payin' rent, then."

Myrna offered a deadpan glance at Dana. Dana couldn't help but stare at the bruising on Myrna's eye. As something of a receptionist, Dana knew just about every patient in Robby's clinic - damn near personally, too. Myrna was a frequent visitor, but never for anything life-threatening. She always came in for small cuts and bruises - things that didn't require a visit to a clinic most the time. It became clear to Dana after the first few months of the older woman's increasingly regular visits that she was coming in not as a patient, but as something of a refugee. And how awful a concept that was to Dana: a woman seeking refuge from her own home. Where she came from up North, domestic violence wasn't at all uncommon, but here in the small town of Franklin it felt so very normal. So unsettlingly casual.

"Oh, Myrna..." she sighed, an overwhelming wave of sympathy drowning her comedic disposition. "When are you gonna leave him?"

"Ugh," Myrna waved her hand dismissively. "He ain't so bad. Ain't nobody else gonna take care of me at my ripe age, anyway."

"You call that 'taking care of'?" Dana asked.

"You don't see him at his best. Takin' care of damaged goods like me. Legs don't work, tits are saggin', teeth are fallin' out."

"A handful of good deeds doesn't make a bad man good, Miss Myrna."

"But a bad man is all I got left."

A pause fell between the two women.

Therein lay the awful reality: it didn't matter what Myrna thought about being beaten on a regular basis. She could not escape it anyway. It was not as simple for her as it could've been for Dana. Myrna did not have the means - financially, physically, and otherwise - to escape the gravity of a bad man. A bad man was like a black hole, sucking in a woman's light and warping it unto nothingness, leaving only the event horizon. A facade of brightness to disguise the truly awful weight underneath. Dana was so very grateful her own husband was not a bad man.

Myrna let out a long sigh and broke that pause with: "Men will be men."

"Bullshit."

The two women exchanged glances— real glances, like they were seeing through the barriers and reading one another. It was rare for Myrna to allow such vulnerability. So for that brief moment, despite the old woman's reality - and despite knowing well that it was inescapable - Dana gave in to her anger. She gave in to the side of her that would've liked to jump straight past the event horizon, deep into the black hole to rescue Myrna from inevitability.

"Fuck 'im," Dana nodded.

Myrna stood still for a moment, not a muscle in her face moved. Then she did something she'd never done in her handful of years visiting the clinic:

She chuckled.

"I'm serious!" Dana defended. "Fuck that son of a bitch. Fuck 'im!"

Myrna calmed her chuckling, rubbing her chest and letting out a wet cough before joking, "Y'think I should just kill 'im?"

Dana sighed, composing herself. Her brow twitched, but she took a moment to craft her response. "I'd turn a blind eye." It was a joke, but Dana thought she might actually be willing to let Myrna get away with it if such an unrealistic thing really happened.

"Don't tempt me," Myrna chuckled and shook her head, wheeling herself back down the hallway and toward the rest area.

The corner of Dana's mouth lifted gently, just for a moment. When it straightened back out, she returned to her appointment log and continued down the list.

Then the front door opened.

When Dana looked up, her eyes widened.

"Mr. Whitaker? What the hell happened?"

Dennis wiped pooled, drying blood from the corner of his eye and tried "I'm alright," unconvincingly.

"Come here. Come on, kid. Get over here."

Dana stepped out from the front desk and ushered Dennis to room A. She left the door open, but sat him in a chair and wasted no time cleaning the cut and assessing the wound.

"What happened to you, baby? Don't make me ask a third time."

"It was my fault," he assured quietly, wincing at the touch of Dana's soft fingers on his skin. "I let down my farmhand. Overslept and he had to stay over and take my workload."

"So it was that Randall boy?"

Dennis' lack of response was... confirmation enough for Dana.

"One missed shift and he does this? What a prick," she grumbled as she worked.

"It was his daughter's birthday today. He was supposed to leave early to be there, but I... showed up late."

The blonde woman shook her head. "He's lucky this ain't a serious wound. You got a little cut above your eye, but nothin' crazy— he's lucky." She pointed aggressively into Dennis' chest as she said it.

"Is Doctor Robinavitch not in? Not— not that I mind your care at all! I just figured... I'm guessin' maybe I'll need a suture?"

Dana snorted. "Suture, huh? So you been readin' those books he gave you."

Dennis offered a sheepish smile.

"He's not here right now, though, no. Took a puncture-wound patient to Williamson for a tetanus shot."

"Ouch," Dennis winced.

"Yeah— hey. You got your tetanus shot?" She raised a brow at him.

He gulped and nodded nervously. "Yes ma'am. Yeah, my town in Nebraska wasn't keen on vaccines but I managed."

"Good kid," Dana said as she tussled Dennis' hair.

"You don't know if he'll be back today, do you?"

Dana backed away after finishing examining the wound, then responded: "He'll be back, for sure. If you want him to suture you so bad, I'll let you wait out in the lobby. How 'bout that?"

Dennis contemplated for a moment, then timidly nodded.

And he did exactly that; Dennis sat in the lobby for what felt like hours, reading away at that same red book that had gotten him socked in the face earlier that day. His eyes flicked around each page as he continued making his way through the book. Sometimes he'd reread procedures that fascinated him. Sometimes he'd zone out - eyes still on the page - imagining what he'd look like as a doctor, helping people day in and day out.

When next he took his head out of the pages, Dennis found it was once again dark outside. The clinic was all but empty, save for Dana and himself. Dana had kept to herself all those hours, quiet, but comforting in presence.

"You get so lost in those books," she chuckled, and Dennis turned to look at her.

He offered an awkward laugh and nodded. "It's been a real treat gettin' to... read. To learn, I mean."

"You in school? Shootin' to be a doctor?"

"Oh, goodness, no... no. I'm not— I don't have that kind of money," he scratched the back of his head. "I'm just enjoying what I've got," he said as he raised the book in his hands.

"Nothin' wrong with that," Dana smiled.

The front door swung wide open, grabbing both Dana and Dennis' attention as they laid eyes on a disheveled Robby.

"Dana, I need you to prepare a room. Now." He demanded, and the haste in his voice left no room for questions from Dana, she scurried straight to work.

When his eyes landed on Dennis, he softened ever so subtly for just a split moment, and he walked over, rough hand grazing near the dried cut on the pale man's eyebrow.

"Long time no see, Mr. Whitaker." His voice became noticeably less frantic. It was soft now. Low, but soft, and warm. "What happened to you?"

Dennis shook his head, and Robby retracted his hand slowly, fingertips grazing skin, eyes fixated on him.

"I'm alright, it's nothing."

Robby stared for a moment without moving, then finally decided on offering a nod.

"Okay," he spoke soft. "I need to clear the clinic, so you'll have to head home tonight, but..." he put a hand on Dennis' shoulder. "Will you do me a favor, Mr. Whitaker?"

Dennis blinked. Then, meekly, he nodded.

Robby leaned in close, lowering his voice.

"Go upstairs and open my refrigerator. There's a chicken pot pie on the middle shelf, haven't even taken a bite of it. Bring it home with you tonight."

"Sir..."

Robby interrupted the younger man's protest with nothing but a stern look, as though he could just tell Dennis hadn't been eating properly for weeks now. The look was strong enough that Dennis decided not to fight it or question it; he simply nodded.

Robby squeezed the younger man's shoulder firmly and said, "Come back and see me sometime."

As Dennis tip-toed up the stairs, Robby made haste to the back of the clinic, exiting from the backdoor and leaving it wide open.

Dana had only just finished preparing the room when Robby busted in with an apparently unconscious young black man in a wheelchair. Following frantically behind Robby was a black woman with soft, brown eyes and a kind of fear on her face that sent a pit into Dana's stomach. The woman's maroon dress was stained crimson with blood.

"Nineteen year-old male, he's got a gunshot wound to the lower right abdomen. It's a deep bullet, very likely hit the iliac artery. Patient is in shock from blood loss. I've barely got him stable. There's a weak pulse—"

Dana's wide-eyed stare didn't go unnoticed. She looked at the woman and then at the man Robby was moving to the bed, stunned. Unable to keep up with Robby's assessment.

"Robby," she shook her head, unsure what to say. "We... can't take—"

Robby turned and spoke with an intimidating sternness: "We will take whoever is in need, zero questions. Is that clear?"

"He needs a real hospital, we don't have enough—"

"They refused to take him," the bloodied woman sobbed into the back of her hand as she looked away, fighting to keep herself together.

Dana's eyes flicked from the woman back to the man bleeding on the bed.

"Robby, what if someone—"

"We'll figure that out after we make sure he doesn't die in this bed, Dana." Robby's voice raised as he said her name. "Now get your head back down to Earth and help me!"

Dana composed herself, taking a deep breath in, then preparing to listen for every word that came out of the doctor's mouth from there on.

 

 

A weight tugged down on Dennis' chest. His heart found a pace not dissimilar to that of a race horse, hooves thumping in the dirt at incredible speed. He clutched the chicken pot pie against him like hugging a book - as though letting it go would be catastrophic. When he made it through the creaking, miserable door of his house, it slammed behind him, echoing into the empty, dark night outside. The boy set the pot pie down on his dinky, small dining table, then turned away, pressing his knuckles onto his lips and inhaling sharp.

A chicken pot pie. One that Dr. Robinavitch gave him.

Dennis kept replaying the brief encounter, analyzing. Overanalyzing. 

He wondered why Robby had rushed him out of the clinic so hastily, and the irrational side of him tried convincing that it was because the man hated him. Perhaps he was a burden. Perhaps there was no tangible reason Michael Robinavitch was so kind to him all those weeks ago when they'd first met. Perhaps he didn't mean it when he'd said "Come back and see me sometime."

Or perhaps it wasn't so complicated at all.

Dennis bit into his knuckle, turning to glance at the pot pie, then turning away again, as though the pot pie were Robby himself. A golden, inviting exterior crust, hiding beneath it layers of creamy chicken, savory vegetables, hearty elements which Dennis himself could hardly refuse.

Afterall, he was starving. He couldn't remember the last time he'd allowed himself a taste of something so harmless - something that anyone would eat. But this chicken pot pie felt different. It was not like other foods; it felt like an invitation. It felt like a kind of attraction that should not be indulged - lest it invite a perpetual craving that never fade away; a craving that demanded he indulge more and more, even when he himself could not safely sustain such craving.

But again, Dennis was hungry.

Creeping over to the table and crumbling to his knees, Dennis lay his cheek on the flat surface near the corner, eyes fixated on the edge of the dish which housed the pie. He lifted his hand, pressing his soft digits onto the cold crust, circling and caressing it until the tips of his fingers tingled. His index finger broke the surface, sinking in and feeling the cool, wet insides of the pie. He swirled the finger around, then fit another finger into the same hole. It sounded wet; the contrasting silence around him made it easy to hear each and every squish and squelch from the pie as he moved around inside it.

When he retracted his hand, Dennis slipped his index and middle finger into his mouth, wrapping his lips around them, closing his eyes, and sucking the savory insides of the pot pie clean off.

It was the taste that fueled the hunger.

Frantically, he scurried to his feet, plopping into the light-brown, faded wooden chair at the dining table, digging into the pie barbarically with his hands, stuffing himself faster than he could swallow. Each glob tasted like heaven - he didn't care that it was cold. Food was food, and Dennis was starved.

He did not stop until the little red pot was empty - stomach full, hands coated. Dennis stared at the empty space where pie had just been, eyes tracing the little flickers of lamp light that reflected from the shiny, smooth bottom of the pot. At some point between ravenous bites, the blood in his body had rushed downward, warming and pooling at his groin - he hadn't noticed until all was silent again, no more pie left to occupy him.

It felt - to Dennis Whitaker, a young man who'd prided himself on not indulging crude thoughts or sinful temptations - very, very dangerous.

"But among you there must not be even a hint of sexual immorality, or of any kind of impurity, or of greed, because these are improper for God’s holy people." Dennis cited, head hung low over the table. "These are improper for God's holy people," he repeated.

The irony of having run away from home in Nebraska to escape the pressures of religious expectation, then settling into a desolate, empty house only to mumble sweet biblical nothings into an empty pot pie bowl; that was an irony that was lost on Dennis Whitaker. In fairness, he was truly far more focused on keeping his hands out of his pants - keeping from relieving the tight, agonizing heat just below his waist. He needed to get up, needed to move. He needed to clear himself of the thoughts trying to creep into his mind.

So he shoved himself to his feet, stripped bare, and ran to the bathroom.

Dennis rubbed at the soft skin of his neck where Robby had squeezed. The shower water was lukewarm at best, but that singular point on his skin felt lit ablaze. It tingled. It was a touch that he craved so desperately for so long now.

As desperation festered and mutated in his mind, Dennis' hand slipped lower and lower, past his chest, down his abdomen, stopping and resting at his inner thigh.

'Come back and see me sometime,' Robby had said.

By this point, his brain was fogged over like frost creeping around a four-pane window. Dennis gave in to whatever affection he craved. He caved as the echo of Robby's voice tickled his ears, as the ghost of his touch grazed his skin. He supposed - in his fit of lust - that just this once, it wouldn't be the end of the world if he closed his eyes and allowed the feeling to fill him. There was, however, a tiny voice buried in the back of Dennis' head reminding him of how disgusted with himself he'd be when he finished.

The pale man wrapped desperate fingers around his aching cock. A soft, light sigh escaped his lips as water from the shower trickled down his face and down the corners of his shyly parted lips. 

He'd regret it. He knew he'd regret touching himself the moment this was over. He'd surely regret having done it with the face of a man in his mind. The voice of a man. The rough hands that had touched him not of sexual desire, but of common contact. Dennis would feel dirty for this. He would beat himself half to death for it or find some way to punish himself for his sins; it's what he always did, in place of his father.

Back home in Nebraska, any behavior short of being a man was grounds for punishment. A belt to the face, a stick to the wrist - sometimes a good old-fashioned knuckle to the jaw. Dennis had felt it all, given how often he was behaving outside the bounds of "how a man needs to." When finally he moved away, the void of anger left by his father was one that Dennis now inflicted upon himself. Sometimes he'd scold himself, or other times he'd bite down on his lip 'till it bled. He'd even occasionally hold off eating, deeming himself unworthy of what little food he possessed when he did not work hard enough or act man enough. All of this was simply the shadow left by his father.

So, for having given in to a pleasure as unbecoming as this; having wrapped his hands around himself and done so with the image of Michael Robinavitch stinging behind his squeezed eyelids, Dennis decided he would find suitable punishment. He'd find a way to make himself regret doing this. He'd find a pain strong enough to convince he never do it again.

But only after he'd allowed just this one taste of what he knew he could not have.

Chapter End Notes

I hope you enjoyed this chapter! As I mentioned previously, this story will delve into bigotry, including that of racism, sexism, and homophobia in the 1960s. I've been doing extensive research on these subjects (especially those that I have not & cannot personally experience) so that my story may stay honest and accurately reflective of the times while maintaining the dignity of these characters, but I am keeping my eyes and ears ESPECIALLY open to input on these issues as the story unfolds. That is to say: I really appreciate any guidance and educational commentary on these sensitive areas of my story, regardless of how confident I am in the research I'm doing for them.

Anyway, thank you for reading, and chapters will to continue to update on a relatively weekly basis. But I won't sacrifice quality for timeliness, so... it might be a little wonky some weeks.
