@moontilts drew this amazing art of *THE* pot pie scene!

Chapter 3: To Be Alone With You

Chapter Summary

Dennis faces the consequences of his actions. Robby, unable to turn a blind eye, struggles with a difficult decision.

Chapter Notes

Chapter 3's song is "To Be Alone With You" by Sufjan Stevens on his 2004 album, "Seven Swans"

Welcome to the final chapter of Act 1!

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Sexual urges were not an easy maze for Dennis Whitaker to navigate.

Naturally, he did not sleep well that night.

The walk to work that next morning was just as haunting as the breathless minute that had followed relieving himself in the shower. Terrifyingly, it was a morning swallowed by a kind of silence that left him to his thoughts, or rather: it left his thoughts to swarm around him like a black blur of crows nipping at his skin. How offensive, having given in to such vile urges while imagining the face of a man as kind and unassuming as Michael Robinav—

"Hell," he groaned, rubbing his face vigorously as he walked.

His ears burned red like a kiss from the sun.

When he finally stepped into Mr. Howell's dinky little outpost at the edge of the fields, he'd composed himself just barely enough to appear presentable. He stood in line behind four other farmhands for a handful of minutes before making it to the front, just across the desk from the large, old boss.

"Dennis Whitaker," the man hummed low like gravel to himself as he wrote the boy's name in. "Clocked in at," he checked his watch, then wrote more, "9 AM... on... the... dot."

Mr. Howell didn't look up to offer Dennis a final glance, just flipped the page of the farmhand log and continued.

"Whitaker, you're with Randall on the northeast field today."

"Randall?"

Mr. Howell looked up. He hadn't expected a word from the boy. That glance alone nearly made Dennis flinch, and his posture visibly melted.

"Sorry sir— I only... he and I didn't get on too well yesterday."

"Can y'blame him, boy?"

Dennis paused.

"No sir."

"Get out there an' kiss him some ass. It'd do you both good. You owe him for what you pulled yesterday."

The young man was nothing if not a pleaser. He did exactly as instructed, clinging around Randall for the whole morning in utter silence, fearful of what else the man might do to him should he overstay his - admittedly already overstayed - welcome.

 

 

"To be clear, Robby—"

Robby shook his head and chuckled dismissively as he stepped behind the front desk, setting down papers and checking the appointment log for the day ahead.

"Oh, oh. Oh wow," Dana scoffed and folded her arms, nodding. Watching as he blatantly ignored her. "Sure— act like I'm not even here. God forbid a woman have somethin' to say about your choices!"

Robby stood up and placed both hands on either of Dana's shoulders.

"Dana."

"Robinavitch."

The full name sent a twitch to Robby's eyebrow. She was stressing him out already and he'd only just come downstairs for the morning shift.

"You've tried telling me this same... same thing. Fifty times now since last night."

Dana huffed and rolled her eyes, "Fifty? Really?

"Fifty!"

"You're such an ass."

"I'm serious. I've been counting."

"I'm trying to look out for you, kid!"

Robby scratched his eyebrow with the back of his thumb. "Don't call me 'kid,' I'm a year your senior."

"I'll call you what I damn well please!"

"Why does everything have to be a tussle?" Robby's voice raised. "Why does everything need to—"

"—Because you brought them here!"

Robby paused.

"That man was dying in the damn Williamson parking lot, Dana."

"He's not the problem. This is about you! It's about you putting yourself in these... these— these situations. Where you're taking on all this baggage like you're wannabe Jesus reborn. Like you can save everyone from this fucked up country!"

"What are you on about?"

"I'm on about you." Dana hissed. "Putting yourself — and him and his friend — in jeopardy by dragging them here because you simply cannot help yourself. What would've happened to them if some redneck had seen them sneaking in or out of the clinic, Robby? Who do you think would've been in more trouble? Hm?"

Robby blinked. He didn't respond, so Dana pressed her point further:

"You? Or them?"

The doctor moved out from behind the front desk, assessing the lobby, then peering down the hallway to make sure it was tidy enough. Dana stood firmly in her spot behind the desk. She didn't move a muscle.

"It doesn't matter anyway," Robby called out from down the hallway.

"How do you mean?" Dana dared to ask, but she was... almost afraid of the answer.

"I promised the woman I'd teach her. Last night. Told her I'd teach her medicine. Procedure. How to tend to a wound, how to—"

Incredulously, Dana sighed "Jesus fuckin' Christ."

"The world's changing, Dana."

"Not fast enough!" She hollered from behind the desk, leaning down and scribbling in the appointment log.

When Robby walked back into the lobby, he lowered his voice. "There's already a lot of buzz about Jim Crow being torn down. Kennedy's campaign looks promising for a lot of folks out here. It won't be long before—"

Dana rolled her eyes, "You think Jim Crow is the root of all racism, Robby? You think it'll disappear the moment those laws are dismantled? Like we're in some kind of fantasy film?"

"I didn't say that. I don't think it'll be easy. I think it'll be a start," he offered. "And in the meantime... I'm going to do what I can with what resources I have. To help people."

"You promised me you'd stop nosediving into risky shit like this."

Robby scoffed. "Dana. You knew - you knew - why I came down to Franklin. You knew what you signed up for because I told you. It's my clinic, and I do these things how I see necessary."

"There are limits, Robby. There are limits to the things - both inside this clinic and in your fuckin' life - that you can control."

"Does that mean we shouldn't be using our resources to help those in need? Does it mean I can't save a man dying in a hospital parking lot? A man who's being refused care from the only major hospital within a two hour drive of us?"

Dana finally seemed to soften a little.

Robby added quietly: "It was here or a mass grave."

The man's unwavering need to help people always worried Dana, not for anyone but his sake. She cared for Robby almost like a brother. She knew what lengths he'd go to—trying to help people. But she also knew a fair bit about the damage he could deal to others in his reckless pursuit of solution, no matter his intention. She worried for that woman. Worried for the woman's friend, whose life they'd saved just the night prior.

"Her name is Heather Collins," Robby opened up, leaning into the front desk. "She's gonna be safe. We worked out a system over the night—one that I think is promising."

There was an awkward pause of silence, so the doctor continued:

"She's hellbent on turning life around for her family." Robby said and looked into Dana's eyes, "She has this— this... need. This need to care for people. Said she couldn't bear going through something like that again—not being able to do anything about it. I could see it in her eyes; she's got that same drive that I had at her age."

Dana's lip quivered, focus wavering.

"Swear to me, Robby."

Robby blinked, processing the emotion in her voice.

"Swear to me you won't fuck this up for her. Or for anyone around her. Do not ruin this woman's life trying to be a savior, or so help me God..." she trailed off, head shaking.

Robby took Dana's hand.

"She'll be coming in for about an hour each night. Long past closing. We'll go over some readings, I'll show her the ins and outs of basic medical care, and that'll be it. No risk. Only you and I will ever be aware of this."

Robby paused, then remembered to add:

"I swear."

 

 

The whole morning, Randall didn't speak, but neither did Dennis. He likely already knew they'd be assigned to work together for the day. Maybe Howell told Randall when he clocked in. Or maybe Randall was just in pure disbelief that Dennis was anywhere near him after having punched the blood out of his brow the day prior.

They worked in the field for hours in silence. Dirtied hands, mud-smeared jeans, cold sweat dripping down their faces. It was a chilly afternoon in late September and only getting colder now that the sun was beginning to set.

"I've been thinkin' today," Dennis finally spoke.

Silence.

"I didn't know how to— I mean... I could just say sorry over and over, and it wouldn't matter. I shoulda been there. You should've been at your daughter's birthday."

Save for the sounds of Randall chewing away at his packed sandwich, there was only silence. Dennis had hoped lunch break would be a decent time to sit and talk about his screw-up.

"I love her so much."

The words caught Dennis' attention, but not as much as the noise of a choked sob which followed shortly after. He whipped his head up to see Randall, teary-eyed, bagging the remaining few bites of his sandwich and leaning his head back against the wooden board of the barn wall.

"Haven't seen her in almost two years. That's almost half her fuckin' life. Half."

A tear finally broke away and slipped down his cheek, clearing dirt from it along the way.

"She probably doesn't even know who I am anymore. Gonna grow up and forget her daddy's name. His face. Gonna make memories and see the world without me. Ellie gave me one chance to do this. To see her. Yesterday was my one chance."

A tear threatened to fall from Dennis' eye. He wiped it with the back of his hand, sucked in a breath of air, and heaved out: "I'm so sorry. I'm sorry."

Randall pinched the bridge of his nose and shook his head. Dennis scurried over and tried comforting the man with a hand on his shoulder.

"Fuck off of me!" Randall pushed Dennis away violently.

The timid boy's eyes went wide. He stood still.

"Fuckin' bastard. Fuck you. Fuck you!"

They spent the next two hours of the shift in even more silence. Dennis, although sympathetic at first, had allowed an anger to fester within him. He didn't like being yelled at in such a way. It wouldn't have been the first time a man's unbridled rage had tipped Dennis' own beyond the edge. But this was, in the end, his fault. So he felt he had no choice but to let it go.

When dusk threatened to bleed over the sky and the chill of night nipped at skin, Dennis tried one last time to make amends.

"If there's anything I can do to help..." he trailed off.

Randall stood from the ground, clapping the dirt from his palms and turning to Dennis. He shook his head.

"Y'know somethin', Whitaker?"

Dennis did not respond, but he didn't need to. Randall was going to continue anyway.

"Only reason I didn't walk right off this stale fuckin' farm yesterday to go see my daughter is 'cause if I don't got this job, I don't got nothin'. Y'know what that means?"

Dennis swallowed a lump in his throat.

"It means the only reason I ain't..." a vein bulged in his forehead as he contemplated the next words, "killed you," he finally seethed, "is 'cause I gotta be here. More than anything. I'm locked in this pit and they put you in it with me. It don't mean we're friends. It means I'm stuck here with your pansy ass."

Dennis felt his brow twitch uncontrollably. He'd tried - all day, through the entire shift - to mind his space, and even when he did briefly attempt to apologize for what was, in fairness, not the kind of thing that could be remedied with an apology alone, Randall had rejected it. But that wasn't what was now setting Dennis off. No, Randall's rejection alone was not enough to get under the skin of someone like Dennis Whitaker. It was the regurgitation of Mr. Howell's own language, the perpetuation that he - Dennis Whitaker, scrawny farmhand who could read and imagined a future for himself beyond this farm - was a 'pansy.' It was the idea that, for being unlike Randall—unlike most men, he was under an inescapable scrutiny; every action begot judgement. For being judged by Randall of all people, after everything he'd run away from in Nebraska, Dennis was finally out of patience.

"You think I wanna be stuck here with you, either?" He said the words so quiet they were hardly audible.

Randall turned slowly, eyeing Dennis.

"What'd you say?"

Dennis grit his teeth, but he didn't immediately respond.

"What'd you say to me, Dennis fuckin' Whitaker?"

"Think about it," Dennis spoke up, stifling a singular hysterical chuckle. "You ramble sun up to sun down about how shitty women are—how hard it is to be you, how pansy I am just for readin' a damned book! You can do no wrong and everyone else is beneath you. You think I wanna be here, either?"

"Got any more gripes to get off your chest?" Randall cackled sardonically. "Did'ya finally grow a pair of balls?"

Dennis shook his head, disbelief curling the corners of his lips down. He was at his limit.

"Gripes— Gee, Randall... let me think! I've got a real big list to pick from."

"Take your time, boy. I know daydreamin's the only thing you ever do. Zoned out, slackin' work, face buried in that damn book you swear's gonna get you off this farm 'cause you're so capable and fancy and the rest of us are just shit-kickers—"

"That's how you see it, hm?" Dennis nodded. "Well... least I can fuckin' read then."

"I've gotten on just fine without. What's your point?"

"'Just fine?' Is that why you lost custody of your daughter?"

The words came out faster than Dennis was thinking. He knew immediately what would come next.

Randall swung hard, slugging his knuckle straight into Dennis' jaw and sending him reeling onto the freshly tilled soil. He landed with a grunt, feeling the hot pulse of pain spread immediately from his jaw to his neck.

"Fuck you, Whitaker."

A spark ignited in Dennis. The pain in his jaw, the soil tickling his nose; he was already falling off the tower, might as well enjoy the view on the way down.

He clasped at soil, squeezing it through his fingertips as he sat on his ass and turned to Randall, practically growling:

"No. Fuck you. Fuck—"

Before he could finish the sentence, Randall's boot smashed into his cheek, pressing him hard into the dirt. The boot lifted. A kick that landed into his wrist with a distinct popping noise. White-hot pain. Another kick to his rib, more bursts of pain shocking through his body. Another kick, and a stomp.

At some point, Dennis raised his good arm toward his face, trying pointlessly to block any strike he could as pleas of mercy spilled from his bloodied lips. The kicks and stomps didn't stop for several long seconds, but when they did, the gift of silence did not fall between them like he'd hoped it would. An intense ringing in his ears ruined that. He allowed his eyes to trail up into the night sky, tracing the hot-white stars which twinkled far off in space, losing track of how long he'd be laying there in the dirt as the night got colder.

 

 

The day had been busier than expected at the Robinavitch Health Center. The appointment log showed only a handful of scheduled patients sprinkled throughout the day, but a handful more of emergency patients had riled up that schedule quite fast. It was nonstop for Robby and Dana. Not that nonstop was abnormal for the two of them; they'd learned to work around such chaotic schedules and learned to work together very well.

"Have you checked on Linda in room D?" The blonde receptionist called out to her doctor as he hasted by.

Robby offered a nod.

"How are the Saunders' doin'? They up and gone already?" She inquired next.

"Prescription form is filled and signed. They'll take it to Franklin Pharmacy and that'll be that," he dismissed.

"Good, good, good," Dana stemmed, tapping the butt of her pen on the corner of her lip. "Did you pass Myrna on your way over here?"

"I did not," Robby shook his head as he ushered a visitor to the women's restroom. "Right down this hall, ma'am."

She nodded and walked toward the restroom door.

When Robby walked back to the front desk, Dana let out a sigh. "Okay. You've got one more scheduled appointment with Mr. Doug Driscoll, then we're on two hours of emergency window and then... done."

"When's Driscoll coming?"

"Uh, should be right about..." she dragged the words out, watching as Mr. Driscoll swung open the front door with impeccable timing. "Now."

"Mr. Driscoll, is it?" Robby extended a hand, and the large man took it, shaking firmly.

"Right. I'm in for heart issues."

"Heart issues, yes..." Robby nodded, guiding Mr. Driscoll further into the clinic. He turned to Dana and asked, "Room A's clear, yes?"

She nodded.

Robby ushered the patient into room A, the closest room to the main lobby, and sat him at the edge of the bed. Clipboard in one hand, pen in the other, he flicked open the arms of his reading glasses and shoved them on his face, scanning the patient info chart.

"So, Mr. Driscoll... tenderness in your left arm... frequent headaches... and more recently, a tingling sensation in your chest at night?"

"Sounds 'bout right. I mentioned to your receptionist lady that it felt like more of an emergency, so I expected y'all to get me in sooner—"

"Miss Evans follows a procedural questionnaire to help us sift through patients who need immediate attention and those who have the luxury of waiting a little longer."

Driscoll blinked.

"A heart issue ain't dire?"

Robby bounced his head left once, then right once, "Hm. Well, it could be. I'll have to assess that. But from what I gather with your sheet - and let this be good news - I don't know that we're dealing with anything immediately threatening."

Driscoll relaxed a little.

"Right?" Robby offered a professional smile. "But, to be sure we're taking every possible precaution, let me check your—"

From outside the closed door, Robby could hear Dana's voice yell a name.

"Whitaker? Oh my god!"

Her yell was muffled, but he heard the name clearly and she sounded distressed. His mind shut off completely for a second, then rebooted like a computer.

Robby turned to Driscoll and said, "Mr. Driscoll, could you give me a moment?"

Before allowing a response, he stood from his chair and slipped out of the room, closing the door behind him.

Halfway through the front door was Dennis Whitaker, arm slung around Dana as she walked him into the clinic. The boy was beaten bad with a bloodied face, cut lip, and dirtier-than-usual brown plaid shirt with jeans. Robby hustled right over, trying to grab Dennis' other arm for support, but the boy yelped. The doctor mentally noted a possible bone break of the left arm.

"Dana, room C, now," Robby directed.

He ran ahead, swinging the door open and clearing the bed, preparing a clipboard and fresh patient log. When Dana walked through the door with Dennis, Robby helped her sit the boy on the bed and leaned him back as he whined.

"Mr. Whitaker, this isn't what I meant by 'come back and see me sometime,'" Robby joked, but his voice was all concern.

The joke didn't land on Dennis; he was too focused on the pain.

"Go as I go, Miss Evans," Robby commanded, handing Dana the clipboard and a pen.

She nodded, face full of a kind of maternal worry.

"Vital signs," Robby called out.

He placed his index and middle finger on Dennis' dirtied neck, holding for several long seconds and counting silently under his breath.

"Strong pulse. Blood pressure," he whipped around and grabbed the sphygmomanometer, wheeling it to the bed and wrapping it around Dennis' upper right arm.

"118 over 78. Good," he spoke, and Dana wrote on the clipboard.

"Respiratory rate is fine."

Robby cut Dennis' plaid shirt straight down the middle with haste, pulling it apart and examining the chest. As he pressed gently, Dennis dully responded to pain around the ribs.

"No apparent internal bleeding, but head trauma is possible. Multiple contusions to face, chest, and ribs," Robby fired away each observation, and Dana kept up diligently.

Finally, Robby examined Dennis' left arm from top down. It wasn't until he reached Dennis' wrist that the boy began to squirm and whine in pain. The doctor examined closer, but carefully.

"Mr. Whitaker, could you do me a favor? Move the fingers in your left hand for me, okay?"

He certainly tried, but the pain that shot up his arm in doing so was visible on his face, so he stopped.

Robby nodded to himself.

"Probable left wrist fracture, I'll need an x-ray." Robby turned to Dana. "Man the front desk. If I'm not out in ten minutes, do me a favor and send Mr. Driscoll back to the waiting area and clear the room."

Dana nodded, a blonde strand of hair falling over her forehead before she tucked it back behind her ear and headed out of the room. Robby scanned Dennis' records, eyes landing on the section regarding patient allergies - Dennis had none.

Turning back to the battered boy on the bed, Robby spoke softer, "Mr. Whitaker, I'm going to have you take Tylenol with codeine to ease up some of that pain, then we're going to check your wrist. And as payment for my good work, you're gonna tell me what happened, alright?"

Dennis did not respond, but he looked a potent mix of frustrated and tired. A single tear cleared a trail of the dirt and blood down the corner of his eye and past his temple.

Robby's lip twitched seeing that tear. Grit his teeth, even.

"Let me, uh..." he spoke soft, standing to his feet and rubbing his gloved hands together anxiously, "go grab those painkillers."

Stepping out of the room quietly and closing the door as soft as possible, Robby walked on eggshells to the front desk, leaning into it and staring at Dana. They simply stood there for a moment, staring at one another. Then Robby shook his head.

"I don't think he'll tell me what happened," Robby sighed.

Dana offered a sympathetic expression, but chose her words carefully: "You do what you can. At a certain point it's up to the patient, right?"

Robby nodded, but he wasn't truly listening.

"It probably happened on that farm he works at," he mused.

Dana sighed. She could already sense where this was going.

"Robby, what's it to you?"

"I'm his doctor," Robby defended. "I ought to know what the hell happened to him—who happened to him."

"And what'll you do with that information besides let it eat away at you?"

"Report it. Report an assault—"

"You and I both know this is a little deeper than that, Robinavitch."

Robby paused. He blinked. He looked up at Dana, who looked back at him with that same stern look a mother would give to her troublesome child. Ironic, given she was a year younger than him.

"I've seen you around him. You care about the kid."

"Is that so bad?"

"Abso-fuckin-lutely not," she clarified enthusiastically. "It's a beautiful thing havin' the capacity to care for people. 'Specially in tryin' times like these. But, y'know, sometimes I worry you let things eat away at you. So I feel like it's my duty to keep you on track whenever possible."

Robby resigned, really hearing Dana's words. He offered an understanding nod.

"That is to say— go do your job and don't invite these kinds of things to eat you alive. If he decides to tell you his situation... well, bada-bing bada-boom. But otherwise, just doin' your part's enough, Robby. Doin' your part is all you can do."

Robby gave one final nod.

"I'll let it go," he assured.

Dana offered a soft smile. "Just do your part. It's what you taught me."

The doctor patted his hands on the desk and pushed away, standing upright again as he inhaled deep.

"Gotta go grab him some painkillers. I'll get the splint ready a little while after. Would you keep an eye on room D for me? Linda's still resting but she should be out of here before closing."

"I got you, doc."

Robby headed back and grabbed the bottle of Tylenol as well as the codeine. He dwelled on Dana's words, trying to mask the foreign frustration he'd felt while staring at Dennis. The boy was bloody, beaten, and trampled on; yet he didn't care to speak at all. Didn't care to mention what had happened. But it didn't matter. Robby couldn't force him to talk. He didn't want to force him to speak.

He stepped back out into the hallway with the pills in a small cup. Walked down the hall and gently knocked on door C. After no response, he quietly pushed open the door.

"Mr. Whitaker," Robby tried, but he was unresponsive.

An unexplainable, small bubble of anxiety floated up Robby's chest, leaving a burning, tingling agony which began to spread through his chest. He stepped over with haste, leaning down and hovering his palm just above Dennis' nostrils.

His breathing was normal.

As he stepped back, Robby squeezed his eyes shut and rubbed at his forehead.

That bubble of anxiety was certainly new to him. Anxiety in itself hadn't been a new feeling, but he couldn't recall a time when a patient had ever made him feel a tangible bubble of fear travel up his chest, and all Dennis Whitaker had done was fall asleep. Robby felt silly; he felt like he might be losing his mind—just a little.

 

 

When next he awoke, Dennis' eyes fluttered open, welcomed by the harsh fluorescent light just above him. He winced, noticing a distinct pounding in his head. A full-body dullness, like he'd been hit by a tractor. A big one.

Tilting his head, he assessed the room. But more alarmingly, he assessed the man sitting asleep in the chair opposite the room from him, head rested in his palm, propped lazily on the edge of the medicine counter.

It was Dr. Robby.

The pale, beaten young man could feel his heart pick up pace, but he did not move a muscle. He simply scanned the doctor up and down, locking in on his face and taking in the man's features. His tired eyes, already forming wrinkles despite being only thirty-nine. The stubble on his cheeks and chin. His unkempt, short brown hair which matched the color of his brows— even matched the color of his shirt.

Trying to get a more comfortable view from his bed, Dennis tried shifting his weight. The moment he tried flexing his left hand, a shock of hot pain slithered up his arm like a trail of lightning.

"Mercy," he hissed, wincing.

Robby sat straight up, fixing his posture, awake again.

"Mr. Whitaker," he managed to speak, but his voice was low and gravelly like he'd been asleep for a while. "Welcome back."

He rubbed the sleep from his eyes, blinking a few times as he adjusted and focused in on Dennis, who still lay on his back, staring right at him.

"Sorry," the bedridden man said softly.

Robby shook his head, explaining immediately: "No, no. I shouldn't be—" asleep watching over you like this. It's a little inappropriate. "I came in to give you painkillers, but you were out like a light by the time I got back."

Dennis stared. Silently.

"I'll take 'em if you still—"

"Yes. Yes," Robby shook his head to clear it, standing frantically and grabbing the cup of Tylenol and codeine, as well as a thermos of water, setting them at the table beside the head of the bed. "Let me help you sit up."

He slipped his hand under Dennis' head, lifting gently and using the other hand to prop two pillows just enough that his head was elevated. Robby grabbed the pills. Dennis held out his good hand as the doctor dumped the pills in his palm. He popped them in his mouth, then Robby unscrewed the thermos and offered to help.

"It's just water?" Dennis asked.

Robby snorted. "Correct."

What else would it have been?

The doctor pressed the metal rim of the thermos to Dennis' bottom lip, tilting slowly and allowing the boy to guide the thermos the rest of the way with his right hand. He sucked down several swigs, long after he'd swallowed the pills, too. Swig after swig, Dennis gulped until the thermos was all but empty, then gently pushed it away. Robby set it back down on the table, then took his seat back on the other side of the room, grabbing his clipboard.

"That should help a lot with the pain over the next few hours," he explained. "I also fixed a splint on your wrist. You've got a fracture, but... that's just about the extent of the damage. Your jaw got banged up a bit, same with your chest and your stomach, but those aren't so severe."

Dennis sighed.

"And, how's your head? Any ringing, any unusual sensation, any brain fog? That sort of thing?"

"Just a headache."

Robby nodded.

As silence filled the room, Robby tried desperately to cling to Dana's words. Tried desperately not to give into the urge and pry into what happened to Dennis. 'If he wants to tell you, he will,' she had said. It shouldn't matter so much to him. Yes, he was the boy's doctor, but at a certain point, one had to practice the art of letting go. The art of understanding what one cannot control. What could he control here? Could he force an explanation out of Dennis? And if he did, what — just as Dana had pointed out — good would that do for Robby? He'd report the incident to authorities and as usual in Franklin, nothing would get done. Nothing would change. Perhaps that was just it:

The only thing Robby could control here, in this situation, was what went on in his clinic. The outside world did not exist in here. Just him and his patient. Robby and Dennis.

"Mr. Whitaker?"

Dennis turned his head on the pillow to meet the doctor's eyes.

"Yes?"

"Was it that Randall fella?"

The question burned in the air between them. The room was silent. Dennis was silent.

Silent.

Robby wasn't going to press further.

"Mr. Howell let me go," Dennis broke the silence, trying to redirect the question.

The doctor's brow furrowed.

"Mr. Howell, my boss. The farm owner. He let me go when he brought me here. I was out in the fields for about a half hour before he found me. Said he'd drive me here so I don't die or... anythin' like that. In the car he told me he was lettin' me go. Wasn't gettin' along with Randall anymore and that was burdensome to-"

"Fired you?"

Dennis nodded.

"I apologize if I'm overstepping, Mr. Whitaker but why— how does this happen? Randall beats you bad enough you need emergency medical attention while he himself doesn't so much as show up, and you're the one walking away without a job? There's gotta be more to this than..." he stopped himself, reeling in his more personal thoughts.

"Well... I don't really blame Mr. Howell. I'd go to those lengths for my own son, too."

That made more sense to Robby. Not the good kind of sense, of course. But it made sense.

"His son, huh?"

Dennis chewed inside his cheek and nodded.

"Well," Robby chuckled, but it wasn't a humorous chuckle. It was dry. Sarcastic, almost. "Mr. Howell won't much need to worry about firing his son. Police'll do that for him when the kid's behind bars—"

"Please don't. Sir."

Robby blinked. "It's my duty as a doctor to report incidents like these."

"Please." 

"Mr. Whitaker, it is my job. If you were assaulted, I have to—"

"He has a daughter. He did this because he has a daughter and never gets to see her and I screwed it all up for him. He— he's got it bad enough as is." Dennis found a kind of confidence in that sentence, the kind no one was used to hearing from him. His pleas landed on Robby's ears like cries from an angel.

Robby didn't want to listen to Dennis, especially given he now had a hell of a reason to get the kid some payback for the lickings he'd endured.

"By the time my wrist heals, he'll prob'ly still have years to wait before he sees that little girl again. It's a fair enough trade."

"Doesn't sound like the son of a bitch deserves a daughter," Robby blurted, not really thinking.

"Probably not," Dennis agreed softly. "But she's everything he's fightin' for."

"...And who are you fighting for, Mr. Whitaker?"

"Well," he huffed, looking down at the splint on his wrist. He looked back up at Robby and joked: "Not myself, evidently."

Robby really tried not to chuckle. He wanted to remain serious. He felt he needed to be serious. But he'd been learning quickly that Dennis Whitaker had a way of disarming him. 

He shook his head as he resigned "damn you," with a faint smile stuck on his face.

As their shared, soft chuckles settled down and silence once again filled the room, Dennis cleared his throat and asked: "Is Miss Evans still here?"

"No, she headed home hours ago."

"Hours? What's the time?"

Robby checked his battered wristwatch.

"About a quarter 'till three."

"In the morning?" Dennis' eyes widened.

Robby nodded.

"What— am I supposed to be here so late? Shouldn't you... be done for the day?"

The doctor didn't have a clear answer to that.

He opted to deflect with a half-joke: "Can't have you losing your job and your life, Mr. Whitaker. That'd be kicking a dog while he's down, wouldn't it?"

"A fractured wrist?" Dennis chuckled. "This ain't the worst injury I can take."

"Oh-hoh," Robby laughed. "Read all those medical books and now you're doctoring the doctor!"

Dennis' ears flushed red, a sheepish smile creeping over his lips as he tried backing down: "I didn't mean it like that."

Robby knew he didn't mean it like that. 

"I'm sorry for keeping you here so late," Dennis apologized sincerely, like it was his fault Robby chose to give him medical care.

"Oh hush," Robby waved his hand dismissively.

A brief silence fell over them. Dennis didn't know what else to say. He wanted to apologize more. Wanted to make Robby understand how sorry he truly was for burdening him so consistently, but he felt confident by this point that Robby would just outright tell him to shut up.

It was Robby who finally broke the silence:

"Have you really been reading them? The books."

Dennis nodded excitedly, taking the question as an opportunity to ramble: "They've been keepin' me going these last few weeks. It's a little silly, but back home in Nebraska I had distant family that worked in medicine, and I'd hear about 'em from time to time... they seemed like real heroes to me. My family didn't have much money, though, so we didn't get books like those at home. And I certainly couldn't go to college and get them."

"Life in Nebraska," Robby mused, leaning back in the chair, resting his arms behind his head, and spreading his legs apart to relax. "That's quite a drive away from Franklin."

"Well I took a Greyhound."

"Running from home, right?" Robby asked. It might've been an overstep.

"...Yeah."

Robby wanted to ask why. He wanted to ask very badly. He was going to ask; he was just about to ask—

"It's three in the morning, Dr. Robinavitch," Dennis stated tenderly. "I think you should get some proper sleep."

Robby stopped contemplating his intrusive questions. He locked eyes with Dennis, silent for a moment, then he resigned.

"You might be right."

 

 

Dennis stayed in that bed for two days straight. Robby came in to talk with him about his life - and how horribly it had been going - every chance he seemed to get. The younger man could tell: the doctor was a very busy man. If it were anyone else, it would've seemed to Dennis as though Robby bit off more than he could chew taking on a job as the sole doctor of a clinic in Middle Tennessee; but this was Michael Robinavitch. For a man under forty, his experience was intimidating, his efficiency unreal. And even as he bounced from room to room over the course of those two long days, caring for patients with all kinds of ailments, he still found time to pop in and fill the seat in Dennis' room. He still found time to get to know the younger man. Being cared for in a clinic - in a clean room without loose nails and warped wooden floorboards, Dennis felt like he could breathe.

But those two days had now passed.

Dana offered a broad, motherly hug, squeezing Dennis gently, careful not to pressure his splinted wrist. "It feels like I'm sendin' you off to war."

Dennis chuckled, eyes darting to Robby, whose own face was painted with a faint little smirk.

"I'm serious!" Dana defended lightheartedly. "Wish the circumstances were wildly different, but I really liked havin' you around the clinic these past two shifts."

The young man nodded, willing himself to crack a joke. "I'll be sure to break a finger or two so you two can keep me longer next time."

"No, no, no," Robby chuckled, shaking his head and waving his arms in a cross. "You've done enough to yourself these past few weeks, hm, kid?"

Shrugging, Dennis pushed the joke further. "I mean... I think I could top a wrist fracture."

"And when you do, I ain't stickin' around to hear you whine about the pain again, Whitaker!" Dana belly-laughed, pointing an accusatory finger at him. "Damn near drained all the Tylenol in this place just to keep you from cryin' about it!"

"Won't happen again, ma'am," Dennis waved a goodbye as he slipped out the front door of the clinic, held open by Robby.

"Better not," Dana said one last time for good measure - just in case Dennis wasn't actually joking - then waved back to him. "Take care of yourself for me, kid."

Dana and Robby exchanged a glance.

"I'll be back within the hour to shut things down here. If Collins gets here early, have her wait in the back, alright?"

The blonde lady nodded at the doctor, a dutiful expression melting upon her previously lighthearted face.

"Drive safe," she offered, spinning around to get back to the front desk.

The front door of the clinic shut behind Robby, cool night air kissing at his cheeks. Dennis stood patient like a little dog just next to the road, eyeing Robby - averting his gaze frantically when Robby eyed him back.

"Hell," the older man groaned, stuffing his hands in the pockets of his dirty brown jacket as he stepped over to Dennis. "This damn town gets colder every year, it feels like."

Dennis snorted. "Still doesn't beat Nebraskan winters."

Robby nudged the younger man softly with his elbow. "I'm from even farther up North, Mr. Whitaker. You can't beat me in that game."

"Well how'm I supposed to know that? I told you damn-near my life story these past few days," Dennis chuckled. He spoke meekly—jokingly, but he really did wish Robby told more about himself. "All I know about you is you're loaded and you're stressed."

Robby burst into a belly laugh.

"That's about all there is to know."

"And... y'don't have to call me 'Mr. Whitaker,'" Dennis timidly nudged the taller man in return.

Robby turned to face him, eyebrow raised.

Elaborating, Dennis said, "You can just call me Dennis. If you want. If— if that's..." He didn't know what he was going to say, so he ended with: "yeah."

Cocking his head, Robby hummed: "Well..." and trailed off for only a moment. "Dennis," he tried the name—carefully. "Let's get you home before we both freeze to death, and then—"

And then what?

Robby was hit with the weight of reality. There was some unspoken, intimate buzz between the two of them, and he'd nearly fueled it. 'And then I'll tell you all about me,' he might've said. 'And then I'll come inside your house and we'll talk all about my past,' he might've also said, had he not stopped himself just then. That wasn't abnormal, though. It wasn't wrong to make a friend like Dennis and open up about your life. It shouldn't have been wrong, but it certainly felt like there was more to it than that, so Robby shut off that part of his mind like an RCA television box.

"Then..." Dennis inquired, looking up at Robby with a hint of confusion - but a heaping of juvenile anticipation.

Robby pulled a hand from his pocket, placing it on the back of Dennis' cold neck. The warmth of his palm nearly made the younger man melt. "Let's get you home, Mr. Whitaker."

The drive to the younger man's little house was - surprisingly to Dennis - a bit awkward; Robby's modest Mercury Monterey rumbled and wobbled on the poorly maintained backroads of Franklin. The car itself was stuffy, so Dennis kept the passenger window down to breathe in the cool night air. Robby - when entirely certain the other wasn't paying attention - would steal quick glances at Dennis, trying to gauge what might've been going through his head. Robby himself had quite a lot going through his head; he'd heard so many stories from Dennis over the last two days, and he knew the boy wouldn't be able to maintain his life here in Tennessee without some sort of miraculous intervention. Dennis was poor, and Dennis was injured. He was just let go at his job and likely had no other options but to return to a home in Nebraska that - as far as Robby had learned the last two days - was most unwelcoming.

Robby had the means to be a miraculous intervention.

"Left turn up ahead," Dennis spoke soft, voice nearly drowned by the whistling wind gushing through the passenger window.

They reached the short dirt driveway to the younger man's house only minutes later. Robby parked the car and hasted out of his seat to open Dennis' door, grabbing his good hand and helping him to his feet. Gently, the older man closed the passenger door, standing awkwardly, not quite sure what else to say. Keep it professional.

"Well, Mr. Whitaker," his voice was low and gravelly.

"This is me," Dennis said. He offered an unreadable smile. "Thank you for everything. I... really, I wish you'd let me pay you for your efforts—"

"Quit that talk. If I needed your money I'd have asked for the check hours ago."

"Check," Dennis chuckled. "All I got's a wad of cash I keep under my mattress." He inhaled sharp. "Emergency money."

"Ah," Robby nodded. "Will that wad hold you over 'till you find new work around Franklin?"

Dennis did not immediately respond.

"Dunno. I only managed to get my job at the farm 'cause of a trashed paper advertisement. Found its way to Nebraska somehow. If you can believe it."

"Funny how that works," Robby chuckled, sparing occasional glances at the depressing house behind Dennis. "Funny how life works."

"More likely than not... I'll go back home." Dennis looked behind him - at the house he currently called home - then decided to correct himself, turning back to Robby and saying, "Home, home."

"Nebraska," Robby spoke like he knew that word was coming—spoken almost like he dreaded hearing that word. 

"Mhm." Dennis nodded. He spoke soft and shy as he said: "So... y'know. Chances are, I won't be seein' you again. You... sure you don't want the money?"

The older man rolled his eyes.

"I don't want your money, Mr. Whitaker."

Dennis pursed his lips, nodding awkwardly.

"Well," he said, taking a step backward. "Thank you for everything, Dr. Robby."

"Take care of yourself, kid."

When Dennis stepped inside the little house, a sense of emptiness washed through him; there wasn't much left for him in Franklin. Or rather - there was an invisible, horrid force pulling him back to Nebraska: the force of financial instability, the force of family and all that's said about how it meant everything. Family did —perhaps— mean everything, because Dennis felt lost without it. Perhaps it wasn't family itself that pulled this aimlessness from his soul, though; he did feel quite at home in the clinic.

"Oh," Dennis' brows furrowed as his eyes landed on the sink-washed pot that had held Robby's chicken pot pie. He'd meant to hand it back to the man before they parted. "Shit."

Dennis grabbed it, hasted to the door, swung it open, and... Robby's beige car was already gone.

The night was quiet, the driveway empty. He tapped his fingernails on the edge of the pot as it hung lazily from his good hand, gnawing at his lip, allowing the cool air to chill him to his very bones. He simply stood there, drowning in the emptiness—the silence. But it wasn't all silent; Tennessee was never truly silent. Trees whistled like ghosts in the distant darkness. Whippoorwills chirped at one another, back and forth like conversations between night-shifters. Even crickets, as uncommon as it was to see them leaping around, aided the symphony of noise which filled the otherwise deafening quiet of the world around Dennis. 

What an odd thing, one's crippling sense of unbelonging having been remedied by the sounds of nature - if only for that moment.

 

 

At two in the morning - right on the dot - Dennis was ripped from sleep by the pounding of a hand on his front door. It nearly rattled his whole house, but that wasn't necessarily saying much considering the house was held together by strings and a prayer. He ripped the thin blanket off him and scurried to his feet, flinching when he put too much weight on his splinted wrist.

"Shit," he hissed.

The struggle of shoving clothes on was a bit too big of an ask given he had only one good hand, especially given he had someone knocking on his front door so late in the night. It was likely an emergency, so Dennis opted to forego pants and a shirt entirely. His underwear would suffice. He shuffled to the front window, trying to peer through as three more knocks pounded like a heartbeat into the door. He couldn't tell who it was, so he decided to chance it. Perhaps it was, in its own way, his form of suicide—opening a door for what could have been a killer. 

As the knocks continued, Dennis spoke through the grogginess in his voice. "Alright, alright, I'm—"

As the door swung open, Dr. Robinavitch steeled himself, retracting his hand from the door.

"Dr. Robby?"

"You can come work at the clinic," he declared, apparently very unaware that it was two in the morning. Or perhaps he was aware, and simply did not see the insanity of banging on a person's front door at such an hour.

"What?"

"I thought about your situation. I apologize for waking you—and your friend."

"My friend?" Dennis squinted, confused.

"You live with a friend, yes?"

Oh, that lie. The one he'd told the day Robby saved him from heat exhaustion so that he could get home without feeling like he owed the man.

"...No," he sighed, the tips of his ears flushing red. "I haven't got any friends, Dr. Robby. Certainly none that I live with."

Robby nodded like he knew Dennis had lied about that, but had simply let him. And, like it wasn't a big deal at all, he continued as normal:

"I'm sorry for being here so late," he was sincere. "I just couldn't chance you leaving Franklin before I at least offered."

He seemed to finally notice Dennis' lack of a shirt or pants, because just as his eyes very briefly trailed away from the younger man's face, they bounded right back up, unwilling to look down again.

"Offered me a job... at a clinic?" Dennis shook his head. "Sir, I... I really do appreciate the gesture, but I'm," he cleared his throat, wiping the sleep from his eyes. "I'm no good. I'm no doctor, and I certainly don't have a degree of any kind, or a—"

"Dana isn't a doctor, either. You don't need to be. I can find work for you. She and I can show you the ropes. It's all rudimentary. Just tracking patient logs, assigning rooms, helping around when necessary..." he drifted off, letting up on his attempt to convince Dennis, who - very visibly - was overwhelmed. "Christ. I apologize, Mr. Whitaker."

The younger man's brow furrowed, but he shook his head. "No, I..." he let the words hang in the air, unspoken.

A hopeful expression flashed Robby's face, but it was well contained.

"You've been reading the books I gave you, haven't you, Mr. Whitaker?"

"I have," he responded timidly.

"Have you got any interest at all in the medical field?"

"Interest," Dennis parroted, like he was analyzing if that was the correct word for whatever he was feeling. "It's more—almost more like a..." he looked up, eyes meeting Robby's for the first time. "I need to do something. For others."

An unreadable expression settled on Robby's eyes.

"Workin' on a farm for people who don't give a rat's ass about others," he elaborated. "It just felt empty. I know what choice I made, runnin' from Nebraska. I know I don't have the right to ask for more, but I wouldn't have even known what more to ask for. I'd never seen... any of the world outside my home, really. Never thought to imagine it."

Robby stood still, eyes fixated on the shivering younger man standing in the doorway.

"But the books," he huffed, almost like a chuckle. "Those books were like a north star for me these last few weeks. 'Oh wow, there's a whole world of people out there who spent their whole lives researching and helping people and doing right by the world,' I thought to myself," Dennis spoke.

He spoke with a kind of confidence that Robby hadn't seen from him in the short time he'd known him. It wasn't much, but there was a clear confidence on his tongue as he spoke about those damned books.

"But you're not well-off," Robby surmised. "You can't afford to dip into a world like this."

Dennis nodded, the passion deflating. "Right."

"The clinic could be an opportunity for you," Robby lured. "I can't— well, I can't say for certain if it'll be fruitful for you long-term. But I can say for certain it'll keep you afloat in Franklin. If... you'd like to stay here."

If he wanted to keep from having to go back to a family that did not want him, was what Robby didn't dare say.

Dennis chewed at his bottom lip, eyes fixated on the whistling grass just beyond Robby's figure. His mind raced. He didn't imagine an opportunity would arise at all after he'd been graced by the chance to work on the farm. Once that welcome had been overstayed, he thought he deserved the outcome. He thought it was as simple as fate. Perhaps God had meant for him to be pulled back to Nebraska—to face his father. Perhaps God wanted him to overcome the sinful feelings nipping at his loins, to make him a man like his family wanted. Perhaps he shouldn't mettle with whatever plan was laid out for him by whatever laws dictated the world in which he lived. Perhaps he wasn't meant to meet Robby, or to take the books, or the thermos, or the medical care without compensation. Perhaps he wasn't meant to take the chicken pot pie home and eat it.

But alas, Dennis Whitaker was hungry. 

He looked up into Robby's warm eyes, nodding shyly as he spoke:

"I'd like to consider it."

Chapter End Notes

Chapter 4: Tomorrow Is My Turn

Chapter Summary

As his composure begins to falter, Robby shares a vulnerable conversation with Dennis. Days later, Dennis discovers his secret.

Chapter Notes

Chapter 4's song is "Tomorrow Is My Turn" by Nina Simone on her 1965 album, "I Put A Spell On You"

See the end of the chapter for more notes

"Freedom Rider in C is stable. Injuries aren't too serious," Robby declared as he passed Dana at the front desk, slapping his clipboard onto the wooden surface and hasting down the hall.

"How 'bout the one in room D?" Dana called out.

Robby turned, but continued walking backward. He shrugged as he spoke. "About to find out. If he gets any worse, though... I'm sending him to Williamson."

Dana whistled. "That bad, huh?"

Robby clicked his tongue, an uncertain look settling on his face. Turning again and swinging open the door to room D, he closed it behind him without having responded to Dana.

Dana shook her head, eyes fixated on the door for only a moment too long before returning to her appointment log and going down the list. She stepped out from behind the desk and prepared herself - mentally, of course - for a round of check-ins. Stepping in front of room A, she took a deep breath in, then pressed the door open.

"How ya doin' Mr. Driscoll?" She greeted casually.

"I've been waiting for—"

"You won't be waiting much longer, I promise you," She raised her palm soothingly. "We've got a full house right now, and Dr. Robby's seein' patients by severity. I just wanted to let you know he's here, and he's gonna see you as soon as he can."

"A failing heart is severe," Mr. Driscoll grumbled. His face was red, contrasting against his pale skin and light hair.

"Okay," Dana huffed, "your heart isn't failing. We've been through this so many times now. If anything, this points more toward medical health anxiety, which—"

"I don't care if it takes five hundred appointments for you to find out what the fuck is wrong with me—"

As the man spoke, Dana slowly began shutting the door.

From the other side, she hollered into the frame with a facetious smile: "Which is why Dr. Robby's happy to see you when he's done caring for his other patients!"

Dana shifted her feet and stomped over to room B, guarded by a police officer.

"You need help with him?" The man in uniform asked, cocking an eyebrow.

Dana shook her head, rubbing her brow with the back of her hand.

"He's never been any real trouble, officer. Just a loud dog with no bite."

The officer nodded, moving out of the doorway, allowing Dana to gently press it open and peek her head through.

"Myrna, you doin' okay, baby?"

"Time of my life," the old lady remarked.

"Deep breaths," Dana reminded, eyes landing on the handcuff keeping the woman from leaving the bed. "You ain't goin' through this alone."

Myrna didn't respond, but she did offer Dana an uncharacteristically soft smile, barely visible as the blonde lady closed the door and returned to the front desk. She wrote on Myrna's patient file: 25th of August, 1961. Patient arrived accompanied by law enforcement. Officer Dwayne Greenhalgh. Sustained blunt trauma to back of skull in domestic altercation.

She stopped to process the words she was writing on the paper, then sighed.

"Why the long face, Miss Dana?" Dennis Whitaker asked softly as the front door closed behind him. He pulled on the shoulder strap of his small duffel while waddling toward the desk, but made stiff eye contact with the officer standing in front of room B. "And... what's he here for?"

"Oh," Dana groaned theatrically, slumping on the surface of the desk as she joked, "just shoot me, kid. Put me out of my misery!"

Dennis lugged the duffel from his shoulder, tucking it in the small space behind the desk and pulling out his pens. He chuckled hearing her humorous, dramatic groans.

"If I did, who'd cover the front desk in the mornings?"

Dana snorted. "Would it kill you to wake up before noon?"

The sarcasm was lost on him. "I don't think so," Dennis answered genuinely.

"It's Myrna," Dana said, pointing lazily at the officer with her thumb. "She actually killed the motherfucker."

All capacity for attention in the young man's brain focused laser-sharp on Dana.

"Myrna White killed her husband?" Dennis hissed in a whisper, leaning in close to her.

"Yup," Dana nodded, lips pursed. "And somehow I feel even worse for her now. Girl's gonna be locked up 'till she kicks the bucket. No doubt about it."

The young man's eyebrow twitched, and he processed what felt like... mourning, almost. It had now been almost a full year since he took up Robby's offer to work in the office. Naturally, he saw Myrna a lot. A lot. Truthfully, she scared him - in the 'creepy old lady hitting on a mid-twenties young man' - sort of way, but his fear was, nonetheless, palpable. Even so, he'd grown to like her, and she surely liked him, too. Knowing that her suffering, on one front, was over—no more abuse from a man who kept getting away with it, that was a nice feeling. It was just... the feelings underneath that. The feelings that reminded Dennis she was going to jail now, probably for the rest of her life. No more Myrna White.

"Gosh..." he sighed, blinking the shock from his eyes and - slowly - coming back down to reality.

"Yeah," Dana nodded. "Yeah."

"What's this?" Dennis asked, tapping the pad of his index finger on a wadded patient log resting next to Myrna's own patient log.

"Driscoll's back," the woman elaborated.

"Again? Isn't this the," he counted with his fingers, "fourth time this month?"

"You know how he is. Givin' himself a heart attack about a heart attack that he never actually has."

"Which..." Dennis looked at the four rooms of the clinic.

"He's in A." Dana pointed. "Still waiting for Robby, but he's tied down in C and D with two Freedom Riders that took a beatin' after a crash on the I-65 headin' into Nashville."

"Goodness..." Dennis huffed. It seemed like he was in for a very busy day.

"I tell you what," Dana agreed. "Seems like every day it's somethin' new in this country. But anyway, yeah. Robby's jugglin' C and D, Driscoll's in A, Myrna's in B until she's cleared of any potential head stuff, and that leaves you with..." she scanned the patient log for the day. "An appointment with Ms. Saunders. An hour from now."

"Ms. Saunders?"

"Real nice lady. Her son—not so much."

Robby stomped out into the main lobby, stopping at the front desk and rubbing his face raw with the palms of his hands. Leaning into the desk, he shook his head and inhaled sharp before speaking.

"Our guy in D needs to get to Williamson. I don't have the resources here to keep him stable."

"Take it up with Whitaker," Dana pointed. "My shift's nearly over, Robby."

Robby met eyes with Dennis, who - per usual - looked like a little mouse, cornered in its cage by the sheer chaos of the clinic.

"Whitaker," the older man almost barked. "I need you to cover the front while I'm gone. I won't be more than twenty minutes because I've got three other patients I'm juggling, but I need you to keep them from losing their marbles having to wait a while longer, can you do that?"

Robby was speaking a mile a minute.

"I— Dana mentioned the guy in D is a Freedom Rider?" Dennis asked, but he kept his voice a low whisper so as not to let the cop across the room hear. 

Robby rolled his eyes—began nodding repeatedly, like he was losing his marbles. 

"Yes, yes, yes. Focus, Whitaker. Are you prepared to cover the front for twenty minutes? Can you do that for me?"

"Yes—"

"Thank you." Robby swiveled, stomping back toward the door to room D.

"Is that a good idea, sir?" Dennis asked with a raised voice, but he tried his damned best to sound kind.

The older man stopped and whipped his head back to eye Dennis.

The younger man lost several pounds of confidence when they met eyes, but elaborated anyway. "It's... just, what if they..."

"I don't have a damn choice, Whitaker. Off my ass. Get to work." The words came out of Robby's mouth like hot fangs—like he was biting Dennis.

Frankly, the young receptionist was taken aback. He'd certainly seen Robby in a tizzy before. It wasn't common, but on a particularly busy day without time for breaths, he could lose his composure. This? What he'd just said and how he'd said it to Dennis? It was like he was a wild animal defending his food for the winter. Even Dana was at a loss for words, lips parted just a little in shock. 

And if Dennis Whitaker thought it couldn't get any more humiliating, Randall fucking Howell came crashing through the front door, arm slung around his father's neck, hopping on one foot while the other looked dark and swollen. Dennis only briefly tore his eyes away from the sight to see if Robby was still in the room. Perhaps he'd want to apologize. But he wasn't there; he was already clearing room D. That wasn't so bad, though. Clearly Randall would need the room as soon as possible—would need some very strong painkillers, too.

"Oh, what the fuck?" Randall seethed viciously through the pain. "What the fuck is this milquetoast doin' here?" He and his father laid eyes on Dennis like they were seeing a ghost—one they both hated.

Dennis was, in a way, a ghost to them; he hadn't seen them since the day Randall's father fired him from the farm nearly a year ago.

Dana stepped out from behind the counter and scooted a wheelchair over to the two men. "Whitaker, come help me with this."

Dennis scurried over, helping Dana to heave the traumatized man into the wheelchair, trying to ignore the fact that it was Randall. The man's father stepped back, not saying a word; he must've been in some form of shock.

"Randall, I need you to tell me what happened, kid. Okay?" Dana ordered, taking place behind the wheelchair and hauling him off without another word to Dennis. She knew not to burden him with caring for the man who'd nearly costed him his life in Franklin that last autumn.

 

 

One thing Dennis loved about working at the clinic was that it simply did not matter how quickly time passed. The clock on the wall in the front lobby made it difficult not to track, and when he did track it, of course it went by slower. But it didn't matter. He quite enjoyed working in the clinic, even on a day like that one, where all in the span of the first five minutes of walking in for his shift, his boss chewed him out and his old co-worker who'd beaten him half to death barged in, then he spent the next three hours bouncing back and forth between four rooms full of trauma patients and regulars. Dana had stayed to help him deal with Randall and the others until Robby returned. She even stayed a while after that just to give Robby an intense scolding for the way he spoke to Dennis, but she didn't do it until Dennis was outside on his break. She figured it would be a bit belittling for him to hear her do that in his stead.

After Dana left, Robby and Dennis worked without addressing the older man's earlier outburst. They skirted around one other in the hall or in the front lobby, and speaking - even professionally, as required by their work - was stiff and awkward.

The funny thing about time was that it actually, somehow, passed dramatically faster while Dennis was so focused on what might - or might not have - been going through Michael Robinavitch's head. It was a fun little game he'd learned to play over the last year of his life working at the clinic. Make no mistake, he was whole-heartedly uninclined to act upon or even entertain that lustful spark in his loins he'd felt when he first met Robby, but there always remained a nagging, nipping pull in the back of his mind—a pull that led him toward the older man. Last year in 1960 the pull was simply attraction, was loneliness, was... hunger. But it had mutated now; he was utterly awestruck by Robby's sheer skill, both as a doctor and as a people person, and how could he not be? He'd been watching the man work firsthand for quite some time now.

Those three hours were quiet - relatively speaking - but they were fast. Before he knew it, Dennis was sending Randall - his final patient, thankfully - out for the evening.

"Per Dr. Robby," Dennis recited as he read Randall's finalized chart to the man, who now stood on crutches with a cast over his foot. "Aspirin should be fine enough for the pain in the coming weeks. If you need, though, come in and he can consider prescribing codeine. If the inside of your cast gets wet, come back as soon as possible and—"

"Doc already told me all that," Randall interrupted rather rudely. "Just tell me when I can come get it off."

Dennis blinked, inhaled, then continued. "I scheduled you for two Fridays from today, noon sharp. It should be a quick appointment—Dr. Robby just needs to make sure your bones are healing nicely and adjust your plan accordingly. If you start havin' any severe pain, numbness, or blue coloration, you'll need to come back immediately. Same if you notice signs of infection like, uh... abnormal redness, pus, or if you catch a fever."

"Guessin' he can't work any while he's healin'?" Randall's father - and boss - laughed like it was a joke. But having worked for the man, even just for a few weeks, Dennis couldn't be too sure if it was actually a joke.

"No, sir. He's lucky it was only a metatarsal base fracture, but that's still gonna need time to heal properly. He shouldn't put pressure of any kind on his foot, and if that changes in two weeks, after his appointment, we'll let you know."

Randall shot him a look. A look of - dare Dennis assume - gratitude. If that wasn't enough, the man with the cast on his foot gave a subtle little head nod at the young receptionist.

Dennis returned the nod, then looked away—awkwardly.

After Mr. Howell took Randall out, Dennis locked the door behind them, inhaling deep and holding it as he walked back to the front desk. He finished his last duties for the evening while listening to the white noise of Robby shuffling around somewhere in the back of the clinic. When all was done, Dennis slung the strap of his little duffel over his shoulder and stepped over toward the front door.

"Whitaker," Robby said softly, guiding Dennis to turn around by placing a firm hand on the younger's shoulder.

Dennis flinched, but composed himself just as quickly. "Sir?" He cleared his throat and pointed his thumb at the door. "I finished organizing next week's appointment log. I... was just gonna head out for the night."

Robby nodded. His hand was still on Dennis' shoulder; it lingered, warming the skin underneath his plaid shirt. "Of course— yeah."

Their eyes met, and for a moment, they just looked at one another.

"But," Robby broke the silence. "I wanted to offer to teach you a bit tonight—like you've been asking. Some of the, uh..." he found the right words, "rural basics. I realize this past year has mostly just been a routine of tossing you the busy work and giving very little room for real growth, so..." he trailed off.

Dennis shook his head. "No, no. I've learned a lot. And being here is keepin' me in Franklin anyway. It's... it's been nice."

The older man rubbed the back of his head with his palm, letting his other hand off of Dennis' shoulder, perching on his own hip. He looked around for a moment, then his warm, brown eyes returned to the younger's as he spoke: "Have you got time tonight? Any early Saturday plans tomorrow?"

The younger, shorter man did not immediately respond, but he blinked. Then he nodded, timid as usual. "I've got time."

The older man nodded, and it looked to Dennis like he was trying to fight off a subtle little smile from the corners of his lips.

"Shall we?" Robby gestured to the front door, which confused Dennis.

"Sir?"

"I've got a home visit to run to. I figure this could be a good chance for you to see how to remain resourceful in these parts of the country."

"Is resourcefulness necessary if I..." Dennis began, but what he wanted to say next was pretentious, so he stopped himself.

"If you?" Robby urged.

The younger sighed. "If my long-term goal were to practice in a more... urban setting?"

The doctor hummed, like he was excited to lecture: "Resourcefulness is especially important in a real hospital, Whitaker," he taught as he opened the door and held it for Dennis, the two men walking to Robby's car in the warm, nighttime summer air. "What is a doctor's use at all if he can't manage supply as related to his patients' needs?"

Dennis couldn't argue with that, so he let the summertime cicadas speak in his stead, filling the silence between them as Robby opened the passenger door for Dennis, lugging his medical bag in the floorboard at the younger's feet.

"And, pardon if this is a little personal, Whitaker, but," The man began as he slammed his own door shut, turning the car on and pulling onto the rubbly road. "I think this is the first time I've heard you outright admit interest in the medical field beyond working the desk in my clinic. It's... nice to hear."

"I'm sure I've made that clear before," Dennis defended half-heartedly, blinking as he stared out the window and imagined all the times he may or may not have stated his long-term goals to Robby. Or Dana, for that matter.

"I'd gathered you had some sort of interest, what with you reading damn near every medical book in my house this past year," Robby chuckled. "But you've never opened up about what that meant to you."

"Oh," Dennis sank into his seat, unsure where else to take the discussion.

"So?" Robby inquired.

"What?"

"You want to practice in a big city, hm? That's what you said, yes?"

"Well— yes. I suppose," he admitted. "But it sounds a little silly sayin' that aloud. I think I missed my window for college, and I—"

The older man snorted. "You haven't missed your window for college, kid. There's no such thing. The only thing that could stop such pursuit is time and money, which... admittedly isn't easy to find, but if you find the drive..." Robby trailed off.

Dennis looked over at the man. "How'd you find your drive?"

Robby's brow furrowed; he debated how to respond. Robby rarely ever opened up - even to Dana, who he'd been working with for years at this point - about his life before Tennessee. But frankly, he'd asked and heard so much about Dennis' upbringing in the past year that it almost felt owed; he felt as though he owed a bit of context about who he was to Dennis Whitaker. Not to mention he'd just, earlier in the day, nearly mauled the kid for no good reason and was now trying to make it up to him in whatever way he was capable. 

"Whitaker..." he sighed, finally deciding to open up to him a little. "I was born with heavy pockets. Even with it just being my mother and I, it wasn't hard to find the time or the money to do... whatever I wanted to do. It just so happened that the people I met set me on a path to be the kind of man that helps those who can't help themselves."

Dennis sat still and he listened. Robby glanced at him to check he hadn't fallen asleep, then continued as he drove:

"I knew pretty early on what I wanted to do with my life, so I went to school. Did my time. Was taught by a man so brilliant—so skilled in his field... you'd think he was from another planet. Spent thousands of hours honing myself for what I thought would be... oh, I don't know—some kind of, 'You've made it! You're helping people, and that's all she wrote! Work until you retire!'"

"Is that... not what you found?"

"The second job I took after my residency was up in Nashville. Ascension Saint Elijah Hospital East. Moved all the way down from Pennsylvania to take up the job, and it... shattered every false reality I'd built about the world. About this country."

"Wow, that bad?" the younger's eyebrows raised. "What, um... what was the first job you took after residency?"

Robby didn't respond. Instead, he shook his head. Dennis backed down immediately, returning his gaze to the dark forest outside the car window, barely lit by the last flames of the evening setting sun.

"Our, uh," the older man redirected the conversation, "the patient we're visiting tonight is Miss Ella Jones."

"The lady you see once a month?"

"Correct. She's an elderly woman—sweet as pecan pie. Lives with her daughter and - for the most part - gets care from her. I come in to check on her every now and then; I check medication, progression of the ailment, quality of life, etcetera. It's a pretty simple thing most nights when I visit. It'll be good for you to see."

"What ailment?" Dennis asked with something of a juvenile tone.

"Heart failure," Robby nodded. "What we'll do is we'll go in, strike up some conversation and observe her behavior, do a simple physical exam, and then check her medication. She takes chlorothiazide and digitalis nightly—we check to make sure she's doing so as instructed and adjust according to the progression of her ailment."

"Not too complicated," Dennis surmised. 

"Not too complicated," Robby agreed, a little smirk pulling at the edge of his lip—he liked when Dennis was confident.

 

 

"It's good to see you, Michael," the woman said as she pulled Robby into a hug while Dennis watched, awkward and stiff on the porch, just behind the older man.

"Likewise," Robby offered, then pulled away and gestured to his young mentee. "Dennis, this is Samantha Jones."

The patient's daughter. Dennis was stricken by her beauty. She had long, messy brown hair. Her bangs were held back by a black headband, and she had tired eyes like duffel bags rested on her face, but despite the unkempt exterior, she was the kind of person anyone could tell was beautiful.

"Hello," Dennis offered with a sheepish smile. He extended his hand, and she took it, shaking firmly.

"Samantha, this is the most recent addition to my team, Dennis Whitaker," he smiled, gaze bouncing back and forth between the two of them. "If it's alright with you and Miss Ella, I'd like him to shadow me while I visit tonight."

"Long as he don't ask for food," she patted Dennis' shoulder, forcing a small flinch from him. "I hardly made enough dinner for mama and I tonight. I'd honestly forgot you were comin', Michael. Or I'd have made extra."

Robby shook his head and joked, "He and I will eat later. His treat."

The two of them laughed, so Dennis forced a laugh himself, observing the unspoken connection his mentor seemed to have with the woman.

When they settled their rather domestic laughter, Robby cleared his throat and asked, "How's she doing this month, Sam?"

Samantha nodded, a restrained smile on her face. "She's doin' real good this month. Been takin' meds like you asked her to. She's not been in as much pain. Even been gettin' more sleep this past week, too. It's really been a good month."

"Oh, come on. I don't believe you!" Robby joked. "You must be hiding her. Let me in. Let me go see Miss Ella."

Samantha giggled, ushering Robby in, who in turn ushered Dennis in front of him with a firm hand on the nape of the younger man's neck. He did not take his warm, large hand off of Dennis' nape until he'd been guided into the living room, where Ella sat in her recliner.

"Ella, you still, kickin', girl?" Robby sat his medical bag on the couch next to the recliner as Ella turned off the box television across the room.

"God doesn't want me yet, baby!" She cheered softly, inviting the man in for a hug with open arms. She groaned and patted his back, saying, "It's good to see you, sweetie."

"I hear you're doing real well this month, hm?" Robby asked, pulling away first.

As Samantha wandered off somewhere else in the house, Dennis took a seat in a chair opposite the recliner, keeping his hands in his lap all polite and timid.

"I'm doin' as good as a woman my age can do. Minus the heart failure," she joked at the end, then glanced at Dennis. "Who's this shy little farmer you dragged into my daughter's house, Robby?"

A surprisingly astute description given Dennis didn't - at least, in his mind - look like much of a farmer anymore.

"This is Dennis Whitaker. I'm doing a little bit of mentoring for him. Do you mind if he hangs around me while I check on you tonight?"

"Do you?" She asked, holding back a gravelly laugh.

"Now what's that supposed to mean?" Robby cocked an eyebrow.

"You're a loner! I've never seen you work with anyone but that sweet young woman at the clinic!" Ella slapped Robby's arm playfully. "But don't you go thinkin' I'm judgin'! He's a lucky boy, gettin' to work for such a sharp guy."

Dennis smiled, meeting Robby's eyes as the man turned to meet his and say, "I'm pretty lucky myself. He's a hard worker."

That... felt pointedly like an apology for his outburst earlier. Dennis felt his heart flutter a little, using all his might to force down the red flush that threatened to creep onto his cheeks. He bit down hard on the inside of his cheek, but kept his smile up.

"Now Miss Ella, I'm gonna ask you my usual questions while Dennis does a simple physical exam. That alright?"

"Go for it, sweetie."

Robby waved Dennis over, and the younger man frantically scurried to the recliner, standing awkwardly.

"Simple physical to start, Whitaker. Pulse, blood pressure, and respiratory rate."

Dennis obeyed, reaching for the medical bag and grabbing the tools needed.

"I heard you slept well last night?" The doctor asked.

"Only woke up once," Ella elaborated. "Usual coughin' fit."

"Ah. So you've been getting better sleep in general this month?"

"Honest to God," she shook her head like she was in disbelief. "Guess our prayers were answered. And at that—who's to say I ain't gonna shake off this old heart issue?"

That wasn't a joke—Dennis dully noted the tone in her voice. He - and certainly Robby, too - knew one could not reverse of the damage dealt by heart failure in advanced cases. But Ella Jones seemed to believe it to be possible. The issue was, Dennis could tell just by spending five minutes in a room with her: Ella Jones' heart unlikely to return to its former glory. But how should one tell a patient something they'd rightfully like not to hear?

"Ella," Robby sighed theatrically, playing like he was offended. "You trying to get rid of me?"

She laughed. "'Course not! You don't need me to be the excuse to come visit! You and Sam still got time to tie a knot!"

It was unfathomable how quickly Dennis and Robby met eyes when she said that. What she'd meant, Dennis had no clue. But clearly it sent some form of shame up Robby's ears, because they flushed red near-instantly. His eyebrow twitched, too; it only did that when something really set him off balance.

"That's all in the past, Miss Ella. She doesn't need the kind of stress I bring," he tried, forcing a shallow laugh and turning away from Dennis.

"Oh, fooey," Ella swatted dismissively. "She still has a big ol' crush on you, whenever you're ready to be a gentleman again."

"Mama!" Samantha shouted from somewhere else in the house.

Dennis didn't have the nerve to interrupt this conversation that he so pointedly wished not to hear, but luckily he didn't need the nerve. He had a job to do: "Pulse is normal. Blood pressure is 136 over 84."

"Ah," Robby seemed delighted to refocus the conversation. "Little high, but we've seen much worse, haven't we, Ella? Whitaker, check respirations."

Ella quieted herself to allow Dennis to focus, and after a moment, he gave a thumbs up. "Respirations are normal."

The rest of the house visit went smoothly, all things considered. Dennis didn't quite know what to do with himself whenever it got quiet; his mind raced, wondering what Robby had with Samantha. He wondered what Ella meant, saying things like 'tying the knot' and what Robby himself had meant when responding so weakly. 'That's all in the past,' was such an oddly unconvincing statement, at least to Dennis.

They finished the visit sooner than Robby anticipated, so he convinced Dennis it'd be a good idea to eat a late dinner out at a diner he liked. In the car, the younger man ripped himself from those thoughts that plagued him, because it shouldn't have mattered what it all meant. The reality of him dwelling on another man's personal affairs felt so far removed from what he'd been focusing on for the last year of his life, he almost burst into a fit of laughter in the passenger seat. How he'd devolved back into the overthinking, timid, pansy version of himself in the span of one single house visit was far beyond him—it baffled him. It was sheer—

"You're quieter than usual," Robby spoke soft, just barely above the noise of the car as it hummed and rumbled.

Dennis whipped his head to face Robby, blinking as he said, "Huh?"

"I said, 'you're quieter than usual.'" Robby spared a quick glance at the younger man. "You alright?"

He silently nodded, face like a frightened mouse.

"You sure?"

"Why wouldn't I be?" Dennis feigned innocence, like he knew what Robby was prodding at.

"I just," the older man kissed his teeth, grip tightening on the steering wheel as he spoke, "didn't get to formally apologize to you. For this afternoon. Yelling at you like that."

Well, that was not, in fact, what Dennis thought Robby was prodding at.

"I get it," he tried meekly, sacrificing his own dignity to appease whatever guilt was settled onto Robby.

But Robby would have none of that. He shook his head and sighed. "No, there's nothing to 'get' about it. It was plain unfair. You work hard and you have a right to speak up when you feel you need to."

"Well, it was a stressful day. Probably one of the most stressful yet. From what I gathered, at least."

The Freedom Riders. Myrna. Driscoll's intensely impatient attitude. The multiple emergency patients juggled solely by Robby as Dennis scurried to fill Dana's absence in the evening. It really was one of the most stressful shifts he'd experienced since he was brought on.
