"But that isn't an excuse to take it out on someone who is equally - or more - affected by that stressful environment," Robby admitted. "I was taught better than that. By a very good man. He'd be ashamed if he saw how I treated you today."

The sudden reference to that man again—a man Dennis didn't even know; it was a little odd to him, but he didn't have time to ask about it, because Robby kept going:

"I took you in to work at my clinic because I couldn't help myself. Couldn't bear to imagine not acting when I knew what might be waiting for you back in Nebraska." Robby paused. "I wasn't totally aware I'd taken in such a tough cookie," he glanced at Dennis, who met his eyes.

"I'm not all that special," Dennis tried.

"I politely disagree," the doctor said softly. "I heard you handling that Randall fella' today. You did an excellent job."

Dennis chuckled. "I was just reading off the page." 

"Yes. For a while." Robby agreed. "Then you convinced his father - the idiot he is - why Randall needs to take this seriously. Used your own judgment to convince him."

Resigning and oddly unable to take the compliment, the younger man said, "I just mean, I'm definitely not you."

"Well," Robby parked the car at a diner, stepping out and jogging to passenger side, opening Dennis' door and helping him out. "I wouldn't want to be me, but that might just be my informed perspective."

It was past midnight now, late enough that even the summer cicadas had stopped buzzing. As the two men walked toward the entrance of the worn-down, late-night diner, a warm breeze tickled Dennis' bangs and ruffled up Robby's own messy, short hair.

They sat down at a booth in the corner. A disheveled, older woman took their orders with the kind of thick, sugary southern hospitality that Robby never totally got used to. Dennis ordered a simple hash, Robby an egg plate with sausage. Before the woman - Mindy - could walk away, the older man said:

"Could I also add a cup of coffee? No milk, please."

She nodded. "Cup o' coffee on the way, baby-doll."

Robby turned forward again and met eyes with Dennis, who looked confused.

"Coffee? It's past midnight."

The older man rubbed his hands together, then palmed his eyes as he groaned, "I've got work to do before I hit the hay. It never ends, Whitaker."

"How does that work?" Dennis mustered the confidence to ask, almost as though he was judging Robby's choices.

He stared at the younger man across from him for a moment, trying to find some sort of enlightening answer, but there was none. "It doesn't," he resigned to say. "Not always. Sometimes... I feel like responsibility is pooling around me. Trying to drown me."

A statement that vulnerable from Michael Robinavitch's own mouth—Dennis was visibly taken back, even if only for a moment. He quickly reeled himself back in to figure out the right words. 

After a brief, awkward silence, Dennis recited: "And the God of all grace, who called you to his eternal glory in Christ, after you have suffered a little while, will himself restore you and make you strong, firm and steadfast.” When he finished, he looked up at Robby again, as though he were looking for approval of some kind; or at least a sign that he didn't look stupid.

"You a believer?" Robby's crossed arms and unreadable face were intimidating to Dennis.

The younger man blinked, brows furrowing as he said, "Maybe not as much as I should be. Or... not as much as I used to be."

With a reassuring nod of his head, Robby responded: "They say the medical field will do that to you." When Dennis shot him a mildly confused look, he elaborated, "Lots of doctors find it difficult to believe any benevolent God would put people through the kinds of things we see every day."

"Are... you a believer?"

There wasn't an immediate response. Robby's eyes tracked the swaying, flickering ceiling light behind Dennis, then he shrugged. "I don't really know anymore, Whitaker. I certainly used to be." He was going to leave it at that, but the ensuing silence felt like unfinished elaboration. So, Robby continued: "When I was a kid, I recited the Shema prayer every single morning with my grandmother. I was a diligent little shit. Grew up holding onto this idea that faith alone could save me from hardship."

"But it can't," Dennis surmised. "Not always."

Robby offered a resigned, hollow smile. "Nope. Not always," he agreed. With a sharp inhale, he deflated into the cushioned booth behind him, saying, "But that's life."

The waitress came back with Robby's coffee - as well as the plates of colorless food - but she was like a muffled white noise to Dennis, who was focused so intensely on the older man's features. He sank into the focus, tuning out the world around him as silence rang in his ears, heartbeat pounding as he studied the man. His tired eyes, his chapped lips, his scruffy beard. His large hands, finger hooked around the handle of the mug as he took his first sip of piping hot coffee.

"You not hungry?" Robby pointed at Dennis' plate, which had - by this point - begun cooling.

He wasn't even going to pretend to know how long he'd been zoned out, staring at the doctor.

"Uh," he stammered, coming back to reality. "I am. Sorry— I'm not usually up so late," he opted to lie.

Robby seemed to have an, 'Oh, duh!' moment, and he let his fork clatter onto the plate as he said, "I apologize. We'll get you home, let me just get the..." he trailed off as he turned to try and wave down the waitress for the bill.

"No, no, no, it's fine!" Dennis tried frantically, reaching across the table and grabbing Robby's upper arm to yank him back. When the man situated again, he watched as Dennis' face filled red with a hot flush of embarrassment. "You don't have to rush for me," the younger man chuckled nervously.

He was a little surprised that he'd mustered the audacity to reach across a damn table and grab Robby like that.

"Whitaker, if you're tired, it's really no trouble."

Dennis nodded. "I'm not not tired. But... I wanna eat." He paused, heavily—heavily contemplating adding the last part: "With you." Before any other thought could process, he shoved a full fork of hash into his mouth.

Robby looked up, fork in hand, frozen in motion. After a moment, he moved again, stuffing a bite of egg into his mouth and chewing—slowly.

"How'd you feel about the visit?" He tried to redirect.

Dennis nodded. "It was nice. Good to see how that works. Interestin' to see what a doctor can assess even without, y'know... a big clinic with ready supplies."

"Precisely," the older man tapped his index finger on the glossy wooden table. "And Ella was a sweetheart, tonight. I think she put on a face just for you—usually she's a lot more vocal about, uh..." he chuckled dryly, "Sam and I."

"Ah," the younger man blinked. There was a pause, then: "You two were a thing?"

They met eyes with one another. Another pause.

"Yeah," Robby finally confirmed. "Yeah, we were. For a while. Couple of years ago, when I first relocated to Franklin."

"She was real nice," Dennis complemented.

"Oh yeah. She's a sweet lady," the older man agreed—a little stiffly. "Taught me how to cook," he added.

The two men met eyes again.

"Is she how you got so good at makin' those chicken pot pies?" Dennis chuckled.

The older man pinched the bridge of his nose, unable to hold back a chuckle of his own as he said, "I'd say she's how I got good at cooking in general. You saying you like the pot pies?"

"Don't you?"

Robby snorted. "Can't eat 'em. So you do like 'em?"

"Every single one you've sent me home with!"

"Maybe I should show you the recipe."

 

 

Dennis spent the weekend replaying that night in his mind. He was - not by choice, but by a generally tight budget - a bit of a homebody, so he usually spent his weekends this way. At least, he'd been spending them this way since he started working with Robby and Dana at the clinic. He'd have a mostly normal week—busy days and slow days, then always at the end of the week, Robby would mellow out. He'd become more personable; he'd lull Dennis back into that sense of attraction that he could almost - but never quite - shake. Every week for a year now, he'd felt that cycle repeat itself. The ebb and flow of lust which pulled him in on Fridays and spat him back out come each new week.

This new week, however, he decided to get ahead of the curve. He'd had such a nice time accompanying Robby to Ella and Samantha Jones' home, and the diner afterward was a breakthrough in vulnerability between both men. It was - as it always goes with Whitaker - a taste of what he could not have—what he knew he would not have. And a taste always led to a craving.

He woke up before the sun had even risen that next Monday. He scrubbed himself 'till he was nearly red as a tomato in his steaming shower. He dressed himself neatly and packed his duffel for work. He wouldn't even be clocking in for several hours, closer to evening, but wanted to get in the clinic early and catch Robby before the man inevitably succumbed to the stress of his own patients and became distant—hollow.

The walk to the clinic was a little cooler that morning; it may have been August, but the chill of autumn was creeping its way into the air. As Dennis closed the gap between himself and the front door of the clinic, he fished into his pocket for the key. Pushing it into the keyhole of the door and turning until it clicked, he took it out and stuffed it back in his pocket, leaning his weight just far enough into the door that it opened with its signature squeak.

Inside, he heard muffled chatter somewhere far in the back of the clinic. Dennis thought it was a tad early for Dana to be in the clinic already for her shift, but paid it no mind, shuffling quiet as a mouse and setting his duffel behind the front desk before walking past the patient rooms, toward the chatter. The closer he got, the less familiar that feminine voice sounded—the less it sounded like Dana, rather. It sounded like Robby was lecturing, or teaching.

When Dennis turned the corner, he saw Robby sitting in the cushy green waiting room chair. Across from him, in the other chair, was Heather Collins. She was a beautiful woman; she wore a cream-colored blouse which sat tucked in a dark green pencil skirt. Her skin was black and the petite cross resting on her chest was gold. It complemented her complexion very well. 

Dennis did not know her—had no idea what had transpired last year when Robby saved her friend's life. He did know that despite the growing hope of the Civil Rights Movements, black Americans still faced unimaginable dangers, especially in the south; as such, he very likely wasn't meant to see her here in Robby's clinic.

A shock of fear zapped through Dennis' nervous system when Robby whipped his head up to face him, eyes widening like a deer in headlights; it was an image the young man would never forget. The shame of having walked in on something he surely was not supposed to know about; it washed over him like a tsunami of guilt and fear and embarrassment. 

"Whitaker?" The name fell out of Robby's mouth stunned and confused.

Heather stiffened a little in her seat, guard raising as her gaze bounced back and forth between the two men.

"I—" Dennis stammered, stumbling backward. "I'm sorry, I didn't—"

Before he'd even allowed himself to finish the sentence, he was turned around, scurrying for the front desk so he might grab his duffel and leave before he made anything worse. He wasn't sure who that woman was, but it was clear Robby didn't want him to know.

"Whitaker!" Robby shouted with an intimidating tone as he bolted past the patient rooms.

"I'm sorry!" His voice shook as he ran for the front door, his duffel not even secured properly on his shoulder.

Just as he opened the front door, Robby's arm reached from behind and clasped over Dennis' own, pulling the door securely shut. He grabbed the younger man's shoulders and turned him around to face one another, back pressing into the door as he looked up at the agitated man.

"Whitaker," Robby demanded his attention. "Please."

Please?

The younger man's brows furrowed, fear subsiding as confusion set in. Robby had just said 'please.'

"Don't report her," the doctor's voice nearly shook.

"Rep—" Dennis stammered, blinking in confusion. "Report her?"

Robby was close. Their faces were mere inches apart, and the man's large hands pressed down into Dennis' shoulders. He nodded, grip tightening as he begged: "To the police, Whitaker! I'm asking you to leave this alone."

"I'm not that kind of— I wouldn't report her! I was just gonna come in and lounge in the clinic early this morning, I— I thought you'd be free! I thought it would be a good time to catch you before..." he cut himself off before he said something he'd regret. "Oh— never mind that! I didn't mean to upset you—or her!"

They stared at one another for a moment.

Robby blinked. Then he sighed, and his hands fell to his sides. He began to realize that Dennis Whitaker was not, in fact, going to be a problem. Not that Robby wanted or expected him to be, but one could never be too careful. It was a dangerous country for a lot of good people, and Robby didn't want himself - or Dennis Whitaker, lest he have been trouble - to be the one to screw it all up for Heather Collins.

"Who is he?" Heather leaned against the wall, arms folded to her chest with a raised eyebrow.

Robby whipped his head around, a bit stunned by her lack of self preservation. After a moment, he turned back to Dennis, rubbing his beard to cope with the stress of the situation.

"Whitaker. This is Heather Collins," he gestured to the woman. "Miss Collins, this is Dennis Whitaker. My..." he struggled to find the words, "unconventional mentee and receptionist."

Heather's short, beige Hush Puppy heels clacked into the vinyl floor as she walked over, extending a hand. Dennis looked down at the woman's hand nervously, then back up, still unsure if Robby would maul him for acknowledging her.

"Hello," he muttered timidly, taking her hand and giving it a pitiful shake.

"You're all bones, hm?" She noted, giving him a once-over.

"I feed him when he lets me," Robby quipped, a hint of anxiety still floating in his voice.

Robby sat them down in the back room, Dennis in one chair, Heather in the other, just across the table. The doctor himself sat on the floor, legs in a 'V' shape under the table, arms folded to his chest.

"I brought Miss Collins in last year," Robby looked at Dennis. "Around the time I met you."

"He saved the life of a friend of mine," Heather elaborated.

Robby nodded, staring into the table as he remembered that stressful day—like it was yesterday.

"Brought her in?" Dennis inquired innocently.

"She's been learning." The doctor looked to the younger man. "I've been teaching her."

Dennis looked at Heather. She was a very guarded woman—had every right to be. She wore a stoic expression, eyes hard to read.

"That's real nice," the young man offered a petite smile. "Have you learned a bunch?"

His utter indifference to the color of her skin didn't go unnoticed. Robby was grateful, but confused. Heather wasn't necessarily convinced of Dennis' innocence, but she let herself soften up a little.

She nodded. "I've learned. And still am."

"Me too," he offered, then nudged his head in Robby's direction as he said, "he teaches me too, I mean."

"So I've heard," she glanced back at Robby, whose eyes were boring holes into Dennis.

"He's told you about me?"

Heather nodded, "A bunch about you. Nice to put a face to a name."

"Hasn't told me much of anything about you," Dennis said innocently.

His apparently harmless ignorance made the woman chuckle. As she did, she quipped, "Can't imagine why."

Dennis picked up what she meant—got real nervous to correct what he'd meant as he stammered: "Well— I meant, I get why. I just... I didn't know I struck him as a redneck."

Robby sighed unnecessarily loud. "I didn't imagine you were any such thing. But I wasn't gonna risk putting Miss Collins in any danger whatsoever. Dana would have my head."

He certainly meant that last part, but more accurately: he would have never forgiven himself. It wasn't untrue that Dana played a big role in keeping Heather safe and convincing Robby of the importance in doing so, but that drive still existed self-sustainably within him, too. He'd never overcome the pain of trying to help the woman and inadvertently being her downfall, had he not been careful. And really, he wasn't careful this time. After all, he'd given Dennis the means to stumble in on them, he was just lucky the kid wasn't a piece of shit.

"Dana would walk you like a dog, boy," Heather chuckled into the back of her hand, laughing harder when Robby swatted dismissively at her from across the table.

"Dana knows?" Dennis asked, a little more personally hurt now.

"She was there the night Heather came in," Robby confirmed. "Helped me keep her friend alive long enough to stabilize him."

Dennis nodded, and in that brief moment of silence, the front door clicked open again, barely audible from the room they sat in.

"Speak of the devil," the doctor groaned.

Dana called out as the door shut behind her, "Robby, ya' idiot! Door's not even locked!"

"Cat's out of the bag, Dana!" Robby hollered back, rolling his eyes.

Her footsteps audibly picked up pace, and when she turned the corner to find Dennis sitting in the same room as Heather Collins, she blinked. And blinked again.

Folding her arms and leaning into the wall with her work bag, she sighed.

"Well howdy, Whitaker."

Chapter End Notes

Wow, okay! Thank you guys so much for the support you've shown on my fanfic these past few days. I lowkey sabotaged myself because the excitement of it all made me release chapter 3 earlier than I should've, so I am... scurrying to keep up with the weekly upload schedule now. LOL

Just finished a "chapter trailer" for TikTok for this chapter that I'm super excited about, so... here we go! I hope you enjoyed the first chapter of Act 2!

Chapter 5: Emotions

Chapter Summary

Emotions run high as things begin to change around the clinic, and Dennis shares a deep secret with Robby.

Chapter Notes

Chapter 5's song is "Emotions" by Brenda Lee on her 1961 same-titled album, "Emotions"

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Over the next few months, Dennis Whitaker would learn that Heather Collins was put on Earth by God himself to become a doctor. 

"Doctor! Doctor! My app—" Dennis fisted his plaid shirt right at his naval, wincing in pain. "It hurts!" Balling the shirt's itchy fabric in his white-knuckled fist, he twisted, and writhed in his chair, feet kicking at the floor. "It hurts!"

Heather glanced at Robby, then back to Dennis, rolling her eyes—trying not to chuckle.

"Possible appendicitis patient," she declared.

Dennis' face went slack as he sat upright, balled fist falling to his side. He looked disappointed as he said, "How'd you guess it that fast?" He would've liked to act it out a bit further.

"Doesn't help that you nearly said 'appendix' in the middle of your theatrics," Robby snorted.

Theatrics?

"I was just, y'know..." Dennis stammered, the tips of his ears flushing red, "tryin' to set the tone for Miss Collins."

"Y'know, you're right, Mr. Whitaker," Heather nodded, half-heartedly playing along. "I should ask you before I diagnose you: are you feeling nauseous at all? Any negative changes in appetite? Have you got a fever?"

Dennis looked less enthused now that Heather had already guessed his ailment, but he went along anyway. "Yes, ma'am. Fever. Real bad fever. And it hurts like a moonshine fistfight right here just to cough," he pointed at his right lower quadrant.

"What course of action do you take, Miss Collins?" Robby asked.

"We don't have much time - assuming it is a case of appendicitis - to act, so..." she set down her notes and crossed one leg over the other in her seat. "We narrow in. Physical examination—we check for sensitivity at McBurney's point."

Robby nodded, then pressed for more: "And..."

"...We could also use the psoas and obturator signs. Determine any pain—"

Robby interrupted, "Let's assume it's a positive psoas sign. What now?"

Dennis focused on Heather, who held a confident expression upon her face. She knew these things like the back of her hand. She'd only been learning for just about as long as Dennis had. And yet, she seemed miles ahead.

"We could perform tests to rule out similar ailments. A urinalysis. White blood cell count, to confirm diagnosis."

"Confirmed. What now?"

"Immediately admit patient for an open appendectomy."

"Walk me through it, doctor."

"Administer general anesthesia, make sure the point of incision is cleaned and shaven."

"Where's the incision?"

"Right lower quadrant, just at the McBurney point."

Heather continued to explain precisely how to perform the appendectomy, and Dennis just sat there - across from her in his own chair - mouth agape as the woman described it so very vividly. So perfectly. It was like she was reading right out of the pages, but she wasn't. Her eyes were nowhere near a book or a page; they were fixated like fiery beams of light directly on Robby. The doctor himself didn't look outwardly surprised by her skillful assessment. His arms were folded, and he sat back, relaxed in his chair, the subtlest of nods bouncing his head up and down as he listened to her.

"—and in two weeks, we bring them back to examine and remove stitches. If we used non-absorbable, of course."

Robby sat there in silence for a moment, the two of them sharing a look.

"Congratulations, Miss Collins. You just saved a life."

Dennis smiled. Heather smiled. They glanced at one another—she had a look on her face like she couldn't believe she'd just walked through the procedure with such grace. A look on her face like she was surprised she had just impressed the doctor. It was all the more endearing to Dennis, who looked up to her like a God.

It had now been roughly two and a half months since he and Heather Collins met one another; that fateful Monday after his little diner getaway with Doctor Robinavitch. Summer had since passed, and autumn was on its tail end in Franklin; the chill of winter began to creep into the air like a familiar, haunting whisper. They'd gotten into this habit: Dennis staying at the clinic far past closing, Heather coming in during the mid of night to study and work alongside Dennis as the doctor oversaw their progress. It was a routine that had made the young man feel like he had a home—not a house, a home. A place he looked forward to returning to every day. A place he dreaded leaving at the end of every day.

"You're up, Whitaker." Robby looked at the young man, nodding.

Heather leaned forward to grab one of her notes from the table, flipping through until she landed on her own scenario, to be given to Whitaker as a quiz of sorts. The young man adjusted his posture, hands clasped together in his lap, ears perked as he awaited whatever ailment Miss Collins had prepared for him.

As she looked up, eyes meeting Dennis', Heather took a deep breath in. She did not immediately speak. The woman's eyes flicked back down to the note in her hands. Dennis could see how the thin paper warped in her grasp, like she was clutching to it tightly—like it might blow away if she wasn't careful.

"Mr. Whitaker," she said, eyes still on the note.

"Miss Collins," he said.

Heather's dark brown eyes finally met his again. "Two patients walk in."

"Two?"

"Two." She inhaled through her nose. "A man and a woman. Married couple. They're coughin' and come up to the front desk real weak-like. You can see their clothes are ill-fitted, but more important; you can see their faces—thinning faces, hollow cheeks and the likes."

Dennis' brow twitched. "How long has the cough persisted? Both patients caught this around the same time?"

Heather nodded. "They tell you the wife picked it up first, about a month ago, but no doctor would see her. The man says the same: he picked it up from his wife just a couple of days after—doctor wouldn't see him, neither."

"Have they got kids? I'd ask what kind of exposure they might've—"

"They've got three kids. Two boys and an eldest daughter. But they isolated themselves well. The kids are fine."

"How can they be sure—"

"The kids are fine," she pressed.

Dennis hadn't noticed how tense he'd become. He glanced over at Robby, like the sight of the man alone could remind him this was all just a theoretical—that it was all okay.

"Have they, um," he licked his chapped lips, cleared his throat, and returned his eyes to Heather. "Have they been coughin' up blood at all?"

"They tell you 'yes.' They say blood's been comin' up for a few days now."

Dennis blinked. "TB."

Robby snapped his finger, watching as Dennis whipped his head to face him, almost eager to be corrected: "Full name."

"Tuberculosis," Dennis corrected himself obediently, "would be my initial assumption. I'd need to physically examine them—check for signs. Elevated temperature and respiratory rate. Pulse and blood pressure, too. Then a chest exam."

"There's a good way to confirm it's tuberculosis, Whitaker," Robby guided softly.

Dennis thought for a moment, then let out an enthused 'ah!' before continuing, "Cavitary lesions. Well— any scarring of the lung. With no prior history, the likeliest cause of the scarrin' would almost have to be TB, no?" Before Robby could correct him again, Dennis corrected himself: "Tuberculosis."

Heather nodded. "There're a number of scars. And visible cavitary lesions."

"What now, kid?" Robby inquired, looking at the young man with anticipation.

"Immediately quarantine. Both patients should remain in one room, and I'd have to give them a cloth mask before they leave. They'd need to be prescribed isoniazid, but given how long it seems to have festered, they should also need streptomycin." He looked to the doctor for approval.

"Don't get ahead of yourself," the older man said. "You need a sample to confirm your suspected diagnosis."

Dennis' brow furrowed. For a moment, he scoured his messy brain, full of jumbled and unpracticed medical knowledge. When he found what he was looking for, he lit up like a lightbulb. "Sputum sample."

Robby nodded.

"Sputum sample," Dennis repeated, pinching his lower lip, then adding, "We'd need to push it to a lab technician for AFB since the patients are as far along as... they are."

"Good judgment," the doctor praised.

Dennis' stomach fluttered.

"Once we confirm tuberculosis in both patients, we keep them quarantined and monitored in the hospital until there's progress. Subsiding fever, less frequent coughs, less AFB, etcetera."

He looked over at Heather for some—any form of approval. She let out a soft sigh and her forehead untensed, like she was relieved to hear Dennis nail the diagnosis. Another expression sat in her eyes, but it was an indiscernible one. 

"How'd I do?" Dennis asked timidly, gaze darting back and forth between Heather and the doctor.

"A few areas to slow down on, in general. Get into the habit of asking every question. Consider every possibility. But, generally," Robby nodded, crossing his arms and slumping back in his seat, "good job, Whitaker. You saved a marriage from the whole 'death do us part' thing today." He snorted at his own joke.

"You're a downright genius, Miss Collins," Dennis smiled dopily, eyes lit up as he watched her shuffle the pages of her notes back into one stack.

She chuckled. "What're you butterin' me up for?"

The young man shook his head. "Just bein' honest. Running through an open appendectomy like that... y'didn't even stutter."

Robby nodded, eyes squinted to hide how proud of her he was, arms folded into his chest. "You did handle it very well."

"If it's so hard, why'd you quiz me on it, boy?" She chuckled at Dennis, ears burning as she deflected the praise from both men.

Dennis shot a look at Robby, who pursed his lips, cleared his throat, then admitted: "I had Dennis throw this one at you. I wanted to see your limits."

The woman blinked, eyebrows unknitting. "I see." After a pause, she added: "You found them yet, Dr. Robinavitch?" She watched a resigned smirk creep onto the man's face, then turned to Dennis and said, "And you did real well, yourself."

 

 

Having stayed up so late studying with Heather and Robby, Dennis had come into his shift the next day with very little sleep, and it was - as he'd have said about every shift he's worked for the last two weeks - one of the busiest days of his life. By the end of it, he was ready to keel over. Winter was no joke in Franklin, and a good chunk of its residents weren't too keen on health and safety anyway, as Dennis Whitaker had come to know.

"Tough fuckin' day," Dana groaned as Dennis walked up, planting his feet beside her just in front of the clinic. 

The boy nodded, eyes fixated on the many different shoeprints in the snow, leading to and from the makeshift parking lot off to the side of the building. "Seems like it gets busier in the winter," he mumbled. 

Dana clicked her tongue, "Ice slips, car accidents—sniffles, cruds, n' bugs, too. Whole lotta people got a whole lotta reasons to see Mr. Doctor." She tapped on the smoke in her hand, turned to Dennis, then added: "And that's on top of your regularly scheduled patients and accidents."

Doing a double take after having finally laid eyes on Dennis, she scolded: "Why haven't you got a damn coat on?"

The smoke of Dana's dimly glowing Winston cigarette was the only thing keeping Dennis from going numb in the snow—the only thing providing light in the otherwise dark night, aside from the harsh, pale light that shone from inside the clinic, through the windows behind them.

"I had a late night. Almost missed my alarm this morning," he chuckled exhaustedly, hugging himself and rubbing his upper arms up and down, vigorously.

"You n' Collins gotta quit keepin' each other up so late." Dana watched him as she took another deep drag. "I'd make you wear mine... but I got a hell of a walk home today."

"Husband's not pickin' you up?" Dennis' eyebrow raised.

"He's outta town—took the ride with him. Seein' family since he's gonna be stuck here with me and the kids for the holidays." She chuckled, but her face melted back into that intimidating, maternal expression as she said, "Bum Robby for a coat before you head out tonight, kid. I'm serious."

Dennis nodded, but he knew he likely wouldn't bug the man for any such reason.

After a moment of snowy silence, Dana off-handedly said, "He's been a real gentleman lately." Her lips held tightly to the butt of her cigarette before she perched it between her fingers, then continued, "I mean— at the expense of sleep, you and Collins seem to really be bringing the color back into his cheeks."

Dennis chuckled, eyelids heavy. "It's all Miss Collins." He tilted his head up, counting each twinkling star in the sky. "She's somethin' else. If life were any easier on her, I bet she'd be up and gone already—medical license in hand, openin' up a clinic of her own just like Dr. Robby."

"One day," Dana shrugged, musing softly about what kind of bright future might've been waiting for Heather. After a moment of soft, snowy silence, she turned to Dennis and assured, "You've got an effect on him too, though."

The young man didn't turn, but ears began feeling all fuzzy and hot. He heard the soft little taps of Dana's finger against the cigarette before she dragged it back between her lips. Facing the dark forest across the street, he mumbled, "How do you mean?"

"He's always goin' on about you is all," she said nonchalantly, like it didn't just rock Dennis' world. "Used to be he was dipping his fingers into too many pies. Trying to take care of the whole world at once. That burden... doing that'll wear you down real fast." Smoke curled from Dana's lips as she spoke. "I remember just last year I'd scold him about it what felt like every damn day after closing." She paused for a moment, brows relaxing as she looked at Dennis. "But I think you grounded him. Seems he's satisfied that itch, if only a little" she chuckled.

A huff of air escaped the young man's nose. If his face weren't frozen still, he imagined he'd look crazed after hearing those words from Dana's mouth. He wanted to pry further, but he was a smart enough man. There was no benefit to knowing more about what may or may not have been going through Robby's mind at any given time. Dennis Whitaker knew better than to paint a picture there. Doing so only ever led to hunger.

Like divine timing, the clinic door swung open behind the two of them. Dana turned first.

"Well lookie what the cat dragged out into the cold!" She fished the pack of Winstons from her pocket and tossed it to Robby as his crunching footsteps in the snow drew closer.

“Why are you two freezing your asses off out here? You don’t like my clinic?” The doctor joked, pulling a cigarette from the little cardboard box and perching it on his lips. He flicked open his silvery lighter, snapping it until a flicker of flame erupted, igniting the end and drawing in a heavy breath. 

“Just wanted some fresh air,” Dana sighed. “Been cooped up behind the desk all day.”

”Why were you working so late, Dana?” Dennis asked, brows knitting together. 

“Needed a few extra hours to cover the application fee,” she winked at the young man.

”Application fee?”

Robby turned to Dana, a shocked smirk creeping onto his lips. “After all these years, you finally did it?” 

After a particularly long drag of her cig, Dana chuckled out a puff of smoke, nodding her head. “I fuckin’ did it.”

”Did what?” Dennis asked meekly, but impatiently. “You’re applyin’ for a new job?”

Robby and Dana let out full belly laughs. 

"Jesus, Whitaker! You want me gone that quickly?" She hardly controlled her laughter, slapping Dennis' shoulder with her free hand.

The young man looked confused, but a smile tugged at his face. "Would you just tell me?"

"I applied for nursing school!" Dana blurted it out, arms raising above her head.

"You'll be a nurse?" Dennis' voice erupted with excitement.

"I might just be a goddamn registered nurse here in a few years!" The blonde woman let her cigarette fall into the snow, grabbing Dennis' shoulders. "Been thinkin' about it more recently, and UT's got a real good program, so... y'know."

Robby watched with a sentimental smile on his face as the two of them hugged and squealed like pigs into one another, rocking with joy. Dana's hair - neatly tucked into her brown hairclip as it was - nearly came undone by the time they'd settled down and let go of one another.

"What's your husband think about it? Your kids?"

"Adam's been pushin' me to do this for years! Same with that old grouch," she pointed back at Robby with her thumb. "Millie and Laura are still a bit too young to really get it, but they'll be proud of momma." Dana nodded to herself. "I'll make 'em proud. Make myself proud, too."

"We're all proud of you, Dana." Smoke slipped from Robby's parted lips as he smiled over at the woman.

"And what is— where is UT?" Dennis asked, a big smile still splitting his face.

And just like that, the excitement died.

Like the flame of a lighter snuffed out by a harsh gust of winter wind, Robby's smile faded. Dana's faded a bit slower, but it, too, fizzled out.

Clicking her tongue, Dana swept a stray bang from her forehead and said, "It's, uh... it's over yonder." She flicked her hand as if to point in its direction. "East Tennessee."

"East Tennessee?" Dennis' smile began to falter. He looked to Robby, whose face was now unreadable.

"It's in a city called Knoxville. About a three hour drive."

"Three— three hours?" Dennis' brows knitted together tightly. "How're you gonna manage that kind of commute every day?" It was a ridiculous question—he knew that. 

"Well," Robby stressed, stepping over and standing just beside Dennis. He nudged the younger, shorter man with his elbow, looking down at him as he said, "Come autumn next year, you'll - if you're up for it, of course - probably take over for Dana here."

"I—" Dennis stammered, blinking as his brows tightened further. Robby was close enough to radiate warmth against him, but even that sensation slipped under like white noise. "Don't... get me wrong, Dana. I'm happy for you, but I couldn't possibly fill your shoes."

He spoke genuinely. Dennis Whitaker did not believe himself capable of filling Dana Evans' shoes. But it was a bit deeper than that; his comment was a front for what was really an inability to process that this little work family he'd grown to rely on would - in the foreseeable future - likely totally shift, should Dana's application be accepted. Dennis was talented, and he was smart. But he was equally unconfident in himself. And more pressingly, he'd lulled himself into a false sense of security, naively dreaming that the dynamic within the Robinavitch Health Center would remain as it was forever. And he was not new to the idea of change. Afterall, he'd moved to Franklin from Nebraska, running away from a family—a bond, that did not belong to him.

"And what about..." he stammered, trying his hardest not to sound like a child. "What about this? The clinic, the... the— us?"

"What about you?" Dana forced a smile, slugging her arm around Dennis and rocking him reassuringly. "You and Robby'll kick ass whether or not I'm there. Collins, too, when the world lets her."

"I just mean—"

"I know what y'mean, kid," Dana interrupted softly, squeezing him closer to her. "Don't you dare make me start cryin' about this," she joked. "This is just somethin' I've gotta do."

Dennis knew that feeling. The pull of fate—knowing that something was meant for you. If moving to Knoxville for nursing school was what was meant for Dana, he was the last person on Earth who should whine about it. Admittedly, it was difficult not to feel the bubble of emotion creep up higher in his chest; his mind ran wild imagining a life at the clinic without Dana Evans. Dana fucking Evans. The woman who ran Michael Robinavitch like a dog. The woman who ran the clinic like a dog. The woman who cared for Dennis like his own mother never truly did. He knew he'd get over it, but he wouldn't get over it tonight.

"Don't psyche yourself out, Whitaker," she ruffled the younger man's short curls. "You're a hard worker, and you've got a hell of a drive for learning. That'll take you real far when you pick up med school. And you better do that eventually, you hear?" She pointed at Dennis as she began walking toward the gravel road. Her giant, puffy coat swaddled her like a newborn. "I ain't even leavin' for another year, anyway. And that's if I get accepted!" Stuffing her hands in the coat, she turned toward the road, back facing Robby and Dennis as she hollered, "Now, I gotta get home and let my nanny off the hook. Robby, put a coat on that boy before you send him home tonight!"

Save for the crush of cold leaves and ice beneath Dana's flat work shoes, it was silent for a moment. So silent, one could almost hear flakes of white snow as they fell and kissed the ground.

"I'm proud of you Miss Evans," Dennis hollered shyly, voice faltering only a little. "I really am excited for you."

Dana wasn't particularly great with goodbyes, and this wasn't even a goodbye yet. Even so, seeing the spark die in Whitaker's eyes the moment he realized she'd leave Franklin come next autumn... it was an oddly emotional image for the woman. Until that moment, she hadn't realized how much the kid meant to her. Hadn't truly realized how much Robby and Heather meant to her. Even Franklin—regardless how passionately she hated the political climate there. As she came to know, Tennessee may have been home to a range of nasty laws and bigoted assholes, but there were good people. Michael Robinavitch was good. Dennis Whitaker was good. Heather Collins was good. 

As she stepped out onto the gravelly road, forcing herself not to imagine what Dennis looked like behind her, a single tear slipped down Dana's cheek. But at the same time, a fond, soft smile crept onto her lips.

"You alright?" Robby nudged close to Dennis, slinging his arm around the shorter man's shoulders and patting his arm reassuringly. The material of his sweater was soft like clouds against the back of Dennis' neck.

Still looking at the road - long after Dana was gone - Dennis replied, "Could I bum a cigarette?"

With his arm still around Dennis, Robby turned, eyes meeting the side of his face as he asked, "Since when do you smoke?"

"I don't," he admitted meekly.

"Hm," Robby let out a low, gravelly hum. He kept his arm around Dennis, but raised his hand up just enough to tap the younger man's bottom lip with his middle finger. Dennis only barely flinched. "Open," the doctor said.

Dennis obeyed, parting his lips just enough for Robby to slip the butt of his own cigarette - which he'd already taken several drags of - in. "Take a good breath," he instructed, watching as the shorter man's cheeked hollowed out a little. His calloused fingers grazed rough against Dennis' soft lips, heat radiating from them—and the cigarette.

He hadn't even finished filling his lungs before the burning in his throat became too much. Sputtering and coughing, Dennis bowed forward, puffs of smoke billowing from his mouth. Robby was already - almost instinctively - rubbing circles into his back with his palm, having tossed the offending cigarette into the snow at their feet.

"First time's never as fun as they make it look," he soothed, helping Dennis stand up straight again.

"I thought it'd knock the stress off," he admitted, wet, red eyes finding Robby's own dark brown pupils.

He looked pitiful. Granted, it was, in part, because he'd just been beaten by a smoke. But still, Robby could sense he wasn't taking Dana's news too well. The urge to assist crept up on him once again, and before he could rationalize, his lips were moving:

"Why don't you come up and get some warm food in your belly, Whitaker. I can cook up a dinner for you."

Dennis' eyes softened and his brows untensed. He parted his lips, but words did not come out.

Robby filled the silence: "What? You've still got me."

 

 

Robby's home above the clinic looked almost exactly as it did nearly two years ago, when Dennis had woken up in his tub, overheated and malnourished.

The walls surrounding them were pinewood panels, darkened to a color like rich amber. The glow of warm, orange lamps lit the walls sparsely and little faded picture frames hung from lazily hammered nails. Even from the kitchen table which Dennis sat at so very shyly, the scent of Michael Robinavitch swam around the home unignorably. The warm spice of his Clubman Pinaud aftershave tangled with the musk of worn leather furniture and tobacco that had seeped into the pinewood walls over many years; if Dennis' sour mood could be cured by anything, it would be that scent.

"Food's real good," he said unenthusiastically after clearing this throat, sinking his teeth down for another big bite of homemade hot water cornbread. He followed that bite with a forkful of smothered chicken, covered in onions and a savory, rich gravy.

Robby, eating at a considerably more polite pace, chuckled at Dennis from across the small dining table. "A patient told me about the magic of cast-iron skillets. Finally got around to buying one last week and decided to put it to use for the smothered chicken—he told me I had to try smothered chicken first thing."

"He was," Dennis swallowed a bite, "onto somethin'."

Robby snorted. He was leaned back in his chair, just watching Dennis.

"You're like a different person when you've got food in front of you, Whitaker. Can I really trust you're using your paychecks to buy groceries? Taking care of yourself?"

The younger man nodded, wiping his lips with a napkin before finishing off the final bites in his bowl.

"You just happen to catch me when I'm hungry, is all," he dismissed, mind clearly occupied.

The doctor leaned further back in his chair, spreading his legs lazily and tapping his finger idly on the edge of the dark, wooden dining table. Dennis didn't look up at him very much while he ate, so the doctor took the opportunity to study him more. He always wore plaid button-downs. His hair was always just shy of greasy. Dirty, light-brown hair which curled at its short ends. He always had bags under his eyes, like he didn't sleep—not dissimilar to Robby's own habits, though he didn't have the eyebags to show for it. If he had to put it into words, Robby decided he would - never outwardly - describe Dennis as cute. A man so greatly burdened by his past as well as the world around him, yet no less kind—no less innocent for it. 

"I wanted to thank you," the doctor finally interrupted the silence.

Dennis looked up, meeting his warm eyes.

"What for?"

Robby shrugged. "You and Collins get along nicely."

The younger man blinked. "I don't see why we wouldn't. We got a lot in common."

"Of course—just, the circumstances of how you two met... it could've gone one of two very different ways. I'm glad I could trust you." He paused for a second, then decided to add: "Glad you found your place here. In Franklin."

"Her secret isn't mine to tell," Dennis shrugged, reciting Dana's words. After a pause and a brief swell of some unexplainable emotion within him, he added: "I've got secrets of my own, anyway. On some level, I think— I think I... understand what it's like."

Robby's brows furrowed. He squinted, as if trying to better read the young man across from him. "Is it," he began hesitantly, "too personal to ask what you might be referring—"

"Oh, please, Doctor Robby," Dennis blurted. He smacked the surface of the dining table with his palm; what followed that thud was pure, sharp silence.

Frankly, Dennis was tired. He'd just worked one of the longest shifts of his short career at the Robinavitch Health Center only to be told - by Dana herself - that she planned to leave next year. The kid wasn't an idiot: he knew his newfound, peaceful little life wouldn't stay exactly the same forever, but he'd certainly developed a sense of routine with Dana and Robby—now Collins, too. The prospect of losing part of that puzzle frazzled him more than even he recognized, until his palm had struck Robby's table; it was the most disrespect he'd ever shown to anyone, not that Robby particularly cared.

Across from him, the doctor sat still. He was leaned back in his chair, but his arms were folded now. He didn't look mad. Just... fazed.

Licking his lips, Dennis croaked, "I'm sorry." Robby was prepared to console him, but he was already speaking again: "It's just... you know what I am—what kind of man I am."

The older man immediately made the connection. He knew what Dennis was on about.

"Never had a girlfriend. Never talked about anyone I was hot for. Never talked about ex-lovers - who don't, as a matter of fact - even exist. Never felt that real deep sense of belonging in school even when I made my friends, seldom as they were. All of that bullshit just to come home each day and feel the real wrath from my own blood family—dusk 'till dawn. 'A real man this, a real man that,' my father and my brothers and my mother would always say. They knew—they knew what kind of boy they brought into this world, even before I knew it myself. And if I could control it, I'd have rid myself of it a long, long time ago just to please them and know what it was like to be held in their approving arms. But in the end, I can't control it. Ran away from Nebraska all the way down south just to take up the same life I was livin' at home, only this time it nearly killed me in that field. 'Course, I guess dyin' of heat stroke on a shitty Tennessee farm is a little more dignified than bein' beaten to death by your own daddy for being a faggot."

He nearly flinched when the last word came out of his mouth, jaw clenching as he braced himself for God's bright, hot wrath to beam through the pinewood paneled walls and strike him down—then and there.

But it did not come.

Robby sat exactly as he had been sitting the entire dinner. He looked concerned, sure, but he did not look disgusted. He did not gaze upon the boy with judgment in his eyes. His face was hardly readable at all, frankly. The only thing Dennis had to go off of was the deafening silence between the two of them as Robby - presumably - processed everything that had just spilled forth from the younger man's mouth.

When the silence had lasted long enough and it had become clear that Dennis was done speaking, Robby exhaled the breath he didn't know he'd been holding. His chair creaked as his weight shifted, leaning into the table.

Softly, he said, "How long?"

Dennis did not respond. His eyes were fixated on his own hands, picking at his fingernail as they rested on the table.

"Whitaker," Robby said. "Look at me."

Slowly, the boy's eyes drifted back up, obediently meeting Robby's.

"How long have you been letting this eat away at you?" He repeated.

The boy blinked, licked his lips, swallowed the lump in his throat. Slowly and messily, he croaked out: "Longer than I've been in Franklin."

A huff of air escaped Robby's nose and his jaw tightened.

"We fought," Dennis nodded, wiping a tear from the corner of his eye with his forearm. "Spring of 1960. Me and my father never got on too well, but... this was different. He went through my room and found my journal one day while I was out working the field." He paused. "I'd gotten used to writing down all my thoughts in it. Always figured it would be good for practicing academics. But it became... something of an outlet—a way for me to let out the undignified thoughts in my head."

"Undignified," Robby parroted, squinting his eyes.

The boy nodded. "When I came back in that night, he blew up. Went on about how disrespectful it was to live off of them while I thought such vile things. He kept yellin' and yellin'... and all my mother did was sit there in her little wooden chair. When he started hitting me, I—" he stammered, wiping another tear. "For the first and only time in my life, I started hittin' back." 

Robby's brows knitted together, stress lines forming on his forehead.

"I wasn't very good at it. Didn't slug him too well, but I think seeing me fight back at all shocked him. Packed what I could carry that same night and snuck out into town. Got on a greyhound and headed off—anywhere but Broken Bow."

Dennis was the first to break their shared eye contact. He looked over to the right, tracing the dark lines of the pinewood panel walls as silence filled the room. Robby did not speak. He did not move. He sat still as a tree, eyes fixated on the young man across from him. Dennis was crying—or rather, tears were slipping silently down his cheek, reflecting shimmering warm light from the dim lamp in the living room.

"Anyway," he croaked, pushing the chair back and standing to his feet hastily. As he wiped more tears from his cheekbone with the back of his wrist, he said, "I've gone and screwed this all up, so, I'll see myself out." Dennis scurried for the door leading downstairs.

He knew it was over. Already, he was scolding himself—beating himself bloody in his mind for having told Robby his deepest secret. For having confided in a man who he had no right to bombard with such troubling information. Michael Robinavitch was a kind man, but he surely wouldn't let Dennis' secret slide, especially given his utter silence as the boy talked. Dennis had already made his mind; whatever family he'd been developing at the clinic and in Franklin, he'd just blown it all up. It was time to run away again, in some capacity. 

Before he could get his hand on the knob, Robby called out from his seat at the dining table, "Dana will kill me if I don't send you home with a coat."

Dennis froze, hand suspended in motion just next to the golden knob of the door. He should've just left. It wouldn't be hard to turn that knob, swing the door open, sprint down the stairs, then sprint out into the cold.

But before he could finish imagining the lonely walk home in the dark, Robby was already behind him, tapping on the nape of his neck. Dennis flinched and gritted his teeth. Slowly, he turned to face the taller man, who clutched in his left hand a thick, brown Levi's sherpa with faded silver buttons and cream-colored wool on its inside.

"Should keep you warm," the older man spoke low, his voice sweet like warm honey.

Before Dennis could even protest, Robby was already slipping the jacket over his shoulders. When the younger man fit his arms through the large sleeves, Robby reached his calloused fingers down to the bottom, just near Dennis' waist. He clicked each silver button into place, slowly—agonizingly slowly. The boy watched as Robby's brows knitted with concentration, all the way until he reached the top button. His warm knuckles grazed against the spot just between Dennis' neck and his chin; it tingled like a teasing flame.

For a moment, Robby raised his hand, and the boy could've sworn he was about to cup his cheek, but he finally let it fall to his side as he said, "You've felt safe here in the clinic for two years now. That doesn't have to change—are you scared it'll change when Dana leaves?"

Dennis bit the inside of his cheek and hesitantly nodded.

Robby shook his head, a reassuring but subtle little smile creeping onto his lips as he repeated: "It doesn't have to change." He paused for a moment, then decided to add: "I won't shower you in dangerous platitudes about acceptance and futures, but..." he inhaled sharply, then nodded, "you're safe as long as you're here. In my clinic."

It was quiet enough to hear whistles of breath as they fluttered in and out of Dennis' nose. He stood there, just inches between himself and Robby, staring up at the man. Both of them had a look in their eyes like they were daring the other to do something they shouldn't. Dennis allowed that dangerous, tempting silence to ferment for only a few moments longer before deciding to close himself off.

"Thank you for lending me your coat, Dr. Robby. I'll bring it back Monday," he declared with a hollow tone. He turned the doorknob behind him and wished the man a goodnight in a soft mumble as he shuffled out the door.

When it closed, Robby stood just in front of the doorframe—longer than he should've needed to. His dark eyebrows knitted together and he chewed at his lip, rubbing his face up and down repeatedly as he processed what had just happened.

"Jesus Christ," he groaned into his palms.

Chapter End Notes

Well there you have it! I guess the question now is: "What will Robby do with this information?" Teehee!

Thank you so much for your continued support on my story. As a surprise, I've been writing and recording an original song called 'The Overs' to accompany the final chapter of the story, and I really can't wait to share it with you.

Chapter 6: Place To Be

Chapter Summary

On Christmas Eve after a particularly grueling week at the clinic, Robby shows up unannounced at Dennis' door.

Chapter Notes

Chapter 6's song is "Place To Be" by Nick Drake on his 1972 album, "Pink Moon"

See the end of the chapter for more notes

With Robby's big sherpa hooked around his arm, Dennis pushed open the door of the Robinavitch Health Center. His eyes went wide as he took in the sight before him. The waiting area was more packed than he'd ever seen it; patients stood around without free chairs to sit in, and a crowd had formed at the front desk where Dana stood, a stressed, focused frown in her brows. It was, as Dennis said so very often for the last several weeks - but he truly meant it this time - probably going to be the busiest shift he'd ever work.

To his left, both waiting chairs were filled. A young boy sat clutching at his ear while his mother and father stood just beside him. In the chair next to him was David Saunders, accompanied by his mother, Theresa; they weren't an uncommon sight at the clinic. On the right side of the room was an older woman sitting while an older man stood just beside her, rubbing her shoulder as she rested her wrist awkwardly on her knee, face contorted in pain. Next to them, but noticeably scooted away was a woman who looked around Dennis' age, though it was hard to tell; she wore a cloth mask, leaving only sickly eyes visible as she leaned back in the chair. She looked on the verge of passing out.

Dennis blinked, then sighed. He shuffled past other incoming and outgoing patients and slithered his way to the front desk, where Dana greeted him with a tired nod.

"I'll be chainsmokin' by New Year at this rate," he quipped, setting his duffel down behind the desk.

"Welcome to it, kid," she chuckled. Noticing Robby's coat hooked on his arm, Dana tugged at it lightly and said, "You might as well stash it somewhere, Whitaker. He ain't stoppin' his rounds anytime soon."

The boy looked down at the coat, as though he forgot it was hanging on his arm, then looked back up at Dana. "Right," he stammered, "uh... should I just—"

"Give it," she chuckled, taking it from his arm and folding it neatly into a big square before placing it delicately on the top shelf behind the front desk. When she met eyes with Dennis again, Dana began briefing him: "Robby's got a full house today. Obviously. All four patient rooms have been full pretty much since sunrise." She slammed her appointment book on the desk, grabbing a pen and pointing around on the pages inside. "Driscoll will be in at four-thirty for a follow-up. You got two more scheduled appointments before the day's out, but your main concern right now is," she pointed her pen out at the crowd behind Dennis, "gettin' these folks ready for Robby."

Dennis looked behind him at the crowd, and when he turned back to face her, Dana was holding out a clipboard with triage sheets and a pencil attached. "I've got David and Theresa situated. And that sweet old lady over there. Eloise Sellars and her husband; poor girl slipped on ice in her driveway and landed on her wrist. We're talkin' at least a sprain." Dana then pointed to the little boy on the left side of the room, then the ill woman on the right side, saying, "Those two just came in."

Dennis nodded, grabbing the clipboard, hanging and preparing for triage.

He walked up to the little boy first, greeting his parents.

"I'm Clark, this is Marian," the father said. "And this is Benjamin," he pointed.

"Hello Benjamin, " Dennis tried in a soft, light tone. "Is it okay if I ask you a few questions about your ear, buddy?"

The little boy wiped tears from his eyes with his free hand, still clutching his ear with his right hand as he nodded.

Dennis began to write on the triage sheet as he examined the boy: Benjamin Giles. Age 6. Male. Pain in right ear, duration of two days. Parents mentioned progressive worsening of pain. Temperature of 100.5°F, 96 bpm pulse. 

He continued writing until the form was chock-full of history information and remarks, Dennis stood to his feet and offered a sympathetic smile down at the boy. "You've been really helpful, Benjamin. We're gonna get you in an exam room as soon as we can, and Dr. Robinavitch will come in and help you from there, okay?"

"Okay," the little boy whined through a sniffle.

Dennis offered a final nod to the parents, then hustled over to the right side of the room, just in front of the other new incoming patient. Behind her cloth face mask, she had a dark complexion, but the color in her face was dull thanks to - what Dennis immediately assumed upon first glance - a possible flu.

"Hello, ma'am," he waved awkwardly, clutching his clipboard next to his chest as she looked up at him.

When her eyes met his, she sat up straight, adjusting her mask and waving. "Hello. Triage?" She pointed at his clipboard.

He nodded. "Isn't often that we see people wearing face masks in-clinic down here in Franklin," he chuckled, "I appreciate it. We all do."

"I'm a med student," she nodded back. "I feel your pain," she tried light-heartedly, but her voice was hoarse—fatigued.

The boy offered a sympathetic look, then asked, "Mind if I ask a few questions?"

"Go right ahead, Mister..." she trailed off, urging him to tell her his name.

"Dennis Whitaker," he shot a sheepish smile.

As he examined the woman, he once again filled out the fresh triage sheet: Samira Mohan, age 26. Female. Body aches, fever, persistent cough and sore throat, duration of four days. Mentioned slight shortness of breath and nausea. Temperature of 102.3°F, 95 bpm pulse. 

He continued prodding the woman for her symptoms, and when he'd filled out the triage sheet entirely, Dennis looked up and asked, "Miss Samira, could I bring you anything while you wait? A blanket? A water?"

Samira nodded, "Both would be nice if it isn't any trouble. I'd appreciate that."

"On it," he smiled, spinning around and weaving through the crowd again.

Dennis was just about to pass the front desk when he caught Robby standing behind it, speaking to Dana. His feet moved before his mind did, and he swiveled, nearly tripping as he scurried over and laid the triage sheets in front of Dana.

"Dr. Robby," he interjected nervously.

"Hey, Whitaker," he smiled—a very professional smile. "You finished up with triage?"

"Just about. I, uh— I brought your coat back," he gestured behind the desk as Dana pulled it out, holding it in front of Robby like a dog treat.

Robby looked at the coat—studied it. Then his eyes flicked over to Dennis, giving him a notable lookdown before opening his mouth and inhaling sharp. He held that breath for a moment before saying, "You keep it. It looks good on you. Better than it did on me."

A hurricane of butterflies began tickling inside Dennis. His stomach buzzed, ears flushing red.

Before he could protest, and more notably, before Dana could process Dennis' visible reaction to the statement, Robby clapped his hands together once and said, "Alright! Dennis, it's a full house, and it's probably gonna stay that way for the rest of the day." He leaned in close, gesturing Dennis to do the same, so the boy did. In a lower voice, Robby explained: "I've got Lawrence Collins in room D—"

"Collins? Like..."

The older man nodded. "Heather's youngest brother. The whole reason we met her, in fact. He's got a pretty serious flu—she brought him in early this morning. Obviously, we can't have anyone go near that room until the clinic closes down."

Robby sounded stressed about the whole situation, but knowing him, Dennis knew he wouldn't have refused caring for the man if it was a real emergency. Still, he was confused about the whole 'brother' situation. Last he'd heard, the man that Heather brought in two years ago for Robby to save was 'just a friend.' The new development confused him, but he paid it little mind, more focused on the stress and exhaustion in Robby's voice.

"That is to say... prepare for a hell of a shift. It'll be busy today and we'll be spread thin." He patted Dennis' shoulder. "Alright?"

Dennis hesitantly nodded, a little afraid of what kind of day he was in for, but he pulled himself together quickly. Robby gave his shoulder one good final pat just firm enough to bring him back down to Earth.

"Good boy," he nodded. "Dana, help transition Whitaker to the desk and make sure things are going smoothly, then you're off the hook."

"Aye-aye, captain," she pursed her lips, folding her arms.

Robby turned, preparing to walk off, then sharply turned back with a snap of his fingers, saying, "Actually! While I've got you both, I was going to ask a question about New Year—"

"Before that," Dana politely but firmly interrupted, "I've got a possible frostbite patient waiting for you in B." She shoved a patient sheet into Robby's chest, then said, "Shuffled him in there almost five minutes ago now. Go deal with that. I promise I won't leave 'till you get your question out."

"Of course," he nodded, pressing his lips together and cramming his hands in the pocket of his black zip-up jacket.

"I've gotta go grab a blanket and some water for a patient, too, so..." Dennis trailed off, awkwardly turning before shuffling away, back through the crowd.

Robby nodded, giving the two of them one final glance before hustling over to exam room B.

His eyes flicked around the sheet on the clipboard as he opened the door, pressing inside as he wore a tiny, polite smile. It masked his overwhelm quite well.

"Mr. Willis," he greeted the older man sitting at the edge of the bed.

"Harold is fine," the man corrected through shivers. He was wrapped in a large, itchy-looking black blanket—one of the blankets Robby always kept in the back of the clinic for such situations. 

"Noted, Harold. I'm Doctor Robinavitch but most everyone around here just calls me Robby," he pulled up the chair and sat just in front of Harold, whose fingers were pale-pink around the joints and ghost-white everywhere else, skin peeling present as well. As the doctor examined them, he glanced briefly at the patient sheet, then hummed. "Were you working outside today, my friend?"

Harold nodded, "Yes sir."

"How long were you out there?"

"'Bout six hours before it got real bad."

"Did you have gloves on?"

"No sir—not for the most part."

"Any numbness anywhere else on your body? Your chart says the only complaint is your hands."

"Just my fingers," he shivered. "It's like pins and needles, too. I can barely move 'em."

Robby reached his hand out, gently and carefully holding up Harold's right hand. "I'm going to give a very light pinch to your fingers, you tell me what you can and can't feel, okay?"

Harold nodded and did just that. By the time Robby had made his way down each of the fingers in the man's right hand, he moved to each of the fingers from his left. It was a quick exam, and it needed to be a quick exam.

"Well, Harold," Robby nodded as he scooted his chair back, standing up and walking to the faucet on the opposite side of the room from the bed. He turned the handle all the way to the right, then looked at the patient. "Luckily, it looks like you got off with a case of superficial frostbite. It could've been a hell of a lot scarier had you stayed outside any longer, but we'll get blood back into your fingers. I'm just going to have you hold your hands under this water for about twenty minutes. Make sure the water stays warm, and when twenty minutes passes, I'll come back and see where we're at. Okay?"

The older man nodded.

Robby helped the man up and got his hands under the flowing water. Then he scribbled onto the patient sheet hastily and offered one more polite nod before walking back out of the room, closing the door behind him. Just as he turned his back to the door, Dennis walked past, but before the boy could get far, Robby gently placed his hand on the nape of his neck, guiding him.

"Woah," the older man stressed, as though Dennis were a car moving too fast. When the boy stood just in front of him, Robby said, "Where're you bolting off to, Whitaker?"

Dennis looked - as he usually did in the clinic when the doctor had things to say - like a hamster cornered in its cage. His tired eyes were wide, and the corners of his lips were downturned. He pointed toward the waiting room with his thumb and said, "New incoming. Dana needed help with triage, so I was..." he swallowed, "just finishing that up."

With a rub of his eyebrow, Robby mumbled, "Yeah, that's— good. Good." Then, he paused, inhaled, and blurted out in a half-whisper: "I need to reiterate that I meant what I said the other night."

Dennis cocked his head in confusion, chewing on his bottom lip while trying to ignore the sheer warmth of Robby's hand on his neck. That pleasure didn't last—the hand slid down his spine and ghosted off his body after a second more. 

"About..." he hummed, "the—"

The doctor nodded. "About 'being safe in my clinic' and whatnot? Yes, that." he confirmed, then nodded with a reassuring glare in his eyes. "You ran out before I could give my two cents, but— if nothing else, I needed to... make sure you knew."

As if waiting for a response, Robby just stared down at him, eyes searching his for a glimpse of an answer that did not immediately fill the silence.

"Uh," finally slipped out of Dennis' mouth—awkwardly. "Yeah," he blinked. "I— yes. I didn't think you were lyin' or anything. If that's," he stammered, "what you mean."

"No," Robby assured softly with a shake of his head. "I just mean... it wasn't bromide to soothe you in the moment and change my mind later."

Dennis' brows twitched, but after a moment more of searching the doctor's eyes for something he did not know to look for, his face untensed, and his lips thinned into a barely visible smile. He nodded, then said, "Okay," in the softest voice Robby had ever heard. "...Thank you."

And like it was a reward for verbalizing appreciation, Robby placed his hand on the younger man's shoulder again, giving a single firm pat. His palm rested there as he looked away, assessing the clinic with a sharp inhale through his nose. He even gave the shoulder a firm squeeze as he zoned back into his duties, clearing his throat and ordering: "If you're done catching up with triage, I could really use your help checking in on Lawrence every so often."

Dennis was a little impressed by how quickly Robby's professional mask slipped back over his face, but nodded. "Of course."

"Heather brought him in this morning with a fever just shy of a hundred and three. It stayed that way most of the afternoon, but I got it down a little these past two hours and gave him some codeine for his cough—which was also persistent," Robby was talking a mile a minute - as he usually did when stressed by any given situation - and Dennis had learned to keep up well. "And that's all on his patient chart, of course. I just need you to pop in once every so often and check in, alright?"

Dennis nodded, but couldn't hold back the little smirk that pulled at his lips.

"What?" Robby was mostly unaware of his tendency to ramble under pressure. But that was, to be fair, because he only ever did it around Dennis Whitaker.

"Nothing. I'm on it, Dr. Robby," he nodded.

The two exchanged indiscernible final glances before splitting off in opposite directions. Dennis shuffled toward the end of the hall where the warm lights dimmed slightly, yanking a disposable mask from the wall dispenser as he passed. When he opened the door to exam room D, he slid inside quickly and shut it just as fast.

Lawrence was sitting upright in the bed, eyes as wide as he could muster as fatigued as he was.

Dennis raised his arms up the moment he fixed the mask to his face, whispering: "I'm just here to check up on you, sir. Per Dr. Robby."

Lawrence relaxed a little as Dennis walked to the counter opposite the bed and picked up the patient chart, reading through.

"Mr. Collins, are you feelin' any better than when he last checked?"

"The nausea ain't swinging me around much now," the man spoke, voice weak and thin. "Codeine helped a bunch, too. Still feels like I'm burnin' up, though."

Dennis nodded, eyebrows raised as he explained, "That's mostly a good thing. Means your body's raising its own temperature to make it tough for your flu to survive. But let me just," he sifted around the counter for the glass thermometer, "grab your temperature real quick."

He shook the thermometer with a simple snap of his wrist, then walked over and politely directed Lawrence to lift his tongue. They waited for two minutes before Dennis took it out and read: "Just shy of a hundred and two. That's a lot better than what I heard you came in with this morning."

"Are you Mr. Dennis Whitaker?" Lawrence asked.

Dennis was already turned around, walking the thermometer to the counter for disinfection as he confirmed, "Yeah. Heather told you about me?"

"Said you were a real busy bee," Lawrence quipped.

Dennis whipped his head around and muttered shyly: "Really?"

A nod.

"Also said you were a real sharp kid. Big heart."

"Hm," his brows furrowed as he worked at the thermometer.

When he finished, he walked to the middle of the small room, standing awkwardly—shyly, and studying Lawrence. The man had the same dark complexion as Heather, only his skin was void of as much color thanks to the rough flu that ailed him. Little beads of sweat sat still following his hairline, and his eyes stayed shut, even as he conversated with the boy.

"She's real good at what she does," Dennis said.

The man in the bed lifted his lips just barely enough to constitute a smile, and he nodded just as weakly. "Just like our mama and daddy."

"They practice?" Dennis' face lit up.

"They're gone," Lawrence corrected softly. "Up with God now."

The light in Dennis' eyes died as quickly as it sparked, and he swallowed as he stammered, "Oh— I'm so sorry."

"It's been a few years," the man dismissed. "But I think their passin' was somethin' of a catalyst for her."

Silence filled the tiny exam room, and Dennis studied the vinyl floor which reflected dim light from the ceiling as he contemplated asking the question that thumped at the front of his skull.

"I heard she and Robby brought you in here a few years ago. Back in 1960. I heard y'all were just friends." He glanced down at the clipboard in his lap, tapping on it with the nail of his index finger before mumbling, "Didn't know she had a brother."

Huffs of air fluttered out of Lawrence's nose as he chuckled. "She's got two!"

Dennis tittered. He thought Lawrence's tone was amusing—like the man was offended Heather hadn't told people about him.

"I'm just giving her a hard time. She was right. She was just bein' careful," he settled as his chuckles quieted to a halt.

"How do you mean?" Dennis asked innocently.

"I mean: black folks get turned away all the time by doctors—for every little reason. Especially 'round here."

It was the first time Dennis had ever heard the word 'black' used in that way. He noted that dully, but kept his attention focused on Lawrence; his voice was quiet—almost easy to miss given the unceasing commotion outside the door.

Lawrence mimed a doctor with his hands as he mocked: "'We'd take you, but you'd tell your family, and we don't have the resources to handle that!' And every other excuse under the sun, no matter how bad you needed help."

He paused to cough, clearly straining himself by chatting so much when he should really be resting.

"Dr. Robby seemed kind, don't get me wrong, but Heather said she wanted to be real careful. Didn't want him to think she'd come back," a dry cough, "later askin' him to treat the whole family—what's left of us. That kind of thing's a big ask outside of Baptist Neck."

"Baptist Neck?" Dennis asked softly.

"Street we grew up on. Used to have a doctor that would actually see us," the man shook his head on the pillow, eyes still closed. "Real kind fella. He ain't here no more." He paused to swallow, then took a deep breath in, which snowballed into a quick fit of coughing. When it settled, he began again: "Anyway... Heather didn't wanna say we were siblings just to get turned away again. She wanted to be real careful."

"Dr. Robby's a good man. He wouldn't—"

"—One good apple in the basket doesn't mean the others ain't rotten," Lawrence interrupted softly.

Dennis blinked.

"And the world's a real rotten basket, Mr. Whitaker."

Lawrence's words rewired Dennis Whitaker's brain in real time, like piecing together a puzzle that he never otherwise would have seen. But once he did see what the man meant, he realized how familiar it all looked. He knew what it was like to lie—knew what it was like to fear rejection - or worse - for something he could not change. 

"Yeah," he nodded, head bowed forward as he picked at the skin of his cuticle, hands rested in his lap meekly. "Yeah," he repeated, weaker and quieter. Before he allowed Lawrence to continue pushing himself by talking instead of resting, Dennis stood to his feet and said, "I should let you rest, Mr. Collins. Like I said, though: I'll be in and out as much as I can to check on you."

The man acquiesced, adjusting until he was laying down flat on the bed again. Dennis had his hand on the knob of the door, but Lawrence said, "Mr. Whitaker?"

"Yes?" He turned.

"...Thank you."

For several seconds, the boy simply stared at the man, whose eyes were closed. "Of course."

He slipped out of the door and shut it behind him quickly and quietly, shuffling to the bathroom at the end of the hall and disposing of the mask. He washed his hands fast, then scurried back down the hall and into the front of the clinic, where Robby and Dana stood once again at the front desk.

As he walked behind Robby, Dennis leaned just enough to whisper, "He's doing better," into the man's ear.

Before he could walk any further, Robby hastily spoke: "Let me borrow you for a second."

Dennis stopped and turned, eyeing Dana, then the doctor himself.

"I'll be quick, I know Dana's itching to get home. We do a small thing every year. It's," he waved his hand dismissively, "nothing wild, just a little get-together and a dinner on New Year's Eve."

"Oh, how fun," Dennis' voice grew bubbly, eyes darting back and forth between Robby and Dana.

She adjusted the blond bun at the back of her head as she clarified, "Real informal sort of thing. Adam and the kids come help me make Mr. Grouch over here feel less lonely," she jabbed her accusatory thumb into Robby's arm. "But he won't tell it to you like that," she snickered.

"I'll keep the food to myself this year, then," the doctor nodded.

"Oh, come on, Robby!"

"No, no, it's alright. It's quite alright. I can eat alone!" His sarcasm pulled a poorly-hidden smile from Dennis' face.

"Oh hush," she swatted.

"But, that is to say: you're more than welcome to join in if you'd like, Whitaker." Robby began rubbing the back of his neck, looking more uncertain as he turned to Dana and said, "Which leads me to my question for you."

He leaned over the front desk that separated the two of them, whispering a question Dana's ear that Dennis could not hear. It felt ever so subtly insulting, but that feeling quickly drowned under the millions of other thoughts racing through his mind. When the older man pulled back, Dennis watched as the two of them exchanged a glance. Dana looked troubled.

She chewed at her lip, resting a hand on her hip as she sighed and said, "Adam and the kids wouldn't give a rat's ass. But—whatever you do, don't be pushy about it. Just make it clear that she's more than welcome to come if she's comfortable doin' so, alright, Robby? Only if she's comfortable."

The man flashed two thumbs up at Dana, already stepping backward as he said, "Great. Whitaker?"

"I'd like to go," Dennis affirmed.

The world stopped for a moment as he took in the little, easily-missed smile that crept onto the corner of Robby's mouth.

"Fantastic," he said. Then, his tone felt serious again as he explained, "Now, I've got to get to a patient. Dana," he pointed at the blond woman.
