He briefed Dennis on the superficial frostbite in the patient's fingers and instructed him to keep them under warm water.

"Yes sir," he nodded, grabbing a clipboard of triage sheets from the desk and hustling over to a new crowd of incoming patients.

 

 

The day did not relent until long past the Robinavitch Health Center's typical closing hours. Generally speaking, as a rural clinic with such limited resources, Robby should've kept the hours strict and tight like just about every other clinic in the south. But he could count on one hand the times in his life he'd closed the clinic on-time—and he'd certainly never closed it early. Even when the place was closed, he'd always make sure to leave a hanging metal sign on the front door, crammed with the information and addresses of nearby hospitals.

After hanging it up on the suction-cup hook and tromping back inside, he took what felt like his first full breath since the day had begun. Rubbing his tired eyes and palming the stress from his cheeks, Robby let a prolonged groan curl forth from his throat.

"I think I'm getting too old for this, Whitaker," he half-joked, hollering toward the hallway where the boy was busy cleaning up the now empty exam rooms—save for room D, which was certainly not empty.

"For the clinic?" He asked.

Robby found it strangely endearing the way Dennis always took a statement literally—took many things literally. He certainly had a sense of humor, but a lot of times, his first instinct was to take a person seriously before assuming they were joshing around. That, to the doctor, was kind of cute. 

"Well, that too," he quipped, but frankly, neither he nor Dennis was sure what that meant. "You did fantastic today, kid."

Dennis nodded, feeling a modest little grin settle onto his face.

"...And, about the other day, Whitaker?" Robby rubbed at the dark beard on his jawline, watching as Dennis' brows raised in response. "I still didn't get to say much earlier. I—"

He certainly didn't like beating dead bushes. But,  to Robby, what Dennis had told him last week at his dining table wasn't a dead bush. It was a lit flame that threatened to continue growing until it swallowed a whole forest.

Perhaps Robby was the forest. Or perhaps he was the flame.

Dennis scampered down the hall, bags of trash knocking at his legs regardless how he shifted their weight in his grasp. "Apologies, Miss Collins," he bowed his head. "Busy day. Brain's all but mush."

"I see," she agreed, heels clacking softly on the vinyl floor as she passed the boy.

Before he walked any further, he turned and raised his index finger as he said, "Your brother's doing a lot better already. I'll come inside in a sec and give you the rundown."

The door closed behind him, and he trudged through the crunchy snow just a few feet forward before heaving the bags of trash up and into the massive bin that hugged the back wall of the clinic.

It was a quiet, dark night in Franklin. Snow fell softly onto the crown of his head where golden, dirty locks of hair caught them. The scary thing about the snow was that it truly muffled every fathomable sound of nature, leaving but one noise for Dennis to focus on: the voice in his head.

He turned his back to the door and leaned his weight into it, pinching the bridge of his nose, then rubbing circles into his temples. Perhaps some combination of stress from the day was mixing poorly with the other thoughts in his mind, because the overwhelm swept him like an avalanche. All at once, the irrational fear of rejection hit him. Robby could say every word in the English language, and none of it would convince Dennis - not while he was so drained - that he truly had no issues with his choices. And the problem was, one could not unhear a confession like the one Dennis had given. Robby would forever be aware of that secret, and that frightened the boy more than anything else in the world.

He allowed himself a reward for his tiring work that day: a single, soft sob, muffled by the palm of his shivering hand.

After a moment basking in the silence, he pulled himself upright and swung the back door open once more. Warm air rushed onto his cheeks, eliciting a buzzing, tingling sensation. "His fever's down to about a hundred and one," Robby nodded, already mid-conversation with Heather about her brother. When Dennis caught his eye, he gestured to the boy and said, "Thanks to Whitaker."

"I didn't do anything," he dismissed lightly, shuffling behind the front desk and squatting to idly sort papers. "Just checked up on him as much as I could."

"I appreciate that, Mr. Whitaker," Heather glanced at the desk. She returned her eyes to Robby's, then firmly offered, "Thank you."

The doctor couldn't lie and pretend it wasn't immensely stressful keeping Lawrence hidden away in one of only four exam rooms, leaving what would've been a relatively normal day to becoming single-handedly the most crowded day he'd had all year.

Even so, he mustered a polite nod and rasped out the words: "Any time."

"And what were you sayin' about New Year?" Heather recalled.

The statement pulled Dennis' attention. He peeked over the surface of the front desk as Robby said, "Right! "Myself," Robby pointed his index finger into his chest, "Dennis, Dana, her husband, two kids. That's all."

Hearing Dana's family be mentioned set a stale look on Heather's face.

Robby, having immediately noticed, explained: "They're good people. Her husband's into a lot of activism up in Nashville and her kids are far too young to have pulled any nasty ideologies from classmates, or what have you..." he drifted off.

"Frankly... and I do appreciate the offer, Doctor Robinavitch, I just shouldn't. I mean, I'm not even supposed to be here," she gestured around the clinic. "At all."

"According to who?" Robby brushed off her point with a shrug.

She shot him an incredulous look. "You weren't born yesterday. Neither was I."

"Well, you're here anyway, aren't you?"

Heather paused. She didn't really know how to argue with that, at least.

Instead, she shook her head and mustered another response: "...I'd not feel too kind leaving my brothers behind for New Year. I'm all they've got. So, I really do appreciate your invite, but—"

"You could bring 'em," Dennis interrupted softly from behind the desk.

Neither Heather nor Robby turned to Dennis, but the boy's suggestion hung in the air between them—in the silence.

"Lawrence and your middle brother, I mean." The boy cleared his throat and continued: "New Year's still two weeks out. He'll be non-contagious and probably back hopping on two feet by then."

Heather could sense that he'd taken a liking to Lawrence, on some level.

Robby shrugged, eyes still fixated on the woman's own dark brown pupils as he muttered in agreement with Dennis, "Why not? Bring them with you. Between Dana and I, we've got enough food to share."

"Heavens," she groaned, rubbing soothing circles into her temples as she shook her head. "I don't know that it's—"

"Just think it over," Robby pushed softly. "Don't stress about it tonight, just keep it on your mind—ask your brothers how they'd feel."

Finally, Heather glanced over at Dennis, who picked at his fingernail nervously, hands hugged into his sternum. He broke eye contact as soon as hers met his, but listened as she sighed.

"I'll talk to them," she resigned in monotone—detached and stern.

 

 

Days later, on the eve of December twenty-fifth, Michael Robinavitch kept his clinic closed. He'd have liked to keep it open and run it himself as he'd done for years prior, but this winter was a different world. Times were changing, and while the troubles of the people grew, the resources to supply and sustain emergency medicine - as makeshift and unofficial as it truly was - did not. Robby had been hearing chatter about it, too—it wasn't just Franklin which suffered from the evolving need for such care; it was everywhere. Every city. Every state. There would not be another shipment of his supplies from local hospitals until after the holidays - which was not abnormal, but having so few supplies left to work with during such downtime was abnormal - so he and Dana had worked out a plan to limit his care to regular house visits.

Thus, instead of serving the people he'd grown to care for after near-decades of working in the small town, he hung up the hospital information sign at the front door, grabbed mail from just beside the street, then locked himself in for the day. The man burned through most of the afternoon in his tub, pruning until the water had gone cool. He'd brought his mail with him, leaving the pile sitting on the little wooden table just beside. Every so often he'd dry his hands off with a free towel, careful not to splash even a single drop of bathwater on the papers as he worked his way down the stack.

Utility bill. Mortgage statement. A cream-colored envelope from the NAACP—Robby skimmed through that one: it urged a stronger push to pressure the Kennedy Administration for a more potent Civil Rights bill. One had passed in June, but it was just groundwork. Robby flipped through another piece of mail from a local church—they were always trying to drag him in there. He snorted, then tossed that one. It skated on the floor until it hit the dark baseboard moulding opposite the bathroom. 

He was about to toss the whole stack, but a name caught his eye on the envelope now at the top of the stack. Robby squinted, lifting himself up just enough to extend his neck and confirm. The envelope looked cheap, and in poor, pencil handwriting, the front said: For Dr. Robby.

Robby's family did not usually send him mail—they hardly likely knew he was alive. The man was horrendous at keeping in contact through long distance, regardless how deeply he loved his family. But mail from family was not necessarily unheard of for him, so he carefully pinched the envelope between his fingers and tore the edge neatly, pulling out a folded sheet of blank paper inside. It had what looked like a tiny stain of food on the top left corner, and the handwriting was hardly legible. 

December 25, 1961

Dear Dr. Robby,

It is Dennis Whitaker, and I wanted to write you to thank you for everything you do for me. I am not sure my behavior last week was reflecting how I really feel, because I really feel scared. I know you are a busy man and therefore should not feel obligated to read my whining any more than you already heard it. So I will say it plain: you know more about me than I wanted you to, and that scares me.

But Doctor Robby, you have also proven time and time again that you are a good man. 

Lawrence Collins told me that even a basket of rotting apples can have one good one left. It does not mean I can trust every apple, but I know I can trust you.

I have seen the ways in which you care for others. I have seen the pieces of yourself that you break away to nourish the needing world around you. I should know because you have broken off so many pieces of yourself to help me.

I will not pretend to be a poet, so again I will say plainly: knowing you as well as I believe I do, it was unfair to cut you off. It was very scary and I did not want to hear what I feared you would say to me about my past, because it took everything of me to run away from it. But I believe maybe you only meant to console me.

I quite enjoy working at the clinic. I will hopefully learn to live without Dana being there. After all, I've got you, too.

Thank you for providing me a place to be, 

D.W.

Robby held onto the letter like it would flutter away if he let up. He let out a long sigh and wasted no time pulling himself out of the tub. He held the letter above his head as his bare body tracked water across the bathroom floor. Placing the letter on the edge of his sink counter, he yanked his soft brown towel from its hanging bar and dried himself off, from his feet to his face.

The pounding in his heart continued as he stormed through his living room, having only just jumped into a fresh pair of underwear as he approached his little golden bar cart in the corner. The man gnawed at the inside of his cheek, contemplating so very heavily.

He knew Dennis had no one to spend the holiday with. And in any case, Robby also intended to spend the holiday alone; he did not celebrate Christmas, and typically stayed huddled in his home during winter months. But he was such a stubborn man; he still hadn't properly gotten his two cents out about Dennis Whitaker's secret.

"Still haven't said my piece," he mumbled, shaking his head as his eyes bore holes into the tied cans of red and white Budweiser beer atop the bar cart.

Robby repeated that phrase in his head over and over as he packed the beers in a neat brown paper bag and dressed himself head to toe in thick, winter clothes.

It was his excuse to go pester Dennis Whitaker.

It was his excuse to - for once in his life, at least since he'd moved to Tennessee a decade ago - allow himself to enjoy the company of another during the holiday. It wasn't like he had anything better to do anyway; this was the first day in as long as he could remember that the clinic would not be open at all. He'd become, as most people did when they drowned themselves in work to avoid their issues, painfully reliant on the noise. Without it, he did not know how to quell the thoughts racing through his mind, and the aching of loneliness which had long since become white noise now panged around deep in his bones. Robby wasn't sure Dennis would've been his first choice of companions on his one day off in months, but the kid was kind, and he owed him a little grace after everything they'd been through together the past few weeks.

He buttoned his black coat, grabbed the handle of the big paper bag chock full of beer cans, then made his way downstairs. Cold winter gusts bombarded him the moment he stepped outside of the clinic, and his feet crunched in the snow as he trudged toward his car.

"Dammit!" He snapped as the bottom of the bag gave out, the cans of beer plopping one after another into the snow.

Hastily, Robby squatted and grabbed as many as he could carry in his arms, shuffling to the car and struggling to raise open the trunk. When finally he succeeded, the cans fell from his arms and into the little space. The man ran back to grab the remaining two cans from their cushion in the snow, placing them more kindly into the trunk and slamming it shut before rushing to his seat behind the wheel.

 

 

Dennis Whitaker's home had become something of a library, increasingly full of notes and journals and medical books as he'd begun earning steady income working at the clinic. He'd grown attached to his little one-story house in his quiet, secluded neighborhood, and though he could probably have afforded to upgrade - even just a little - he didn't.

He sat at his newly-purchased oak desk, scribbling notes into his most recent journal while his eyes flicked from paper to page. Something about writing down the information he was reading helped drastically improve his ability to remember it—to turn it into muscle memory. Working as busily as he did at the clinic over the winter had also played a large role in honing his ability to assess under pressure and take on a broader medical perspective. 

The boy was so deeply focused on the page he'd been scribbling on, he nearly missed the hundredth knock at his door, even as it grew louder and louder.

When it finally pulled his focus, Dennis shot up from his desk, scurrying over with a cocked eyebrow and folded arms. He swung open the door, and there stood Michael Robinavitch, bundled in a black winter coat, cheeks flushed in the cold, hair still damp from what could only have been a bath.

"It's a little chilly out here," Robby quipped, shivering against the pure-white, snowy backdrop.

Dennis' eyes met the beer cans stacked precariously in the man's arms, then they returned to his pupils as he stammered, "Dr. Robby?"

"Can I come in?" He urged, and Dennis was already nodding.

"Yes— please."

"Thanks."

"Are you alright?"

"Why wouldn't I be?"

"...I," Dennis blinked, following with his eyes as Robby tracked snow into his home and set the festive cans of beer down on his dinky wooden dining table—the same table he'd eaten so much of Robby's leftovers at whenever the man would send him home with them. "No reason. I just wasn't expecting you, is all."

Robby turned as he stacked the beers. "I got your letter."

"You did," Dennis' brow twitched.

"Figured you'd finally let me talk. About the thing. The—"

"Right," the boy sighed.

He wasn't actually prepared to hear what Robby had to say about any of his secrets, but he'd sort of signed himself up for that with the letter he'd written, mailed, and forgotten about.

"Right," he repeated, rubbing his forehead with the back of his hand as he closed the front door. "On Christmas Eve? Is that the greatest time to have this conversation?"

"I don't celebrate Christmas, Whitaker," the older man said matter-of-factly.

"...I'm aware. I do."

"Oh, really? Got any plans?" Robby looked around the empty room, tongue sharp with wit.

Dennis swallowed. "Well— no, but..."

Robby cracked open the top of a can and turned to face Dennis with his nose in the air, shrugging as he spoke, "Just a few beers and a talk. Can I ask for that?"

The boy blinked, lips parted with a hint of disbelief at the older man's audacity, but still found himself reaching for the can as Robby extended his arm.

Dennis sat on one end of the couch, Robby on the other, leaving lots of space between them. The pitiful-looking fireplace across the room did a surprisingly good job of warming the living area, and it left a golden glow on the older man's face that Dennis could not help but stare at.

For a while, they sat in silence. Dennis sipped modestly at his can of beer, Robby already on his second.

As he cracked open the top, the older man finally placated, "I'm very sorry I didn't make you feel safe hearing what I had to say about it. Your past."

"You couldn't have done any better."

"I'm not good enough?"

"You know that isn't what I mean," Dennis rolled his eyes.

"I know," Robby chuckled, sipping.

"What I mean is... well, it's just what my letter said. You proved over and over that you're a good man, I— I dunno why I'm so scared of... the knowing. You knowing me deeper than I wanted. Or..." he trailed off, rambling, "maybe I wanted you to know, and it was just gonna be scary no matter how the cookie crumbled, I don't know. You know what the APA says. It's a mental disorder. And I don't have it in me to plaster that on my record."

A look settled onto Robby's face, one that screamed like he needed to say something.

"I've met many, many people in my life, Whitaker. You don't make it as far as I have without meeting a lot of people. And the more people you meet, the more you realize how much things like this shouldn't matter. There are more than three billion people on this planet. Did you know that?"

Dennis shook his head.

"Three billion. That's unimaginable. Out of three billion people, why should it shock anyone that some of them are different?"

The question hung in the air, and Dennis did not answer it.

"I couldn't fathom becoming a father, only to shun my child should he become a different man than I expected to see." Robby paused, contemplating the worth of continuing. His brows knitted together and his head shook as he blurted: "I couldn't fathom being shunned by the very people who brought me into this world. For something I could not control."

An unreadable light flashed in the doctor's eyes. He sniffled with a twitch of his nose, then tipped his head back to sip the last of his third can.

"When did you know?"

"What?" Dennis blinked.

"When did you know you were a homosexual?"

"Okay," Dennis leaned forward, prepared to reach for Robby's can. "Maybe three's enough for you. You're startin' to ask things you really don't mean to ask—"

As Dennis reached his hand out, Robby grabbed firmly to his wrist, stopping his motion as he held the beer can in his other hand pointedly away from him.

"Dammit, Dennis Whitaker, you owe me some transparency," Robby snapped. "If I wanted to shut you out or admit you for it, Jesus Christ, I would've done so by now!"

The boy's lip twitched. He knew he owed Michael Robinavitch transparency—or at least a little grace. The man had spent the last two years picking up what few pieces of himself Dennis had smuggled to Franklin, putting them back together at his own steady, firm pace. He was the reason Dennis was alive, for God's sake. He was the reason Dennis had a job, and the reason he still had people he cared about.

Without letting go of the boy's wrist, Robby took one more hefty swig of his can, then set it down on the little coffee table in front of the couch. When he leaned back into the couch, his hand shifted, ghosting over Dennis' palm until he was just barely holding it—rubbing circles over the knuckles with his thumb. His eyes fixated on those rough knuckles. That thin, calloused, farm-worn hand.

"...I guess I always knew," Dennis mumbled. "I imagine it's just somethin' I figured out because I was the only one of my brothers who didn't drool over all the pretty girls at church. Or school." He paused. "I could live like that forever—I think. When you grow up without love, you never really learn to miss it."

"God," Robby groaned, shaking his head.

"What?" Dennis asked, almost defensively.

"Why do that to yourself? I mean, why—"

"Because of the fucking APA? Because I could ruin my future, I could... what do you mean 'why'?" Dennis scoffed, pulling his hand away from Robby. "I don't even know why I'm still telling you all of this!" He let out a singular, hysterical laugh. "You're askin' dumb questions because you don't know anything about it!"

"Dumb questions?"

"Dumb questions!" The boy spat.

"Really?"

"Yes! What else is there to say? You couldn't possibly understand what it's like."

Robby clenched his jaw tightly, turning to Dennis and demanding: "Then make me understand it."

The boy stared, dumbfounded, at the doctor.

Silence filled the living area, save for the crackling of the fireplace.

"You've gone batshit crazy," he shook his head.

"Have I?" Robby dared.

"You have."

Robby nodded, "Will you then, per the APA, admit me to psychotherapy? Or maybe shock therapy would fix me?" He mocked his own tone—that tone he'd always use when providing Dennis or Heather with medical hypotheticals. 

"Fuck you," Dennis genuinely whined.

"I'm serious."

"You're an asshole!"

"Oh, please. It's no different than assessing any other ailment, right?"

"It's—"

"Diagnose me, Whitaker. So that I might be cured of wanting you."

Those last words weren't necessarily supposed to come out. Even Robby himself seemed a little shocked he said it, but it was nothing compared to Dennis' reaction. He visibly flinched, eyes widening like a deer in headlights as his lips parted—perhaps he wanted to say something, but words did not come out. The older man stuck his nose up as the edges of his lips curled down, as if to double down on his behavior—as if to really dare Dennis Whitaker to do something. 

Before he knew it, Dennis was straddling Robby, their lips smashed together with near-painful force. The older man's large hands hovered just over Dennis' ass, shocked for just a moment before they finally settled. He felt the doctor's curious hand drag up to his hip, stopping to grab firmly at the curve of his waist with possessive force. Deep, gravelly groans escaped Robby's mouth as Dennis shifted in his lap, ghosting his own hands up the small of the man's back over his sweater and tangling his fingers into dark, shaggy locks of brown hair.

The scent of the older man overwhelmed him; it was a musk that Dennis had become familiar with drowning in any time he was invited up into the man's home, but his skin also smelled faintly of bath soap. His dark hair was still damp, too, between Dennis' fingers.

The doctor mumbled against Dennis' wetted lips, "Only you."

The boy wasn't sure what that meant, but it was just enough of a break between the kiss for Dennis to really snap out of it.

He jerked back, trying awkwardly to shuffle out of Robby's lap and to his feet. 

"I don't know what the hell's gotten into us," Dennis' voice shook, heart pounding with crushing force against his chest. "But that's... quite enough, I think," he added, unconvincingly.

Be it the alcohol in the older man's system or pure madness, he reached out and grabbed Dennis' wrist, keeping the boy from straying too far as he said, "It's a little late for that now, is it not, Whitaker?"

The overwhelm of lust revived in Dennis' loins as quickly as he'd pushed it away. Quicker, even. His lips shone wet with Robby's spit, hair disheveled, flannel tugged at the collar like he'd been mauled.

"No one's watching us," Robby added softly, like he was equally trying to convince himself of that.

"God's watching us!" Dennis frowned, maintaining the feeble distance between them.

It took a lot for Robby to keep himself from rolling his eyes. He'd all but given up on God—long ago, too. In his opinion, if God truly cared, the being would have stopped a great many unfortunate events throughout Robby's life. Thus, he didn't necessarily see the harm in partaking in an unfortunate event of his own choosing, for once.

Alas, he could never bring himself to pressure Dennis Whitaker to do this, especially not when it was something he himself had never even fathomed trying; intimacy with a man.

An unheard of thing, really. A dangerous thing.

"...Okay," Robby resigned softly. Thoughtfully. He let his hand slip away, falling to his lap as he eyed the boy in front of him. 

But, for Dennis, it was different now that he was being given the chance to pull away. 

It was a chance he was reluctant to take. 

Dennis may never again be given the opportunity to taste a pleasure like this. He knew he'd either die alone or marry a woman and feign attraction until death do they part. That was an inevitability he'd long since come to terms with. It was a long, mundane life he'd come to terms with. The issue was, until he'd met Michael Robinavitch, there wasn't a third option to consider. There was no exciting, dangerous third path for Dennis to dare himself to walk toward. 

This reluctance which kept him from backing out from between Robby's legs was born of a hunger that had been festering within him for almost two years now.

What would be the cost of not trying it? Even if only once?

"Will..." Dennis began, so very hesitantly as he avoided Robby's eyes, "...will we ever get a chance like this again?"

The question was perplexing. Robby's brows furrowed, and he watched the frustrated expression on Dennis' face melt into one of openness. A timid, reluctant openness to entertain this horrible idea that sat in the air between the two of them.

Truthfully, Robby didn't have an answer to the question, and he wouldn't act as though he did. "All I can say for certain," he breathed heavily, "is that I'm here right now."

"...One time," Dennis mumbled, lower than a whisper.

Robby was already nodding, legs opening again as lust and holiday beer coursed through his veins. "One time," he parroted softly.

"Then this'll pass," Dennis added, almost like a plea to God. Or a plea to himself.

With a foot forward, the younger man stepped back into Robby's space, breath heavy as they began, once again, to swim in one another's eyes. 

Slowly, Dennis melted back into Robby's lap, partaking in a stalemate with the older man, both wanting to steal the first kiss, but neither daring to. Their exhales danced into one another and Robby searched for permission on Dennis' face, keeping himself at bay and allowing the younger man to go this at his own pace. 

Seconds later, Dennis' hands found Robby's cheeks, planting and feeling as if to make certain that the older man was real. He felt the stubble and the beard on Robby's cheeks and jawline, fingers ghosting further back until they tangled in the man's hair. 

"You're acting like I'm gonna slip between your fingers. Like a fish," Robby whispered.

"I'm just nervous," Dennis spoke with pathetic honesty. 

Robby swallowed. Then, he offered a little transparency, saying, "...I am, too." 

That mutual understanding gave Dennis just enough confidence to dip his head down, initiating the kiss. He pushed everything else out of his mind: God, his family, his future. None of it mattered, for just as long as his lips danced clumsily along Robby's own. 

Robby reciprocated the kiss tenderly, sliding his hands over Dennis' hips again to ground him as they warmed up to this horrible idea.

The door slammed shut, and all Dennis could do was watch as Robby's car pulled out of his makeshift driveway, turning and heading back down the road from which it came, disappearing into the cold winter night.

 

Chapter End Notes

So... actually- you know what, I'm gonna keep my mouth shut.

I hope you enjoyed this chapter, and please note that as we break into the final stretch of this story, I WILL be updating and adding tags. Thank you so much for your continued support on my story <3

Chapter 8: Once I Was

Chapter Summary

As everything begins to change, Dennis begs for Robby's affection, further complicating a difficult dynamic.

Chapter Notes

Chapter 8's song is "Once I Was" by Tim Buckley on his 1967 album, "Goodbye And Hello"

See the end of the chapter for more notes

August 11, 1962

Mama,

I should first apologize for New Year. A lot was going on in my life at the time. Frankly, there's still a lot going on in my life. I still won't deny your arrival at my home was ill-timed, but I know you meant well, and I don't want that to sit in the space between us for the rest of our lives. It's why I'm writing now. 

I want to set the record straight about Dr. Robinavitch. He's a good man. I'd like to believe he's a good man, it's what I've chosen to believe for two years. I don't imagine it's fair that should change just because of your assumptions. But I've been considering your perspective more and more since that day. Life in Franklin has felt more lonesome this year. Friends come and go, of course, but processing that is a little hard.

I think I've begun to long for Nebraskan air. Maybe I've become homesick. Maybe it's the lonely chill of autumn creeping in. Maybe it's me. Work has been very busy, and I've been feeling more isolated. I hardly know who I am right now, but I know who I want to be. I know very well—

Dennis crushed the unfinished letter in his hand, cursing under his breath and tossing it indiscriminately behind him. The crumpled ball bounced off the lampshade on his nightstand with a rattling thud, tapping softly when it landed on the floor beside his bed. Elbows propped on the work desk across the bedroom, the boy rested his head in his hands, sobbing softly as he slumped further in his chair. Tears pooled in little shimmering puddles on the wooden surface and his nose began running until he caught the rolling snot with violent sniffles. With the back of his hand, he wiped several times just at his nostrils.

Eight months had passed since the New Year's Day incident, and Robby hadn't been remotely the same since. The man had built up an impressive wall around himself since then. The last time Dennis could remember a real vulnerable conversation between them was back in winter, when Robby broke to him that Heather Collins wanted to stop showing up at the clinic. Of course, atop everything else, the older man's swelling infatuation for Dennis had all but died—which is what really left the boy aching. Robby was, truly, a hollow shell of a man working in his own clinic. Detached, isolated, robotic. 

Mind trailing off, he began to replay his name coming from Robby's mouth last winter, the only times he'd ever heard 'Dennis' from the man he so desperately—

Pushing the chair with the back of his legs as he forced himself back down to reality.

"Don't," he warned himself, biting hard on his inner lip until the faint taste of blood hit his tongue.

The heat in Franklin was like a wet, suffocating blanket that day, sun beaming down on Dennis' pale skin harshly as he stepped outside his home, work bag on his shoulder. He walked over to his sorry excuse for a new car, having proudly purchased it at the local dealership in the outskirts of town back in the spring. Dennis lugged himself into the driver seat quite like how he'd just lugged his duffel back into the back seat, starting the creaky old hunk-of-junk with the key and gripping the steering wheel.

For Dennis Whitaker, it was time to head into another long day at the clinic that had become the subject of his nightmares.

 

 

"Mama's strong," Samantha squeezed Ella Jones' hand as it lay just off the edge of the patient bed. "She has to pull through this, Michael."

Robby gnawed at his cheek. He already knew very well the situation that was about to unfold. Ella, having been in decline since the day he'd diagnosed her with heart failure years ago, was likely in her death bed. Room C of the Robinavitch Health Center had never felt so much like a tomb. Robby would have liked to scold Sam for not having taken Ella to a genuine hospital, but that was never Ella's thing, nor her daughter's. It's why they liked Robby's work—why they'd spent the last several years having the man perform home visits.

For his part, it had given the man an opportunity to settle into Franklin: Ella was a warm soul—like a mother. He'd accepted that warmth alongside the affection from Samantha while, in exchange, providing the services required to keep the elderly woman happy and - as much as could be willed - healthy enough, despite her disease.

The morning - and the day in general, for that matter - had already been unkind to Robby prior to Samantha's arrival with her dying mother, but he could see it in his ex-lover's eyes the moment she barged through the front door with the elderly woman slumped in her wheelchair. That haunting look in her eyes. The look of desperation. The look that many people had given Michael Robinavitch throughout his life which screamed for help. Death had come knocking at Ella Jones' door, and Robby was the man expected to ward it off.

But it had now been nearly an hour. It was clear to him: death would not relent this day.

"I need you to wait in here again, Samantha," Robby instructed frantically.

"Are you coming right back?"

"I'm coming right back. Just grabbing that morphine for the pain. But..." he wanted to begin the conversation. That nightmarish one wherein a doctor was tasked to break to someone that their loved one did not have much time left. 

But he couldn't. Not just yet.

"Thank you," she mustered, body wracked by emotion as she clutched her unresponsive mother's hand.

The doctor's brows furrowed, and he offered a polite, silent nod in place of verbal response—he didn't want his voice to quiver. The situation with Ella was hitting closer to home than he'd ever comfortably admit, but it was on top of everything else going on that day in the clinic. And in his mind. 

The door clicked shut as he entered the hallway, but he did not immediately walk to the front and update Dana. Instead, he stood with planted feet, back facing the door, jaw clenched as he ground his teeth down, rubbing the back of his head up and down with his palm. It took longer than usual for the man to calm himself, and he nearly bent the patient clipboard with the white-knuckled grip of his free hand. When, finally, he did calm himself, Robby's demeanor snapped back into place like a rubber band—like he was a robot having been rebooted; he put one foot in front of the other, finding his footing and making his way back to the front of the clinic.

Triage looked busy. Dennis stood on the opposite side of the front desk from Dana, duffel still on his shoulder—he'd likely only just come in.

Robby pointedly paid little attention to the kid as he slammed the patient sheet on the table. "Pulmonary edema."

The blond woman locked eyes with the doctor, a little shocked.

"Poor girl..."

"She won't make it to sunset. She's got a lot of fluid in her lungs and she's hardly responsive—even in the best case scenario, she'll be lucky to go peacefully and painlessly."

"Gosh."

"I don't know how or why she didn't get here - or to a hospital - a hell of a lot sooner, but," Robby shook his head, digging into his inner cheek with clenched teeth, "she's not gonna make it. And I'll have to break it to Sam, and she's just going to—"

"If you need any help, I—"

"I've got it," Robby nearly barked, jerking his arm away from the hand Dennis tried placing reassuringly. After processing the confused look from the boy and the disappointed look from Dana, Robby repeated more calmly, "I've got it."

He did not, in fact, know what he was going to do, but imagining the tenderness of Dennis' touch agitated that little warning sign flashing in the back of his brain. The one that had kept him mostly at arm's length from the kid for eight months now.

"Robby," Dana warned sternly. "The kid just got here. He's only trying to help."

Rather than acknowledge the warning - or his abrasive attitude - Robby only nodded, stepping away without so much as offering an apologetic glance at Dennis. The good thing about having been swamped with nightmarish tasks and cripplingly overwhelming thoughts all day was that: adding one more to the pile - in the form of an insulted and belittled Dennis Whitaker - did not hurt Robby as badly as it otherwise would've.

Or perhaps it did, and the pain just hadn't registered yet.

In any case, the doctor made his way to the back of the clinic to grab morphine. It was common to allow a patient some of it as they passed if it was going to be a relatively peaceful ordeal, but deaths weren't all that common in Robby's clinic. He was a very talented doctor, but more than that, Franklin was a small town, and he was lucky to have had a major hospital relatively nearby to redirect truly dire cases to. But 'relatively' was not enough for Ella Jones—not in her condition. She would not have made it to Williamson in time, and even if she did, Robby knew very well there was little even a major hospital could do for the elderly woman at this point. Fingers wrapped around the morphine, he eyed a diuretic on the shelf below, imagining how useful it might've been if Ella had come in sooner.

Would've, could've should've.

Swiveling and striding back where he came from, he passed the front desk, glass ampoule of morphine secured in his clenched fist.

"Dr. Robby—"

"Not now, Whitaker," he dismissed once again, barreling down the hallway and practically slugging himself into exam room C to avoid facing the kid.

Meeting Samantha's wet-pearl eyes, Robby bit down once again on his inner cheek. He stepped over to the edge of the bed, breath shaky. The quiet of the room around betrayed him; those quivering inhales and exhales fell heavy on the weeping daughter's ears.

"Michael?" She asked.

He now bit down hard enough to draw blood from the flesh inside his cheek. It tasted of iron and stung like guilt. It was not his fault that Ella Jones was dying in his clinic, but it certainly felt so. He'd known the woman for years, regularly visiting her—and her daughter. He'd grown fond of the two of them, even long after his fling with Samantha Jones had ended. They still cared about one another. But it made the whole situation far more pungent. He sat in his chair like God, prepared to break to the poor woman next to him that her mother, her life, her world, would soon pass. It was like he was God, declaring that another kind soul was to be taken from the world, and it stirred every other troubled whisper inside his body to do so.

"Samantha," he murmured, inhaling a sharp breath through his nose and drumming his fingers against the ampoule in his hand. "I don't believe this is something your mother will recover from. Not this time."

The woman froze, lips parted, jaw going slack.

The world itself seemed to quieten outside the patient room, almost supernaturally so. Robby did not dare move—not even an inch. His dark, warm irises stayed fixated on the elderly woman's cold, pale hand as it rested on the milky-white sheet at the edge of the bed. Really, it was a little odd; Michael Robinavitch had dealt with dying patients many times throughout his career already, but never one that felt so personal. Or even remotely personal. He'd always been able to keep a sympathetic - but relatively detached - approach to the matter. 

This was different. It felt different.

"I can make her very comfortable," he raised the morphine ampoule. "This—"

"—Can't we take her to Williamson? Can't we—"

"It won't do her any good at this point."

"Dammit, Michael."

"It would only," he choked down a rather unexpected lump in his throat. "Would only serve to bring her more discomfort."

"What changed? Thirty minutes ago you were givin' her oxygen, talkin' about diuretics, and... and—"

"Nothing changed," he nodded. "She was in this state the moment you walked in. I'm sorry."

"...Was there anything I could've done differently?" She asked, but the question felt directed more toward a higher power than to Robby himself.

He still obliged: "There's no way to know for sure."

"There has to be something you can do, Michael. Surely—surely there's something?" 

The doctor held his breath for a few beats.

"Right now, Ella's suffering from pulmonary edema, Sam. She's got a lot of fluid in her lungs, and the retention elsewhere in her body on top of her unresponsiveness, discoloration, labored breathing, and cool limbs just shows—"

He could not finish the sentence. How odd, in Robby's mind, that he could not finish that sentence.

Fully understanding what the man was implying - and forcing herself to process, knowing none of this was Robby's fault - Sam grit her teeth. It took all the strength in her body to muster a singular affirmative nod. She wiped her nose with the back of her hand, choking out a sob. "This is it?"

The doctor wetted his lips. He did not look up—did not meet the woman's eyes. But he did nod. Slowly. Hesitantly.

"As I said," his voice was low and meek, "I can make her comfortable."

Samantha Jones did not respond. She tore her eyes from the doctor and laid them, wet and desperate as they were, upon her mother's face. Her ears drank in every labored breath that escaped the elderly woman's chapped, ghostly lips. It was a sign to her that the woman was not dead yet—sign enough that she had at least a moment more to think of some solution. It was her heart's futile attempt to deny the part of her brain that knew very well her mother did not have any time left.

She shook her head. "I can't do this."

"I understand," Robby comforted.

"I can't let her go. It can't be here. It can't be now. Surely not."

A pause.

"I'll give you some privacy. A moment to process. But Sam," Robby offered as he stood to his feet, setting the ampoule in the tray beside the bed, just next to the sterile syringe.

Samantha did not look away from her mother.

"She should be comfortable. This won't get any easier."

He waited for her response, but it did not come. Thus, he offered a polite nod, holding himself calm just long enough to exit the room and shut the door behind him. But the very moment that door clicked closed, a searing bubble of overwhelm churned and rose in his stomach, up to his chest. It burned a path up his insides, leaving his fingertips tingling and his lungs at loss for air. It was a swelling sense of doom, a loss of control.

Robby stepped forward and down the hall, digging the heel of his palm into his sternum as he wobbled his way toward the bathroom. He needed a moment of real silence. Needed a moment away from the world to shut everything out and forget this part of himself. Forget his past. Forget why this day had become so overwhelmingly unmanageable. He knew it would be an awful idea to, for the first time in his life in Tennessee, work the eleventh of August. It was a horrible decision, and yet he could not bring himself to keep the clinic closed, even for a single—

"Oh, no, no," Dana scolded, grabbing his arm and tugging him past the bathroom door, walking the man like a dog to the back of the clinic before letting her tight grip loosen. "You wanna tell me what the fuck is goin' on with you today?"

The man's eyes were wide, bewildered.

"What's going on with me?" He mumbled in repeat, then huffed. "Sam's mother is dying in my clinic, Dana. That's what's going on with me."

He tried pushing past, but the woman's firm hand was already firmly planted into his shoulder, keeping him right where he stood.

"You've had a pretty thorny fuckin' stick in your ass all day—since way before Sam and Ella came in. I'm not an idiot, Robby."

"I think I may have shared it with you the way you're tugging me around," he remarked.

"Don't give me that attitude right now, kid," she bit back, as though Robby wasn't 41—a whole year older than her. "What the hell has gotten into you with your mentees this year? Ditchin' Heather Collins after promisin' her the world wasn't enough for you?"

"That's different and you know it—"

"No, actually, I don't know! Because you won't tell me shit about it."

Robby hissed in a quieted voice: "Because I can't. Do you really not trust that safety is the only reason I'd make such a drastic decision? On her behalf?"

The woman did trust Robby that much, at least. She knew him very well; at his core, he wouldn't have made such a choice without good reason. Still, she had other issues to poke at the man about, especially after seeing his behavior toward Dennis:

"Then brushin' Whitaker off like he's a nuisance in your clinic? Really? That's a whole other kind of cruel. That kid has been lookin' up to you since the day you dragged him here to work for you. I can't recall a time he hasn't looked up to you like you're God himself, reincarnated and walkin' on two ungrateful feet. And even as much of an asshole you've been to him since January, he's kept on workin', workin', and workin', just to please you. I see it in his eyes—he ain't doin' this for the love of the game like he used to, he's doin' it for you. Ungrateful son of a..."

"He caught me at a busy moment—"

"Every day in this place is a busy moment, Robby! And it's only gettin' busier every year!" She clapped her palms together, making the man visibly flinch. "That's my whole damn point! How am I supposed to move to Knoxville on Monday knowing I'm leaving that poor kid behind to get eaten alive by the very man who begged him to come work in this hellhole?"

"He's not—"

"No, really, Robby. Really." Her eyes were fiery with a maternal ferocity. "You can't be actin' like this when I'm gone. You can't fall apart in your own stompin' ground like this. That boy needs you."

"This isn't an everyday thing, Dana. Today's just... today's different. It's..." he trailed off, unable to bring himself to explain it. Unable to open himself up just enough to show the pain squeezing at his heart.

"How is it 'different'?"

Something flashed in Robby's eyes. Something that Dana could not decipher, but very much noticed.

He shook his head. "It just is."

"You aren't gonna tell me? After everything we've been through?"

"There's nothing to say," he successfully shut himself down. "It's just a rough day."

Robby built his walls high, keeping something very close to his chest. The woman could see it; she could see that he wanted to say more, perhaps needed to. There very clearly was something to say. Something more complex than the pain of losing Ella Jones, something more deep-rooted than his gripe with Dennis Whitaker or his distancing from Heather Collins. Robby had a dark cloud over his head—but he wouldn't budge.

"Dammit, Robinavitch—what the hell is so special about today?" Dana blurted shamelessly, voice raised.

Such prodding rendered Robby silent. His voice would not work, so in lieu of a verbal response, he shook his head. The look on his face begged Dana not to prod any further. She drank that look in, letting it simmer in her mind for a moment before deciding it was no longer worth hounding him for.

"...It's not my business," she sighed, finally retreating.

The man's jaw loosened just a little, eyebrows untensing.

"But that kid out there workin' his ass off for you? Day and night for almost two years now? That is very much my business—until I'm out of Franklin. You treat him with respect, or so help me God, I'll make my last few days here Hell on Earth for you."

Robby finally pushed past Dana, making his way toward the hall as he said without turning back to face the woman, "I've got to check on Sam."

"I'm deadly serious, Michael Robinavitch," the woman warned once more—for good measure.

The man dug into the flesh inside his cheek once again, drawing more blood as he gripped the doorknob of exam room C, knuckles white. He couldn't bring himself to twist it and push the door open—not yet. Instead, he took a few hasty, frantic inhales and exhales, letting the pounding of his heart thrum in his ears like the beat of an apocalyptic drum. Only when it became unbearably loud did he finally twist the knob and slip inside the eerily silent room.

Samantha did not look up; she was still slouched in her chair, lurching over her mother's hand with forehead pressed to wrist as soft sobs of premature mourning grazed past her lips.

Robby stepped over gently, jaw clenched tight as he took his seat to the other side of the woman, making room to examine Ella Jones' pale, cold body. He sat with Samantha for several minutes, keeping a professional distance, but eyeing her. Matted, long brown hair hid the anguish on her face. Every so often, the doctor would glance at Ella, nearly unable to hold his eyes upon her given how well he'd known the woman. She wasn't just his patient—she'd, in a way, taken care of him when he first relocated to Franklin. Took him in when he'd had relations with her daughter. Pieces of Ella Jones' puzzle reminded Robby of his own mother, who he'd taken care of in her own final years. Her passing left a mark on him that very clearly hadn't faded yet—

"Her heart's stopped," Robby spoke aloud, forcibly ripping himself from his thoughts.

As he pulled the stethoscope back into place around his neck, a dragged, high mewl poured from Samantha's throat. It fell upon the man's ears, utterly haunting as it sent jolts up his spine.

"She's gone," he hardly managed to get the words out—the quiver in his voice betrayed him.

Immediately, and for the first time in Michael Robinavitch's life, he laid guilty eyes upon his patient, mind flooding like a raging river with what could've been. What might have become of Ella Jones if he'd sent her to Williamson on his last home visit? What could've been, had he told Samantha that she'd need to take Ella to a major hospital if such a situation arose? What could've been, if Michael Robinavitch had been any more equipped, any more talented, any more of a miracle-bearer?

But he was only a man.

Each soft sobs coming from Samantha dragged him further and further underwater, and once he felt fully submerged, unable to take a breath in, Robby began to panic.

"I'm very—I'm so sorry," he stood from his chair, wobbling toward the door. "I'm sorry. I need a moment," his palm slammed against the wall, free hand yanking the door open.

He cursed under his breath the very brief moment he met eyes with Dennis from across the clinic.

From the front desk, Dana whipped her head around, blond bangs slinging across her forehead, eyes widening as she watched the doctor stumble out of the room like an idiot. Without a second thought, she was in motion, dropping her pen and hasting over to Ella's room, taking his place. She did not know why Robby was stomping down the hallway, face red in his hands, but she'd cover for him anyway.

"Fuck," Robby hissed into his palm, slamming the back door open with his shoulder and stumbling out into the grass.

His breaths were shaky, quick, and shallow. The lightheadedness swamped his senses like a blur of intoxication, and the world seemed to spin at his feet. An inexplicable weight crushed down upon his chest, making it all the more difficult to fill his lungs and ground himself. Perhaps the ground itself had slipped from beneath his feet—he certainly felt as though he was falling. Beads of sweat raced down his temples as his back slammed against the outdoor wall. In the man's fit of fear and falling and instability, there was a distinct lack of control he felt. Would it have been more frightening that he could have done something to save Ella Jones? Or was it more frightening that he could not?

A choked sob bubbled forth from Robby's throat as he groaned a prolonged, "No."

He was a very smart man, but as he'd come to know in his life, doctors were, ironically, notoriously bad at helping themselves. There lay a truth in the life of a medical professional: many of them cared for others in the absence of caring for themselves. Many of them took control of the world around them so as to assume a mimicked control of the world within them. But as real of an illusion it had been for decades, Robby felt the seams coming undone in real time; he was not an idiot—he could feel the false sense of control he'd established beginning to crumble. 

For his mother. For Ella Jones. And for the man that had made him who he was—the man that had died on this very day, the eleventh of August, just seven years prior. That man's face, warped and colorless, swelled in and out of Robby's mind as he sat with his back against the wall, knees crumpled into his chest, head lurched down and covered in his sweaty palms. 

He was so taken by the overwhelm, he hardly noticed when the back door opened once again, shutting quietly as a pair of feet planted themselves in front of him.

"Dr. Robby?" Dennis tried softly.

He did not answer—did not raise his head out of his palms or give any indication that he heard the boy. But he did hear him. His sobs had all but quieted, if only to save himself the embarrassment of explaining what he was experiencing to Dennis Whitaker. But 

"Dr. Robby," the boy spoke with a little more concern, squatting in front of the older man.

"I'm here," the older man finally croaked.

"Are you?"

A pause.

"...I can't do this."

"Do what?"

"This." Robby finally raised his head, cheeks flushed, jaw clenched. He met Dennis' earthy, soft eyes, blinking through the tears which wetted his own. "I just... can't do it."

Dennis did not immediately respond, carefully crafting his response.

Slowly, he explained: "You have to. We need you in there."

"Ella Jones needed me, too," Robby lamented.

It was through that response that Dennis picked up what had happened—Ella Jones, dead. His brows furrowed as he tried recalculating his response, entirely unsure how to navigate the minefield that was Michael Robinavitch, slumped against the back wall outside his very own clinic. The boy knew what that old woman meant to Robby, to an extent, at least. He knew the man had history with her—and her daughter. But even then, Robby was not a personable man; Dennis knew that much, painfully well. Ella Jones alone could not have been enough to transform him into the utter trainwreck of emotion he had become. 

Setting aside every ounce of bitter tension that had festered between them over the last eight months, Dennis prepared himself to save the drowning man before him.

Sitting on his backside, knees huddled into his own chest with his feet on either side of Robby's, the boy cleared his throat. "There're a whole bunch of people in there that you can still save. People who need you. I won't pretend I know how bad you're hurting right now. I'm not gonna act like I could handle it any better than you. But me and you both know what happens if you stay out here and dwell on something that you can't change—something that wasn't your fault."

The boy frowned when Robby chuckled, nigh-hysterically. The older man wiped fresh tears with the back of his hand before meeting the younger's eyes again. The ground felt tangible beneath him all of a sudden; he felt a full breath of air fill his lungs as he steeled himself just long enough to speak—deliberately.

"...Sounds like," he croaked, "something he would say."

"Who?"

Robby did not response.

"Dr. Robby, who? Like who?"

The older man reached out his arm, placing his hand on Dennis' shoulder, touching skin just above the neck of his scrub shirt. The boy could feel it; it was a hollow attempt to kindly push him away. Something Robby had gotten very comfortable doing since New Year.

"Forget about it, Whitaker," he brushed off, adopting a weak, fake smile.

"No."

The first push-back. Robby blinked, brow twitching as his body processed that single word. A word that had never once come out of Dennis Whitaker's mouth in such a way: so soft, so quiet, yet so firm. In the eight months Robby had spent distancing himself from the boy, it'd just happened—Dennis had simply allowed it to happen. But for the first time, right then, the attempt to close off was met with... resistance.

He shook his head, eyes firmly locked on Robby's face, watching as the older man struggled to anticipate what would come next.

"Who are you talking about?"

The doctor's jaw clenched. The ground began to feel unsteady once more. That uncanny sense of dread began welling up in his chest and the words from Dennis' lips bounced like sharp needles in the shells of his ears.

"It's nothing," he tried again—futile.

"Why won't you tell me?"

"Why does it matter?"

"Because it matters to you."

Robby paused.

"...I'm not fucking doing this," he grumbled, palms digging into the grass at his sides. "Today of all days—I'm not doing this."

"Dammit Dr. Robby, you owe me!" Dennis yowled, rendering the world around them utterly silent.

Robby froze, brows knitting together contemplatively as he studied the pained expression on the boy's face. He looked angry—beaten. It looked like he'd finally endured enough. Robby knew this would come eventually; he just liked to deny that he'd truly have to deal with the fallout of having involved himself intimately with Dennis Whitaker, only to sever the bond mere days after it began. That day had finally come. The boy sat in front of him, tears welling in his earthy, tired eyes, cheeks flushed with frustration, nose scrunched. 

"You owe me," he begged again, voice softer than silk—bluer than rain.

Robby bit down hard on his cheek to restrain the guilt banging on the walls of his heart. "Don't look at me with those eyes, Whitaker," he cupped the boy's cheek in his rough, clammy hand, grazing the pad of his thumb under the boy's eye to catch the single tear that betrayed him.

"I've been real good," he choked out, nuzzling ever so subtly into the touch of Robby's palm. "Gave you space for months."

The older man parted his lips, but words refused to come out.

"I just want to know what's on with you," Dennis finished.

Using Robby's own words against him—how messy of the kid. Robby recalled that evening on the twenty-fourth of December nearly a year prior. He'd said the same thing to Dennis. He'd practically barked at the kid to be transparent about what was truly going on in his life, yet he himself never afforded that same honesty. Even before he began pulling away from Dennis.

Grasping for a truly filling breath of air through his nose, Robby closed his eyes, hand still firmly cupping the boy's cheek—but it was shakier now. He cleared his throat, opening himself up to the idea of allowing just a glimpse of who he was. A glimpse of himself to the sweet, delicate lover who he'd been shutting out for far too long.

"Today's the eleventh of August," Robby admitted, as though that wasn't common knowledge.

Dennis blinked. "Yes."

"Seven years ago this day," he began tentatively, "a man very important to me died. A man who... frankly, was much of the father I never had."

As he spoke, Robby seemed to find a rhythm—seemed to find footing. It helped, too, that Dennis sat close enough to touch.

"I grew up in Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania. Hill District. Every kind of person you could imagine lived there. Every color, every background, every culture. My father passed quite early in my life. My mother managed. Around the time I turned seventeen - it was 1938 - I scored work at the clinic on my street to help pay for pre-med. It was the place mother always took me when need be."

"That's where you met..." Dennis surmised.

"Dr. Montgomery Adamson," Robby mumbled, "was his name."

Saying that name sent a cold shudder up his spine. Dennis noticed the tensing muscles in his face, realizing immediately what must've been going on: Robby was being haunted by ghosts of his past. "He taught me most everything I know. Made me who I am, really. He was, uh," the older man's voice faltered just a little. "I'm sure he was the reason I even made it as far as I did in school. "He showed me a perspective about people... that I never would've been able to see without him. Showed me the importance of caring for anyone—anyone, who needed help."

"After my residency, I moved back to Hill District to care for mother—she wasn't well," Robby shook his head, eyes fixated on a blade of grass bent under Dennis' shoe. "This was... around 1952. Dr. Adamson took me on as the 'boy of the graveyard-shift.' Turned his humble place on our street into a twenty-four hour makeshift hospital. We did a lot for people. Lost some lives—saved a lot more. I thought I'd finally understood what it meant to be a doctor."

"But mother passed away a few years later. Summer of 1955." He looked up, locking his own shameful eyes with Dennis' as he admitted: "I didn't take it very well."

A pained expression contorted the older man's face. Dennis wanted, desperately, to reach out and touch him.

"I took time off—to grieve," he stuttered. "I didn't think he'd..."

"I should've known." Robby's chin trembled. "He told me he'd handle my absence and I thought it'd be fine." A pause. "Ended up overworking himself. He was that kind of man, though. I should've... known he wouldn't slow down—not after everything we accomplished for Hill District."

"So... what? You blame yourself?" Dennis asked in disbelief.

"Who else is there to blame? It was my shift he was covering—"

"He made a choice."

"And that choice killed him," Robby seethed. "I... might as well have killed him."

"No," Dennis leaned forward, grabbing Robby's face with both hands and forcing their eyes to lock. "Look at me, Robby."

"I shouldn't have opened today," he shook his head as much as he could in Dennis' hands. "I knew it was a bad idea. I can't fucking focus."

"Hey," Dennis cooed. "Listen to me, Dr. Robby."

The older man bit down hard enough on his cheek to stop his groveling—just enough to hear the boy out through the pained look on his face. Dennis looked around at the ground for a moment, searching for the right words and putting them together.

The older man hesitated for a moment, then nodded slowly, sharp puffs of breath whistling in and out of his nostrils.

"She said that everyone deals with these things differently. We never know how good we got it until it's over. But... she didn't let that stop her," Dennis shook his head, brows tense like bowstrings. "She fought harder because of it. Took control in every way she could with the whole wide world workin' against her."

The kid was keeping Robby from drowning—he could feel Dennis pulling him back toward the surface with each second he held him. Each second he spoke to him.

"You've done that same thing... whether you see it or not. You brought a practice to Franklin that people need. You carried on these past seven years despite it all and gave these people something that they never would've had otherwise. How long would Ella Jones have lasted if not for you, Dr. Robby? Huh?"

The older man did not respond, but he knew what Dennis was getting at; Ella Jones wouldn't have been able to afford proper care without him—likely wouldn't have lived nearly as long.

He looked down, allowing the doctor only a brief moment more to bask in his pain on the ground before extending his hand out.
