The older man rested his hand on the back of Dennis' neck, pulling him in close enough to feel the heat from his body as he said, "Thank you—"

"—Robby?" Dana called out.

Before the back door of the clinic had even fully opened - before the thought could even process in his mind - Robby pushed hard, sending Dennis tumbling backward until he fell flat onto his backside in the grass. A pitiful gasp flew from the boy's mouth, and quicker than the older man could spare a second glance, Dana closed the space between them. She slammed her palm hard into his cheek with a loud smack that seemed to echo into the trees beyond the clinic.

Dana barked, "Robby, you son of a bitch!"

The sting of that slap burned like a searing hot cast iron skillet crushed into flesh, but yet it utterly paled in comparison to the pain of turning his head and laying eyes upon the kid, who appeared... totally shut down.

"Whitaker, are you okay, kid?" Dana spoke as she knelt over to check on the boy, but her voice sounded like it came from underwater among the ringing in Robby's ears.

His gaze stayed fixated upon that face—it was the first time he'd ever bore witness to a Dennis Whitaker void of any emotion, eyes having lost the light as quickly as it came. No fear, sorrow, no anguish, no pain, nothing. Just a husk of a man, shut down by Michael Robinavitch's own guilty hands.

 

 

Dana invited Dennis over for dinner the following Sunday. He accepted.

Her house was, as Dennis presumed, far more empty than it may have been days or weeks prior. She was only to work one more Monday at the Robinavitch Health Center, and then she'd move to Knoxville that same day after her shift, starting her journey as a nursing student.

The walls around the dining room were dimly lit. Floral wallpaper spanned the interior, supporting a white popcorn ceiling. The cream-colored carpet at his feet was cozy, and more fanciful than he'd ever seen. At home in Nebraska, the floors were only sparsely decorated with rugs, none of which fuzzy or clean like Dana's floor.

The rosewood dining table was decorated with bowls and large plates full of food. Steamed rolls, savory green beans, buttery mashed potatoes, corn bread, all surrounding a main course of creamy chicken and dumplings. Elena and Maggie had long since served themselves and picked at their little plates like chickens with feed. Adam stayed invested only in the rolls, mashed potatoes and dumplings. Dana served herself, and Dennis, a little bit of everything.

But Dennis didn't have much of an appetite—not while Robby haunted his thoughts. Not while the thought of Dana leaving in two days prodded away at him.

"I'll take your roll if you don't want it, Dennis," Adam asked politely.

Dana scoffed, swatting dismissively. "Eat your own food, hooligan."

The toddlers giggled.

"I'll eat it," the boy tried meekly. "Sorry, a lot's on my mind. I'm, well... y'know. It's tough knowing you're leaving."

"Aww," Dana's lips curled downward, hand over her heart. "We're gonna miss you, Whitaker. But really, we're only ever a daytrip away, hm? You and that hunk of junk can make it to Knoxville and see us sometime, right?"

"I'm sure I can!" He half tried cheering himself up. "I just don't know how often. With Dr. Robby and I at the clinic alone, it'll probably be a tight schedule."

"Robby'll just have to make do," the woman's tone hardened a bit as she stabbed a dumpling with her fork. "He can't chain you down more than you let him."

"I do need a livin'," he chuckled.

"I hear you," Adam chimed in, grabbing a fourth roll from the woven basket. "I'll tell you what—living certainly is cheaper in the south, but not by much. And it's only getting worse every year, it seems like."

"I'm lucky Dr. Robby pays well."

"He should pay you more, the way he treats you," Dana remarked, stuffing a dumpling in her mouth.

Dennis paused, fork tapping on his plate for a moment. "He's... he's a good man." The words came out as though he were trying to convince himself of it. "Just has a lot of baggage."

"Trust me, Whitaker, I get it. Been workin' with the man for years now. He's a prickly pear. My issue is: I didn't think he had it in him to push someone around the way he's been doin' to you. I mean, really, what is it?"

Dennis did not know if she was legitimately asking a question, so he did not respond—only looked up at the woman.

"I'm deadly serious! What's got him so bothered about you, kid?"

Before the boy could respond, Adam added, "Heard he was a real ass on Friday."

"Oh god," Dana groaned. "Friday. That shift was hell. And Robby kept goin' on about it bein' an 'especially bad day,' or some such thing—you don't shove an innocent, hardworking boy - who's only trying to help out around the damn clinic, mind you - to the ground. There's just no good reason to."

The scrutiny of being talked about as though he was not there—it felt odd. 

"He tried sayin' it was solely 'cause of Ella, but he was being a pain in the ass since that morning—way before sweet Ella came in." Dana paused for a moment, letting go of a grieving sigh. "She really was a sweet old thing. Knew Robby since he came down from Nashville in '56."

"Ah, she's the one with the..." Adam trailed off.

"Daughter, yep. He and Ella's girl had a thing for a year or so. I'm not entirely convinced he wasn't just doin' it to keep Ella happy, though. That woman wanted her daughter a husband real bad."

Dennis interjected: "He was very torn up about her passing. I know that much."

The blond woman across the table chewed and swallowed her bite of chicken and dumpling, then shook her head. "I get that. I really do. But there's no good reason to take that pain out on anyone else—even Robby knows that. I'm just thinkin' there was a lot more going on in his mind than he wanted to tell me. Did he say anything to you, kid? He tell you what kind of stick is up his ass n' why he keeps making it your problem?"

Before Dennis could even respond, Dana picked up the glint in Dennis' eye. She could see he knew something about Robby's behavior, and her brows furrowed as she tried to read inside his pupils as though they held an answer.

"It's a personal thing, I think," he feigned. Swallowing the lump in his throat, the boy added timidly: "We're working it out, though. Working through it."

"...That's good," the woman nodded, trying hard to fight off the urge to pry further. "Good to hear."

"You should keep your boundaries clear with him," Adam offered. "So this kind of thing doesn't become regular."

"I already talked to him Friday afternoon about his behavior toward the kid," Dana interjected. "Told him he can't act this way when I'm gone - not to talk about you like you're a child, of course, Whitaker. I'm just sayin'... I made it clear to him that he can't do you like he did Collins."

Dennis blinked.

"What?"

"Hm?" Dana turned to him, a harmless look on her face.

"What'd you say? About Heather."

"I said 'I made it clear to him that he can't do you like he did Collins.' The whole... kickin' her out thing."

Bewilderment melted upon Dennis' face. Robby had only ever told him - since Heather Collins had stopped showing up at the clinic eight months ago - that she'd decided she no longer felt safe at the clinic.

"He—he kicked..." the boy stammered, "Collins out?"

"That's what he told me. Said he had no choice. And I'm not criticizing him for doing it—I don't think he'd lie about a safety concern if it pertained to her. I scolded him about it two years ago. Told him he needed to go all the way if he was gonna take her under his wing—couldn't half-ass it or jeopardize her well-being. Maybe he felt like it was in her best interest, I dunno. But I do think that was the first time I realized he was off the deep end, at least a little. This year's been rough on him, anyone can see. "

"He didn't... say anything else about it?"

The words spilled from Dennis' mouth before thought could process. It almost sounded as though he didn't believe Dana—that wasn't his intent.

She shrugged, scraping the last of the mashed potatoes from her plate. "That's all I know about it, kid. Evidently, I know him a lot less than I thought I did. I still love him like a brother, but... he's made some choices."

Dennis clenched down on his jaw—hard. Robby had actually lied to him. 

"Yes he has," the boy practically seethed.

The anger grew like wildfire, uncontrollable in Dennis' chest. He clenched his fist around the fork he held, nearly shaking as he tried - and failed - inhaling quietly through his nose, exhaling through his mouth. When that frustration refused to subside, he stood from his seat, standing awkwardly for a moment.

"I should go," he tried to speak through the red-hot anger behind his eyes. "Thank you for everything. Dinner, your company, your family, thank you."

"Whitaker? Don't you wanna take any leftovers home with you?"

Dana and Adam had stood from their chairs, ready to at least walk the boy out to his car, but he was already gone, swinging the front door open on his own, letting it slam shut behind him.

 

 

The rattling bang, bang, bang of the clinic's front door shook Robby's untouched glass of whiskey, even from all the way upstairs in his home. Having interrupted the confession he was struggling to write, he slammed his journal and pen down on the coffee table, scurrying to his feet from the couch. No one was supposed to be over that night. The clinic had long since closed, too. With each creaking thump down the stairs and into the clinic, the doctor's heart began thumping in its own frantic rhythm. It only settled itself when he laid eyes upon Dennis Whitaker, who stood outside waiting to be let in, illuminated from behind with a warm orange glow from the distant streetlights.

Robby froze in place for a moment, brows knitting together as he contemplated the worth of letting the boy in. They hadn't formally spoken since he shoved him to the ground that past Friday—just as Dana had walked out and nearly caught them being... overly fond of one another.

The boy looked angry.

It was, of course, inevitable that he'd want to speak about what happened eventually. Robby just hadn't anticipated it being so soon, and on such a quiet night—a Sunday, the night before Dana's final shift at the Robinavitch Health Center. 

As he rattled the key and unlocked the door, swinging it open, Robby said, "What are you doing here so late—"

"You lied to me."

"What?"

Not at all the words he'd expected from the kid's mouth.

"You lied," he seethed, shoving Robby pitifully weakly as he stomped inside the front of the clinic, door slamming shut behind him. He did not turn to face Robby, who now stood behind him, frozen and bewildered. "This whole year. Almost a whole year."

"Whitaker, get specific."

"Heather Collins," he finally turned, tears welled in his pupils like wet pearls.

"Jesus Christ," Robby sighed, burying his face in his hands and rubbing. "Can we do this after Dana's moved? Tomorrow'll be awkward enough as it is."

"Thanks to you," the boy nodded.

"To me?"

"I didn't ask you to shove me like that."

"Ah, of course! So you'd rather she have seen us kissing? Is that it?"

"I'd rather you have treated me like a human from the get-go eight months ago after I gave you my innocence—but here we are." His lip quivered, and he paused a moment before blurting: "I'd have liked if you'd treated Heather like a human, too."

"What the hell is that supposed to mean, Whitaker? Speak your damn mind."

"You told me she left this clinic of her own volition, Robby. You swore it."

"Did she not?" Robby's sarcastic tone drew forth more red-hot anger from the boy.

Dennis shook his head incredulously, lips curled downward.

"...I had dinner with the Evans' tonight. They said you kicked her out."

The doctor's jaw clenched and a huff of air whistled out his nostrils.

"...It was a mutual understanding—"

"—Bullshit!"

"It was!" Robby flailed his hands forward.

"Then what in God's name possessed you to tell Dana you 'kicked her out' of your clinic? Huh?"

"You're joking. What 'possessed' me?" The man scoffed. "You fucking possessed me!"

"I don't have a clue what you mean," the boy spat.

"I can't tell Dana why Collins had to leave—I could hardly tell Collins, herself. I'd have had to explain your mother to the both of them, and how the hell would I have done that without putting this—us in danger?"

Dennis squinted, baffled by the man in front of him. "My mother? Is that what all of this has been about? All this time?"

Robby's nose twitched, crossing his arms as he watched the boy put the pieces together.

The doctor barked like it was stupidly obvious: "She threatened to call the police—"

"—She said she ought to."

That correction forced a dumbfounded scoff from Robby.

"What do you think would happen if a couple of cops came strolling in one night on a tip and Heather Collins was prancing around in the clinic?"

Dennis only shook his head.

"What do you think would happen if you and I were reported as homosexuals? We could be charged with sodomy! My medical license, our jobs, our freedoms—our lives... would be over."

"My mama told you that night—she wouldn't do somethin' like that knowing it'd hurt me, too. She isn't like that."

"You ran all the way here to Franklin just to get away from that family, kid. I wouldn't put it past any one of them. And I sure as hell wouldn't put myself, you, or Collins at risk betting on a stranger's sympathy."

"...So you pushed us away without any warning?" Dennis croaked through the lump in his throat. "Didn't even flinch?"

"Heather knows exactly why I did what I did. We talked about it in January just after your mother—"

"And you didn't talk to me about anything?" The boy whined. "You left me in the cold for eight months, Robby!"

Unable to resist and unable to ignore the aching of his heart, Robby closed the space between them, cupping Dennis' face with his hands. He frowned, looking down upon his weeping lover with guilt and with shame as he stammered: "I didn't want you to feel guilty. You never would've forgiven yourself thinking you ran Collins off—thinking you ran me off! I—I wanted to protect you."

"I didn't need protecting!" Dennis bit back, but the anger melted into pain just as fast—he trembled as he whispered, "I just needed you."

Against all rational judgment, Dennis closed the remaining gap between them, whines quelled by the soft press of the older man's lips on his own. He stood on tip-toes as his arms wrapped around the firm figure in front of him, clinging like he was close to the edge of a cliff—like he was not allowed to let go. It was a desperate kiss, and Robby fueled it just as equally, hands raking through the boy's hair and finding purchase at the nape of his neck, warm and soft. A tongue swept across a bottom lip, teeth grazed sensitive flesh, noses nuzzled against one another; the taste of Dennis sent jolts of hot arousal downward in Robby's loins. It was a sensation he hadn't felt since eight months ago when he first took the younger man.

It was a sensation that he'd learned to be terrified of.

Robby pulled back, holding Dennis at a safe distance with his fist balled in the boy's plaid shirt. Dennis tried to close the gap again, letting out a soft, pitiful grunt when the man did not budge.

Robby groaned, anguish laced in his gravelly voice. "We can't right now."

A kind of finality settled into the air around Dennis Whitaker. He trembled as the doctor stepped back, far enough out of his space that he could no longer feel the heat of his body.

"Then when?"

Robby did not answer. He tore his hungry eyes away from the boy, boring holes into the floor avoidantly.

"Dr. Robby," Dennis tried. "When?"

"I don't know, Whitaker." He admitted frustratedly, rubbing the back of his head with his palm. "We shouldn't at all. It's not smart. It's not safe."

The boy closed his eyes, nodding. The front room of the clinic was silent like a cold winter night. The lights above were dimmed, leaving only the isolating company of darkness to envelope the two men as they stood, several feet apart now.

Dennis Whitaker decided in that darkness that he'd had enough. Two years ago he made the mistake of digging into a pie that he knew he'd struggle to resist in the future. That had festered and mutated into an uncontrollable need for Michael Robinavitch, one that had - by some stroke of unfathomable luck - become like white noise in 1961 when the man first took him under his wing. He thought he might be able to go the rest of his life not knowing what it was like to sate that hunger—truly sate it. But his luck ran out when Robby knocked on his door that fateful Christmas Eve. Too close to resist, he allowed himself to be taken by the man. Had allowed himself to give in to an urge that he knew very well he'd never again be able to resist.

"I can't go on like this."

Those words cut through the silence like a knife.

Robby looked up from the floor.

Dennis continued: "I won't survive this."

"Don't talk like that."

"How can I not? I'm not allowed to dream of a life with you."

The words squeezed tight over Robby's heart. He had to bite down on the sore flesh inside his cheek just to hold himself from visibly reacting.

"...You're allowed to do what you please."

"And it'd kill me to do so." The boy paused, eyes focusing on Robby's pupils as if searching for something. "But you wouldn't get it, would you?"

Robby stood in silence.

"You couldn't understand—because you don't feel that same need, do you, Dr. Robby?"

"Whitaker, you couldn't possibly fathom how much I 'get it'."

"How can I trust that? When you left me to hang? All this time, you left me in the dark and it never once hurt you the way it hurt me."

"I was hurting." Robby defended. "I am hurting."

"But I'd never know it, because you don't talk to me!"

"I told you about Hill District!" The older man dissented, voice raised.

"Only after I pried it out of you!" Dennis returned. "After subjecting me to your cold shoulder and your sharp tongue—something you've been doing for eight months! Don't you get it?" He huffed. "It took all that time of suffering in silence for me to put my foot down and demand you show me a little respect—a smidge of trust. Imagine how doomed I'd be trying to stick by your side for another eight months? Or years, even?" Dennis wiped a fallen tear from his cheekbone. "I'd be dead of a broken heart long before the rest of the world could kill me!"

"So—what? You're just done?" Robby scoffed. "Hanging up the keys?"

Ignoring the charged response, Dennis took one single step forward.

"If you let me walk out that door," he began tentatively, feeling the swell of nerves thump in his heart. "I won't come back."

The doctor's eyes widened so subtly it was hardly noticeable.

"Whitaker—"

"If I walk out that door," he repeated more firmly. "I won't come back."

Dennis took one daring step forward.

"Whitaker," Robby demanded the boy's attention.

Another step forward. His eyes were cold and hurt, brows angled in a determined frown.

Robby balled his fist and seethed, "Whitaker."

With the third step, Dennis brushed shoulders with Robby, but neither man met eyes. They stood firm in stance. The boy picked up pace, light taps turning into stomps as he stormed for the door, and the doctor did not dare turn around to watch.

"Whitaker," he hissed one more time, but it came out far quieter, almost like a plea.

The door was quick to creak as it swung open, and quicker to slam when it shut. The noise rattled every bone in Robby's body. He knew it was over. He could see it in the boy's eyes; he'd been pushed beyond his limit. He likely very much meant what he said about not coming back, and Robby would now have to navigate what that meant—for his clinic, and for his heart.

Whatever flame he'd lit inside of the boy was over, and Michael Robinavitch was still unaware of the flame that would continue to grow inside himself.

 

 

Dennis made the drive to the nearest payphone—dangerously quick, speeding through the lonely streets of Franklin with a kind of anguish in his blood that couldn't be settled. Even after stopping the car, he gripped onto the steering wheel with white knuckles, forehead pressed into it as he sobbed. A lone, dim streetlamp hung over his car, spotlighting it in the mid of night for no one to see.

When he finally pulled himself upright after several minutes—having poured every tear from his eyes, Dennis leaned his head back onto the headrest, eyeing his mother's notebook, which sat tempting in the passenger seat. She'd written the home phone number in it all those months ago on New Year. Frankly, he never imagined he'd use it—but some dreadful voice in the back of his mind told him he should keep it. Just in case.

This was the case, it seemed.

Stepping out of the car with the notebook clutched in his quivering right hand, Dennis shuffled to the payphone, dispensing a day's work worth of coins, dialing as he pressed the handle into his ear, counting every click and churn of the machine.

The call hummed, trying to connect for almost a full minute before another distinct click came from the speaker, and a voice said: "Hello, this is your operator. I'm sorry to say we can't reach the person you're trying to contact. It is quite late."

Dennis swallowed. "...I see."

There was an awkward pause.

"Could I leave a message?"

"Of course, I can deliver a message. What would you like me to say?"

The boy paused again, thinking for a moment, grip tightening on the handle of the telephone. His lip quivered, voice trembling as he inhaled a shaky breath, choking out the words:

"Please just let them know it's Dennis. Tell them I made a mistake... tell them I'm coming home."

Chapter 9: Old Friends and Bookends

Chapter Summary

Everything changes now. Dennis discovers there is no escape from himself. Robby prepares to confess his feelings.

Chapter Notes

Chapter 9's song is "Old Friends" and "Bookends" from Simon and Garfunkel's 1968 album, "Bookends"

See the end of the chapter for more notes

The drive home to Nebraska was as trying as Dennis had expected it would be. It was primarily a fever dream of long hours on curving roads where hills and trees spread inward and outward. The sun had risen by the time he'd made it halfway through the drive, but he made several stops along the way: gas, maintenance, and food. He'd linger at each stop, as an odd way of delaying the inevitable reunion he'd soon have with his family. The family he had run away from years ago—the mother who drove the nail between Robby and himself. 

He thought about that in particular many times along the way back to Broken Bow; all this time, it had been the words from his mother that set Robby off. In a way, that was almost relieving, but equally, it was a wake-up call. He and Michael Robinavitch could not have survived love—that was his conclusion.

"My sweet bird," Adeline Whitaker squealed as she bear-hugged her son, who stood at the front door in awkward stance, unsure what to do. She rocked him in the embrace, squeezing and running her fingers through his greasy hair. "My boy. My baby."

"Hi," he offered, voice muffled.

Adeline pulled away, but her hands remained clinging to Dennis' arms. Her dark brown eyes searched his, sun-kissed skin of her face scrunching into a sympathetic stare. "Was the drive alright? I like your ride," she pointed to the boy's sorry excuse for a car, parked just behind him. He did not turn. "I got your message as soon as I woke up, and," she inhaled, placing a hand over her heart, "you can't imagine how excited Thane and I were."

"Pa's home?"

"He's at the table," she grinned. "Waitin' with a plate full of eggs and sausage—oh, and your favorite hash."

"There's breakfast?" Dennis' stomach betrayed him.

His mother giggled hearing the rumble. She always made breakfast in the mornings. "Didn't light up the stove 'till an hour ago. It's still hot and crispy the way you always liked it."

Leading her son through the front door of the dinky wooden porch, Adeline tugged his wrist, looking back to see him process the familiar home around him. The smell, the look, the warmth. Pain, nostalgia, and an overwhelmingly unreadable light flickered behind his eyes as he took it all in. Each step they made toward the kitchen elicited cries from the wooden planks beneath, and those cries echoed as groans in the walls around them. It was a terribly old house.

Yet it felt indomitable to Dennis Whitaker.

As they passed the living room, the boy's brows knitted together, eyes halting upon the very rug he stood upon the day he'd had his final argument with Thane. The anger on his father's face—the searing hot rage in Dennis' own blood. It was a goodbye that felt unarguably final, but yet here he was once again. Only just over two years had passed.

"Thane!" Adeline called out just before she stepped into the kitchen with her son. "Dennis is home!"

There was no verbal response, but he could hear a distinctly wet coughing fit ensue from the kitchen. Adeline looked back at her son and sighed, whispering, "He's caught a nasty cough this past few weeks. Think he's just gettin' old."

When they crossed the corner and into the kitchen, the boy met his father's eyes. The old man was sitting at the end of the dining table, having already picked away at his plate of food. Dennis could feel the muscles in his body tense, almost reflexively, like he was prepared to fight—he knew there would be no fighting. The man looked far older now than he did two years ago. Perhaps managing things around the farm without Dennis' help had truly beaten away at him. Or perhaps whatever cough had riddled his lungs was sapping the remaining life from his eyes. He would've liked to imagine Thane deserved such a fate, but the man was still his father. A kind of soreness swelled in his heart. 

Despite sizing one another up for a very awkward handful of seconds, neither man broke the silence. Dennis half-expected his father to—he figured it was the least the man could've done, but he did not.

"Now don't be shy," Adeline chirped, pulling her son toward the table. "Sit down, honey."

He plopped down into the chair that his mother pulled out for him, avoiding meeting his father's eyes anymore as he waited for her to bring his plate to the table. A cream-white plate full of steaming, scrambled eggs, plump sausage, and a crispy, golden hash. It was so very nostalgic—in taste, smell, and sight.

Eagerly, his mother watched as he stuffed himself, as though waiting for him to praise the food. "Is it everything you missed down in Tennessee?"

"It's good," he settled.

It did not taste like Robby's cooking. But that should not have mattered anymore.

"You ate better food down there?"

Dennis looked up. "No," he lied.

Adeline smiled.

"Mark's moved out?" Dennis asked, looking between his mother and father.

She nodded. "Last bird to leave the nest. But you'll never guess where he ended up."

"...Where?"

"Got into University of Nebraska," she spoke proudly.

The boy's eyes widened, but he contained the shock—confusion.

"How'd he afford that?" Dennis asked through a mouthful of hash.

"Swallow your food, boy," Thane grumbled, cutting through the tempo of the conversation for a brief second before being interrupted by another fit of coughs. He raised his hand up to his lips and hacked away.

Adeline eyed her husband with a tinge of concern. As the fit died down, she looked back to her son and said, "Merit- somethin' somethin'."

"Merit..." the boy squinted. "A merit scholarship?"

"Mm," she hummed through her fork. "That."

A curious look settled onto Dennis' face. He was the youngest of his other three brothers, but Mark had always stayed home to help out around the farm—long after he'd turned eighteen. Simon and Howard however, had both led quick lives, moving out with high school sweethearts mere months after they'd become adults, many years ago. None of his brothers were fond of Dennis, nor particularly kind, but Mark was the closest he'd ever had to a friend.

"What for?"

"He was always a sharp kid in school," Adeline defended.

"I meant his major."

"Oh." She blinked. "Psychology."

"Sounds like Mark."

"Anyway—tell me about you, baby."

Dennis' lip twitched, and he swallowed a lump in his throat. "What about me?"

Adeline scowled. "Tell me all the nice things about Tennessee!"

Quicker than Dennis could speak, Thane stood from his chair with a loud creak. He cleared his throat and pointedly left his plate. "I don't wanna hear about it."

They watched as the man took a step forward, stopping to let out another gross hack into his balled fist.

"...That's a real nasty cough you've got." Dennis spoke softly. "I learned a lot at the clinic. I could try and—"

"I don't want you pokin' me, boy," the man spat. "Don't need no doctor."

Adeline and Dennis watched in silence as the man grumbled to the sink to wash his hands before hobbling out of the kitchen. The house creaked with each step, until there was the slam of the front door.

Dennis realized very quickly that his mother had - very heavily, and very desperately, it seemed - sugarcoated his father's 'change of heart and attitude.' Thane seemed very much the same man he was when Dennis ran off in 1960—save for this new ailment likely festering in his lungs. Tired, closed off, disappointed, disgusted. 

Disgust. The only look he ever saw in his father's eyes.

The air in the kitchen felt ruined. To ground herself, Adeline stood from her seat, grabbing Thane's plate and bringing it to the sink.

Looking up from his own plate, Dennis parted his lips. "Evidently," he began, then inhaled shakily. "If there was anything nice to say about Tennessee, I wouldn't be here right now to say it."

 

 

At the Robinavitch Health Center in Franklin, Dana had expected Dennis to come in by afternoon so they could begin their informal handover, as they had done for over a year. It was her last day, too—she'd been looking forward to seeing him one last time before embarking on her nursing school journey. Issue was, the further past noon it became, the more clear it was that Dennis Whitaker would not show.

"I can count on one hand the number of times that kid's been late for a shift," Dana chuckled. "Wonder if he's having car trouble again." She nudged Robby as he focused on the patient chart laid out before him.

Robby looked up. "Kid probably just overslept."

The night prior, Dennis had made it very clear that he wasn't coming back, and yet Robby still allowed that sense of finality to slip him come sunrise. He'd convinced himself that Dennis did not mean it—convinced himself that things would not change so drastically before he could fix them.

"He stopped by the clinic last night, didn't he?" Dana asked.

Confused how she knew, Robby locked eyes with the woman.

He contemplated lying for a moment.

"...He did," Robby admitted instead. "Wasn't here late, though."

"Well, did he say anything to you?"

"Nothing out of the ordinary," he lied. "I've gotta deal with this," Robby waved the patient chart as he stepped past the desk, headed for the exam room.

He spent several minutes in there with his patient before coming back out. He'd hoped Dana would be tied down with triage, but she'd already cleared the ones waiting. She stood there behind the desk, eager to pry him further. It was, in his eyes, her favorite thing to do.

"Nothing out of the ordinary," she repeated, pondering. "So what's ordinary, then? What'd you two talk about?"

"Why's it matter, Dana?"

She shrugged. "I dunno. He left our dinner pretty flustered last night. Right after tellin' us that you told him Collins chose to leave."

Those words. Those damned words.

Caught in his fib, Robby clenched his jaw, lips flattened together in a pained smile.

"He was a little upset I didn't tell him the whole story about the Collins thing, is all."

"Well why didn't you tell him the truth, Robby?"

"I didn't want him to blame himself—" the words came out faster than he could think, frustration clouding his judgment before he could cut himself off.

As soon as the words fell upon her ears, Dana squinted.

"Why would he blame himself for Collins' leaving?"

Robby did not respond.

"You dug the hole now, Robby," she chuckled, but there was no humor in it. "Might as well tell me what the fuck is going on."

"I would if I didn't have patients to get to."

Again, Robby stepped away from the desk, leaving Dana to watch as he bounced around the clinic and avoided her for the next half hour. He trailed in and out of exam rooms with patients, several minutes at a time. His mask was very impressive—the stress and fatigue he'd shown in his brief conversations with Dana were entirely hidden when he was with a patient.

His mask only slipped again when Dana called for him, just as he tried passing the desk again.

"What are you gonna do if he doesn't show up?" She sighed. "What am I gonna do?"

"You're going to leave as scheduled and do what you need to do."

"And where does that leave your sorry ass?" Dana still worried about the man.

Robby shook his head. "I won't ask you to overstay your shift. You've got a hell of a day ahead of you."

"I can't tell if this is you showin' real concern for me, or just tryin' to shoo me out before I can ask what the hell's goin' on between you and Whitaker."

The man's jaw clenched. Defensively, he nearly bit back, but an inexplicable swell of anguish choked him up before words could fly out. He had to bite it back and swallow it down, standing awkwardly in front of the desk for only a moment longer before stomping off again.

He spent the rest of the shift like that—hoping that Dennis would show up. Praying that the kid would show up. And he never did. It was exactly as he had said: Robby let him walk out the door the night prior, and he did not come back. On top of the rapidly growing fire that was Dana Evans, Robby now felt the heavy concern of Dennis' wellbeing knock at the back of his skull. Was the kid okay? Was he still at home? Did he actually try to come into work and something had happened?

In the end, Dana did not leave until Robby closed the clinic for the evening. He insisted that he'd survive just fine, but she would not hear any of that. Truthfully, too, she was nervous that Dennis wouldn't show up. It hurt like a dull ache in her heart to know that this was likely Robby's doing, and that it had now stopped her from getting to see the kid one last time.

As Robby stepped back inside after hooking the medical referral information sign to the front door of the clinic, Dana watched him. She just stood behind the desk, leaning into it, watching the man like a hawk.

"You fucked up real bad, didn't you, Robinavitch?"

Robby stood there, staring in a fit of what looked like shame.

Wiping a tear from her cheekbone, Dana got vulnerable. "I wanted to say goodbye to the kid, dammit."

"...I'm sorry," he croaked.

She nodded. "Me too."

"What have you got to be sorry for?"

Dana pursed her lips. "For making you feel like you couldn't be honest with me all these years."

Robby's eyes fell to the floor. His jaw clenched, fist balling at his side. A lump formed in his throat, but he swallowed it quickly as he asked: "What haven't I—"

"Is it love?"

Three words. They were only three words, and yet they nearly stopped Robby's heart. He did not respond to Dana—did not answer her question. His brows knitted together, eyes burning holes through the floor of the clinic. 

But Dana did not need him to respond: the silence was more than telling.

"...I may be sick."

Dana chuckled. "Maybe you are."

There was a pause, and she took a moment to find the right words:

"But maybe he's the cure."

Robby looked up through reddened, teary eyes at Dana. She still remained behind the desk, leaned into it. Her tears seemed to have dried up, but she still offered the most vulnerable, sympathetic look toward Robby that he'd ever seen from the woman. It hit - in that moment alone - that he'd likely not see that face again, at least for a very long time.

"I love you like a brother," she sighed. "That's why I'm saying this with all the kindness in my heart: you need help, Robby."

He nodded. "He's the only man I've ever—"

"—Not for that," she interrupted, speaking like he was an idiot. "For what's goin' on in your heart. You run the most difficult clinic in the whole damn state, and you've been doing this for years now with no breaks. These patients don't leave you when they walk out of the clinic. You absorb their pain. You... take in their trauma. It sticks onto you. I can almost see it like a thickness in the air around you."

Robby fell silent.

"It's made you a real troubled man," she admitted. "Because the Robby I met five years ago wouldn't have pushed away so many people he cared about. Not like this. Not Collins, not me—not Whitaker."

"I know," he admitted, biting down on his lip in hopes of stopping the tears from spilling. "I'll get help."

"Good," she cooed, stepping out from behind the desk and closing the space between them. She took the man in a hug, rubbing his back as he sobbed silently into her shoulder. "You better be good to him when I'm gone, Robby."

He did not verbally respond—instead he nodded into her shoulder. 

"And you better write me every chance you get," Dana said, pulling back and wiping a tear from his cheek. "You hear me, you big asshole?"

"I will," he promised.

"You better tell me all about the clinic. And all about your adventures. And all about how big and successful Collins gets when you bring her back in—you better bring her back in. When it's safe."

Robby nodded again.

"...And tell Whitaker I'm pissed off he didn't say goodbye to me. But I still love 'im."

Dana and Robby said their final goodbyes. When Adam pulled up, she dragged Robby out to say goodbye to her husband and her daughters, who sat politely in the back. When they drove off, Robby stood there in the dark summer night, feeling the silence swell upon him—over him. Like a warm wave creeping up his feet, up his knees, up his hips, until it had submerged him entirely. 

Only then did he close his eyes. And in the blackness, he saw Dennis' face.

Robby turned and stormed into the clinic, rushing up the stairs and swinging the door open to his home. He ran over to his living room and tossed books around—tossed stray pages and rummaged. Once he found his journal, in which he'd written his confession, he clutched onto it like it was the last true writing in the world. Like if he let go of it, it'd disappear. 

As quickly as he'd run up it, Robby scurried down the stairs, barging out of the door to the clinic and into the driver seat of his car. He kept the journal in his lap, never buckling his seatbelt as he drove damn-near recklessly, all the way through Franklin, toward Dennis Whitaker's home.

His determined heart did not sink when he pulled into the empty driveway, void of his lover's car. Instead, he clutched at his journal, stepping out and walking up to the little porch. Robby sat himself down, fidgeting with the pages of the journal as he challenged himself to remain composed.

His heart pounded imagining the words he'd written on the pages coming out of his mouth—but he knew they needed to be said. He needed to tell Dennis. He needed to fix what he'd begun to break between them. For his own good. He scanned the pages over and over, reciting each line like it was scripture. Like it was sacred. To him, perhaps it was.

Michael Robinavitch sat on that porch, waiting for Dennis Whitaker to return.

 

 

Several months later, deep into the fall of '62, Dennis lay under an old oak tree far out in his family's backyard. It stood tall at the edge of a forest which stretched on for a mile, riddled with jagged, rocky cliffs and patches of plains.

Behind his eyes, images of Tennessee flickered like the lights of a car streaking in bright color past his peripheral. Images of Robby and Dana and Heather melted together—it was like he was in mourning. Months had already passed since he ran back home, but the knife still sat deep in his gut. Or perhaps that was only the root of the oak tree, poking awkwardly into his back where he lay.

Dennis opened those tired eyes, allowing the shimmering light of soft evening sun to dance into his pupils, flickering and twinkling between reddening oak leaves.

His father finally passed away a few weeks ago. Adeline was bedridden for days. There was no formal funeral—Thane did not have friends who might attend. Instead, Dennis' brothers had come in along with their wives to bury the man at the family cemetery a few minutes south of the farm. Dennis did not go, and Adeline knew not to press him to go. Something seemed to click with her when the man finally passed away. In Dennis' eyes, it was like she'd been freed of a burden that she otherwise never would have escaped—a burden of love, when love was hardly left.

"You used to love reading under this old tree."

Her voice snapped Dennis from his thoughts, and he scurried upright, leaning his back against the trunk of the tree. Adeline held a pitcher of steaming apple cider in her right hand, the handles of two mugs hooked in the fingers of her left.

"Still do," he tapped the book in his lap.

"What's that one about?"

He glanced down at the cover. "It's... 'Principles of Internal Medicine.' Just a medical book."

"I know that didn't come from inside my house," she chuckled.

Dennis offered a weak smile. "No. It's from the clinic. In Tennessee."

"You used to love fiction," she reminisced, plopping down beside her son and handing him a mug, which he balanced on the cover of his book like it was a small table. "Used to bury your nose in it and sit out here for so long I'd have to rub aloe all over your face by the time you came back inside."

The boy wasn't sure what to say.

"You grew up quite a bit, didn't you, baby?"

"...I think so," he admitted. "Sure."

She didn't press further. Did not want to upset her son—the only son she had left at home. The only family she shared a roof with anymore. 

"In some ways, you were right," Dennis broke the short silence. "About him."

Adeline knew who he was referring to.

The boy nodded as he spoke, "I do believe he's a good man—don't mistake me. Every single day of his life he works to save people. Anyone." He paused. "...Anyone but himself."

"Well it ain't your job to save a man," Adeline stated, much like she was reciting something she hardly believed in anymore. "That's a woman's job."

Dennis turned to his mother. "Did you save daddy?"

She swallowed. Her lips pinched together into a pained smile and she blinked. Setting her mug next to Dennis' on the cover of the book, she lifted the steaming pitcher, carefully filling the sweet cider evenly into one mug, then the other.

"...I don't altogether know anymore," she began, more honest than she'd ever been, "if it should matter much who's doin' the saving."

Adeline looked up at her son with eyes like she was seeing for the first time—allowing herself to see for the first time, the boy that had raised himself. Seeing for the first time a figure that she hardly recognized, but loved no less. 

Jaw loosening, Dennis' brows knitted together as he searched his mother's eyes for meaning.

"God'll love you all the same. So why shouldn't I?"

Those words forced an unignorable swell of emotion up from Dennis' chest. Hearing his mother pronounce her acceptance in such a way was earth-shattering. It was like hearing the bells of Heaven, gates opened and waiting for him. As the woman sipped into her mug, Dennis processed.

"You don't think it's strange?"

"I sure do," she chuckled. "I think it's unnatural."

He huffed.

"But I'm your mother. It ain't my place to push you away. Not for anything. Not like those Tennessee folk did."

Dennis chuckled softly, "They're not bad people, mama."

"My ass," she kissed her teeth. "I know how bad you wanted out of here. Nothin' woulda sent you runnin' back unless it was really, truly awful."

"Honest," Dennis declared, "it wasn't so bad there. Real good people live there. ...It just wasn't home."

Adeline's lips pulled up into a cautious smile. "You realized this is your real home."

Dennis stared at his mug of steaming cider.

"No," he breathed out tentatively.

His mother's face fell softly.

"I don't think this is my home, either." Dennis looked up at Adeline. She knew, too. He could see it in her eyes: she knew what he'd say.

"You and Pa left me long before I left here. It... was never a home. Just a house."

A silence fell between them, interrupted only by Dennis' elaboration:

"I can't help but love you. And pa, too. Simon, Howard, and Mark. I can't will myself not to love you all. But I don't even know that I should. After everything. I don't know that I should have to subject myself to loving a family that I never truly belonged to. One that... did not know what to do with me."

His mother sat still as a stone, listening, with her fingers clamped tightly around her mug.

"I imagine it was easy enough raising my brothers. They're your first children. And they're normal. Not weak or different. But I was the afterthought, wasn't I, mama?" Dennis nodded. "It's why they're leading normal lives."

"Dennis," she sighed.

"Before you all went to pa's burial, Simon and Howard kept asking me questions I couldn't answer. Asked me why I looked so messy like I hadn't showered in days. Asked me why I hardly showed myself around town. Asked me why I came back at all, even." Dennis inhaled. "They meant well, I imagine. But those questions weren't any that I had answers to, mama."

He exhaled.

"I don't know why I can't get myself together. I don't know why I feel so ashamed to show my face around here."

"It ain't your home," she surmised.

Adeline's brows raised in realization. She truly, finally, saw what lay behind her son's eyes. The pain, the unknowing, the uncertainty. The country—the world was pitted against him, and he had no real way of navigating it. And she was of no help to him. 

"It's not my home," he nodded. "...I stayed up every night as a boy. Praying that I could change. Praying I'd stop steering off the beaten path and rocking the boat. But those prayers weren't ever answered. I wanted to stop feeling so... different. Wanted to see some approval in pa's eyes. Wanted to stick around my brothers without feeling guilty. Like I was marking their image."

Tears fell from Adeline's reddening eyes, and she tried quickly to wipe them.

"My sweet Dennis," she wept.

The emotion in her voice caught his attention. His eyes darted up, catching hers in a glance as she wept more.

"My little bird," she reached out, grabbing his cheek. "This is your cage, hm?"

Dennis Whitaker was, to Adeline, a delicate, fragile bird. And as she now saw it, Nebraska was his cage.

"I wish..." she tried. "I wanted to believe you came back because you needed me... I wanted you to need me."

He let go of a strained, prolonged sigh. He knew that pain very well—wanting to be needed. Robby had dug that knife into him months ago in Franklin.

She began to shake her head. "But you don't need me. Not any more than your brothers did. You just haven't found your footing yet."

"I do love you," he reminded. "Very much."

"I know," she pulled him into a hug, weeping into his neck as she assured, "I know."

He held his mother for several minutes, rocking her gently as they sat beneath the oak tree. Dusk had nearly settled over the skyline, painting the world above dark blue as clouds rolled in, casting dancing shadows on the sides of their faces, filtering the pink rays of setting sun.

"I think I always knew. Or maybe 'dreaded' is the right word," he said, pulling back and facing his mother again. "I always dreaded knowing who I am. I figured it out pretty young. But there are other things, too," he nodded.

"Other things?"

"There are other parts about me that I need to chase. Parts of me that I discovered in Tennessee. Parts of a person that... I need to become—want to become."

"What person do you want to become?" Adeline asked with a timid curiosity in her voice, watching the determination melt onto her son's face.

His eyes fell to his lap, landing on the book. Principles of Internal Medicine. It sat so very unassumingly in his lap, but it was the subject of every thought in his mind. Every silent moment he had between working on the farm, he'd open the book and read, just as he'd done years ago in Tennessee with every other book he'd been given. This one was the final one. The last book he'd been given to study before he left.

The book was a guiding light to him—a piece of the man he'd fallen in love with. That man's face and voice was nothing but a muffled echo in the back of his mind anymore, but his drive was the fuel in Dennis Whitaker's heart. His drive to help people. His drive to care. Dennis learned everything he knew from that man—witnessed his unwavering capacity to serve those in need, no matter what. Michael Robinavitch, without question, had become the north star by which he would follow for the next decade of his life.

Adeline looked down at the book that had Dennis' attention, then back up at him. For the first time since the day he was born, her son had the look of a man who knew exactly what he needed to do.

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

 

Pittsburgh, July 1972

 

"Mr. Dennis Whitaker?"

He stepped forward once called, resting his untidy stack of papers on the desk before him. "That's me."

"Hello," the woman behind the desk smiled courteously. "So," she glanced down at her own papers. "You're here to finalize everything for your rotation tomorrow."

"Yes, ma'am."

"Internal Medicine, too? Hoh-oh..." she chuckled. "Presby's ED just got overhauled to hell and back. You'll be in and out of there a lot this month. It'll be a real fun time, if the things I hear about it are true."

"I've heard. I worked in a clinic," he nodded. "About a decade ago—a pretty chaotic one. So I figure it'll feel a little familiar."

"Oh, really?" She grinned. "Then you'll be just fine. In any case, they've got a great team in the ED. Now, I'm gonna get you set up here real quickly—won't take long. Let's just..." she sifted through her papers, adjusting her glasses with a knuckle before looking back up at Dennis, "make sure your paperwork's in order, to start."

"Yes ma'am," he smiled sheepishly.

"Have you got your student ID? Hospital badge?"

Dennis tugged both from the pocket of his cargo pants, laying them out for the woman. She nodded with a kind smile.

"Lovely. Keep those on you at all times in Presby. We don't want our student doctor getting tossed out! Now—immunization records are here... let me just confirm with you: smallpox vaccine?"

"Yes ma'am, got it a few years ago."

"Tetanus shots?"

"Got my booster last year," he nodded.

"Beautiful," she sang. "And I've got a negative TB test from you, as well. Flying colors, lovely, lovely. Okay—patient confidentiality. I imagine you've heard this a million times in your courses, so you know you'll be held to the highest standard with regard to patient privacy. What you see and hear in Presby ED stays in Presby ED, right?"

"Yes ma'am."

She nodded. "Amazing. And—oh! I nearly skipped over," she chuckled. "Current Pittsburgh address?"

Dennis blinked.

"Address?"

She nodded. "Address, yes."

"...Oh," he chuckled dry. "I—" he stammered.

The girl behind him in line shuffled impatiently.

Swallowing the nervous lump in his throat, Dennis asked, "Is it necessary? I've just... never needed it for my previous rotations."

"New standard," the desk lady smiled innocently. "You've got an address, haven't you?"

"I," his brows furrowed. "I—well... to be honest, I'm kinda between places. Could I put down... a previous address? Would that work?"

She shot an incredulous look at the boy. "No, Mr. Whitaker. I'm afraid I'll need a valid and current Pittsburgh address. That's just how it works."

He did not respond. His eyes darted around the room, behind the woman and to the clock on the wall. Down to her typewriter, ledger, and manila folders, which lay scattered across the massive desk.

"Mr. Whitaker?"

Dennis' world began crumbling beneath him. It was his final rotation of med school. How poetic that now would be the time he hits a wall. After everything he's been through. The lump in his throat began to feel suffocating, and his brows tangled together as he imagined how screwed he was. He was so, so very screwed.

"I don't..." he tried. "I don't—"

The girl behind him huffed dramatically, then shoved beside him to interrupt with: "312 Meyrick Street, Apartment 2. In Oakland."

Bewildered, he turned to the girl. She looked around his age, perhaps a little younger. Her hair was jet-black and pulled into a tight ponytail, skin totally clear, pupils dark. She looked a little annoyed.

"...Is this... actually his address, ma'am?" The desk lady cocked an eyebrow. "He just said he's between places."

The girl nodded. "He's a dork. Real socially awkward, too."

She looked at Dennis and nudged his side with her elbow, using some force as she urged, "Isn't that right, Huckleberry?"

Huckleberry?

Dennis squinted, but did not question her. Instead, he turned to the lady at the desk and tentatively nodded.

"Trinity Santos," she introduced herself, mostly to the desk lady, but inadvertently to Dennis, too. "He's my roommate," she nudged her head in his direction.

He added to the fib: "I'm just looking to move out soon. She's a very mean roommate."

She let out a teasing whistle. "I'm not mean. He's just really high-strung." She seemed to relish insulting Dennis, who she did not know; she spoke with a dramatic head nod and a crinkle in her nose.

"Proving my point," he chuckled nervously, still staring at the desk lady through squinted eyes.

The girl kept a polite smile on her face, but leaned in and whispered to him in agitation: "I just saved your ass, Huckleberry. I wouldn't call that 'mean'."

"—I'll put the address down," the desk lady interrupted.

"Thank you," Dennis said, but he couldn't tell if he'd directed it to the woman at the desk, or the girl beside him.

"You should..." the lady spoke as she typed, nose pointed up, "...be all good. Your shift begins tomorrow at 7:00 sharp. Someone'll come in and take you from there. Sound good, Mr. Whitaker?"

"Sounds great. Thank you. So much."

He exhaled the nervous breath that had stayed suspended in his lungs.

"Wonderful. I hope Presby treats you well," she said with sincerity, but quickly directed her attention to the next person in line—the girl.

"Now, how about you, Miss..."

"Santos," the girl repeated with a nod.

"I assume you're also here for rotation?"

She shook her head and stepped up to the desk. "It's an internship. He's the med student," she pointed back at Dennis with her thumb.

Dennis waited at the door, listening to the strangely cocky girl speak. Listening to her fill out her papers and verify her information. He wasn't sure why, but he waited until she'd begun walking away from the desk, papers in hand, smirk on her face. His eyes met hers, and she seemed surprised he was even still there—if not a little disturbed.

As she passed by, he fell into step beside her, and the two of them walked out into the bright, summer light. The streets of Pittsburgh were busy and noisy. Traffic jammed in the road, and little crowds of people trickled by on the sidewalks.

"Shouldn't you be house-hunting?" The girl quipped insensitively, shooting a distasteful glance at Dennis.

"Why'd you do that in there?"

"Do what?"

"Whatever that was, I don't know." He shrugged. "You helped me."

"I gave you a fake address is all."

"It's fake?" He nearly yelled.

"No, dumbass. It's just not your address," she corrected like he was an idiot. After a pause, she turned to face him as they walked, squinting her eyes and raising a brow. "You didn't... you don't think I actually just offered you a place to stay, did you, Huckleberry?"

"My name's Dennis," he corrected timidly.

"You look more like a 'Huckleberry' to me. Sound like one, too."

"What's that mean?"

"Means you don't strike me as being from Pittsburgh."

"You're right," he stated. "Not that it matters."

"You from Texas?" She tried, almost offensively.

Dennis grimaced at her painfully wrong guess. "...Nebraska."

"Damn, so the middle of nowhere," she sighed. "I helped you 'cause you sounded like you were gonna melt into a puddle if you didn't get into Presby." Trinity shrugged it off like it was nothing. "That, and you don't look like the type to rob me—or murder me."

The sun beamed down on Dennis' face as he glanced at the girl. "Thanks," he mustered shyly.

"Yeah. So, you're in for a..."

"Four-week rotation," he finished for her.

"Right. That."

"What about you? I heard 'internship' in there."

"Yep. Intern. First shift is tomorrow, bright and early. Guess we'll be working together, hm?"

"You're working in the ED?"

Trinity nodded. Then she stopped walking. Dennis stopped, too, looking at her. She was staring at a bright red red building—a sit-in pizza place. 

"Say, Huckleberry," she mused. "You hungry at all?"

He glanced back and forth between Trinity and the restaurant.

"Can't afford it."

Trinity sighed. "I'll buy your slice."

 

Chapter End Notes

Aaaaand there it is... after a whole month, we've come to the end of act 2. Thank you so much for supporting my story and reading along with each release. It means the world to me. This STORY means the world to me. And seeing it connect to so many people means the world to me, so thank you. <3

Well... now that we're 10 years into the future, Act 3 will begin next week to kick off the new year!!

Note: I've finished my little song for the story, and I'll be putting it on all streaming platforms just before the release of chapter 14 (the final chapter)

IMPORTANT NOTE: I've... decided on an ending. Tags have been updated accordingly. There shouldn't be many more tag additions/revisions as we near the end of the story, and if there are any, they'll be very small changes at most, I assure you.

Chapter 10: Les Fluers

Chapter Summary

Ten years after leaving Robby, Dennis struggles to let go of his past while a new friend pushes him to loosen up.

Chapter Notes

Chapter 10's song is "Les Fluers" from Minnie Riperton's 1970 album, "Come To My Garden"

See the end of the chapter for more notes

"So," Trinity spoke through massive bites of her spinach and feta slice, "your family shoves religion down your throat. They shut you out. You had enough of that. You moved to Tennessee all on your own. You almost died on a farm..."

"'Almost died' is a bit dramatic, I think—"

"—Whatever. You almost died. You get saved by the same guy that later takes you in and teaches you medicine at... his own private practice?"

"...There's a bit more to it than that... but," Dennis blinked, then nodded. He nibbled at his own pepperoni pizza slice.

"So you're a homosexual," she smiled.

Dennis nearly choked on his bite, dropping the slice in his hand onto the cardboard and looking around, as if to check if anyone heard the girl.

After a proper swallow, through the scowl on his face, Dennis hissed in a whisper: "What the hell is wrong with you?"

"What?"

"A—" he looked around, then leaned into the table. "A homosexual?"

"What?"

"Where is this coming from?"

Trinity snorted. "What the fuck else would you run away from home for? And also, it's kinda weird that a dude who put you in his bathtub just happened to take you on as his mentee a couple months later and trust you to work in his clinic with zero prior experience. That's damn near misconduct. No normal guy would risk his career like that."

"He... well, in retrospect, he wasn't much of a 'normal guy'." Dennis shook his head and winced. "But that's not the— I'm not a queer."

"Oh?"

"Yeah."

"My bad," she shrugged, chomping into an untouched side of her slice. "You be what you wanna be, Huckleberry."

Dennis sighed. He was quickly becoming complacent with the nickname, and he'd only known this woman for about thirty minutes. He turned to the window beside their booth, looking out at the busy streets full of people.

"So, why Pittsburgh? Sounds like you had it made in Tennessee, anyway."

"I'd heard a lot about it, is all. Also, I didn't come straight here from Tennessee."

"You moved again?"

"I moved back home," he glanced at the woman. "To Nebraska."

"Oh..." Trinity's lips curled down facetiously. "A breakup."

"Are you going to take me seriously? At all?"

"Sorry," she fleered, raising her hands in retreat. "Go on."

Dennis stared for a moment, eyelids slack with agitation.

"It was about ten years ago now. I had a... situation. And so I decided to move back home."

"Was that fun?"

"...No."

Dennis looked down at the table, frowning.

"My father had gotten a lot sicker since last I saw him. He died a few months later, actually."

"Oh. You liked your dad?"

"Not really," he smiled, but there was pain in it. "But... he was my dad."

"I get that," Trinity spoke with a hint of sincerity for the first time. "So, was med school a way of, like... taking control of it? Of what happened to him?"

Dennis cocked his head, pondered, then kissed his teeth. "Not really. I got the urge to pursue medicine when I was in Tennessee. The doctor who saved my life, he... was really good. I watched him a lot—the way he handled his clinic. The way he handled patients. Oh, and there was this lady that he took under his wing, too. She was like... a prodigy. Way, way better than me."

He watched Trinity from across the table as she chomped away at the remains of her slice.

"What brought you here?"

"Talent."

"...Right. But—I meant like, y'know," he gestured, "what led up to your internship. At Presby."

"I got a full-ride scholarship for my undergrad degree," she nodded. "I didn't actually realize I wanted to get into med school until after I started college. But I have the 'control' thing."

"The 'control' thing," Dennis half-asked.

"Yup," she swallowed the last bite of crust. "We were poor growing up. I decided I didn't wanna live like that forever." Trinity shrugged.

"Money's why you became a doctor?"

"Well... among other things. But it's not really worth getting into," she chuckled. "It'd put you to sleep."

"Try me."

"No thanks," she offered a guarded smile.

Dennis blinked.

Redirecting the conversation swiftly, Trinity jabbed: "You're kinda dumb, hm?"

"'Scuse me?"

"Didn't you know they'd ask for an address? For your rotation?"

"Oh. I... well," he pondered. "In the past, others haven't asked."

"You mean you've been homeless for a while?"

"Homeless," he tried on the word like an itchy sweater, cocking his head and squinting. "Not necessarily. Just— again, between places. I crash in the backseat of my car. What's left of it, anyway."

"Right." Trinity stared. "How long have you been 'between places,' Huckleberry?"

"...Little under a year," he shrugged.

"A year?"

"Yeah. It's really not a big deal. I just couldn't secure housing for fourth year."

Trinity shook her head. She took a greedy swig from her glass Coca-Cola bottle, then slammed it down and studied Dennis. "So you don't have anywhere real to stay?"

"I think worse things have happened."

"It probably will be the worst thing. Presby's no joke. And, I mean, you heard the lady at the desk, right? This whole field is changing. That ED's gonna be... like nothing we've ever seen."

"Yeah, I heard."

"To be honest, it's kinda weird to think we're at the forefront of it all."

"Isn't that pretty normal in Pittsburgh, though? It's part of why I moved here for med school," Dennis said.

"Moved where? You're homeless."

"You've reminded me a few times now."

There was a pause. They looked at one another in an odd stalemate, empty pizza plates laying in front of them on the table. Trinity broke first.

She let out an agitated sigh. She rolled her eyes, too. "Tell you what, Huckleberry."

Dennis squinted.

"You can crash in my spare room tonight."

"What— really?" 

"Don't give me time to change my mind," Trinity snorted. "If you... don't weird me out or piss me off by the end of our shift tomorrow, I might let you keep the room for a little while."

He winced. "I can't. I couldn't pay you."

"You couldn't pay me for this slice, either," she remarked. "Find other ways of making yourself useful."

Dennis shot a look at Trinity that bordered on terrified.

"...Not like that, you dumbass," she sighed, then paused for a second before adding: "I'm not all that into men, anyway."

Dennis looked up, frowning at her.

"You're very open."

"Only around homosexuals," she shrugged.

"I told you, I'm not—"

"Right, my bad," she apologized nonchalantly, sliding out from the booth.

As she stood to her feet, Trinity sighed, stretching her arms and glancing out at the bright streets beyond the dirty restaurant window.

"Alright. Let's go get your shit."

Dennis scooted out of the booth and stood awkwardly, looking even more awkwardly at Trinity. "Are you... seriously offering me a place to stay?"

"Jesus, dude," she groaned.

"Okay, okay," Dennis raised his arms up in retreat."

 

 

Pittsburgh was a bigger city than Dennis had ever seen— save for Nashville the two times he'd ever passed through it. The sun seemed to shine at all times, and that was a hauntingly familiar sensation to him. That heat, the beaming rays of golden light, cooked him evenly as he and Trinity Santos sauntered across the city by foot. 

As they passed by the road leading to Hill District, Dennis' eyes remained glued, boring holes through the street sign. Trinity took quick note.

"You know people in The Hill?"

Dennis did not immediately respond, so the woman flicked his ear.

"Ow—no. Not really."

She chuckled.

As they continued their walk past Hill District and into the more unkempt streets of Pittsburgh, Dennis began to lead them in a series of left and right turns. Hopping from one street to another, they continued until tall buildings cast cold shadows upon the streets, cooling the pale boy's cooking skin.

"Don't mean to sound like a dick," Trinity fidgeted with the belt hoop of her bell-bottom jeans, "but you're not taking me somewhere to kill me, hm?"

She looked around at the secluded, cramped parking lot they'd walked into.

"That's not funny," Dennis sighed, jamming his hand in his pocket and fishing out the key to his utterly dead car.

"Wasn't a joke," Trinity grimaced at the ugly old hunks-of-junk lined up in rows like it was a car junkyard. Only it wasn't. This was just a square lot surrounded and swamped by tall buildings. 

She watched as Dennis jammed the key into the handle of the driver door, wiggling it like he had a grudge against it. His face contorted as he yanked and pulled, and in any other circumstance, Trinity would've laughed at the sight. But the car - his car - was absolutely pitiful. The thought of him having been living out of it for nearly a year was almost depressing, even to her.

"There's no damn way you've been living out of this tin can," Trinity shook her head, watching as Dennis finally yanked the door open with a painful metallic screech.

"I do," he stated. "No one ever bothers me here. Not sure what this lot was for, though. Guess that doesn't matter now."

"Let's just," she looked around, almost paranoid, "get your shit and go."

In the silence, interrupted only by distant honks and passersby hollers, Dennis lugged what few clothes and toiletries he had into his school bag, along with an impossibly thick stack of his textbooks. It didn't all fit, so he enlisted Trinity to help carry his notebooks, which she wasn't thrilled to do.

They began their trek to Central Oakland, which was a surprisingly short walk from the little abandoned lot he'd been sleeping in. Trinity guided Dennis through the streets, clearly familiar with the grounds. They passed students as classes let out, passing the massive and - very familiar to Dennis - 'University of Pittsburgh' sign.

He gave it a prolonged glance as he asked, "So you graduated this year?"

"I did," Trinity nodded, agitated that her hands were full of his belongings.

"How'd you keep your housing?"

"It's not school housing," she huffed. "It's an off-campus apartment."

"Gosh," Dennis gawked. "And I'm supposed to believe you grew up poor?"

"I very much did," she shook her head. "I just worked really fucking hard in undergrad. Lotta grants and scholarships, baby."

Dennis wanted to ask more—he knew well enough, that kind of drive did not come solely from a need not to be poor. There must've been something more to Trinity Santos' story than the woman wanted to reveal, but they'd only known each other for an hour, give or take; he decided to leave it alone.

Her apartment complex certainly wasn't anything fancy, but she kept her own apartment neat. It was clean, and very urban to Dennis. He'd never seen a view like the one she had on her terrace, jaw-dropped and nose pressed against the glass as he looked out at the street a few stories below.

"Girl," she sighed, rolling her eyes as she watched him gawk like a child.

The slam of notebooks on her kitchen counter was the only noise that pulled Dennis from his thoughts with a flinch.

"Your room's the one next to the door. It's got its own bathroom. And a walk-in closet. Not that," she squinted at his tiny backpack, "you'll need it."

"Thanks so much," he turned to face her, a touch of sincerity on his tongue. "For this. It's real kind of you."

"Okay," she chuckled dismissively, shuffling into her room. "You can unpack your stuff—or whatever."

After drinking his fill of the view just a little longer, Dennis shifted on his feet and shuffled timidly toward the door to his bedroom. His own room. 

"Woah," he sighed, looking around at the space.

The bed wasn't big, but it was a bed. The closet was big. The bathroom looked a little untouched—dusty, perhaps, but it was a bathroom. With a shower, and a sink, and a toilet. And a mirror. He could see himself in a mirror that wasn't cracked like his rearview or stained like the many disgusting public restrooms he'd freshened up in this past year. It was a solid, clear bathroom mirror.

Dennis sat his school bag on the edge of the bed, watching as it pressed down onto the cloudy black comforter. After a moment, he unzipped it and began pulling out his clothes. He was lucky he'd just taken them to the laundromat the day prior. He pulled out his fresh pair of scrubs, laying them out on the bed. Then he pulled out his textbooks. At the bottom of his bag, he'd saved two more books— not textbooks. Those belonged to Michael Robinavitch, a long, long time ago.

He pressed his finger onto the cover of the red one, 'Principles of Internal Medicine,' and he ghosted his digits across it. After a moment, he pulled it out of the bag, holding it with both hands and reminiscing. He was in that man's hometown. He didn't think he was meant to end up in Pittsburgh, but the opportunity had arisen, and as hard as he'd worked in undergrad, he couldn't pass it up. It always scared him—the thought of moving to a place that had raised the man who changed his life, for better and for worse. 

In the ten years since they'd last seen one another, Dennis thought about him almost every day. The man haunted him at every corner. Every page he turned in medical school. Every word he wrote on paper. Every hour he stayed up to hone his knowledge and his skill. He hated to admit it, but it was all in the name of a Michael Robinavitch that he thought he knew—a Michael Robinavitch that he'd created in his mind. One that, in this past decade, had become something of a legend to him. An unsung hero. A myth.

Perhaps that intangibility was a form of coping for Dennis Whitaker—

"That doesn't look like a textbook," Trinity chuckled, leaning against the doorframe as she pointed. "Looks old as fuck."

Torn from his thoughts, Dennis blinked. Then he turned his head to eye Trinity. Then he eyed the book, and finally eyed Trinity once more. "It—" he stammered, "yeah, it's not from school."

"Hometown book? Parents had it stashed in their house?"

"Absolutely not," he chuckled. "My parents were the furthest thing from doctors," he raised the book up and pointed at the title. "It's a medical book."

"Oh," Trinity nodded, lips curled downward. "Rad."

"I, uh... got it from Tennessee."

Trinity gasped. "Oh, I see. Your doctor friend, hm?"

"My doctor friend," he nodded. "Yeah."

"Well, you know what they about old junk like that, right?"

"No?" Dennis raised an eyebrow.

"It haunts you like ghosts," Trinity widened her eyes theatrically, pushing off the doorframe and walking out into the kitchen.

Defensively, Dennis followed her out, book still clutched in his hand.

"It's not some necklace or old strand of hair or anything!" He stammered with embarrassment. "It's a medical book. I keep it 'cause it's good information—good knowledge."

"Huckleberry," Trinity pulled a grape from its vine in her fruit bowl and popped it in her mouth, chomping as she spoke: "Answer me a question, hm?"

He did not respond, eyelids slackening with agitation.

"When's the last time you genuinely read that book?"

"I—" Dennis licked his lips, shaking his head. Unable to answer after several awkward seconds of silence, he resigned with: "I dunno."

Trinity snorted. "You know what I think?"

"Do I want to know what you think?"

"I think," she started anyway, "you need to loosen up a bit."

"Loosen up?"

"Loosen up!"

"I'm loose."

"Don't say it like that," she grimaced.

"What?" Dennis shrugged.

"My point is: you're like a rubber band. You're so high-strung I really think one snap would send you flying across Oakland. And that book?" She pointed to the book still held in Dennis' hand. "That's a whole other situation. It's depressing."

"It's a book," the boy sighed.

"It's a cry for help." She popped another grape in her mouth and stepped forward. "I think you need to get out and dance a little tonight."

"Huh?"

"My street's got a lot of bars. There's this one place, The Gem... they've always got live music in the back room. Lots of people, dark room, everyone's doing their own thing."

"I'm not a 'party' kind of guy," Dennis scratched the back of his head nervously.

"Oh, trust me. I can tell," Trinity yawned. "Lucky for you, it's not a 'party.' Think of it like a... like a blind dancefloor. No one sees you 'cause they're all doing their own thing in the dark. The music's too loud. The lights are flashing too much. They're too busy sipping the good stuff. You're basically in your own little bubble."

They stood in a stalemate for a silent minute. Trinity leaned on her kitchen counter, popping grapes in her mouth as she eyebrow-prodded Dennis, who stared like a cornered mouse, hugging his medical book tight to his chest for stability.

"...No," he finally decided, shaking his head. "I shouldn't. We shouldn't. We have to be at Presby at 7:00 tomorrow. It's just... not a good idea."

"We wouldn't be out that late," Trinity shrugged. "But... alright."

She stepped past Dennis with a sly look on her face, sauntering into her bedroom. He stood staring at the half-picked vine of green grapes in her fruit bowl, letting out the breath he'd been holding. Admittedly, he realized how unhealthy it was to have been so unsettled discussing the book. That alone lit up a bulb behind his eyes, shining a light on the stupidity of having held onto it for so long without ever having any real reason to. Sure, he had a lot of respect for its previous owner, but he also had a very complicated relationship with that man—or rather, what was left of that man in his mind. 

"I'm gonna go, though!" Trinity called from her bedroom over the sound of fabric shuffling around. "I could use a drink and a little grooving to knock off the nerves of tomorrow."

"Will that be safe?"

She peered her head out from the frame of her bedroom door, apparently topless. She glanced at Dennis with quizzical eyes. "Why would it not be safe?"

"Well, I dunno. A woman walking in an urban area by herself, right? Isn't that—"

"—You think I can't handle myself, Huckleberry?"

Dennis blinked. Nervously, he stammered, "No. I— that isn't what I mean. I just..."

Trinity cackled, slapping the wall to keep herself from keeling over. "I'm fucking with you."

Dennis' eyelids slackened, unamused.

And so, the woman showered and finished dressing herself, stepping out to show off her outfit particularly theatrically for Dennis as he sat on the couch, scribbling in his notebooks and flipping pages of his textbooks. After receiving his tentative - and mildly confused - approval, she stepped toward the front door, jingly purse on shoulder.

Turning her head, Trinity instructed, "I'll be back before midnight, but I'm just gonna assume you'll be asleep by then. There's leftover pasta in the top shelf of the fridge if you don't know how to cook—it's a few days old, though... eat at your peril." 

"...Thanks."

"And make sure the door is locked when I leave, okay? Sometimes this piece of shit doesn't lock right and I come home having to bet whether or not I've been robbed."

"Yeah," Dennis nodded, half-listening.

Noticing the lack of attention he was paying, Trinity sighed. She jangled the flimsy doorknob of her front door and stepped out into the world, leaving Dennis to himself in the silence. He allowed a few seconds after the slam of the door before looking up from his papers, leaning back into the couch and running his palms over his face.

The darker the sky became outside the glass door to the terrace, the livelier the street grew. Students flooded in waves, a cacophony of cheers and Friday night buzz filling the space between each tall apartment building—all muffled inside the little apartment. 

A familiar loneliness crept inside his chest as Dennis allowed the noise to fall upon his ears. His eyes froze on a single word at the edge of his page, unable to continue his work. His brows furrowed into a soft frown and his lips curled downward, as if to brace for the dull, low ache of pain which had learned to gnaw at his insides over the last decade.

Dennis Whitaker was very isolated.

It was - perhaps for worse - by choice, too. He'd spent the last decade of his life expending blood, sweat, and tears to achieve the goals he'd begun to realize. Tomorrow's first shift at Presby's Emergency Department was the final stepping stone until the next chapter of his life, and yet it felt so very mundane.

His eyes finally moved, flicking to land upon the cover of Robby's old medical book.

The dull ache swelled inside of him. Everything he'd accomplished, he did for himself. He had no one to share the success with. He had no reason to celebrate his progress aside from the excitement of being one step closer to saving lives, but no man was wholly selfless—even Dennis would have liked to indulge a brag or two with a familiar face. But that was a difficult thing, when most faces he passed nowadays were altogether unfamiliar. 

"Stop," he warned himself, digging his canine into his bottom lip to rip himself from that darkening trail of thought. "Stop."

He allowed a strained sigh to drag itself from his throat. His eyes could not peel away from the cover of the medical book. It was, oddly, difficult to ignore the validity of Trinity Santos' sentiment.

Perhaps Dennis did need to loosen up.

 

 

That night, Dennis Whitaker tried to loosen up, and it nearly killed him.
