Weaving through crowds of excited students and passersby was a little awkward on his own. It made him feel small, but Dennis managed to slip out onto the street just below Trinity's apartment, taking it all in nonetheless. Music flooded from within several different bars and restaurants.

His heart pounded with the anticipation of having made a choice. It was like the fear of setting himself upon a path was worse than traveling the path itself. He'd soon slither his way into a crowded bar for no reason other than to pry approval from a woman he'd known only since afternoon.

Well, that wasn't entirely fair.

Dennis knew well enough that approval in itself did not mean much, especially not from Trinity Santos, kind as she was to offer him a roof and a bed for the night. What really haunted him was that damned book she pointed out. It didn't occur to him - even after a decade of clinging to it as though it were some dark scripture with the secrets to life itself - that it might've been holding him back, spiritually and otherwise.

It hadn't occurred to him that the ghost of Michael Robinavitch was still holding him back.

Perhaps it was pride. He couldn't gauge either way what it might've been, but pride came to mind as he assessed himself, stepping through the thickening crowds on the street. Pride that he refused to swallow. Pride that demanded he feign superiority over a man that had, in every way he perceived, rejected him in Franklin ten years ago.

"Sorry," he said, bumping shoulders with a man before bowing his head timidly.

Perhaps pride wasn't quite correct.

He was at least self-aware enough to know that if pride had anything to do with his inability to accept that Robby still haunted him, he'd have likely had a lot more of it to spare in other aspects of his life. But he simply didn't: Dennis was a pushover, and a timid little creature scurrying from library to classroom to bathroom to street. If he'd had any spare pride in his bones, he had plenty more urgent places to aim it than at a mere ghost from his past.

Unless, of course, Robby was a larger ghost than he wanted to admit.

Dennis scoffed at himself, shaking his head and pulling himself out of his mind, recalibrating as the world around him carried on.

Most of the open bars on the street were overflowing with men and women, many of which linked by arms like couples. All different colors and sizes of people, a thing that Dennis had only just recently become familiar with given his contrastingly conservative and rural upbringing.

As he stepped stiffly down the street, Dennis' eyes gravitated toward the only bar that did not seem like a one-way ticket to overwhelm; a bright, neon-purple sign hung over the massive entrance reading 'The Gem' in cursive letters. It was by no means empty, but certainly less of a headache to navigate than the other options along the street. And above all other reasons, it was the place Trinity had mentioned. She'd likely be inside, which meant he'd know at least one person—it would be his cop-out for getting out of the house. 

The Gem was lively inside, and three very unignorably attractive men in black mesh shirts shuffled around while they worked behind the bar counter. Tall men and women, and everyone in-between, passed by Dennis as he tried not to eye them. Men in wigs and makeup, women with their girlfriends and boyfriends, it was like a circus—colorful and rare. 

The further he made it into the bar, the more prominent the live music in the back room was becoming. Trinity did mention that there was always live music, but Dennis' music taste - as he imagined, entirely presumptuous - wouldn't have aligned with the place he'd walked into. Still, before he knew it, he had a bright, unassuming drink in hand, ordered from the bar without really knowing what it was. He balanced it best he could as he shuffled to the back, where the crowds really began to thicken.

It was sweaty and hot, and Dennis was absolutely certain his drink had splashed over onto the backs of several unassuming partygoers, but no one seemed to care. In fact, he began to realize no one cared at all about him—he wasn't being watched. The music thumped and rattled every bone in his body from the hum of the floor, almost forcing him to move to the beat. Forcing him to melt into the groove of it all.

Dennis looked around almost desperately for Trinity Santos, hoping that she'd actually ended up in this place—hoping he wasn't alone. In the corner of his eye, two men - who were practically sucking the lips off of one another's faces - leaned against the black wall of the venue. It made Dennis' heart jump. Made him take a big, bitter swig of the exceptionally strong drink in his hand.

That was when the performer on the little stage cut through the noise with a new song.

"'Scuse me," Dennis said politely, and nervously, as he bumped into an older, taller man.

The man turned and looked at him, nodding to the slow, swinging groove of the song as it began. "Oh hell, I love this song."

They locked eyes, and the warm buzz of alcohol settling in his stomach gave Dennis just enough courage to maintain that eye contact. He was, however, too awkward to respond, so the man spoke again:

"You like to dance, frozen fruit?"

"What?" Dennis called above the increasingly excited roar of the crowd as the song's introductory beat became louder and louder. He took another embarrassingly large sip of his drink.

"It's Minnie Riperton!" The older man called out again, confusing Dennis more.

"I don't—"

"—Les Fleurs. Greatest hippie groove of the decade? Oh, come on. Dance it with me!"

"I'm not so good at—"

Interrupted by the man's guiding hand on his shoulder, Dennis stepped into beat, allowing the world to spin a little as the singer on the stage broke through the hypnotic thrum of the drums.

She sang, "Will somebody wear me to the fair?"

The man's fingers trailed more securely around Dennis, curling at the nape of his neck. He put his other hand on Dennis' waist, guiding them closer. It sent Dennis' heart into a beat like a racehorse, thumping far faster than the beat of the song.

"Will a lady pin me in her hair?"

Dennis looked around frantically, as if to assess if it was even safe to let his guard down like this. Safe to allow a touch from another man.

"Will a child find me by a stream?"

The man's face was hard to see in the dark of the venue, but he vaguely reminded Dennis - in physical appearance only - of a man he used to love. The only man he ever loved. It tugged at his heartstrings just enough that he allowed himself to pretend—if only for a moment, that it was really him.

"Kiss my petals, weave me through a dream."

Dennis could feel the warm touch of the man's hand on his neck, caressing gently as he kept them in a sway matching the beat. Their shoes tapped against one another clumsily—it was clear that Dennis was not much of a dancer, but he did his part well enough.

"For all of these simple things and much more, a flower was born. It blooms to spread love and joy, faith and hope, to people forlorn."

The man did not speak above the singer, instead he grabbed Dennis' attention by tapping his middle finger on the boy's neck. When Dennis looked up, it was like the man was asking, with his eyes, if he was comfortable.

Dennis' jaw clenched. Then he nodded, tentatively.

"Inside every man, lives the seed of a flower. If he looks within, he finds beauty and power."

The instrumental began to swell, like it was building up to some big, anthemic, explosive chorus. The stranger took that as a kind of theatrical excuse to pull Dennis closer, closer, and closer. Their faces were mere inches apart, and it had become quite clear he intended to kiss Dennis right there in the middle of the crowd. Be it the alcohol in his system or the general overwhelm of the room, Dennis could not find it in him to care—until something far behind them caught his eye. Just as their bottom lips made contact, Dennis saw a glimpse of a burly man he could've sworn was staring daggers into his skull. Judgmental. Disapproving.

Just like home.

Dennis pulled away in a violent flinch, leaving the man he danced with a little startled.

"Sorry," he mumbled, but it was drowned out by the noise.

The man reached his hand out slowly, saying something that Dennis could not hear, but the boy stepped back again, stumbling as the world spun beneath his feet. He hadn't noticed how intoxicated he really was until that sudden movement; it was as though a wave of nausea had been holding itself at bay until he moved his head.

"No," Dennis shook his head. "Enough," he spoke, unsure if he was talking to himself or someone else.

Robby's face burned behind his eyes as they squeezed shut. The face Robby had made in their final argument at the Franklin clinic, ten years ago.

The singer's voice cut through as Dennis stumbled backward through the crowd:

"Ring all the bells, sing and tell the people everywhere that the flower has come!"

Heart slamming against his ribcage, Dennis pushed past a woman and her friend, spilling his drink on the ground. No one seemed to notice or care but the woman. She tried barking at him, but he did not hear her.

"Light up the sky with your prayers of gladness, and rejoice for the darkness is gone!"

He stumbled and stumbled until his palms finally found purchase on a wall, leaning all his weight upon it, cheek smashing against it as he heaved. His immediate assessment was clear: he'd sipped that potent drink far too fast - especially for someone who never drank at all.

Dennis slid along the wall, trying to find the exit.

"Throw off your fears let your heart beat freely, at the sign that a new time is born!"

Unsure he'd actually make it out of the venue alive, Dennis' eyes squeezed shut. Then—and only then, did God send him a little rope, in the form of Trinity Santos, who finally bumped into him and grabbed him by his bicep.

"Woah," she chuckled. The humor didn't last long, though, when she realized how intoxicated up Dennis really was. Her next "Woah" was tinged with much more concern.

"Hey," she said, leaning down so her voice would reach his ear over the noise. "What the hell happened to you? Why are you here?"

"Wanted to loosen up," he bleated pitifully. "Too much blue drink."

Trinity groaned, "Jesus Christ."

The woman said goodbyes to her little cluster of friends, keeping Dennis' arm slung around her as she carried him out onto the street. The warm, summer air of night shocked the boy's system back into shape as he inhaled, bringing him back down - at the very least - to planet Earth. His heart still slammed in his chest like he'd been running from death itself, and his hands were clammy, curly little bangs clinging to his forehead all sticky-like. The whole way back, she prodded him with questions like she was on the clock giving a patient his exam. Aside from the alcohol, Dennis was mostly fine.

"You're lucky it's a short walk, dumbass," Trinity groaned, shuffling the weight of Dennis awkwardly so she could unlock the door to her apartment and shove it open with a kick.

"I'm sorry," he moaned sincerely.

She ignored it at first, lugging him onto the edge of his bed and panting. Glancing at the pitiful mess Dennis had become, Trinity's brows furrowed.

"What the fuck even happened? Weren't you gonna stay in tonight?"

"I'm sorry," Dennis tried again.

Trinity roll her eyes.

"You don't need to be sorry, Huckleberry," she tried to sound agitated, but beneath it was concern. "Just— what happened? Did someone try..."

"No," he shook his head. "No."

"Okay, well—"

"—I'm a coward."

"What?" She cocked an eyebrow.

"I ran away," he said, delirious.

Trinity assumed he was referring to The Gem.

"Yeah, you did."

"I did. I ran away. I shouldn't have. I shouldn't have run away."

"...Well," she tried very poorly to comfort the boy, "you're all good now."

Dennis let out a confusing little hum of approval.

When he settled into a wave of silence, Trinity stepped back out of the doorframe, letting out the air that she'd held in her lungs. She smeared her palms across her face, deciding not to go back out for the night.

"You owe me, Huckleberry. You're gonna... have to do dishes for a whole week."

"Yeah," he hummed into the sheets.

"Clean and vacuum, too."

"Okay," Dennis hummed again, putting up absolutely no fight.

"...Idiot," she sighed under her breath. Before she could walk any closer to her room, she stopped, swiveled, and called out: "We have to be up in nine hours. Better make sure you're all sobered up by then!"

Before stepping forward into her room, Trinity glanced over at the coffee table in the living room. Atop his textbook lay that damned medical book. 'Principles of Internal Medicine,' closed and resting over the other like an indulged temptation. A tinge of guilt tickled at her heart, but before she could dissect that, she stepped one foot in front of the other, shaking her head and closing the door to her room behind her.

The next morning went quite smooth, all things considered. Dennis showered and dressed himself, enjoying the luxuries of his own bathroom—allowing those luxuries to drown the noise of last night from his mind. When he left his room and entered the kitchen, Trinity sat at the counter with a bowl in front of her. Baron Von Redberry box in hand, she poured the cereal in, then milk, and ate, all while Dennis watched. 

"Get a bowl if you're gonna eat breakfast," she grumbled.

The sun hadn't even risen yet.

"I'm not much of a breakfast person," he smiled politely. "Stomach's sensitive in the morning."

"Probably because you don't eat breakfast," she raised an eyebrow.

"Maybe."

A soft silence filled the room as Trinity crunched away at each spoonful of sweet, berry-flavored bites. Dennis only broke that silence because he felt he needed to say:

"...Thanks for helping me last night."

Trinity looked up, jaw stopping its chewing for a moment.

"You wanna tell me what happened?"

Dennis' jaw clenched. It was clear he wanted to tell her, but only because he felt he owed Trinity—not because he actually wanted to talk about it.

"I," he sighed, scratching his eyebrow with one hand, fidgeting with the neck of his scrubs with the other. "It's nothing. It won't affect me again. I mean—it won't put you in a position like that again. Needing to babysit me, I mean."

She eyed him for a moment, trying to dig deeper into his pupil's for some hidden meaning, like she'd be able to read what really went on last night. But of course, there was no way of knowing on her own, so she sighed, stuffing another spoonful of cereal in her mouth and shrugging. Building her walls back up in preparation for the shift ahead, she offered a sarcastic smile and said:

"Good."

 

 

Dennis and Trinity walked into Presby separately, but were lumped together all the same with the other two new faces.

"I'm Victoria Javadi," the girl raised her palm with a fidgety smile. "MS3. We're all new, today?"

"Think so," Trinity looked around at the crowded waiting room, hand clutched onto the shoulder strap of her bag.

"Your name is..." Javadi tried.

"Dr. Santos," she nodded, nose held high.

"Doctor?"

She sighed. "Internship."

"Ah! Another resident?" The other woman cheered with a giddy smile, picking at her nail as she gave a little hop. The excitement wasn't well-matched, so she dialed it in quickly and suppressed her smile, opting for: "Dr. Melissa King. Well— Mel. Second year. First year at Presby, though."

Javadi eyed Dennis, pulling him from his focus on the dull ache behind his forehead. "Dennis Whitaker. MS4." He nodded with a sheepish smile for good measure.

A man sitting in the waiting chair next to them stood and impatiently asked: "You all work here?"

"First day," Trinity spoke first.

"Fucking hell," he groaned. "Can you get me in a room, please? I've been waiting for hours."

Javadi looked at the other three, perplexed. Then she looked at the man. Innocently, she said, "It's our first day. We're probably not allowed to—"

"So just get a real doctor to come take me in."

Mel tried chiming in, perhaps blissfully unperturbed by the man's sharp attitude. "We can certainly find," she looked around with an innocent little smile curled on her lips, "...someone." Her eyes locked on a woman, and she clapped once in excitement before trotting over, speaking quickly and pointing in the direction of the group.

Dennis blinked, noticing the woman's familiarity. It was unplaceable, but he knew her from somewhere. He'd absolutely seen her before. As she stepped over with Mel, avoiding tripping over the legs of sitting patients, she prepared a professional smile, nodding at the student doctors before focusing her attention on the man.

"Sir, could I ask your name?"

"Bill Adler," he grumbled, watching as the doctor flipped through pages on her clipboard.

"William?" She asked for clarification.

He nodded.

The woman took only a moment to tap the pen at the edge of the clipboard, reading his file, then she looked up. "Mr. Adler, we've got you in for some abdominal pain, I see. Exam's been done, and we're waiting for results on some lab tests. Until then, it's best we keep you out here to make sure patient flow remains... well, flowing," she pinched her lips together in an impatient smile.

His nostrils flared. "How the hell does that make any sense?"

The woman gestured around the room. "I ask myself that same question every day. This department is rapidly evolving, and we're hardly able to keep up with the patients we see," she paused, perhaps a little dramatically. Then she leaned in close and spoke quieter, "but we do keep up. And we do everything in our power to help."

The words seemed to disarm the man. Dennis and the others watched, fascinated.

"If you'll have a seat, we'll be right here if your symptoms get any worse suddenly. Until then, let's wait on those lab tests and go from there."

She ushered him to sit, then redirected her attention to the fresh faces again. With a toothy smile, she gave everyone a once-over.

"You must be our Pittlings."

"Pittlings?" Javadi casted a confused smile.

"Fresh faces in Presby's ED, I mean," the woman nodded. "Apologies. I'm Dr. Samira Mohan."

A light flickered behind Dennis' eyes. He knew that name—knew those eyes. She'd visited Robby's clinic in Franklin over a decade ago while she was in med school. He vividly remembered assessing her in the waiting room, masked up and sick as a dog, visiting from her campus in Nashville. How odd that she was here in Presby all these years later. As an attending physician, it seemed.

"...And you guys are?"

The group introduced themselves once again, far more formally this time.

"Victoria Javadi, MS3."

"Trinity Santos, intern."

"Dr. Melissa King, second-year resident," she smiled.

Lost in thought, staring awkwardly at Mohan, who he'd still not wrapped his head around having recognized, Dennis simply stood there. Trinity nudged his side with her elbow, eliciting a flinch from the boy. His ears flushed red and he cleared his throat.

"Dennis Whitaker," he paused. "MS4."

Dr. Mohan gave him a fascinated look, cocking her head. It lasted only for a moment, though. She was quick to return her attention to the group as a whole, guiding them through triage and explaining the ropes of Presby's ED to them.

"You're gonna meet a lot of doctors and nurses today. Listen to all of them," she nodded. "They're here to help, but so are you. Our job is to save lives and do so in the smoothest way we know how."

Dennis wondered if she recognized him. More than that, though, he still wondered why she was here. It couldn't have been anything more than a funny coincidence, but it was a very funny one.

"Speaking of which," Dr. Mohan stretched her arm out, pulling another male doctor over by his bicep and guiding him in front of the group. "The first one I'll have you meet is Dr. Langdon."

"Hello," he nodded.

The man's face was firm and rigid, hair well-kept over his forehead. He seemed a little surprised to have been pulled over, but locked himself into the moment nonetheless, casting a smile upon the Pittlings.

"Dr. Langdon, I'm so sorry, will you show them around? I've gotta get back to a patient inside."

"Will do."

Mohan offered one more kind nod to the group before hustling off, disappearing beyond the door into the main floor.

Turning back, eyes landing on Mel, Langdon cleared his throat. "I'm Dr. Frank Langdon, senior resident at Presby." He was a little awkward, but gestured around him. "So! This is our front room. Patients will check in over at the desk," he pointed, "and we have nurses in and out of the area to make sure they're not gonna keel over imminently," he chuckled. 

Dennis clenched his jaw. Hearing that, especially as nonchalant as it was, sent his guard up. He knew he'd be dealing with a department that involved many potential casualties - it was the emergency department, after all - but facing that reality head-on was always different than anticipating it internally.

"If the ticker ain't stopping anytime soon, we move them to a triage room for a quick exam. Labs, X-rays, vitals."

"You've got triage rooms here?" Mel gawked, fascinated.

"Fancy, hm?"

"Very," Dennis chimed in, noting the luxury of such accommodation. An accommodation they certainly did not have at the clinic in Franklin all those years ago.

Langdon turned to Dennis, interest piqued. "You worked in many emergency departments before, Mr. Whitaker?"

Dennis shook his head. "Just a clinic back in '62."

The senior resident nodded, lips curled downward. "Rad. Y'think it was as busy as all this?"

Awkwardly, Dennis looked around.

"In its own way," he settled with a distinct lack of confidence. 

"Is it always... like this?" Javadi asked, noting that she'd never seen such a busy department—so many patients shuffling around and being shuffled around.

Langdon ushered them through the doorway and into the main floor of the Presby ED, where the lights became far harsher and the tiled floors became blindingly white. "It's... an evolving realm of the medical world. 'Busy' is only the first of the obstacles you'll find in Presby."

Langdon turned the corner, guiding the Pittlings into the hectic main area at the center of it all—the nurse station. As they approached the desk, surrounded by doctors and nurses, Langdon finished his little tour with: "So it's a good thing you're in good hands with Dr. Robby."

"Wha—" Dennis blurted, but shut his mouth as quick as it opened.

Trinity casted a look at him, raising her eyebrow. As they made their final steps toward the desk, a nurse stepped out of the way and scurried into a patient room, revealing Michael Robinavitch—his Michael Robinavitch—sitting in a chair, staring down at a patient chart with a pair of black reading glasses over his eyes.

Dennis froze.

Every cell in his body seemed to halt, crashing against the confines of his skin, nearly sending him forward and onto his knees. It was like the world had stopped turning, and he was to be hurtled into the nearest wall at speeds that'd flatten him into nothing more than a red stain. Only, that was very certainly not happening; the ED and its busy bees continued shuffling around like normal, and the world kept spinning, all while Dennis drank in the sight of his clearly aged ghost, sitting so very unassumingly in that chair.

His first thought was that it was all, naturally, a dream. Perhaps he didn't actually land a rotation at Presby—perhaps he was just sleeping and he'd wake up back in Nebraska by the end of it. Unfortunate it was, that his sweet dream had turned into a nightmare in mere seconds, heart pounding as he eyed the man.

Robby stood from his chair, offering no more than a casual glance at Dennis before nodding to everyone—a glance so casual he must've known he'd see Dennis. The mask of professionalism sat tight upon his face. He was as tall as Dennis always remembered, rubbing his hands together after pulling his glasses off. It shouldn't have been jarring to Dennis that Robby wasn't - at least outwardly - surprised to see him; it was fair to assume that an attending physician would know, at least by name, which new faces would be coming into his department month by month. But yet, it was jarring.

Robby seemed entirely unfazed.

"New faces, hm?" Robby spoke, voice more gravelly than Dennis remembered, but dark and sweet all the same.

"New faces," Langdon nodded, taking his proud place by Robby's side.

"Welcome. Good morning, good morning."

Robby gave a look, prompting the Pittlings to - for the third time - introduce themselves. They did it quick this time, like a recital of words they'd long since tired themselves of saying.

"Trinity Santos, intern."

"Victoria Javadi, MS3."

"Melissa King. Everyone calls me Mel," she smiled. "Second-year resident."

Finally, Robby looked over to Dennis, eyes boring holes through the boy's skull in anticipation.

"...Dennis Whitaker," he recited shakily. "MS4."

Eyes fixated solely on Dennis, Robby offered a professional, unreadable smile.

Meant for everyone, but directed like a soft light at the boy, Robby said: "Nice to meet you."

After one more pause, he declared, "Welcome to The Pitt."

Chapter End Notes

Welcome to act three, the big final act, hehehehe

Thank you guys so, so much for all the support on this fanfiction. I genuinely read every comment you all leave, and I smile and I sometimes tear up and it's so beautiful seeing how deeply you guys relate to this story and its characters.

Chapter 11: Weird Fishes

Chapter Summary

Faced with Robby for the first time in ten years, Dennis' first shift in Pittsburgh's busiest emergency department goes terribly wrong.

Chapter Notes

Chapter 11's song is "Weird Fishes" from Radiohead's 2007 album, "In Rainbows"

See the end of the chapter for more notes

This would quickly become one of the worst days of Dennis Whitaker's life.

"My name is Dr. Michael Robinavitch, but most everyone here at Presby calls me Dr. Robby."

It had already secured top five.

"You've met Dr. Langdon," he gestured to the man, who waved with cocky eyes. "And this is Dr. McKay. They are your senior residents. You report to them, they report to the attendings, myself and Dr. Mohan, who I'm sure you four met on your way in. Now, over here," he pointed to an older lady with crimson red hair, busy on the phone, "is Flora Pender. She's our charge nurse. Real sweet lady, but you better listen to her like lives depend on it, 'cause they do."

Robby's unreadable eyes rarely left Dennis. When they did, it was only a brief, blink-and-miss glance, like he was trying to remain composed, but unable to quit drinking in the sight of the boy.

The third time their gaze connected, Dennis' heart really stopped, and he jerked his eyes away, unable to control the stress-twitch in his fingers.

"'Emergency medicine,' as some of us in the medical field are calling it," Robby cut through Dennis' derailing train of thought, "is pretty new to us all, and Pittsburgh's sort of the home of development. That means we are the test monkeys of a chaotic, evolving system that, frankly, is gonna knock your socks off."

Langdon leaned his weight on the desk behind, making it visibly clear - not unlike a statement - that he was familiar enough with Robby to invade his bubble. Familiar enough to cozy up with a puffed, arrogant chest. Dennis made note of that, and had no idea why.

Perhaps top three. 

"Here at Presby, we've got trained and certified EMTs, or Emergency Medical Technicians, and paramedics who provide advanced medical care to patients, from ambulance to hospital. Our job here in the ED is to stabilize our incoming patients and keep them alive long enough to see specialists. That means we take on a shit-load of cases every single hour." He paused, offering daggered glances at everyone in front of him. "Some of which cases," Robby nodded, "will change you."

Hell of a warning to administer, mere minutes before these poor students began their first shift.

"Today'll be nonstop, and a lot of it's gonna feel like a madhouse. So work together, communicate, observe, listen, and absorb it all."

Mel was already nodding with an excited, innocent grin on her face. Javadi and Santos seemed a little unfazed.

Dennis could not look Robby in the eyes.

"Let's go save some lives."

 

 

The Pittlings were immediately thrust into the action.

Dragged into the minor treatment area, Dr. McKay guided them with Dr. Robby to a simple room. Dennis had zero time to process that the ghost from his past stood right next to him, close enough to touch. Real. Flesh and blood.

"Mr. Dever, do you mind if I walk some student doctors through while we get you fixed up?"

"If it gets me outta here faster, fuck, I don't care," the stinking man slurred. He reeked of alcohol. It swallowed all the air in the room.

McKay blinked. "...Perfect."

She turned to the group, then began with a professional smile: "Jared Dever. Assault victim who came in with some minor facial injuries, and is otherwise stable. Patient is intoxicated, so as you can imagine—"

"—I din't even drink tha' much," he mumbled, swatting his hand, "crazy lady. Crazy fuckin' lady."

"As you can imagine," McKay continued, "he's been a tad uncooperative."

"Does he not need..." Javadi leaned closer to McKay and lowered her voice, "restraints?"

McKay didn't love that Javadi asked in such close proximity to the patient, but she hid that well. "We like to make sure everyone's in safe hands - doctors and nurses included - before handling cases like these. So if need be, we can. But Mr. Dever here hasn't been that much trouble." She looked at the man with a smile as she said his name. "Just a yapper."

"But he needs to be sober before any serious examination can be done," Trinity surmised.

"Exactly." McKay turned back to Mr. Dever and shuffled over to the side of the bed, checking vitals as she spoke. "Even with minor injuries like Mr. Dever's, we don't want to discharge before giving him time to sober up and allowing ourselves to do a thorough exam, because, simply put, we don't know what's going on inside his body. Beneath the surface."

"I feel fine," Jared Dever spoke, though his babbling indicated otherwise.

"I bet you do," McKay nodded, leaning upright again and flicking the clipboard in her hand. "But get cozy. You're gonna be in here a while."

The man groaned, then looked at Dennis and the others. "This place is fucking noisy." Then he turned, eyes landing ambiguously between Mel and Javadi as he said, "Least you got a good supply of eye candy."

"Okay," Dr. Robby sighed, arms crossed with a kind of disappointment. He hadn't moved from his spot next to Langdon, simply observing as McKay taught. His eyes, however, remained on Dennis as much as possible. The kid could feel it on the back of his neck.

"Lest you scare off my student doctors, Mr. Dever, we're gonna step out and let you sober up." Dr. Robby concluded, ushering them out of the room. Quietly and efficiently, he whispered to McKay, "Have a nurse check on him every quarter hour... male nurse."

Dr. McKay nodded and stayed behind, calling for nurse Jesse.

Langdon took the lead now, guiding the Pittlings into another room several steps down the hall, where a man lay still in the bed. He was hooked up on IV fluids and had simple machines monitoring vitals. Dennis tensed a little, but the man opened his eyes, and they lit up having seen the doctors arrive like angels.

"Patient is Phillip Herron. Fell three stories and hit his head at a construction site in Hill District. I've got," Langdon pulled a sheet from his clipboard, flashing it to Dr. Robby, who adjusted his glasses and squinted, "his X-ray."

The Pittlings huddled behind Robby, peeking at the picture.

"Linear skull fracture," Dennis mumbled.

Robby didn't turn, but he did nod. Langdon hummed with approval, smiling back at the med student before pointing at the offending injury on the picture.

"Skull films do show a linear fracture just over the parietal bone."

Robby hummed. "Call down a neuro consult." He looked up, peeling his glasses off and speaking softly, "Mr. Herron, we're gonna have a specialist come down and help assess the extent of your injury, alright?"

"Is it... serious?" Phillip shuddered, teeth clattering.

"It could've been a hell of a lot worse," Dr. Robby assured, placing his hand on the foot of the bed comfortingly. "But you did bonk your noggin. We like to be safe around here, so we're gonna get word on what the best course of action is. Alright, my friend?"

"...Okay," the patient breathed nervously.

Turning to Langdon, Robby whispered, quick and quiet: "He'll need neurological checks every fifteen minutes, and as far as I'm aware, neuro's tight today. He's gonna be laying here awhile."

Langdon nodded with a stoic look on his face.

Mel cleared her throat with fingers pressed to skin, "I could hang back and help. At the VA we had a lot of head trauma cases— elderly patients fall quite often. I could be of use," she added innocently, almost bubbly.

Searching for Robby's approving eyes - which were, in fact, present - Langdon looked between the two of them for a moment before nodding. "Great," he smiled, then turned to the patient. "Mr. Herron, Dr. King and I are gonna keep you some company, alright? We'll wait for that specialist together."

Robby ushered the remaining Pittlings out of the room with a playful "Let's go, let's go," walking them down the hall until they'd once again reached the massive nurse station. At the same time, Dr. Mohan sauntered out of a room from the opposite side of the hall, hands in pockets, eager smirk on her face.

"Dr. Robby, do you mind if I steal your students for a left shoulder GSW? I think it'd be a good warm-up for the day," she raised her eyebrows. "He's very cooperative, too," she spoke the words like they were carrots on a stick.

"Good timing," the chief attending was already nodding, rubbing at his scruffy beard. Dennis had only just noticed—the man had begun to grow grey hairs. There weren't many, but there were grey hairs. 

"A shoulder GSW is a little simple for a warm-up, no?" Trinity protested with an unamused face.

Dr. Robby turned to the intern. "Simple is rare around here, Dr. Santos. Savor it."

There was a kind of sternness in his tone that set Trinity straight. She swallowed, then nodded. Dr. Mohan cast another smile - as professional as usual - then began walking down the hall. Trinity, Javadi, and Dennis followed like little ducklings.

"Whitaker."

Dennis stopped, watching as the others continued forward without him.

He hadn't even made it five steps forward.

Dr. Robby was already behind him, before he could protest or squirm his way out of confrontation. Of course, he knew he'd have to talk to Robby at some point, but he didn't expect it to happen so immediately. And he certainly didn't expect Robby himself to initiate. As he mustered the strength to turn around, albeit slowly, Dennis looked up into his eyes. For the first time in over a decade, he looked right into the man's warm, familiar eyes, resentment faltering.

The world around them fell impossibly silent, and both men were afraid to be the one to break it.

Robby followed the light behind Dennis' eyes. That light had once led him to the edge of the world.

"Pittsburgh?"

There it was. 

He knew Robby would wonder how he ended up in the man's hometown. Truthfully, he'd convinced himself the decision hadn't actually had much to do with Michael Robinavitch. In a way, that wasn't untrue. Dennis had been looking to drown himself in work, and Pittsburgh seemed a very good place to start when he began med school.

He chased, for years, a dream of impossibility. It kept him from dreading the proverbial light at the end of the tunnel, because how could one dread such light, if one knew the tunnel never ended? That simplified a lot for him. 

Overworking simplified a lot for him.

"I wouldn't have taken this position if I knew you were in town," Dennis responded almost sardonically.

"Oh, I'm sure," Robby remarked with excessive honesty. "But you're here, so... why Pittsburgh?"

Dennis impatiently tapped his fingers at his sides, speaking like it was obvious: "It's diverse. Good medical field, nice people— great food."

"Nothing to do with me?" Robby half-joked.

That he even uttered those words, Dennis scowled.

There was a pause which stifled their rhythm. Dennis swallowed nervously, but stood his ground nonetheless. Still, it was Robby who, against his better judgment, prodded further:

"I assume you drove back to Nebraska. That night."

"...I did."

"Did it treat you well?"

"I'm here, aren't I?"

The older man cast a look, as though that answer wasn't really an answer. He always figured Dennis would've made it into med school, regardless where he ended up after that fateful final night in Tennessee. That was a given. What he actually meant, selfishly, was 'have you been hurting as bad as I have all these years?'

But he'd never ask it aloud.

"What do you want me to say?" Dennis asked, squirming under the older man's quiet gaze.

Robby snapped out of it. "No— it's good to see you. To see you're doing well."

Before Dennis could respond, a charred man was wheeled into the main hallway from ambulance intake by two overworked EMTs. He and Robby hurried over to the stretcher as it barreled toward them. Like vultures to a carcass, two nurses flocked around, keeping up just as well as Robby and Dennis did.

"Patient is Teddy, in his late 20s. Got burned in a gas tank explosion, but no smoke inhalation. Vitals stable en route, about 90% BSA," the female EMT noted. "We managed to get a 20-gauge in his right AC, already pushing LR."

"Trauma one's open," a nurse called out from somewhere behind as they raced further into the ED.

"Princess," Robby commanded, and she scurried ahead into the room. He shot a look up at the male EMT, then looked at the other nurse by the stretcher, Perlah, who'd already begun her examination.

"Vitals are good, heart rate is 138 bpm, BP is stable. Respiratory rate is around twenty-four," Perlah looked up, watching as the man struggled.

"How are we feeling, Teddy?"

"Can't feel much," his voice was like sandpaper.

Dr. Robby then directed his attention to Dennis, who looked frantic. "What do you see, Whitaker?"

"Uh..." he stammered, "Burns are mainly third degree," he looked closer, brows furrowed, struggling to ground himself.

Robby noticed the med student's unease.

As they entered the room and settled the patient, Dr. McKay rushed in, relaying: "He's got singed nasal hairs and soot in oropharynx."

Dr. Robby nodded.

"Let's set up for intubation," McKay continued, gloving herself and stepping to bedside. "Whitaker, Parkland Formula, okay?"

Dennis nodded, reciting that term in his mind. Parkland Formula. It had only just recently been developed. A formula they could wield to estimate the fluid required for a burn patient's first day to ensure they remain stable.

Thank you Charles Baxter, he thought, while scribbling into his dinky notepad.

Dr. Robby glanced behind Dennis as Trinity barreled into the room, nudging shoulders with Dennis as she examined the burn victim who lay writhing. The boy had a look on his face when they'd first wheeled the patient in—a look that didn't sit well with Robby. It looked like he'd been caught in headlights, like he wasn't prepared for such a case. Wasn't prepared to see it all so suddenly. 

Dr. Robby redirected his attention briefly and cast a nod at Princess. "Start morphine." Then he stepped back as he continued, "page burn unit. I'll be back for intubation. Whitaker, finish Parkland Formula then step out if you need to."

Step out? Dennis' eyebrow twitched.

It was such a casual, off-handed comment, but it dug right under his skin. Robby saw that reaction, too. As he passed by, he leaned just enough to whisper low in the boy's ear, "Get it together," then walked out of the trauma room like he'd said nothing at all.

Dennis finalized his calculations - all the while, juggling Robby's select few words in his mind like heavy weights - and gnawed at his bottom lip while McKay guided Trinity to prepare for intubation. Princess monitored the machines at the head of the bed.

"Update me on fluids, Whitaker," she called, turning to the med student.

"Yes," he nodded, forcing himself down to Earth as he scanned his little notepad with scribbles. "Per the Parkland..." and he fired away, stating his calculations, eyes flitting back and forth between the notepad and the doctors in front of him, who responded quickly to each number he spoke.

Dennis surprised himself, having found his tempo so quickly.

McKay offered him a few proud head nods as he finished calling out his calculations, and Princess made adjustments to fluids accordingly.

Trinity snorted, voice coated in her usual playful sarcasm as she said, "Good counting, Huckleberry."

"Okay," McKay sighed, hands on her hips as she gently pushed past Trinity. "Let me find Dr. Robby and we'll intubate."

She stepped hastily out into the hall, closing the space between Teddy's room and the nurse's station, where Robby stood, scribbling at breakneck speed into his patient log.

Charge nurse Flora Pender called out to the man from her chair: "Robby, honey. Jefferey was looking for you a minute ago."

With pursed lips and a shake of his head, he groaned, "Starting early today, hm? I've got the energy, but not the time."

"Go easy. That's our CMO. You piss him off, he takes it out on all of us."

Robby kissed his teeth, "Not that he's supposed to—"

"Dr. Robby," McKay interrupted, halting at the edge of the desk. "We're ready."

The man offered a playful glance at Flora and shrugged. "Saved by the bell."

"You're gonna have to talk to the man at some point!"

"Tell him to come find me, then."

He and McKay barreled back into Teddy's room, and he spared Dennis a mildly confused glance, like he wanted to tell the kid to step out, but decided not to. Not immediately. Dennis caught that glance, though. It set him on edge once more, tempo lost.

"Alright, Teddy. We're gonna need to put in a breathing tube now to prevent your throat from swelling and making it difficult to breathe," Robby began, then paused to spare yet another glance at Dennis. "Whitaker, earlier you referred to Teddy's burns as third degree. We refer to them as full-thickness or partial-thickness."

Trinity's brows furrowed, eyes flitting between the two men, noticing the stare they shared.

"...Yes sir," Dennis nodded.

As wrappers crinkled and metal scraped against trays, McKay and Princess helped prepare the tools for intubation. Dennis stood back, but he watched as Dr. Robby's attention finally settled upon Teddy. It morphed, too. Robby's jaw clenched, and he seemed to tense as he examined the patient's body, like he was coming to some sort of realization. A kind of realization that he couldn't, until that very moment, fully confirm.

As the older man's head whipped back up and his eyes met Dennis', the boy flinched, looking away and pretending he wasn't staring.

"Whitaker," he called from across the room.

Dennis willed himself to look back into Robby's eyes. "Yes?"

"I want you to step out on this one," he declared.

Frankly, not the words he expected to hear.

The room fell oddly silent. McKay looked up. So did Princess. Trinity crossed her arms, eyebrow cocking even higher than it had already been as she studied the attending and the med student.

"What?" Dennis stuttered.

"Step out," Robby repeated sternly.

Michael Robinavitch, he decided, was out to get him.

Dennis dreaded that possibility, but hadn't ruled it out. Not from the moment he laid eyes on the man when the day started. A sinister voice in his mind would argue Robby had a right to have a kind of grudge against him, too. After all, Dennis ran away from Franklin, effectively leaving Robby with no assistance at his - increasingly busy by the year - clinic. A grudge was a simple and plausible explanation here.

Alas, he did not struggle against it—Dennis Whitaker was not the kind of man to put up a fight at all, let alone against a chief attending in front of a patient and three other co-workers. That would've been a kind of suicide, regardless how comfortable he may have been laying into Robby if they were alone.

More peculiar to him anyway, was his distinct lack of willpower to fight. Instead, in place of that boiling rage which, perhaps, should have festered, was a heavy, dull ache.

It was an ache that forced him to tug at the neck of his scrubs, wincing as he obediently nodded.

 

 

The first thing he did was lean against the closed stall in the restroom.

Sinking his teeth into the skin of his knuckle, Dennis forced out the anger that had simmered in that short walk. He couldn't even - more frustratingly - determine if the anger was because of Robby's order, or because of his own unwillingness to fight against it.

Not that he should have felt comfortable fighting against it.

But equally, not that Robby should have felt comfortable administering it.

After hardly three minutes of that rather unrelaxing silence, Dennis washed his hands, then meandered toward the patient board like a dog with its tail between its legs, hands clasped behind his back as he squinted up at the small letters. He leaned into the edge of the desk, letting the pain drown his shame and his frustration as it dug into his stomach.

"There's a big, dark cloud over your head, baby."

"Sorry?" Dennis asked, blinking as he focused his eyes on Flora's bright red hair.

The fluffy older woman wheeled over in her rolling chair. "Looks like you fell ill with a case of the blues."

"Ah," he nodded in understanding.

"You're Dr. Whitaker, right?"

"Just mister. No doctor, yet. Not for a few more months," he mustered a weak smile.

"Well, what's got you down, Mr. Whitaker?"

Dennis really contemplated being honest for a moment. Just tossing Robby under the bus and blurting his concern for - what he strongly believed to be - the man's grudge against him.

But alas, his tail was still tucked between his legs.

"I'm alright, ma'am."

"Call me Flora. 'Ma'am' makes me feel old."

"Flora," he corrected himself softly with a kinder smile. "I'm alright."

She didn't verbally respond, but she did offer him an unconvinced glance.

"Thanks for checking on me," he tried, with all the sincerity he could muster. "It means a lot."

"Y'know, baby," she coddled, almost maternally, "first days are never easy. It's okay to drag along a little."

"...Right," he admitted, pressing on the dent in his knuckle that his teeth had left.

"What isn't okay, though," she continued, "is leaving yourself on a little island. Doctors - student ones, too - can be fantastic at saving others. Piss-poor at saving themselves, though."

A little huff of air, maybe even a soft chuckle, escaped Dennis' nostrils as he finally offered the older lady a real glance. "I've heard that before. I'd say it's true."

"Oh, I know it's true. Take Cranky-Robby for example. You'll get to know him while you're here."

"What about him?" The boy felt ashamed at how quickly his interest had been piqued.

"He's a good example of what happens when you don't take care of yourself, is all I mean," Flora snorted. "Always grumpy, always snappy. He's lucky he's fine at sweeping it under the rug, when it matters. But... don't take it personally. He just gets like that some days."

Dennis' lip twitched.

"How, uh," he worked up the courage to ask: "How long has he been working here—"

"—Excuse me?" A ghostly pale woman tapped on Dennis' shoulder, grabbing his and Flora's attention.

"How can we help, honey?" Flora leaned into the desk.

"My—" she choked up, wiping tears from her red eyes. "My husband's here. Teddy Miller? I was in the shower when they called the landline. They said he was in a," another choked sob, "serious accident?"

Dennis' heart plummeted.

As quickly as shining Flora Pender had nurtured him back upright like a sunflower, again he wilted.

"Let me see," Flora pulled her clunky glasses up, reaching for the patient log.

"He's in trauma one," Dennis interjected, stopping Flora's search. "He's being intubated right now to prevent any issues with breathing."

That sentence seemed to amp up her distress. She choked back a sob, bottom lip quivering.

Only then did Dennis completely alter his approach, placing a hand on her shoulder. He lowered his voice, soft and comforting. His eyes, though topped with furrowed brows displaying his stress, were grounding. "What's your name?"

"Amy," she managed through quiet sobs, "Amy Miller."

"Okay, Amy," Dennis nodded. "Let's get you over to Teddy."

He cast one more nod at Flora before guiding the pale, weeping wife through the halls, all the while reassuring her and explaining things that she probably hardly knew about. Medical jargon.

"They're putting a breathing tube in his throat so that he stays stable. We've got him on fluids and we're working to get our burn unit down as soon as possible to help figure out our next steps, but..." Dennis' eyes softened, "all in all, I think he'll pull through, Miss Amy."

"You think?" Hope flashed in her eyes like heavenly light.

A feeble smile curled up on the boy's lips as he halted, just in front of the entryway to trauma 1. "I do. And I'm praying for him."

Amy wiped another tear from the flushed bones of her cheeks, a dimpled grin creeping onto her face. "Thank you. Thank you."

He whispered a word of caution to her regarding silence in the room so that her husband could rest, and she acknowledged. Ushering her past the door, she stifled the anguished mewls bubbling up in her throat — well enough, too, that only Robby looked up from the utensil he fidgeted with by the bed. 

"This is Mrs. Miller," Dennis introduced, polite and soft.

"Is he in pain?" She blurted. "Is he..."

"He's heavily sedated, ma'am," Robby nodded, speaking in that tone.

That low, comforting tone that Dennis had known once. Had known intimately.

"Will I get to talk to him again?"

Clearly, and it showed on Dr. Robby's face, there wasn't a solid answer to that.

Amy choked back another sob, creeping over to the edge of the bed and looking down at her burned husband. The man she loved so deeply, laid bare as Death's hungering growls echoed from just above.

"We're hopeful," the attending opted for in place of a definitive yes or no. "He's stable right now, and we're waiting to hear from our burn unit."

Clenching his jaw, Dennis stepped out of the room, offering no glance at Dr. Robby. In the hallway, he leaned his back to the wall next to the trauma 1 door, palming his forehead and letting out a strained sigh.

Robby's clinic in Franklin prepared him well enough for this shift. Had he not been privileged enough to have such prior experience, he might be far worse off on his first shift at Presby.

"Looking for work?" Dr. Mohan's voice cut through the silence.

No, I'd like to sit and process my emotions for a moment, my old mentor and secret male lover works here and I'd like to quit, he wanted to say. "I..." is all that actually comes out.

Dennis looked back through the glass door of trauma 1, eyeing Robby before turning back to Mohan. "I was with a burn victim. Dr. Robby told me to step out."

Dr. Mohan snorted, "Step out, hm? What'd you do?"

"...I'm still figuring that out."

She offered a more genuinely confused expression now, cocking her head.

A brief, awkward silence fell between the two of them as Dennis worked up the courage to ask:

"Dr. Mohan, do you..." he began to trail off. "Do you recognize me?"

Now she really looked confused.

Samira Mohan squinted, eyes flitting away from Dennis for a moment, as if to search elsewhere in her mind for the answer to that question. "Should I?"

Dennis chuckled nervously. "I, uh... I think I helped treat you for a flu at the Robinavitch Health Center in Franklin. Back in '61."

"Huh," she looked genuinely fascinated, but clearly she didn't remember him. "Wait— you worked for Dr. Robby?"

"I did, yes," he nodded. "He didn't... mention me?"

"Well," she crossed her arms, "he didn't speak all that much at the clinic."

Dennis' brows furrowed.

Explaining herself, Dr. Mohan said, "I came in asking for work during med school since it was a short enough commute. Spent about a year and a half working for him before I left for residency."

"No kidding," he breathed.

She nodded with a grin. "Yeah. Once or twice he mentioned teaching someone he swore was a prodigy. But that's quite literally all he ever said about it— he was always a private person. Maybe even more than he is now. But my hunch is," she leaned closer to Dennis, speaking more discretely, "that's probably because he has less to be private about nowadays."

A man with nothing at all has nothing at all to hide, she probably meant.

"I see," Dennis swallowed. "No— I..." and then he chuckled fondly, "that wasn't me. He was teaching another person around that time. A real prodigy."

Dr. Mohan put her hands on her hips, looking around while Dennis' eyes remained fixed on the floor.

He mustered the courage to speak: "If it's not too odd to ask, how did you both end up here—"

"Dr. Langdon," Samira grinned, tugging the senior resident over by his bicep before he could walk any further past them. "Why don't you and Whitaker do a round of triage together?" She turned to Dennis and said, "Could give you some good insight on incoming patient assessment."

The two men shared a look. It was... mutually understood discomfort.

"I'm quite familiar with triage," Dennis chuckled.

With a huff, Langdon said, "Ditto."

Dr. Mohan smiled. "Good! Then it'll be all the more efficient with the both of you."

Doubling down, Langdon protested: "Plus, I've gotta update Mr. Herron's chart."

"Do that," Samira patted the man's back. "Then take Whitaker to triage."

 

 

"Sometimes when it's really busy, we'll help nurses call patients back and thin the herd out here, so to speak," Langdon taught, hands on his hips as he and Dennis eyed the crowded waiting room.

"I did a lot of this back at the clinic," Dennis nodded.

"Ah. The one you mentioned earlier? '62, was it?"

"Yeah."

The two of them stepped into the room, ears bombarded by whining complaints, groans, and shuffling from the ill.

"So you've been practicing medicine for a long time?"

"Well," Dennis chewed inside his lip, "only for as long as I've been at the clinic."

Langdon looked a little confused, but he didn't care to ask further, it seemed. He glanced at his clipboard, and called out a name:

"Elijah Dombrowski?"

A man, perhaps a little older than Robby, stood from his seat eagerly, rubbing his soothing palm into his chest as he said, "That's me."

Dennis and Dr. Langdon walked the man back into an exam room with nurse Jesse, all the while sympathetically explaining to the patient why he'd been sitting in the waiting room for hours.

"There's just been a pressure in my chest. Since this morning. It's like someone's fuckin', I dunno, sitting on me," he explained, laying as Dennis listened at bedside.

"Vitals are good," Dennis nodded at Langdon, who watched from across the room with crossed arms. "BP's a little high."

"Sir, do you do a lot of smoking?" Langdon asked, and the patient nodded shamelessly.

Dennis put his stethoscope to use quickly, pressing the butt-end into Elijah's chest gently and listening close. After a quick assessment, Dennis confidently stood upright and fired off his observations to Langdon, who nodded the whole while, impressed by the med student's skill.

"Good," Dr. Langdon nodded. "Let's get Mr. Dombrowski on oxygen, and push morphine carefully. Order an EKG."

They did so quickly, and minutes later, Langdon was reading the results of the EKG. Nodding with his lips curled downward, the senior resident walked over to bedside and explained, "Mr. Dombrowski, you're jumping the line today, bud. We're admitting you to CCU. They've got telemetry and they'll do a hell of a lot better keeping an eye on you than anyone down here will."

Fear washed into the patient's eyes. "What's that mean?"

Soothingly, Dennis spoke up: "We're sending you to our coronary care unit. They've got the technology and expertise to manage your symptoms and help assess if this might be a heart attack. If it is," he assured confidently, "you're in the safest place you can be."

And so, they admitted him.

"Thinning the herd," Langdon nodded proudly, nudging Dennis' forearm as they sauntered back toward the waiting room. "You're a natural at this, Whitaker."

"Oh, not really. I just, y'know— the guy looked worried. I told him what I'd have wanted to hear."

"You've got the same kind of energy about you as Dr. Robby." Langdon stated it with good intention, but Dennis wished he hadn't heard that.

Because he taught me long before he knew anyone here, he wanted to say.

"Not sure what that means," he opted for instead, feigning ignorance.

"I forget you're new," Langdon chuckled. "So you don't know him yet. He's the best of the best down here in the ED."

"I bet."

"I've been working with him for about three years now. He taught me every good thing I know about working in an environment like this. In the chaos."

"...You two seem close."

Dennis shouldn't have allowed himself to say that. He shouldn't have cared.

Dr. Langdon mused, "Yeah, I guess we are. Well— as close as one can be with a homebody like him. It's more of a 'work-only' kind of friendship, y'know?"

"He doesn't socialize much?"

"Hell no," Dr. Langdon nearly cackled. "No. Robby? No. He's about as closed off as a man can get. And that's saying something."

Don't I know it, Dennis wanted to respond. Instead, he said, "I get that impression."

"Everyone does. I think even Samira does."

"Dr. Mohan?"

"Yeah. I just mean, of all the people he'd be open with, I thought it'd be her. Y'know, for a while, a lot of us thought they were a thing. He referred her to Presby for senior residency in... I think... 1970?"

Dennis' brows knitted tightly like frazzled bowstrings. "Are they? A thing?"

Langdon slapped Dennis' shoulder with a teasing smirk. "No, God no." Then, he leaned closer, speaking more discretely: "I love the guy to death, but I'd feel awful for any woman cozying up to Robby."

At some point, they'd stopped walking. In the doorframe between the ED's main room and the waiting room, Langdon and Dennis dove - unnecessarily - into gossip about their chief attending. Dennis couldn't help it. Langdon was a yapper.

"Why's that?" The boy asked, almost... defensively.

Dr. Langdon shrugged. "He's just a troubled guy."

"Troubled?"

A look settled onto the senior resident's face. A look like he knew he shouldn't have said anything, but wanted to continue. He let out a long, dragged sigh, rubbing at the stubble on his chin while looking down at Dennis, who stood only a little taller than the man's clavicles.

"Listen," Dr, Langdon began.

Nothing good ever began that way.

"I'm a real big fan of Robby's. And again, I love the dude like family. But he's got the tell of a man who doesn't really care when God comes knocking. Know what I mean?"

"...I guess?" Dennis squinted.

"He's never been the happiest guy, don't get me wrong. But I feel like... as years've gone by, he gets worse and worse. More isolated. More... cranky? I dunno if that's the best way to put it, but—"

"—Don't beat around the bush, Frank," Princess walked up, forcing a violent flinch from Dennis. As she nudged the senior resident, she looked at Dennis and said, "Sorry."

"I'm not beating around the bush," Langdon defended half-heartedly.

Princess huffed, casting a dissatisfied look at Dennis. "He's protecting his idol."

"He's not my idol—"

"I'm just psychin' you!" She laughed, slapping Langdon's bicep twice. When she reeled herself back in, she looked back to Dennis, more serious, and said, "But Langdon really is beating around the bush."

Dennis looked back and forth at the two of them. "What do you mean?"

Langdon, very clearly, wasn't keen on saying more.

Whispering her gossip, Princess spoke in the man's stead: "It's gotten so bad with Robby that even Abbot's about ready to jump on his case. That's what I heard."

"Who..." Dennis squinted. "Who's Abbot? And why?"

"Robby's reckless with himself, apparently. Even worse: he pretends not to be. One of our night shift attendings, Dr. Abbott, saw him riding in on his motorcycle for the morning shift from the rooftop a few weeks ago and hasn't... really let it go since."

As Princess spoke, Langdon began looking more and more uncomfortable. Clearly he wanted no part in the gossip, especially not about the attending he looked up to like God. Dennis would've once said the same thing, a long time ago. Now, though, all he wanted was to drink in every corner of Robby's life, convincing himself it was for any reason but the one that scared him the most:

Limerence.

"...What'd he see from the rooftop?" Dennis could not stop himself from asking.

"He wasn't wearing a helmet," Princess gossiped. "Then he walked in for his shift, helmet on his arm, trying to act all natural— like he wasn't being reckless. Nobody loved the idea of him getting into motorcycles anyway, just 'cause... well, y'know. But everyone thought he was at least being safe about it. Abbott was unsettled."

"This building's pretty tall, maybe he wasn't seeing the guy right," Langdon tried to diffuse the gossip, sighing. His discomfort had begun to bubble over. "Any physician knows better than to do that. Robby knows better."

"I dunno," Princess shrugged, pursing her lips. "That's just what I've heard."

Dennis and Dr. Langdon spent another hour back and forth thinning the herd in the waiting room. All the while, the boy processed and digested what few bits of the puzzle he'd acquired from his conversation with Princess. He could've prodded Langdon for more, but didn't. Couldn't bring himself to learn more about whatever dark spirits might or might not have been circling above Robby's head.

I shouldn't care, is what he continued to remind himself. Until the hour was up.

By the end of it, he and Langdon had hardly dented the crowd in the waiting room, despite having examined, admitted, and sent home almost a dozen patients total. Each one they'd pick out was replaced, mere minutes - and sometimes seconds - later, another new patient filling the empty seat and leaving Presby's doctors one step closer to graying hairs and wrinkled skin.

"Princess," Langdon called the nurse over, putting an arm on Dennis' shoulder. "I need to speak with you about a patient— Whitaker, head back to Flora and see where you're needed, alright?"

Dennis nodded timidly, "Will do."

"Hey," Langdon punched the boy's bicep playfully, "you did great out here. You're a fucking natural."

With a shy smile, Dennis accepted the compliment, nodding to Princess, who also smiled at him. He felt a little buzz in his heart; the joy of success couldn't be weighed down by his frustratingly persistent inability to keep Robby out of his head. He'd found a tempo that worked for him, and that meant something.

Dennis Whitaker was built to withstand the horror of the Presby ED.

As he sauntered back into the main hall, Dennis caught sight of Robby, who stood at the edge of the nurse station, scribbling something down. The boy picked up pace, hustling over, brows knitting, mind devolving once more. His moment of pride had melted away, replaced again by his frustration.

"Dr. Robby," he asked, more meekly than he'd have liked.

"What can I do for you, Whitaker?" The older man spoke without looking away from the paper he scribbled on, dark, sweet voice trying - and almost succeeding - to disarm Dennis.

"Can we talk for a second?"

"I don't have a second," he mumbled. "Neither do you," he added. But then he looked up from his paper, meeting the boy's eyes and saying, "What do you want to talk about?"

Dennis gulped.

"Why did you toss me out?"

Robby knew what he meant. But still, he played dumb: "I'm not sure what you..."

"Teddy. Why did you tell me to step out?"

"Sharp tone, Whitaker," Robby cocked his head.

Remembering that the man was his superior, he backed off very quickly, devolving into that weak little voice he hated so much: "...Sorry."

Robby did not relish seeing Dennis shrink like that. "You acted like you've never seen a burn patient before."

"We rarely ever had burn patients in Franklin—"

"—I expect more from you of all people. I taught you better than that," Robby lowered his voice, but it remained stern. "And you're gonna see a hell of a lot more cases like these before today's over. This isn't Franklin, Whitaker. This isn't a makeshift clinic where we can send tough cases off to the big hospital and bestow our 'hopes' and our 'prayers'."

Dennis gulped down the lump in his throat, willing his body not to shake.

"We are the big hospital now. We are their hopes and their prayers."

The boy nodded in understanding, eyes locked like chains onto Robby's own. Clearly, a glint of pain - or some such feeling - had flashed inside his pupils, because it visibly disarmed Robby almost as quickly as it washed through Dennis. The older man untensed his face and his eyes. His entire demeanor changed, and then:

He placed his warm, large hand on Dennis' shoulder.

That physical contact that had once served to ground him from even the most unbearable heights. That hand which he'd known so intimately for years— it finally reached him once again.

Limerence, Dennis cursed in his mind.

"Listen to me, Whitaker," Robby cooed, even lower than a whisper, but cutting through the noise of the hospital like a blade. "Teddy's odds aren't great. I don't want you to attach yourself to something like this first thing in the morning. I do not want the guilt to crash down on you if he..." doesn't survive.

Dennis sighed. In his softest whisper, he declared, "...You can't do that to me."

"I'm your attending. I certainly can."

"You can't do that to Teddy," the boy changed course, voice raising a little.

"What— you think you alone are the deciding factor whether he lives or not? You think you can facilitate miracles, kid?"

Realizing his audacity, Dennis backed down, shaking his head.

"You did your job. And everyone in there is doing theirs just as well. But sometimes... everything we can do just isn't enough. I've handled cases like these. They can go south really fucking fast. And I just..." he sighed, squeezing Dennis' shoulder protectively, "I don't want you near that yet."

The man's firm hand, for the first time in Dennis Whitaker's life, did not feel grounding. It felt patronizing.

"...You haven't changed much, huh," the boy looked up, defiantly, into his attending's eyes. "Coddling me like I'm made of glass."

Dennis pulled himself back, then turned and walked away before Robby could protest in any way. The older man stood there, perplexed, watching as his med student stormed down the hall, disappearing around a corner. He found himself unable to look away.

Limerence.

 

 

Two more patients and one pair of scrubs later, Dennis stepped out of the makeshift changing room, adjusting the ill-fitted, black clothes as he walked. He nearly bumped into Dr. McKay as he turned the corner, not looking where he was going.

"Excuse me," Dennis offered apologetically.

"Change of clothes?" McKay eyed him up and down, suppressing a grin.

Awkwardly, he nodded. "I... got peed on."

"Oh," she said, like that was a normal thing. "A warm welcome to The Pitt."

Dennis' nose wrinkled.

"I'm— that was a joke, sorry," McKay finally chuckled, guiding Dennis to walk down the hall with her.

"Oh," he huffed awkwardly, mustering a smile. "Of course."

"Inappropriate," she sighed. "Well, aside from the scrub change, how's Presby treating you so far?"

"Uhhh," he dragged.

"Oo," McKay grimaced, nodding like she just knew. "Rough day?"

"A little," he admitted. "But I've learned a lot. It's had ups and downs."

"Naturally. Say—" she turned while they walked, eyeing the boy, "why don't you help me clear Jared Dever?"

"The drunk guy?"

"Yeah! He's sobered up now. It'd give you a little break from the chaos. We could all use one from time to time."

From far across the hall, Dennis caught sight of Robby behind the doors of triage 1, consulting doctors on a case that Dennis deserved to sit in on. It stirred frustration in him, but he forced himself to let it go.

"...Sounds good to me," he responded. "Yeah."

And so Dennis Whitaker had his break from chaos. McKay stood in as he examined Jared Dever, who'd become far more cooperative than he was in the morning. Dennis enjoyed it, even— chatting casually with the man, whose life had led him to this point. A point where drinks became the cure to all issues. 

For Dennis, it was a means of showcasing - to himself, privately - what he could not afford to become. But sympathy still allowed his heart to ache as he listened to Mr. Dever's story. Listened to all the ways in which the man had ruined his life, his relationships, by giving in to the urges which itched at those who suffered arduous lives.

Dennis Whitaker was one such man.

Up to this point, the only urge which itched at him was work. But even work was simply a means to prove to himself - and to the ghost of the man he once loved - that he was useful for something.

Worth was everything, by this point.

If Thane, his very own father, could not find worth in him, and if Franklin, Tennessee could not find worth in him, then Dennis Whitaker would find worth in himself. Through ten years of unrelenting work in the wake of his father's death and during the most uncertain times of his life, Dennis succumbed to his own urges. Sure, it wasn't drinking— he was not Jared Dever in shared addiction. But he was Jared Dever in conscious self-destruction.

That made it all the more beautiful, understanding the man, even if only in pain.

"I think you've got something special, Whitaker," McKay praised tenderly, standing next to him as they watched Mr. Dever walk off toward the hospital exit.

"Hm?"

"You have a kind of... empathy," she smiled, "seems like it touched Mr. Dever. Empathy makes for a good doctor."

"I'm not so sure it was empathy," he shook his head. "Just... a bit of understanding through similar experience."

McKay faced Dennis. "You were a drinker?"

"No— no. God, no," he chuckled, waving his hands defensively. "I just meant— I've been where he's been. In pain. Unsure where he was headed."

"Well," she searched for the words, "you've both still got your whole lives ahead of you."

"Don't you?" Dennis raised an eyebrow as they meandered back toward the main hall.

"Ugh. I'm almost 43. The only life ahead of me now is my son." She added quickly: "Which I'm perfectly happy with. But, I get what you meant. About... being so uncertain."

Dennis eyed her, expecting an explanation.

"I, uh..." she began, tentatively, rubbing her palms together stiffly, "I was an addict. In my late twenties. It didn't help that my ex-husband was a prick. Total prick. But I lost custody of my son in the middle of all of it. Now I fight every day for him. Saving lives is important, of course, but part of this drive..." she tapped her chest, gesturing at her heart.

McKay recited, "Part of it's to prove to myself I deserve his love."

"I'm sure you're a great mother," Dennis comforted.

"Oh, I'm a damn good mother," McKay snorted. "A damn good mother paying for a lot of poor choices."

As they rounded the corner walking toward the nurse station, their conversation became stagnant as Dr. Robby's voice became clearer and clearer. Dennis watched as the attending stood, arms crossed in agitation as he spoke to an expensive-looking man next to him.

McKay and Dennis eavesdropped on the gradually heating conversation.

"—We need more funding if you want those results. How many times have I asked for your help voicing the importance of emergency medicine? And how many times have you shot me down?"

"It's too much of a risk, Michael. The investments in such a development are too high not to have some major plan together, and you're only one man. It's pretentious. I'm sorry."

"I'm not one man, I'm this whole ED. I'm every doctor and every nurse in here who works their ass off day and night to save lives using tools from a system that simply cannot keep up with the demands of the people. We need a better system. The times are evolving, and modern medicine isn't keeping up."

"I hear your concerns—"

"And it isn't fair that Presby has to be the big ol' test monkey for it all with no safety nets to ensure our patients get proper care. To ensure we stay safe. I know it's 'pretentious' to expect immediate change, to— to expect you to wave a wand and create a system that suits us from thin air. But you're not even trying, Jeffery."

Jeffery's nose was raised high, chest puffed condescendingly. "Frankly, Michael, it's such an outlandish idea, anyway... this discussion, though better tabled, shouldn't ever be happening anyway. We're wasting time better spent discussing your department's metrics."

"...Metrics," Robby seethed.

Jefferey shrugged in his pantsuit. "At the end of the day, it's all about the money for my friends up top. You bump up your metrics, I'm more likely to take your concerns seriously. You let those metrics continue to fall," he warned, "I'm more likely to replace you."

"If you want better metrics, hire more nurses. Invest in more beds, and more rooms. Train more staff to keep up with our constantly increasing intake of patients. And for God's sake, pay them a livable wage!"

"It's a budgeting thing, my friend," Jeffery spoke with agitating nonchalance.

"My ass," Robby growled. "My doctors pull your slack every day with what few scraps you give. Give us something to work with."

"I can only give what I get."

"Fucking hell!" Robby yelled, far louder than he meant to.

McKay turned back to Dennis, eyebrows raised high.

"Who is that?" Dennis whispered.

"Jeffery Lowe," McKay whispered back. "Presby's CMO."

"Oh..."

"Yeah," McKay nodded with a facetious smile. "This is an everyday thing between the two of them. They'll leave each other alone."

From trauma 1, where Teddy still lay, Trinity walked out, sauntering toward Dennis and McKay until she was close enough to butt into the conversation with a chuckle, "Is that the dude Dr. Robby's been screaming at?"

McKay hummed in confirmation.

"Heard from Princess and Perlah he was a troubled guy. Guess I see it, now."

"He's fighting for us," Dennis defended softly, not turning to Trinity, eyes still boring holes in the side of Robby's head from across the hall.

"Ah," Trinity nodded indifferently. She then turned to McKay. "Got any fun cases I can help with?"

"No, but," she pointed at the chart on the desk, "we can find one, hm?"

Trinity wedged herself between Dennis and McKay. He watched as nurse Flora rolled over in her chair and set down another chart. "You've got your pick of the litter, ladies."

McKay rested her index finger on a name. "How about Mr. Luca O'Moore."

Trinity leaned in, reading the chart. "Multiple rib fractures, shortness of breath, pain in side, but vitals are stable with no signs of pneumothorax or internal bleeding— that's kinda boring, no?"

"I'm so sorry, Dr. Santos," McKay remarked sarcastically. "C'mon. Let's go."

Trinity groaned. McKay was already walking away, but Dr. Robby, finally having finished his confrontation with the CMO, walked over and leaned himself against the desk.

"Still moaning and groaning about saving lives, Dr. Santos?"

"No sir," she forced a smile at the chief attending, offering Dennis an annoyed glance and then strutting off behind McKay.

Robby turned to Dennis, crossing his arms and casually asking, "What's her bag?"

"She's a character," Dennis responded, like that alone was enough elaboration.

"You two've got the same address."

Robby knew he shouldn't have prodded about that. Then again, he knew a great many things he shouldn't do and still did.

"You're spying on me?" Dennis did not face the attending, but they leaned against the desk together, almost hip-to-hip.

"It was in your student file," he corrected softly.

"That why you weren't shocked to see me this morning?"

"I was shocked to see you."

"But you knew I was coming," Dennis surmised.

"Didn't make it any less shocking to see your face."

"Why, 'cause I got older?"

"Don't talk to me about old," Robby almost chuckled.

Betraying his attempt to remain guarded, Dennis quipped, "51 doesn't look so bad on you."

"Gee, thanks," the attending snorted.

Dennis might've continued loosening up, if not for Amy walking out of trauma 1 and shuffling over to the two of them. They watched as she did, and when she stopped just a foot in front of them, she wiped her eyes, clutching her purse as she spoke:

"Thank you... so much. For everything you're doing for my Teddy."

Professional mask securely on his face once more, Dr. Robby nodded. "Of course. And we'll continue doing everything we can."

She nodded with a grateful smile on her lips. "I need to go pick up our son. I can't afford to keep our nanny working any longer. Will, um..." she struggled to find the strength to ask, "will he be alright? While I'm gone? It'll only be thirty minutes."

Dr. Robby could not immediately find the right words, and that silence was a little daunting for Amy.

"The burn unit still hasn't arrived," he spoke honestly, and the fear in Amy's eyes began to return.

Before it could get any worse, Dennis chimed in: "But he's stable. He came to Presby in pretty bad shape, but the first step - our first step - is always to get the patient stable, and we were able to do that for Teddy."

He nodded as he spoke, and funny enough, it seemed to lure Amy to nod as well. Emotion connected the two of them, and she seemed to hear his emotionally charged response far more clearly than she heard Dr. Robby's honest and concerned response.

"Thank you," she whispered in a quiver, through the lump in her throat. "Are you from the city?"

The question was unexpected. Dennis blinked.

"Uh," he stammered, "no. I'm not."

A pitiful little smile crept onto Amy's pale face. "I could hear it in your voice. It's faint, but I can hear that twang." Before she made herself sound any more peculiar, she elaborated, "Teddy and I are from rural Pennsylvania. Even now, he still works on a farm, so we keep in touch with our roots."

"That's nice," Dennis smiled, fidgeting with his fingers over his stomach. "Yeah, my, uh... the twang used to be a lot more prevalent."

A fond grin betrayed Robby's lips.

"But I still think of home all the time."

"If you ever miss it," Amy spoke softly, "if you ever miss home... you should come visit us sometime. Teddy's a great cook."

There was a kind of sincerity in her voice that tugged not only at Dennis' heart, but even Robby's. As she said her goodbyes and walked away, the two of them stood in silence for a moment, hearing all of the unsaid words between them as they were bombarded with memories of Tennessee.

Of what was once their home.

"...You handled that very well," Robby praised, voice almost hoarse.

Dennis shrugged. "Yeah."

After another pause of contemplative silence, Robby decided to lean and whisper in a mumble: "I'm sorry."

Dennis turned to face the older man.

"I apologize for coddling you," he nodded. "You deserved to be in that room helping save Teddy's life."

"...Thank you," the boy offered tentatively.

"Why don't we, uh... go give Teddy a follow-up? I'll have you watch how I do it. Could be good for experience."
