Dennis swallowed, trying to suppress the smile on his face as he mumbled, "I'd like that."

"Have you got something to write on?"

Dennis fished his little notepad and pen from the pocket of his ill-fitted scrubs, waving it for Robby to see.

"Alright. Let's go."

So, they walked back into trauma 1 where Teddy rested. Having been apologized to and invited back in, a kind of warm light shone on Dennis' chest like it was a new day. Suddenly the stress of it all didn't matter as much, especially not with Robby at the helm of the decision. Michael Robinavitch, his own source of sorrow for ten years, had just given him a little bit of hope.

How strange.

"We're going to observe Teddy, is all this really is," Dr. Robby taught. "He's stable now, of course. But a burn patient can change very, very fast."

That little hope morphed quickly into a swell of anxious buzz. "Will it... change?" Dennis asked nervously. "I just told Amy—"

Immediately sensing his nerves, Robby interrupted, "If it does, you need to focus. You're watching for his breathing and alertness. The silent danger with these kinds of patients is swelling in the airway— it can always turn bad fast if you're not monitoring, and we're in real shit because our burn unit's already tied down to hell. We're all this guy's got until they can take him."

Dennis nodded, trying to force down the nerves.

When they checked him, Teddy was fine. Oxygen saturation was normal, as well as pulse and respiratory rate. Robby had Dennis jot down a bunch of notes about monitoring and what to do in cases like these, where specialist departments were too tied down to be of use for prolonged periods.

But just as they left the room, Teddy's nurse barreled back out, telling Dr. Robby he needed to return.

"Patient's in respiratory distress," Robby assessed as Dennis observed in fear. "Whitaker, I need you over here now."

Dennis obeyed, walking up to bedside opposite Robby and awaiting further instruction.

"He needs an escharotomy," the attending nodded, looking up at Dennis, who clearly dreaded that term.

"Oh God," he whined. "I can't— I've never done one!"

"That's what I'm here for, kid," Robby reminded sternly. "The ventilator is struggling to get him air because of all of these full-thickness burns. You need to cut at the burned skin and release his chest."

"Dr. Robby, I—" 

"Whitaker, if I didn't think you could do it, you wouldn't be here right now."

Dennis swallowed the impossibly hard lump in his throat, teeth bared as he nodded.

Robby grabbed a sterile scalpel, walking it over to Dennis carefully and guiding the boy's hands as they prepared the incisions. His own large hand covered Dennis', and he hovered behind while watching intensely at each motion of the blade. The fried skin very visibly released pressure with each inch severed, and once they'd finally connected both lines on the sides with a final horizontal line over the chest itself, Dennis stepped back, letting go of the breath he'd been holding in his lungs.

The nurse looked at Robby, nodding as she called out "Stabilizing," and he patted Dennis on the back.

"Look at you," he whispered, trying to calm the boy. "Look at you."

"Is he going to be alright? Did I—"

"You did fantastic, Whitaker."

They stayed in the room for several minutes. The nurse was in and out, but since the burn unit still hadn't arrived, and since Dennis had now inextricably tied himself to the case, Robby felt unable to leave until he knew Teddy would be handed off safely. It was fascinating to him, how quickly that mindset had stuck to him. Caring for the patient as though the patient were Dennis.

In a way, he was.

"You did very good," Dr. Robby praised again idly as he checked the machines and examined Teddy with his eyes. "This is just a waiting game now, until that fucking burn specialist shows up."

"Is this normal?" Dennis asked, a bead of sweat hanging at his brow.

"The waiting? Unfortunately. The ED is always the first point of contact for a patient, but even the specialized departments get full quickly. Teddy's just been a bit unlucky today. Just..." Robby began trailing off as something caught his eye, "a bit..."

The blood pressure machine was showing a sudden and dramatic drop.

Dennis' heart began to race again, brows knitting together as he watched Dr. Robby scurry frantically from the BP machine to Teddy's bed, stethoscope pressed above the heart. A look settled onto his face, like he knew what was coming. A look of dread.

Robby knew if Teddy died in here, especially now, Dennis would've carried all of that blame himself.

"Attempting resuscitation," Robby called out, immediately beginning chest compressions.

"But— he was fine. I thought I..." Dennis walked over frantically. 

"I don't know, Whitaker," Robby admitted while pushing up and down. "Your cuts were fine. It's something internal we missed."

"Won't he die with that kind of force?" Dennis blurted, watching as Robby pushed intensely on the man's chest with each pump.

"He's dying regardless," the man gritted through clenched teeth, struggling to effectively perform CPR over the burned, inflexible skin of Teddy's chest. "Get some extra hands in here. Now!"

Frantic, as though Teddy were his own blood, Dennis tripped over to the door, peeking his head out and yelling, "We need an extra set of hands in trauma one! Now!"

Dr. Mel and Dr. Langdon rushed over together, barreling into the room with Princess following just behind, trying to assist resuscitation efforts in every way possible. Each of Robby's compressions further stressed the incisions Dennis had made on Teddy's chest.

Each push felt like hammering one more nail into the man's coffin.

Perhaps Robby was doing it on purpose—perhaps he was doing it to shoulder the burden of the loss, taking it from Dennis.

In any case, he needed to do it. Even on a patient as burned and weakened as Teddy, because, "Weak cardiac output is better than none," Dr. Langdon recited as they watched.

Minutes went by. Everyone in the room scurried and obeyed each order directed by Dr. Robby as he continued compressions, but it was all to no avail. After pushing the limits of what was allowed for a patient, Robby finally gave in, stepping away and watching as Dr. Mel checked for a pulse. When she stood upright again, shaking her head at Robby, the man's face fell, skin going pale with guilt. Dennis simply watched in horror, imagining what Amy was going to say when she returned to find that her husband had—

"Time of death..." Robby panted, jaw clenched. "15:24."

 

 

Dennis decided it was the second worst day of his life.

As he vomited, his head hung low in the toilet bowl, knees pressed uncomfortably into the hard bathroom floor as his wretches echoed. He clutched at the sides of the toilet with white knuckles, mumbling with wetted lips.

"Please forgive me, God," he tried. "Please forgive me, God," he tried again, and again.

Over and over.

He only stopped when he heard the bathroom door open outside the stall. No longer alone to beg for forgiveness for - in his mind - being at fault for Teddy's death, Dennis just sat there. Knees on the ground, head lurched in the toilet bowl, trembling in silence. He only dragged himself to his feet because whoever had entered the bathroom was making no noise; it made him think they might be watching him, or perhaps wondering if he'd died on the toilet.

No more humiliation necessary, he decided, wiping his lips and flushing the toilet.

Dennis unlatched the lock to the stall and swung the door open. Robby, who leaned against the wall with his arms crossed, looked up from the ground, worry in his eyes.

"I'm alright," Dennis spoke first, before the older man could even ask.

The look in Robby's eyes made it clear he didn't believe him. Dennis walked past Robby and toward the sink, turning on the water and letting the noise drown out the quiet shuddering gasps in his throat.

"Dennis."

Michael Robinavitch's voice was supernaturally disarming.

Hearing his name in that dark, sweet, comforting tone loosened every emotion hiding behind the dam in his eyes. Turning the sink off, Dennis leaned into it, choked sob echoing through the small bathroom.

"I killed him," he gasped for air.

"You did no such thing."

Robby was already behind the med student, turning him around so his back was pressed into the sink, face burying in Robby's chest. He wrapped his arms around Dennis, feeling the tug on his scrubs as the younger man clung desperately, breathing heavy into the fabric. They stood like that for a minute. Robby rubbed wherever he could feel, trying to calm Dennis' beating heart which he could almost feel against his own chest.

"Shhh," Robby reached an arm up, scratching the back of Dennis' head soothingly and rocking him.

"He died because I..."

"He died in cardiac arrest," Robby corrected softly, pressing his warm fingers into Dennis' scalp. "You kept him alive. You did everything you could."

"He went into cardiac arrest because of me!" Dennis pushed away, brushing past Robby, who stumbled back a little. "I did it wrong—"

"Cardiac arrest wouldn't relate to the escharotomy and you know it, Whitaker. We missed something in the initial exam. Something internal. He was bound to die long before you got in there. It had," he stressed the last few words with a pointed finger, "nothing to do with you."

Dennis stopped at the door, hand on the knob.

"Don't let this turn you into a pillar of salt," Robby implored.

The boy looked back, but he didn't respond. Only looked at Robby, into his eyes. The older man did not move, mindful of Dennis' bubble. He didn't want him to feel trapped.

"I'm alright," Dennis assured less convincingly this time.

After taking another moment to fully compose himself, he swung open the door to the bathroom and stepped out, nearly bumping into Trinity Santos.

"Woah," she huffed, but froze when she looked up and saw Robby standing awkwardly in the bathroom. The bathroom that Dennis just walked out of.

"Sorry," Dennis mumbled, equally stunned. "Can we— can we walk?"

"You two all good in there?" She joked with a cocked eyebrow.

A warm flush buzzed the tips of Dennis' ears. He glanced behind him at Robby just as the bathroom door finished gliding closed, then looked back at Trinity. The look on her face nearly made his own burn beet red.

"What?" He stammered. "Are you insane?"

"I'm kidding," she laughed as they began walking down the hall toward the nurse station. "That would be really unethical." Then she pursed her lips and frowned. "Then again, I guess I can't say much; that Dr. Garcia lady's been eyeing me up and down all afternoon like she wants me for breakfast." She nudged Dennis. "Even let me do a fasciotomy."

Dennis nearly tripped hearing that, noting her flirtatious tone.

"You are insane."

"Oh please. My secret couldn't be any safer than with another queer."

"I'm not a queer," he reminded her—for the thousandth time that day, it seemed.

In a particularly playful mood, Trinity hummed, "Then I don't feel so safe with you staying another night in my apartment."

Dennis offered a defeated look, eye twitching as they continued walking. "I'm just..." he struggled, "...open-minded."

"An open-minded homosexual?"

Dennis grumbled, "Y'know what? Whatever helps you sleep."

"Transparency helps me sleep, Huckleberry," she beamed as the boy picked up pace, leaving her behind.

Standing still for a moment, Trinity wondered if, perhaps, she'd gone a little far that time. She knew pretty well that Dennis wasn't confiding in her, and it shouldn't have agitated her, but in a way, it did. She gave him a roof over his head. In her mind, it made sense that he should be able to trust her enough to share a secret that she'd shared with—

"—What you got there, kid?" Flora snuck up behind Trinity, gesturing at the paper in her hands as she whipped her head around to face the woman.

"Wha- oh." Trinity chuckled, waving the paper. "Just a prescription form. It's for one of my patients. Actually, I was looking for you. I, uh..." she contemplated asking something.

Flora raised an eyebrow.

Trinity licked her lips and cocked her head, "Do you happen to have a log of which prescriptions in our inventory are signed out? And... by who?"

"Of course I do. Why?"

"I just wanted to check on an irregularity with one of my patients. He visited earlier this morning. Got a full prescription, but came back hours later with only half the pills."

 

 

Amy returned to the hospital - with her little son trotting by her side - only five minutes later than she'd promised. Dennis nearly bumped into her on his way to the nurse station. His inability to keep himself composed was, pretty immediately, very alarming to Amy.

It was clear that something had happened to Teddy.

Dennis didn't need to say anything, but he did anyway. Through shaky breaths and a meek, trembling voice, he explained that Teddy had died mere minutes ago. Amy sobbed and her knees slammed onto the bright hospital floor as she clutched at her little boy.

"You said he was stable!" She wailed, which especially gutted Dennis.

Because he did say that. And perhaps he shouldn't have.

He didn't have it in him to tell her the details after that. Couldn't bring himself to verbalize his concern that he was to blame for the man's death, regardless of what Robby believed. And Dennis knew Robby was probably right—Teddy's death probably had very little to do with him given the circumstances. 

But the guilt fell heavy upon his chest anyway.

Amy spent a solid minute in guttural anguish on the floor as Dennis awkwardly tried calming her—and himself. Eventually, Dr. Mohan rushed over, helping to calm Teddy's grieving wife and unsettled toddler. She was good at handling the little boy, but only because she'd already gone through the pain of losing her own father early in life. She knew what kind of shock he was in. Knew how to navigate that maze.

When they calmed Amy down enough, Mel came over and offered to take her son to the cafeteria. Dennis and Samira walked her into trauma one to view her husband, who'd now been covered with a plain, white sheet.

A choked sob escaped Amy's throat when her eyes landed upon her covered husband.

"This isn't real," she whined. "He was just fine this morning before work. We kissed and said I love you and— and..." she trailed off, gasping for air.

Samira rubbed her back while Dennis walked over to the edge of the bed, haunted by the body that he'd failed to keep alive. He pinched the edge of the sheet, peeling it down only just enough to uncover Teddy's face, leaving everything else unseen. It was better that way.

Seeing her husband's face only drew more cries from Amy. It only served to further stir the raging pot of anguish in her chest, and it killed Dennis to see that.

"Excuse me," he apologized, covering his mouth and sprinting past Dr. Mohan, leaving the room before she could say anything.

Once again, Dennis found himself in the bathroom on his knees. This time he dry-heaved into the toilet bowl. His body gave its involuntary reactions, but he forced himself - with a deep, crunching bite into his cheek - to hold back the tears. There would be no more tears. He could not allow himself to give into such emotions this far into his career. Not after everything he'd accomplished.

This time, Robby was not waiting to comfort him outside the stall.

This time, Dennis wished he was.

 

 

The evening hours of Dennis Whitaker's shift did not have any mercy on him. Not because of the shift itself, but because of the guilt which sat against his snapping ribs, forcing the air from his lungs. Forcing the life from his already tired, baggy eyes. Forcing the color from his pale, pasty skin and his chapped lips.

As much as he hated to admit it, too, he might've just walked out and never looked back, had it not been for Samira Mohan.

"Mr. Whitaker," she spoke softly as they exited a patient's room. "You've been awfully quiet the last three hours."

Dennis looked up at her as they walked. "I think I've been fairly quiet since this morning, no?"

"Quieter than normal."

Robby walked past them with Mel at his side. He and Dennis exchanged a glance. Samira and Dennis kept walking the opposite way.

"I've just been processing a lot of things today."

Samira nudged him. "This is a big step up from the clinic."

Oh, right. She would know, he reminded himself.

"Bigger than I hoped it would be, I think." Dennis stopped walking, then so did Dr. Mohan. With a kind of vulnerability in his eyes, he looked up and asked, "Does it ever get easier?"

Samira cocked an eyebrow.

"Losing a patient," Dennis clarified.

"Ah," she nodded once. "It... well, no."

The boy sat in that response for a moment, wishing it'd been a different answer.

"But I don't know that it's supposed to."

Dennis looked up at the woman.

Samira continued: "What we do is difficult work, not just based on our own abilities, but also by virtue of our humanity. The majority of us are doctors because we care about saving lives. Of course it'll hurt when we fail to do so. But if you're a doctor - especially in a place as crazy as The Pitt - you're going to fail sometimes."

"I'm afraid of that," Dennis nodded, like he was coming to that realization for the first time. "I wanted Teddy to make it. Not just for my sake... for hers. And if I couldn't save him, then why—"

"—Mr. Whitaker, we don't take patients with a certainty that we'll save them. We take them because they need us."

Dennis opened his mouth, but words did not come out.

"I'll make the assumption you're not a doctor because you expected to save every single life you came across for the next forty or fifty years. And it isn't supposed to be easy when it happens. You're human, and humanness is a blade without a handle. It cuts you while you carve your path."

"Can I drop the blade?" Dennis chuckled, but there was no humor in it.

Samira crossed her arms. "Then how will you carve forward?"

They paused for a moment.

Before Dennis could respond any more, lest he continue combating his willingness to help in the hospital, Dr. Mohan cleared her throat and guided him two steps forward to the patient board.

"Let's pick up a patient together."

And so, Dennis tentatively agreed.

But Samira seemed, unfortunately, to have given him no choice but to settle for a simple case. A lower risk patient who certainly wouldn't leave Dennis with any more scars to bring home that night. The decision felt almost patronizing, but he did not protest. They walked into the room together, chart in Dennis' hand as he introduced himself and Dr. Mohan.

"Mr. Jean Torres, severe hand injury from work equipment. Bleeding controlled in triage. Patient is awake, of course. Any other injuries we aren't aware of, Mr. Torres?"

"No," the oddly familiar man shook his head politely.

Dennis tried his best not to stare, but he couldn't shake the familiarity.

"Any, uh..." he trailed, "dizziness or fainting?"

"No, sir."

"Time since injury?"

"About two hours. We were moving boxes for inventory. At my job."

Samira stood back, watching with a smile on her face as Dennis took over the case like a natural, almost unenthusiastically—like he'd done this a million times today. 

"Tetanus status?"

"I've got my shots," Jean nodded.

Dennis spent a second confirming other information. When that was done, he walked over and removed the dressing to find that the skin integrity was good. There was no obvious tendon laceration, no exposed bone, no vascular compromise, no severe crush or avulsion, and:

"No gross contamination that we can't irrigate right here," he nodded, looking back at Samira, who approved with a smile. "We should prepare lidocaine."

"I agree."

"Lidocaine?" Jean asked softly.

Dennis turned to the man. "Local anesthetic. We'll numb your hand so irrigation isn't uncomfortable."

"Oh, rad."

After preparing the lidocaine, Dr. Mohan handed it off to Dennis, who injected it swiftly and carefully, waiting a moment for the numbing to set in.

"You're a student doctor?" Jean asked, eyes fixated on the badge hanging from the waistband of Dennis' scrub pants.

Dr. Mohan prepared the tray and tools for irrigation, setting them where Dennis could reach from his seat at bedside.

"I am."

"Coulda fooled me. You're very good at this. I don't usually feel too comfortable with doctors."

From the corner of the room, Samira smiled.

Dennis disregarded the compliment, as though he did not hear it, instead explaining, "I'll need to spend some time irrigating your hand with saline to get out any potential dirt. Prevents infection and whatnot."

"I get the idea," Jean nodded, eyes locked to Dennis'.

"Whitaker," Samira called out. "I've gotta step out for a second. You got this?"

He nodded, jaw clenched.

When they were alone in the room, Jean just watched as Dennis focused. He had a frown in his brows, and he bit his lip, squinting down at the damaged skin as he carefully flushed it.

Breaking the silence, Jean cleared his throat and asked, "You live in Oakland?"

That stopped Dennis' tempo. He looked up, relatively unassumingly, and said with a tentative voice, "Uh... yeah. Sorta. How'd you guess?"

"We danced together at The Gem last night." He watched as the med student's face pointedly did not change. "Or do you not remember?"

Dennis swallowed, but he still did not react.

"I'm not sure I know what you're talking about."

There was a kind of icy professionalism in his voice that stifled their conversation while he finished irrigating. Neither man spoke again until Dennis had finished his work, re-dressing Jean's hand and standing from his seat at bedside.

Jean chuckled—innocently. "I'm not gonna tell anyone anyway. I'd be damning myself, too, y'know."

"There's nothing to tell."

Dennis walked over to the wall phone and dialed, speaking with an orthopedic consult about Jean's situation. With a click, he stuck the phone back in its place on the wall and turned to the patient's bed.

"I just need to check with Dr. Mohan and then you'll be all set to discharge," he spoke quickly, like he was trying to escape the room.

He turned, striding for the door, and before he could push it open, Jean said, "...Well," and sighed. "You were beautiful out there. When you didn't care what others thought."

Jean finished with: "Anyone would be lucky to see that side of you."

Dennis paused for a split second before walking out.

 

 

The nurse station was a little more crowded than usual. New faces in scrubs sauntered around like they owned the place. Of course, that made sense to Dennis—it was the end of the day shift. He'd heard that Presby's ED was a true twenty-four-hour experience, with a schedule that allowed rotating shifts to keep the department staffed and tended to at all times.

With Jean's patient chart in hand, Dennis walked toward Samira, who stood next to Robby and a rugged, silver-haired man in scrubs. The three of them seemed deep in conversation, but Robby had a knack for sensing Dennis when they were in the same room. He looked up, eyes locking onto the boy's, and he offered a quiet nod.

"Orthopedics cleared Mr. Torres," Dennis informed as he wedged himself between Dr. Mohan and Dr. Robby. "Just needs your approval for discharge."

"Let me get to that. Then I'm out of here for the rest of the weekend!" Samira joked as she stepped back.

"Better not forget drinks with the group tomorrow night," the silver-haired man pointed at Samira. "It'd crush me!"

"Oh shut it! I already said I'd go," she swatted dismissively, chuckling as she disappeared into the patient room.

"Whitaker, this is Dr. Jack Abbot," Robby introduced awkwardly.

"Pleasure," the man said sardonically as he took Dennis' hand and shook it firmly. "What brings you to this lovely department?"

"He's one of my new med students," Robby spoke for him.

"Oh, are you the one that passed out this morning?" Abbot asked insensitively, then looked at Robby, "Samira said a newbie passed out this morning."

"Javadi," Robby corrected.

"Ah," Abbot nodded. "Well, you made it through this shitshow in one piece. That's a feat, kid."

"'In one piece' is a stretch," Dennis chuckled, but he meant it. "But... I'm still here. I think."

"What doesn't kill you..." Abbot winked.

Dr. Abbot was a very jaded man with a funky sense of humor, but he had a heart. Dennis, of course, wouldn't know that, though. At least not tonight. "I'm gonna borrow Whitaker for a second. You mind checking with Samira for handover?"

"I mean," Abbot snorted, "I don't. But she probably wants to go home."

"She's tough. And you two get on well."

Abbot spared one last glance at Dennis and joked while pointing at Robby: "This guy'll try to scare you out of the ED. Don't let 'im."

As the silver-haired man walked off, Robby and Dennis found one another's eyes quickly. They searched for a moment, finding pain. Trying to find common ground. Dennis was clearly still haunted by Teddy Miller, but he'd certainly mellowed out a little.

"How're you holding up?"

Dennis sighed, "Dr. Mohan thinks I'm frail."

"What makes you say that?" Robby crossed his arms.

"She gave me a whole lecture about the importance of saving lives, then had me pick up... the easiest case I've taken all day."

The older man shrugged. "So she's trying to ease you into things again."

"I don't need that."

"Is there something wrong with needing that?"

"No, but I don't need it."

"Whitaker."

Dennis looked up. He hadn't really noticed his eyes had fallen to the floor.

Robby said, "You lost your first patient today." He shook his head as he explained in his low, gravelly voice, "That's never easy. And every other doctor who's been here more than a minute knows it."

"But I'm your..."

A foreign feeling bubbled in Dennis' throat. It was like a monstrous combination of guilt, frustration, and egotism. The weight of importance crushed him, like it was his duty to be the best while under Robby's watchful eye. Like he was not allowed to make a mistake, which he'd done all throughout the day.

"My what?"

Dennis shook his head, swallowing the lump in his throat.

I'm your protege? Your mentee? Your best man? Your strongest soldier? Your most devout acolyte? Your brightest angel? Dennis Whitaker knew that after ten years, he was absolutely none of these things to Michael Robinavitch. But, once more under the taller man's gaze, he felt the weight of every one of these things.

It crushed him slowly.

"I—"

Before Dennis could start his sentence, two EMTs barged through the doors of the ambulance bay, wheeling in a man who looked like he'd been mauled.

"William Harris. Thrown from his motorcycle in an accident. Complained of sharp chest pain in the left side before losing consciousness. Tachycardia and hypotension with a slight dyspnea. Bruising on the left side of the chest near the ribs," the male EMT fired off as Dennis and Robby hurried over.

"Blood pressure is trending down," Perlah called out from the machine.

His heart pounded. Perhaps because he was in such close proximity to Robby. Or perhaps because he was now standing in the same room where he'd, mere hours earlier, lost a patient he all but swore would live.

"Heart rate is 120, BP is 90/60 and dropping. Dyspnea," Princess confirmed, nodding at Robby, who studied the patient intensely.

With all the anxiety buzzing at his throat, Dennis licked his lips and mumbled, "Pneumothorax."

Robby ignored him.

Langdon quickly cut Will's shirt, peeling it open to reveal the bruising at his chest. "Bruising over the left ribs, but no apparent chest wall instability. He's got," he listened with his stethoscope, "diminished breath sounds on the left, and no dramatic tracheal deviation."

Dennis' ears rang. He wanted to believe it was a simpler situation than it was. He found himself wishing this stranger's ailment wasn't like Teddy's. An undetectable, unpredictable phantom. A kiss from Death itself, pressed upon the insides of the very man Dennis was tasked to save.

So again, Dennis almost deliriously muttered to Robby, "It could be pneumo."

This time, the attending acknowledged him: "Possible. Keep examining."

Robby uncrossed his arms, moving to the head of Will's bed and examining the neck. After a moment of squinting, he noted: "Neck veins are full."

Princess looked up from the machine in front of her and paused.

Dennis frowned. "Trauma. Shortness of breath. Pneumothorax would easily explain hypotension."

"So would tamponade," Robby cast a sharp look at Dennis, who, surprisingly, returned the look in kind.

The whole room could feel that tension. Trinity cocked her head, eyes darting between the two of them as she pieced together whatever was going on in front of her.

"Tone down the attitude, Whitaker," Langdon chimed in pungently, looking surprised. "Listen to your attending."

Dennis looked at Langdon, then back at Robby, who said nothing. The boy's heart pounded. Was he really deep in his own head? Or was he, as others had been telling him all day, actually good at this job? Did he know for certain the patient in front of him was suffering a collapsed lung, or could it really have been something far more serious? Something he did not want to confront again just before his shift ended?

Was he scared to confront the idea of losing another patient?

Seeing the frustration and the fear behind his boy's eyes, Robby sighed, planting his stethoscope down on Will's chest just above his heart and handing the other end to Dennis, saying, "Listen, Whitaker. Do you hear how quiet that is?"

Dennis frowned, trying desperately to explain why the sounds of Will Harris' heart were so faint.

"It—" he stuttered, "it could be Mr. Harris's body habitus. Or noise—"

"—Christ, look at him. He's thin," Robby snapped in aggravation, patience waning. "You need to observe with your eyes, Whitaker, not your imagination."

Dennis retracted, like being bitten by a dog.

Louder for the room to hear, but staring holes into Dennis, Robby lectured: "Tension pneumothorax gives you air hunger and it spirals very fast. It gives you a collapsing chest and veins that flatten. Mr. Harris here does not have that. His veins are up, his chest isn't hyper-resonant. His breath sounds are reduced, yes, but not gone altogether."

Trinity, to her own utter shock, couldn't focus on the exciting prospect of a tamponade patient, because the tension between Dennis and Robby was far more intriguing to her.

Breaking the brief silence, Dr. Langdon spoke up: "Something's stopping his heart from filling."

Robby nodded. "Thank you, Dr. Langdon."

Trying to reel himself in, Dennis blurted: "Blood?"

"Pressure," Robby half-agreed. He then turned to Langdon, and after a pause of contemplation, said, "Prepare for pericardial decompression."

The whole room froze.

The panic in Dennis' eyes flashed bright enough to blind Robby right there. He'd only just lost Teddy Miller mere hours ago for having tried a risky procedure, and now here he was again, in the same room, watching another patient prepare to undergo a procedure that could go quite wrong.

"Pericardial..." he breathed, shaking his head.

Robby was already in motion, directing Langdon and Santos.

One last time, defensively, Dennis blurted, "Dr. Robby! Should— should we not decompress the chest first?"

Robby barked back, "Whitaker. If I'm incorrect, we lose a few minutes. If you're incorrect? We lose Mr. Harris."

Dennis blinked.

He watched in slow-motion as Langdon and Trinity helped prepare the procedure. He just stood and watched, feeling the weight of expectation finally snap his ribs with a crunch. For all the years of work he'd put into this job, he seemed no better than the boy he was at the clinic in Franklin. He seemed no stronger, no smarter, no more capable.

Dennis watched just as closely as everyone else as Robby inserted the needle, finding resistance. Mere seconds later, there was a distinct release, and dark blood filled the syringe. Will, though unconscious, exhaled deeply.

Dr. Langdon whipped his head over to the machine and grinned. "BP is climbing back up."

There it is, Dennis thought to himself.

His fall from grace. Well, his final fall. Had they listened to him, Will Harris would have been dead in minutes thanks to the pressure around his heart. All because Dennis Whitaker was too afraid of the idea that he'd have to involve himself in another risky procedure to save - or end - a man's life.

Samira Mohan may have been right to lecture him about the importance of saving lives. She may have been right to remind him that he did not take this job thinking it would not come with risks, but to save people. She may have been right to coddle him into a case as simple as Jean Torres, because he could not handle a tougher case. But if he could not handle a tougher case now, then - in his mind - perhaps he could never handle it. Any of it. Dennis had, in his own cowardice, nearly lost another patient.

Perhaps the blade with no handle was better at cutting his palm than it was at carving a path forward.

Dennis was no longer sure if that blade represented his medical career or the ghost of the man he once loved, who stood mere feet away from him, totally the same yet wholly unrecognizable.

Perhaps, he thought, it's time to finally to let go. 

 

 

"Alright, I was kinda joking earlier, but," Trinity leaned closer to Dennis as they walked, "seriously, what the fuck is going on between you and Dr. Robby?"

They'd just finished their shift, only a few minutes after handing off Will Harris to the night shift, and even fewer minutes after Robby's closing speech to his day shift doctors. He'd huddled everyone around the nurse station and thanked them for their relentless work. For the most part, that speech seemed a bit recited. A bit tired.

"It doesn't matter," Dennis shook his head, moping. "He was right."

"No shit he was right," she huffed. "You sounded dumb in there— paranoid. But that back and forth with him? That was weird. Really weird. And come to think of it," Trinity squinted, "that's been going on like... all day."

Dennis shrugged. "I dunno what to tell you."

"Do you two know each other?"

"No," the boy assured defensively.

"You sure?"

Dennis met eyes with Trinity sternly. "I'm sure."

She stared back defiantly for a moment, then rolled her eyes when it became clear the boy wouldn't budge. "You're such a Huckleberry."

Trinity pulled the strap of her bag, tightening it on her shoulder as the warm night air of Pittsburgh hit their faces. Stepping back outside after a full twelve hours in the hospital was a euphoria that Dennis had never expected to know. The air was crisp, the breeze cool on his skin.

"...Y'know," she sighed. "I can tell it bothers you."

"What does?"

"Teddy Miller," she spoke carefully. "You really weren't the same after that happened."

Dennis only nodded.

Trinity looked over at him, stopping on the sidewalk at the front of the hospital. "I'm saying you're a good doctor." She admitted that like it pained her. "I think you just lost your footing a little."

The boy's lips curled downward, but he looked up at his roommate, surprised by the gentleness in her voice.

It was a fleeting moment, though. As soon as she seemed to notice how supportive she sounded, Trinity put her walls right back up, huffing and rolling her eyes and laughing it off as a joke.

But it did not feel like a joke to Dennis. Especially not coming from Trinity Santos. It meant something, and he couldn't really explain why.

"I'm grabbing groceries before I go home, so if you're coming, let's go," Trinity said, acting totally nonchalant about it.

That felt like her way of formally declaring she didn't mind Dennis crashing at her apartment for the time being. For how long, he did not know and would not ask. But if she didn't mind him, he wouldn't mind her. And perhaps he'd even learn to let 'Huckleberry' slide.

"I'll uh," he tried, "I'll head home first. I'm pretty beat."

She scanned his face, brows furrowing for a moment. Then she relaxed, nodding and swiveling on her heel to walk toward the center of the city.

"Alright, Huckleberry, but I'm not gonna guess what food you like—"

"Trinity?"

She stopped walking, but did not turn to face Dennis.

"Thanks," he offered, voice frail.

Trinity didn't verbally acknowledge him, but she did respond in the only way she knew how: a little nod of her head as she resumed her walk forward, never once looking back at the boy she'd saved.

Dennis watched her for a while as she walked further and further away, then looked around, hearing the wind in the trees, imagining home. Imagining Tennessee. Those soft whistles of cool night air in the fall—it was his favorite time to be outside in Franklin. This felt akin to that, perhaps only because he'd been crammed in a hospital with blood and urine and sweat and medical jargon bombarding him all day. 

It was, without a doubt, the second worst day of Dennis Whitaker's life.

But he survived it.

"What doesn't kill you," he muttered to himself, reciting Jack Abbot's little hum.

Only then did he feel the dam of tears burst through his eyes. It was... a little odd. He could've sworn he wasn't feeling that emotional in the moment, but the tears poured out and fled down his cheeks anyway. Perhaps it was the residual weight of the day finally hitting him all at once, like an afterimage. Perhaps he'd gone insane. In any case, he let those tears fall. Allowed it—like a reward for enduring the pain of Presby.

He let the tears fall in utter silence, looking up at the trees, interrupting the peace only to occasionally mutter in a soft whisper, "What doesn't kill you..."

"Everything okay, Whitaker?"

Robby's voice called from behind, ripping the boy from his little moment. He wiped the tears from his face with the sleeve of his zip-up hoodie, sniffling as he turned around, acting as normal as possible.

"Yeah— yes. Of course. Hey." Dennis cleared his throat. "You're still here?"

Robby pointed behind him at the Presby building with his thumb. "Had to finish my end of handover."

"Right."

There was an awkward pause, but both men stood there on the sidewalk, illuminated only by a streetlamp.

"...This is so stupid," Dennis finally admitted in utter defeat.

"Hm?" Robby crossed his arms, adjusting his backpack as he cocked his head a little to the left and squinted.

"What are the odds," the boy shrugged, "after all this time?"

"It's certainly bizarre," Robby agreed. "I'm a little worried about you."

Dismissing the man's concern, Dennis continued, "I dunno if it's supposed to be some sick lesson but... of all places. Of all people. It's you."

"Whitaker," Robby demanded the boy's attention.

And the boy, with his eyes, obeyed.

Robby said, "Tell me you're alright, kid."

It should've been easy. After all, Dennis had been lying it through his teeth all day. I'm alright, he said more times than he could count. I'm okay, he assured to more faces than he could remember.

But there in the dark, wet-pearl eyes clinging to Robby's like they held him afloat, Dennis could not bring himself to lie. So instead, a tear betrayed his right cheek, streaming down all by itself as his chin quivered.

"I'm..." he tried, choking through the honesty: "I'm hurting a little, Dr. Robby."

"A little?"

Robby's voice was sweet and dark, cradling Dennis from across the sidewalk.

"I'm useless," he redirected his pain into a kind of dull self-hatred. "I almost lost a second patient tonight. All because I'm a coward."

"You were afraid."

"Doctors shouldn't be afraid."

"A real doctor is always afraid, Whitaker." Robby corrected softly. "He just learns to trust himself."

Dennis peeled his eyes away from Robby, but couldn't bear it for more than a moment before returning them. His lip twitched. He wanted to respond, but he'd probably jump into oncoming traffic if he choked up in the middle of a sentence one more time. He'd truly had enough of that for one day.

So, wiping the last tear from his cheek and clearing his throat, Dennis nodded, stepping forward, just a little closer to Robby.

"...I don't know that I expected it to affect me this much today. I didn't expect you to affect me this much."

Robby hummed. "How so?"

"I think I overwhelmed myself. Trying to impress you. Trying to... appease you." Hearing himself say it nearly made the boy chuckle. "I don't know why, either. I haven't seen you in ten years. You're a stranger now. I shouldn't give a damn how I look in front of you. Shouldn't give a damn how you see me."

"But you do," Robby surmised.

Dennis bit inside his cheek. "...Maybe I do."

"Maybe you do," Robby whispered so quietly he wasn't even sure Dennis heard it.

"I guess..." Dennis began, not really knowing where he was going with the sentence. "...I guess I didn't expect it to affect me."

"This ED is a tough place—"

"I mean you," Dennis interrupted timidly, fidgeting with his fingers. "I didn't expect you to affect me this much. I was shocked to see you this morning, but I thought... it'd pass. Throughout the day. I wanted to believe I wasn't clinging to you all these years."

"Clinging to me?"

I've still got your books and I see you in every face, Dennis wanted to elaborate. But he did not say it aloud.

Instead, he shook his head, brushing off that side of the conversation. So, they stood there in another moment of silence, aided only by the occasional whistle of trees which lined the big streets around the front of the hospital.

Robby looked around, scratching the beard on his chin and tugging the strap of his backpack. His helmet was latched at the bottom, dangling uselessly.

"How are you getting home tonight?"

Dennis looked back at the older man. "By foot."

"Santos' place?"

"Yeah."

Robby's eyes darted to the ground, and he contemplated. Then he looked back up toward Dennis - not in his eyes, though - and mumbled, "You have any aversions to motorcycles?"

"...I've never been on one."

The older man nodded. Then he gestured for Dennis to follow him, and against the boy's better judgment, he did. He trailed awkwardly just a step or two behind the senior attending as they walked around to the side of the hospital where a black and silver motorcycle rested in its parking spot.

"I might as well just walk home. I'm not going to take your helmet," Dennis spoke as they walked, eyes fixed onto the helmet as it dangled off Robby's backpack. "If you don't have a spare."

Robby chuckled as they stopped just behind the motorcycle. "I don't mind," he admitted a little too nonchalantly. "It'd look better on you than it would me, anyway."

The boy's brows furrowed tight.

"God... they were right."

"What?" Robby cocked an eyebrow. "Who?"

"People around Presby. They said..." Dennis contemplated if it was a good idea to be honest about it. "You don't wear a helmet."

Robby could hardly stand to look at the sad smile on Dennis' face.

"Why, Dr. Robby?" He asked, voice soft and sincere. "What if... what if you ended up in an accident?"

"I'd be in good hands at Presby," the older man half-joked. "It'd give me an excuse to have you examine me."

"That's not funny," Dennis defended very seriously. "It's not a joke. You and I both saw firsthand tonight what can happen—" Dennis couldn't finish the sentence. Will Harris was still a sensitive, fresh subject for him.

The older man shrugged as he settled: "I wouldn't get in a wreck."

"What the hell's gotten into you?" You were never this reckless in Franklin, Dennis wanted to add.

Robby squirmed under the boy's gaze for a moment, struggling to answer. He slung his backpack off, resting it on the seat of his motorcycle as he took a deep breath in. He shook his head, rubbing his palms over the strained smile on his face.

"Can I be honest, Whitaker?"

"That would be a first for you," Dennis remarked sharply.

The older man tensed at that, but he nodded—accepted the remark. It was warranted after everything that'd happened in Franklin.

"The way I treated you," Robby blurted, voice tinged with a kind of grief. "The ways in which I... pushed you out and left you. On your own. It's killed me more times than a motorcycle ever could."

Dennis didn't expect that kind of honesty. At all.

Robby looked up, forcing himself to look the younger man in the eyes, nodding as he admitted, "It haunts me every day. It's been haunting me in evolving ways since the night you left."

A deer in headlights, Dennis stood utterly still, just watching as the older man's face contorted with different emotions. Anguish, regret, guilt. Like a flashing sign. Like he'd been holding it all in under his professional mask the whole day.

"I drove to your house that night," Robby admitted. "Drove there thinking I could fix it again. Like I always did. Fixing the problem with a bandage and moving forward. But you weren't there. You never came back. True to your word."

Dennis swallowed.

Robby continued: "I figured you packed your things and went back to Nebraska. Not that I knew for sure, but I hoped... I think I hoped you didn't. It just would've meant that I pushed you back to a family that was gonna get you admitted, or hurt, or... killed." He paused, swallowing the lump of emotion in his throat. "And every day since, I imagined what could've been, had I not been a fucking idiot and let you slip away."

"Then why'd you let me leave? That night in the clinic."

With total sincerity, Robby softly asked, "Would anything I could've said have stopped you?"

Dennis paused. His eyes admitted more than he wanted to say. But, to be kind, he opted for a very unconvincing, "...I don't know."

In truth, he didn't really know or remember what he wanted to hear that night, all those years ago. He knew it felt like his world was ending, that was a distinct, unforgettable feeling which stuck with him even now, ten years later. But as for the rest, he'd truly and honestly forgotten almost all of it. Robby, as a person, had become little more than a ghost from his past, one which stuck around like a whisper in the wind as he trudged through undergrad and medical school.

"I don't remember," he added, quiet as a mouse.

A somber silence fell between the two of them.

Robby watched as Dennis looked anywhere but at him, hands clasped together in front of him, fidgeting as he always did when he felt anxious or nervous. He relished every second that passed, standing just in front of his boy. Standing just in front of the only person on the planet who shared his most vulnerable secret.

There was a twisted kind of bond in that.

Knowing that Dennis Whitaker was the only man who'd ever seen him explore a new kind of love. Knowing that he was the only person who'd ever seen Robby so painfully enamored by the one thing in the world he would not allow himself to have, all those years ago in Tennessee.

Because for Michael Robinavitch, needing a woman was natural.

But needing a man— no, needing Dennis Whitaker, was utterly inimitable.

To need Dennis Whitaker was to swim against the current of the raging river. To need Dennis Whitaker was to search for a dim light in the crushing darkness at the bottom of the ocean. To need Dennis Whitaker was to hold tightly to the handleless blade, carving the path forward despite the cuts to his palms.

It was a force that, all these years, possessed Robby to weather the pain, if only for a taste of what could yet lay beyond.

"...If it really bothers you," Robby broke the silence tentatively, "I'll wear my helmet home tonight."

"I'd appreciate that," Dennis stated. "But that shouldn't need to be said—"

"—If you'll let me kiss you goodbye."

Dennis blinked, taken back.

"What?" The boy chuckled nervously, like he believed he heard Robby incorrectly.

The older man didn't respond, though. Didn't elaborate. He just stared intently, which was answer enough to Dennis that he did hear correctly.

"...I'm not so sure God would like that," he muttered, pinching his wrist.

"Do you still believe in God?" Robby asked with total sincerity. "After everything you saw in there today?"

Avoiding having to answer that heavy question, Dennis posed another point: "And, we don't know each other anymore. Not like that."

Robby figured that might be the answer. He was, of course, aware of how pitiful he sounded asking for a goodbye kiss from a stranger he hadn't known in ten years, but that stranger wasn't so strange to him. Dennis Whitaker was the last dim, familiar light remaining in his life.

He just wouldn't admit it.

"I know," Robby nodded. Quieter and more ashamed, he said, "I know."

There was a pause.

"...Only if it gets you home in one piece," Dennis spoke like he was trying to convince himself that was the only reason.

Robby looked up, eyes lighting subtly as he met the younger man's gaze. He looked around at the dark, small parking lot. Totally empty.

Before Dennis could change his mind, Robby closed the space between them with an embarrassing eagerness—a primal hunger. He cupped the younger man's face in his hands, leaning himself down just enough to steal those familiar lips. They were chapped, and the taste on his tongue was stale. It wasn't a glamorous kiss. In fact, it was quite messy. Quite unpracticed. But Robby didn't care.

He couldn't care at all, because it was Dennis Whitaker.

Chapter 12: I'm Your Man

Chapter Summary

Robby makes up for his mistakes and opens up to Dennis, but an incident with a patient completely shuts Dennis down.

Chapter Notes

Chapter 12's song is "I'm Your Man" from Mitski's 2023 album, "The Land Is Inhospitable and So Are We"

See the end of the chapter for more notes

"I don't want you to wait."

"Jesus Christ..." Will truly shuddered. His voice raised and bubbled with anger as he spat out: "All those people - and the men - he's been touchin'. Who knows what perverted thoughts are goin' through his head when they're under."

If there was a chance that Dennis would feel comfortable in his identity anytime soon, that chance was now, probably, utterly mangled like roadkill.

Dennis tried to speak up as shaken as he was, "I'm not—"

Robby placed a hand on his shoulder, stepping between the two men and casting a very unpleasant look upon Will. "This kid saved your sister's life. She came in with a skull fracture gnarly enough to kill a man and is gonna live to tell the tale to her kids and grandkids thanks to-" he pointed behind him to Dennis "-my student doctor."

"Doesn't change the fact he's sick in the head," the man grumbled stubbornly, resisting Robby's positive redirection.

That only served to agitate the attending more. Far more.

"Mr. Rowland— it's Mr. Rowland, right?" Robby took another step forward, voice low. "You are in my hospital right now. We put up with shit you could not imagine in this department and we do it with smiles on our faces every fucking day. Let me remind you," he took yet another step forward. "You're in my hospital. And in my hospital, you will treat my doctors and my nurses with respect or so help me God—"

"What? What'll you do?"

"What'll I do," Robby chuckled, cocking his head.

"Dr. Robby," Dennis tried meekly from behind, but the man shushed him with a waving hand.

"You're sucking ass to a faggot, my man," Will nodded.

The attending rubbed his beard and shook his head with a pungent smile on his face.

"I'll toss you out of my fucking hospital."

Will yelled, "Toss me out? Toss him out!"

"For what? Unfortunately," Robby said sarcastically, "you can't prove he's done anything to warrant repercussions-"

"-Robby, I don't need you fighting my battles-"

"-and good fucking luck getting anyone to bat an eye at a homosexual right now. Pittsburgh's got way bigger fish to fry." Dr. Robby's voice was raised now, too.

The situation was devolving very fast.

He and Will were in each other's faces now, and Will was shouting nonsense by that point, all up in Robby's space. Not a minute later, Dr. Abbot and Dr. Langdon ran in, quite shocked to see their attending so out of character: totally heated.

A far cry from his usual reasonable and relatively composed self.

"The hell's all this yelling for?" Abbot raised an eyebrow, stepping between Robby and Will. "Hey, knock it off. This is a hospital, my friend," he said to Will.

"This fuckin' dude's letting a homosexual run around the hospital touching patients!"

Dr. Abbot looked back at Dennis, who Will was pointing to. The kid stood stiff as a tree, jaw beyond clenched.

Then, Dr. Abbot looked back at Will, and he whispered, "I don't give a damn what your reason is. Stop yelling like a hooligan in this hospital. Let us save lives in relative peace, hm?" He looked down at Janie, finding ways to persuade the man to calm down. "This your sister?"

Will nodded, begrudgingly.

"Hey," Abbot directed Will to look at him. "These fine gentlemen are saving her damn life, my friend. Best doctors I know. Why don't you and I step out and let them do that? God forbid we fuck something up in here and ruin her recovery."

Dr. Langdon placed a reassuring hand on Dennis' back as the two of them watched Abbot talk Will down from rage. Robby stood aside, at the foot of the bed, refusing to look in their direction as he gnawed at his lip.

When Abbot finally - after another minute of rambling and spat insults - convinced Will to calm down, he walked the man out of the room, leaving Langdon and Robby to assess Whitaker.

"Don't worry about him," Dr. Langdon tried. "You've done good in here today, Whitaker. He's just a prick."

Dennis did not respond, verbally or otherwise.

So Langdon tried adding: "I don't believe the guy anyway, but even if... y'know," he couldn't bring himself to say the words. "Robby's right. Pittsburgh's got bigger fish to fry."

Oh, fan-fucking-tastic. He heard all of that, Dennis thought to himself.

When, once again, Dennis did not respond, Langdon took his awkward leave, nodding and leaving Robby and Dennis alone in the room with the - thankfully - sedated Janie Rowland.

The silence was short-lived, but it was deafening, and not even the chatter and cacophony of the Presby ED could penetrate it.

Dennis was the one to break it:

"I told you I didn't want any trouble."

Robby looked up from the floor, into Dennis' eyes.

Dennis clarified sternly: "I appreciate you trying to stick up for me, but that was..."

"Ah, you had it handled?" The attending huffed.

"I would've," Dennis defended himself.

"With that asshole? I don't think so."

"Well I wouldn't have gotten in his face and incited a tantrum," Dennis bit back. "Now the whole ED..." knows.

"Nobody heard anything," Robby denied irrationally.

"Langdon heard!"

"Christ," Robby groaned, palming his eyes. "Langdon doesn't give a shit, Whitaker. This is a goddamned hospital. He's not gonna run around the department telling everyone he thinks you kiss men at college bars."

The way Robby said that, he almost sounded jealous. Or maybe just bitter.

"...I don't kiss men at college bars," the med student spat.

"What does it matter anyway? It's not my business what you do."

"You sound like you want it to be your business."

"Well maybe I do."

The words came out like punches in delivery, but Robby having said that turned the air between them into a weird... intimate hodgepodge of unspoken emotions.

Sure, they'd been having sex with one another - quite regularly - for almost a month now, but neither Dennis nor Robby had really thought too terribly much about it aside from their occasional conversations after the deed.

Their relationship was wholly undefined, guided only by their intense need for one another.

The two of them never spoke about what that need really meant. What that need indicated. What that need would say about whatever future they may or may not have dreamed of having with one another. Robby certainly wondered. Robby certainly wanted.

But he really wasn't sure whether or not Dennis did.

"I've got other patients," the younger man sighed, trying to disregard Robby's comment as he stepped out of the room.

Dennis' heart slammed against his chest with every foot forward.

 

 

Unsurprisingly, Dennis ended up closing off, deciding to go home with Trinity that night.

She rushed him into their apartment, slamming the door behind him and ushering him into their quaint little kitchen. With a concerned look on his face, Dennis tensed up, hardly meeting Trinity's eyes.

"Why were you and Robby arguing in that patient's room today?"

For once, her tone held no sarcasm, no humor - just concern.

"We weren't arguing," Dennis shrugged. "What'd you hear?"

"I only caught the tail end of it. After Dr. Langdon walked out."

"If you'd hear more, you'd know—"

"—Dennis," Trinity's voice cut through the noise.

He'd never heard his real name from her mouth before.

It forced him to pause.

"Just... can you be honest with me? I've been a pretty fucking good friend. And I haven't bugged you about it in weeks, but I hardly know a thing about you!"

"I'm being honest."

"No you're not," Trinity scoffed, then entirely against better judgment, she blurted: "I know about you and Robby!"

The boy bit down on his cheek, eye twitching.

Calming himself and tempering his emotions, Dennis forced a shrug. "I don't know what you think you know, or what you think you heard, but it's—"

"You're lying."

"I'm not lying."

"You're lying!"

"I'm not lying, you just have an insatiable need to be right!"

Trinity paused.

"I'm sorry," he was already apologizing, wincing. "I'm sorry."

"It's fucking ridiculous that I had to learn it from Langdon," Trinity shook her head.

"Langdon told you?"

"He asked about it a few weeks ago and I connected the dots. He was just wondering why you and Robby have been so..."

"What? 'So' what?"

"Up each other's asses! I don't know!"

A very strange wave of emotions swallowed Dennis whole, right in front of Trinity Santos. His anger fizzled out, leaving only pain. His frustration melted, leaving only a hollow sense of... defeat.

"My God," he groaned, losing his balance for a moment.

Trinity nearly reached out to catch him, like he really was going to fall. Her brows knitted with a newfound kind of concern as he seemed to lose his marbles.

"My fucking God," he mumbled into his palms, smearing them over his face. "I'm so tired."

"What?"

"I'm tired," he repeated, forcing the air from his lungs. "I'm so tired of it."

"Tired of what?"

"All of it. The lying, the hiding, the... the fear of someone knowing, the... the-" he choked up "-the big spectacle of it all. I can't do what I need to do without feeling like everyone's watching me and judging me and I'm stuck inside of myself, and I've been stuck like this for so long that I don't even know who I am - just who everyone else wants me to be. And, and- I have moments of clarity where I beg myself to loosen up and I really, really try to, but I flinch at every touch and I bottle up every single—"

"I don't even know what you're saying anymore—"

"—I'm in love with him!"

Trinity blinked. And then she opened her mouth, and no words came out. Then she closed her mouth, brows tensing together like bowstrings as she processed.

"You're..." she asked, very confused, "in love? With... Dr. Robby?"

Quite immediately, it dawned on him that Trinity may not have been referring to his relationship with Robby, but rather his past in Tennessee with Robby. The moment that realization struck him - the moment he realized he'd just confessed, possibly, his deepest secret, to Trinity Santos - the tears behind his eyes lost all mercy.

He bit down with gritted teeth, staring at his friend as the tears began to pour silently down his flushed cheeks.

"I don't want to be," he seethed, no longer able to deny Trinity the truth. "I swear I don't want to be."

"Hey," she frowned, pulling him into a hug.

A hug.

What an odd sensation it was, to be cared for - even physically, for that matter - by Trinity Jagged-Edged Santos, a woman so very emotionally closed off. The concern in her eyes before she rested her head on his shoulder stuck in Dennis' mind like the frozen frame of a video. She wrapped her arms around him, rubbing his back soothingly as he wept so very quietly on her shoulder.

They stayed like that in silence for several minutes. To calm himself, Dennis focused on the distant, muffled chatter of college students in the streets below their apartment. He was above it all in that moment, hidden in his own little bubble. Tucked away.

Safe and sound with the only woman in the world who, now, really knew him.

Even Trinity's capacity for emotional vulnerability had its limits, though:

"You better not have gotten snot on this jacket," she joked, pulling away slowly and scanning Dennis' face.

His features were puffy, and the bags under his eyes were far more prominent than usual. His curly little mullet was a mess, and so was the hair atop his head.

"Is he..." she tried, "good to you?"

"He doesn't know," Dennis admitted. "We just..."

"Oh," Trinity nodded. "Sex."

"...Yeah."

"You don't feel pressured or anything, right?"

"No, no— God, no."

An unexplainable wave of relief passed across Trinity's face.

Dennis added: "I know how bad it looks. I'm aware."

"He is... your attending. And he's like, a whole decade-and-a-half older than you."

"We've been failing since 1961," Dennis cleared his throat. "I'm aware it won't work out."

"Yeah," Trinity agreed. "I mean, fun's one thing." She jabbed her thumb into her chest, "I love fun. Sex is great. But beyond sex is this... whole weird thing. And it can be really tricky when you're trying to navigate this thing with a guy who's got years and experience and professional seniority over you."

Be that as it may, true as it may be, Dennis Whitaker loved him.

That, he could not control.

And Trinity saw it on his face. She saw how he reacted to her logic, jaw tensing, eyes watering ever so slightly. He wasn't denying her logic, in fact, he seemed to agree with it. But agreement and execution were far from mutual topics in this conversation, and she recognized that.

So instead, in trying to protect Dennis, she offered a different, but still very honest, approach:

"He's your attending, Huckleberry."

"I know," he lamented.

"...If you really love him, you need to get the fuck out of there. You'd both lose your whole lives if that ever got out at a place like Presby. His career, your career. It would be a bomb."

Dennis looked up, meeting Trinity's eyes. She sighed, offering one more bit:

"You need to take care of yourself out there."

In lieu of a verbal response, which he was not prepared to try, Dennis only nodded.

 

 

Three days later, Dennis Whitaker neared the end of his final shift at Presby.

"You could've told me you didn't want to meet on Thursday," Dr. Robby spoke with crossed arms as he walked Dennis down the hall.

"I'm sorry."

"I sat out in the parking lot for thirty minutes," the attending chuckled, almost bitterly, but not quite.

"Trinity needed me at the apartment."

"Did she?"

"...Yeah," he lied.

"I imagined you were just really put off by the Will incident."

Dennis looked up into Robby's warm eyes. He, in fact, did not look bitter about being left in the dust on Thursday.

"That," Dennis cleared his throat, "well, that definitely didn't help."

"I know part of it's because you don't need me stepping in for you when things like that happen," Robby stopped, guiding his hand up to Dennis' shoulder and urging him to stop walking, too.

They stood in a secluded doorway just a few steps away from the commotion of the nurse station. Robby could see nurse Flora scribbling away from where he stood.

"And I want to apologize," he spoke low to Dennis, hand still on his shoulder. "It'd do me well to have a little more faith in you."

The med student tensed for a second, then relaxed. He nodded, and looked up at his attending through timid eyelashes as he muttered, "Thank you. Sir."

Dr. Robby pulled his hand away slowly, shoving it in the pocket of his black cargo pants, but he did not seem to retract from the conversation itself. Dennis searched the man's eyes, studying the crow's feet of his face. Studying the tired eyes and the weathered skin that he'd known for so long now.

Robby looked like he had something more to say.

"I've got patients," Dennis reminded softly.

"Right," the attending nodded. "Just... really quick, I want to ask you about your rotation."

Here it comes, Dennis dreaded.

"You've enjoyed things here at Presby, hm?"

"...Yes, sir."

In his tone, Dennis tried to remain as neutral as possible. He needed to remain as detached as possible. But he knew what was coming. He'd already suspected Robby might ask him to stay. It was his last shift, after all.

"I know it's a little informal," the attending cleared his throat. "And I'm not even entirely sure what your school schedule looks like for the next few months," because you won't tell me much about you, he did not say.

Dennis nodded, but he no longer met the man's eyes. He fidgeted with his fingernails, hands clasped together in front of him.

"...But I'd like you to consider taking an internship here."

"Dr. Robby, I..."

"I know, I know. It's gonna be a lot to think about. But you're a natural at this, Whitaker. You've got experience that the others here don't have. You'll be a capable doctor wherever you end up, but... why not consider Presby?"

What Robby really wanted to ask was: why not consider me?

"I've already got an internship lined up, Dr. Robby."

The words came out quick and impersonal, but they hit Michael Robinavitch like bombs. He was, still, quite good at forcing down any reaction his body wanted to make, but his eyebrow twitched, and he cocked his head, with a surprised little smile. "I see," he tried, then paused for a second, swallowing the lump in his throat. "Where at?"

"Mercy Hospital," Dennis nodded. "I applied to a few different places a couple of weeks ago. Before my first day here. Needed to line up a job."

"Of course," Robby agreed, albeit begrudgingly. "No- yeah, that's fantastic. And they've already accepted you?"

"That's what 'lined up' implies," Dennis joked.

"Right."

"...Yeah."

The air between them fell stale for a second. They'd begun to overstay their welcome away from the bustle of the ED.

Breaking the silence, Dennis tried: "I did enjoy it here, Dr. Robby."

They met eyes. Robby looked like he had very little light left in his.

"That's good to hear," he spoke wearily.

Looking around - and nearly squirming by this point - Dennis pointed to the hall behind him and stepped away, saying, "I should... go pick up a patient."

"Whitaker."

The boy stopped. "...Yes, sir?"

"Let me at least send you home with the forms."

Tears put pressure behind Dennis' eyes. He was, this entire time, trying to fight against the urge to take Robby's offer, and the man wasn't making it any easier. Trinity's words echoed in his mind; he knew only bad things could come from staying at Presby, but Robby must've known that, too. Robby probably known even better than Dennis what kind of trouble they risked by continuing their... routine.

This needed to be a goodbye.

"I..." Dennis gnawed inside his cheek, looking utterly defeated.

Defeated by love. Defeated by hunger. Defeated by what he'd long ago cursed himself with; an insatiable need for the only thing in the world he could never truly learn to live without.

"Just find me after your shift," Robby practically pleaded. "I won't ask you to come home with me tonight. I'm only asking you to take the forms. And tell me you'll consider it."

"Dr. Robby," Dennis sighed, trying to fight back the emotion as he looked up from the floor.

"I don't..." he strained, "want to hear a decision right now," then he winced, shaking his head. "Just take the forms after your shift, Whitaker."

It was spoken almost like a barked order.

But Dennis could hear the unexplainable desperation in the older man's voice.

 

 

 

 

 

 

Six days later, Robby endured what quickly became one of the most grueling day shifts of his life at Presby. Trinity Santos had come to him in the afternoon and confided about her discovery; Dr. Frank Langdon had been stealing drugs from the hospital. And there was no way of knowing how long he'd been doing it. His star student, his golden prodigy who he'd raised up to the position of senior resident much in the same way he'd once raised up Heather Collins. Much in the same way he'd once raised up Samira Mohan.

Much in the same way he'd once raised up Dennis Whitaker.

Robby had left a trail of prodigies in his wake throughout the years, but he never imagined he'd have taught a doctor who would do what Frank Langdon had now been caught doing.

He kicked the man out in a dramatic, very public scene in the middle of the hospital, leaving nurses and doctors alike quite confused. Then it was up to him to spend the remainder of his shift acting natural. Acting normal. Acting functional in the hopes that the ED would not fall apart after losing one of its senior residents so very suddenly.

The shift nearly tore him apart. The isolation nearly tore him apart.

"What're you doin' up here, bud?" Dr. Abbot called out from behind as he approached Robby, who leaned against the railing at the edge of the Presby hospital rooftop.

"Ohhh, you know me," Robby tried to dismiss comedically. "Just stargazing."

"The stars are up there," Abbot pointed up at the sky, which Robby was clearly not looking at. "And you got a shit view of them with all this light pollution."

Then, the silver-haired man snorted and added: "That's kinda like us, if you think about it."

"I don't follow," Robby sighed.

"The light pollution. We're surrounded by bright, agitating lights. Makes it tough to see the stars sometimes, hm?"

"Hm," Robby pondered half-heartedly.

"But they're there."

Abbot leaned against the railing, just next to Robby. He minded his distance, but kept close enough just in case. He and Robby had had conversations like these on the rooftop countless times over the years. Sometimes Robby was the man looking beyond the edge, sometimes Abbot was.

But in all cases, they kept one another grounded.

"Don't you have patients to torture, Jack?"

"Tryin' to keep you from being the first one, tonight," the man quipped.

Robby allowed himself to smile a little, though it looked pained.

Taking on a more serious approach, Abbot tried: "You've been falling back into old habits this week, my friend."

"You spying on me?"

"If I was any good at that, I'd have taken it up in the second World War and won myself a medal of honor," Abbot joked, busting out into a laugh as he slapped the railing. When he reeled himself in, he continued: "It's not hard to see, Robby."

"That so?"

"Mhm. Smoking every chance you get, eyebags popping out again like you haven't been sleeping, on edge every time we do handover," Abbot listed off each offense. "And that fuckin' helmet."

Here we go, Robby mentally prepared himself.

"You were doin' real good in July, I'll give that to you."

"Had to set a good example for my med students," Robby tried to dismiss, like nothing was wrong.

"Oh I believe you, my friend. Had to set a real good example."

Robby didn't want to analyze that comment.

Abbot continued, standing upright as he turned away from Pittsburgh and faced Robby, "But if you were only doing it for Javadi and Whitaker, then you weren't really doin' any good for yourself, were you?"

Dennis Whitaker's face flashed through Robby's mind like blurry streetlamps.

"Well, the med students weren't the only familiar faces we lost this week," the brown-haired man quipped against his better judgment.

"Langdon?" Abbot surmised.

Robby only nodded, nearly wincing now that he had to openly address that wound.

"Flora was yapping a little about it to me down there," Abbot said. "What went wrong?"

"Dr. Santos."

"Your intern?"

Robby nodded. "Tough girl. I had to peel Langdon off her case the other day and... she took it well. All this time I hadn't thought much about it, but," he kissed his teeth, "she was on his trail. Found out he's been stealing meds from us."

"From Presby?"

"Yep."

"Yeesh," Abbot hissed. Then he leaned against the rail again, shaking his head. "That's gotta be tough on you, man. I can't imagine."

Robby huffed, then - for the first time during their conversation - turned to face Abbot as he joked, "You giving me permission to keep smoking?"

"Smoking? Sure." Abbot's lips curled downward, and he nodded. "Riding that funny bike of yours without a helmet? ...I dunno, man."

They shared a look. Both men stood firm, unwavering.

After a soft pause, Robby calmly ordered: "Let me dig my own grave, Jack."

"Brother," Jack groaned. "You're already stuck in the trenches. Digging a grave takes more energy than finding the flowers in the mud, my friend."

Robby rolled his eyes and pushed off the rail, grabbing his stethoscope and hooking it back into place around his neck as he began walking toward the door leading back inside Presby.

"I'm serious, man. The flowers are there," Abbot shrugged, unmoving against the rail. "You just gotta reach for 'em."

Robby did not reach for the flowers that night.

Instead, when he escaped Presby and made it to the parking lot, he fastened his helmet to the strap of his backpack as he revved his motorcycle, letting time pass only long enough for him to burn through a cigarette.

After he'd enjoyed that familiar burn in his throat and his lungs, he rumbled out onto the main streets of Pittsburgh. The night air wasn't cool, but the hint of autumn sat upon it like a teasing song as it whistled in his ears and ghosted through his messy brown hair.

He passed the time of the idle drive by assessing Abbot's words. Robby knew he was living a painful life, stuck within his own trenches, but he also knew there were no more flowers to find. Abbot had meant well, but after everything, Robby had - this past month - failed to cultivate those flowers once again, in the form of Dennis Whitaker.

It wasn't dissimilar to the ways in which he'd failed Heather Collins all those years ago, pushing her aside until the love she was able to give him was only a fragment. It wasn't, perhaps, dissimilar to the ways in which he'd failed Dana Evans, promising the woman he'd keep in contact with her only to never again send another letter upon leaving Franklin, too ashamed of everything that'd happened to confront her.

It was even, perhaps, within the realm of the way in which he failed Frank Langdon, raising a doctor who felt it necessary to steal medicine from a major hospital's very own inventory to fuel his secret addiction.

There were many flowers Michael Robinavitch failed to cultivate during his long, long stay in the trenches.

Too many, in fact, to address in his mind before a car slammed into his motorcycle at the four-way intersection, just a few blocks from his apartment.

Chapter 13: Lover, You Should've Come Over

Chapter Summary

An old friend shows up in Pittsburgh while Dennis struggles to navigate a devastating situation.

Chapter Notes

Chapter 13's song is "Lover, You Should've Come Over" by Jeff Buckley on his 1994 album, "Grace"

Chapter warnings: brief mention of childhood trauma, referenced canon-compliant suicide

See the end of the chapter for more notes

The clock ticked away on the wall in the living room behind Dennis.

He'd been sitting at the kitchen counter for nearly two hours scribbling away at the forms for the Presby internship. It'd now been six days since his final shift in the ED, and six days since Robby practically begged him to take the papers and consider them. To consider the internship. To consider him.

The part Dennis Whitaker did not want to admit - even to himself - is that he knew quite well - and admittedly, from the jump - he'd choose Presby over Mercy Hospital. He'd just convinced himself to take his time with the decision and find tangible reasons why it was a good idea. So as not to concern Trinity Santos. So as not to come across desperate to Robby.

Why should it matter? Dennis asked himself countless times those last six days.

Perhaps it shouldn't have mattered at all, but alas, for better or for worse, staying at Presby was for, ultimately, one main reason:

Michael Robinavitch.

Dennis just wouldn't admit it aloud, or to himself.

The front door of the apartment squealed open after the lock rattled for a moment, and when Trinity stomped in, Dennis greeted without looking up from his papers: "You're home later than usual."

She did not respond, only walked into the kitchen, dumping her bag in the dining room chair and pacing.

"You're gonna hate me, I think," Dennis admitted with butterflies in his stomach.

"Oh yeah?" Trinity exhaled as she paced, watching as Dennis scribbled away. Watching in distress. "Why's that?"

"I'm going with Presby," he snorted, leaning his head further down to erase a pencil error. "And before you judge me, before you shit-talk it, let me just-" he cleared his throat "-explain. It's a good job. I've really, actually enjoyed my time there. I love working under Dr. Mohan, and I love learning from everyone. McKay, you, Dr. Abbot, Javadi and Mel. Hell, even the night shift crew is fun to be around. And if I were to swap to Mercy, I'd be restarting. And it's not like that'd be the very end of the world, gosh no. But it would suck. And I know... my situation with Dr. Robby hasn't been the most riskless choice I've ever made, but I'll make it clear to him we can't be... anything. We just have to survive with what we've got. And what we've got," Dennis looked up, eyes meeting Trinity's own, "is Presby."

He swallowed, squinting as he scanned her face. She looked very pale. And anxious. She picked at her cuticles idly with a clenched jaw. Almost invisible to the eye were her little shudders, but that was what really concerned Dennis.

"Trinity, what's wrong?"

"Dennis," she stifled her trembles. "I need you to listen for a sec."

His brows furrowed.

"Robby's at Presby right now," she stated quickly.

"...I don't—"

"—In the ICU."

It was... akin to a gunshot right against the ear. Dennis could hear the ringing drone as its volume swelled and the noise of the outside world muffled under dark water. His heart fell out of his chest with a slam against the bottom of his stomach, knocking the wind from him. He planted his palms on the kitchen counter to keep himself stable, swallowing an impossibly hard lump in his throat.

"Pardon?" He tried in a whisper as thin as paper.

"I was..." she crossed her arms, "just about to clock out when they brought him in. He got in an accident."

"An accident?"

Trinity nodded.

"What kind of—"

"Vehicle crash. A car hit him from the side. Ford Mustang. Tipped his motorcycle and crushed his leg as it slid. There's head trauma." Trinity spoke distantly, like she was simply reciting a patient's ailments. Like it wasn't Robby.

Dennis shuddered, lips parting.

"Head trauma?"

She nodded as though it pained her.

"No," Dennis shook his head. "No, that isn't right. That can't be right. He promised-" a choked sob "-he promised he'd wear his..." his eyes searched Trinity's for answers. "Was he wearing a helmet? He was supposed to be wearing—"

"—Whitaker, I have no clue. He didn't come in with one on. I didn't ask. Dr. Shen and Dr. Mohan were there to help. Neuro took him up to the ICU and I didn't hear much after—"

"He can't do that. He can't do that, I... I-" Dennis crumpled the Presby internship papers in his hand and waved them around frantically as he stood from his chair. "I made my choice. I want to be there for him... I chose Presby. This can't happen now. After everything."
