"It was just a freak accident," she sighed, but there was a kind of empathy in her voice that could not be denied. She felt his pain.

Dennis made haste to his bedroom, grabbing a bag with a kind of urgency that Trinity hadn't seen from him before.

"What're you doing?" Trinity asked from the kitchen.

"I need to see him."

"They won't let you, and you know that. ICU has rules."

"They make exceptions."

"You know they won't make any for you, Huckleberry. You're not family," she tried reasoning, but still, Trinity knew the kind of pain he was in. Her words came out soft, even while laced with her typical edge.

Dennis stepped out of his room, sparing no glance at his roommate when he said, "Then I'll wait in the hall."

She couldn't even get a final word in before the door had slammed shut behind Dennis with a creak and a rattle. Trinity leaned into the kitchen counter, crossing her arms and tapping her foot anxiously on the vinyl kitchen floor. She wondered if it was a bad call to tell him so soon.

 

 

Dennis tried exactly that.

He tried asking to be let into Robby's room. Even flashed his expired student doctor badge, as though that might sway any opinion. It, of course, did not.

So he waited just outside the room, in a stiff chair under the fluorescent, harsh hospital light. For hours, he sat there twiddling his thumbs, only stopping when sleep took him at around one in the morning. The way in which he slept gave him a nasty crick in the neck when a nurse woke him up, at half past three in the morning.

"Excuse me," she tapped his shoulder politely, but he startled awake. "Sorry," she said with a sweet smile.

"Huh?" He mumbled, sleep coating his voice like a thick syrup.

"I wanted to let you know," she tried, "because you've been waiting out here for hours. Dr. Robinavitch won't be allowed visitors anytime soon, I'm afraid. I just... didn't want you to feel stuck here all night and all day tomorrow waiting to be let in."

"Oh," Dennis licked his lips, voice hoarse, brows knitted. "Sure. Yeah."

"Yeah," she parroted with a nod, then raised an optimistic point: "But, he's stable. He's been unconscious since he came in, and Dr. Lounds says it's possible he won't wake up for..." she trailed off, trying to find more optimism, "an extended period."

"Extended period?" Dennis' heart dropped.

"It's hard to say how long he'll be out, and there's always a chance he doesn't wake up. But, Dr. Robinavitch is a strong man. We all hear the work he's been doing down in the ED."

Dennis swallowed.

"Do you..." she spoke carefully, "know him personally?"

Quickly, he glanced down at her badge, reading Nurse Kim Tate. Then he looked up and nodded. "He's, uh, my mentor. Well— was. He's my attending nowadays. I, uh, work down in the ED. I'm taking an internship here."

"Ah," she smiled, eyebrows raised. "How exciting! Well, then I'm sure you know better than I do how tough he is. A little strength goes a long way in incidents like these."

Nurse Kim was already stepping back, prepared to walk away, when Dennis stood from his chair abruptly, swallowing the lump in his throat before asking with a weak voice: "Nurse Kim?"

She stopped, turned, and smiled. "Hm?"

"Could I ask you a favor?"

She offered a polite little nod.

"Could the hospital, uh, write down my landline number? And... and could you have them call if anything changes? With Robby."

Nurse Kim did not immediately respond. She studied Dennis' face for a second, smile fading, before finally offering another nod and guiding him over to the nurse desk down the hall. He wrote down the landline to Trinity's apartment on a little notepad and handed it over, thanking the woman for her kindness.

Against her better judgment, as Dennis was already walking down the hall to leave Presby, Nurse Kim said, "Sir, I know it may not be my place to say it, but..."

Dennis stopped, turning to face her, waiting for her words. On her face sat an expression like anguish. She looked at the floor now, clipboard hugged to her chest as she found the right words:

"He only had one emergency contact and she hasn't returned any of Presby's calls. I can't imagine what he's going through in his personal life... to have no one else looking out for him." She nodded, "It warms my heart that you are. It really does."

Hearing that was less of a pat on the back and more a punch to the gut. Dennis clenched his jaw, forcing down the little, sour bubble of emotion that threatened to rise up once again in his throat. He eyed the hall behind the nurse, then met her own eyes once more and offered a single, curt nod.

Looking down at the tiled floor, Dennis said, "He's looked after me for a long time, so... it's only fair I do the same."

 

 

Dennis spent the next two days tossing and turning in his sleep when the moon shone over the apartment. Whenever the sun rose over Pittsburgh, he was a hermit, rotting under his covers, mind unable to peel away from the mere image of Robby's face. What it might've looked like just before the crash. The fear that must've swam in his pupils. Or perhaps there was no fear.

That would've been a problem.

The pain of wondering such things weighed Dennis down as though he were anchored to the bottom of a river, tormented by the raging current and unable to swim up for air. He did not want to imagine what might've been going through Robby's head, but couldn't hold himself—

"Hey," Trinity called softly from outside his bedroom door with a knock.

"What?" He returned, muffled under his blankets.

"I haven't seen you eat in two whole days," she sighed, resting her forehead against the door just gentle enough that it didn't make a sound. "Let's go out and get some fresh air, hm? Some pizza, too."

"I'm alright," he tried politely, voice hardly audible behind the closed bedroom door and under the covers. "But thank you. Really."

"It wasn't an offer," Trinity clarified. "You gotta get out and get some air. Burying yourself in the apartment isn't serving either of us."

There was a pause of silence as she waited for a response. Then, less muffled though still behind the closed door, Dennis called out, "Give me twenty to shower."

With a fond little smile threatening to creep onto her face, Trinity said, "Hurry up, Huckleberry."

So he took his shower, leaving the water cold as punishment for his inability to stop Robby from his reckless habits. He didn't shy from the icy stream, he deserved it. Old habits died hard, and Dennis was painfully alive right now. He hoped Robby was, too—

"Nope," he hissed under the showerhead, digging a nail into his side to force himself away from those thoughts.

The rest of the shower was a challenge of internal silence, and Dennis almost made it five minutes without his train of thought changing course and barreling straight back into Michael Robinavitch.

When he stepped out, dried, dressed, and headed out of the apartment - with Trinity Santos by his side, watchful the whole way - Dennis led them to the pizza place they'd shared slices at last month. The first place they really got to know one another, he and his roommate. How wrong he was about her; Trinity did have a heart, as heartless as she presented.

And her heart was especially big for those who teetered on the edge of cliffs.

"Thanks," Dennis muttered before chomping into his pepperoni slice.

He sat across from Trinity in the window booth, as they'd become accustomed to doing the handful of times they ate here.

"Yeah," she tipped the glass Cola bottle up, sipping at the sweet soda. When the bottle tapped back onto the thick table, she eyed her roommate, but said nothing.

She wanted him to address the elephant in the room, but that was entirely his decision to make. Trinity wouldn't push a man like Dennis Whitaker to speak about the demons nipping at his insides, lest he cry himself to death right there in front of the pizza cooks.

Sensing her urging stare, Dennis did, in fact, speak up:

"I'm doing better," he lied.

"Didn't say you weren't," she shrugged.

"You've got that look in your eyes," he peeled his own eyes away, letting them fall onto his plate before filling the brief silence with another bite of pizza.

Crossing her arms, Trinity leaned back against the booth. "Can you fathom why?"

"'Cause I've been holed up in my room. I know."

"You're simplifying it."

"I'd hate to overcomplicate it."

"Love is complicated, Huckleberry."

He looked up again, tentatively meeting Trinity's eyes, jaw clenching defensively as he tried processing her admittedly undeniable words. Love was too complicated, and it had been complicated since the day he fell in with Michael Robinavitch, many years ago.

Funnier yet, how deceptive love was; ten years apart lulled Dennis into a sense of control that he never truly had over his heart. It was all just... a sense of it. Right up until he wound back in Robby's air.

Trinity leaned forward again, into the table this time as she lowered her voice: "Life can be so... fucking hard." Searching very carefully for the right words, her eyes darted around, scanning Dennis' face. "But there are people that can and want to help you."

Unable to suppress the silent little shakes, Dennis kissed his teeth, rolling his eyes.

"Dr. Robby wanted to help me," he nodded. "...And look what I did to him."

Realizing now that, on some level, Dennis blamed himself for this outcome, Trinity shook her head, scoffing. Her carefully selected words began falling apart as emotion took over:

"What, you think a grown man's decisions are your fault?"

"I think that I—"

"—By that logic, I put him in the hospital, too. Got his favorite resident kicked out of Presby, after all. You think it's fair to blame me for his choices?"

"No," Dennis clarified quickly.

"Right. So it isn't fair to blame yourself, either, Whitaker."

"...But it's more complicated than that. There's so much you couldn't understand about this."

Clenched jaw and balled fist, Trinity decided it was time to open up.

"I understand these things a lot more than you know."

Sensing her vulnerable change of tone, Dennis shrank in his booth across from Trinity.

After a contemplative pause, deciding if she was really about to open up, Trinity said, "I had a best friend, growing up."

The quiver in her voice was nearly impossible to hear, but it weighed on the air between them.

"She meant... everything to me. Then came a man."

Dennis' brows furrowed.

"My friend and I went through something pretty vile. That man... he took advantage of our age and I'm sure at least a handful of folks knew it. 'Course, no one would bat an eye. It's a man's world, as you know."

The commotion around them seemed to silence, and Dennis' attention lasered in on each of Trinity's words, stunned by her vulnerability. Stunned that she'd open up to him at all, ever—let alone like this. 

"Things got so bad that my friend... she, uh," Trinity chewed inside her cheek, "took her own life."

Dennis' lips parted, like he wanted to say something or console Trinity in some way. But words did not come out, and instead, a brief, soft silence hung in the air between them as they looked at one another. A new kind of understanding in their dynamic shone down, like their friendship was forged in more than just luck; it was forged in trauma. A shared bond that neither spoke about, but both - on some level - always recognized in the other.

"Every single day of my life since, I've regretted not being able to stop her," Trinity nodded. "I spent so much time wondering what I could've done to make things go any different. Any better."

"That's not fair to you," Dennis finally muttered.

"You're starting to get it a little. It's not fair to carry that pain just because you think you should've done better—or could've done better. At the end of the day... all of us make our own choices, sometimes the worst choices we can make. But they're all our own."

"Did it ever get any easier? Trying not to blame yourself for..."

"Whitaker," Trinity sighed. "You know Robby didn't try to do that to himself."

"No, but—"

"—Witnesses confirmed it was a freak accident at a four-way intersection. Whatever guilt you're carrying, it's..." she shook her head, "something that's been inside you a lot longer than this."

Blurred images of Tennessee flashed through his mind like a faulty filmstrip. Images of him walking out on Robby ten years ago. Images of their final argument. New light shed upon him, knowing how much pain the man had really been in all those years.

Trinity continued: "This is something that you have to understand isn't your—"

"—But his helmet."

Trinity paused, allowing Dennis to grasp at straws.

"He wasn't wearing it when he crashed," Dennis stated, as though that wasn't already known information thanks to Presby's diagnosis.

"...No," she agreed begrudgingly. "He wasn't."

Dennis shuddered.

"He may not have done it to himself," the boy looked up into Trinity's eyes, "but he chose not to stop it from happening."

Trinity saw the emotion behind his pupils, like the window to a soul that had known only suffering for the brightest parts of his life. Every glimmer cost a nightmare, every hope cost a dream. And love, it seemed to cost whatever left Dennis Whitaker had to offer.

She did not pity him, she understood him.

"There's not a day that goes by I'm not grateful I didn't go through with what my friend did," Trinity added tenderly to her previous point, then shifted back to: "I don't know if I'll ever really understand your dynamic with Dr. Robby. He's older than you, and he's your attending, and... that kind of thing is really fucking tough to make work fairly."

Dennis let out a strained sigh, biting down to stifle the emotions bubbling in his throat.

"But..." Trinity began again, "I've seen the worst choices a person can make, Whitaker." She shook her head. "You're not so bad for one another."

"I-"

"Just," she stopped him, eyes closing as she prepared to build her walls back up, "whatever happens with him, I need you to promise me you'll look after yourself."

Dennis searched Trinity's eyes which were full of concern and care, like a sister. Much like a sister. One he could only dream of having had in Nebraska, where it all began. One who, if she had been there for him in Nebraska, might've altered the course of his turbulent life. Might've mellowed it out.

"Promise me you'll do what works for you," she stated again in the silence.

It was, perhaps, the first time in his life Dennis was certain he meant it when he said:

"I promise."

 

 

When they returned home that afternoon, the landline was already ringing, vibrating against the wall of their apartment. Dennis hurried over to pick it up, leaving Trinity to catch the front door.

As he'd dreaded, it was Presby. The lady on the phone introduced herself as Nurse Kim and asked if she was speaking to Dennis Whitaker and he confirmed just as quickly, giving her a moment to say that Robby had come to. She said he was moved to Presby's step-down unit for moderate observation, and that they allowed visitors on that floor.

Before she could finish another sentence, Dennis had already dropped the phone, leaving it to sling against the wall as the cord stretched. He bolted toward the front door, nearly tripping as he swung it open.

Trinity didn't need to ask where he was going. She saw the hopeful little grin on his face before he bolted.

The run to Presby had never felt so long, and it was a particularly hot day out, leaving Dennis to weave through busy crowds of students on their apartment's street before he crossed and navigated down several more blocks, all the way to the massive Presby hospital building.

He nearly took Dr. Abbot out as the silver-haired man walked out of the main entrance.

"Woah, woah," the older man chuckled, slinging the strap of his lunchbox over his shoulder. "Whitaker? Good to see you, man. You here for..."

"Dr. Robby," Dennis confirmed. "He's awake?"

"Yeah," he nodded. "I was up there for a few minutes with Dr. Mohan. Came in to bring her a lunch, but the nurses in the ICU came down and broke the good news."

"So he'll be good to go soon? They'll discharge him?"

Dennis waited for Abbot to respond, but that response didn't come. Instead, the man offered a challenging glance and a pained grin.

"Well," Abbot croaked, "he's, uh... as I'm aware, he's not gonna be doing much on his own anytime soon. I don't know when they plan to let him go, but I'd guess it'll be a few weeks."

Dennis could feel his heart sink into his stomach with a loud thud as he squinted, trying to assess what Abbot might've meant. He wasn't sure, but it couldn't have been worse than not waking up at all, which was the original concern.

"I oughta get in there and see him while visitor hours are on," he nodded, stepping past Dr. Abbot.

"Yeah, hey-" Abbot called out, turning back to face Dennis "-he's got a visitor right now. Blond lady, real sweetheart."

The boy wasn't really listening though, just nodding as he continued speed-walking into the entryway of Presby, and by the time the doors had closed behind him, Abbot was facing forward again, letting out a sigh.

Dennis ran through the main lobby and crammed himself into the elevator, then pressed the button for the step-down unit, waiting anxiously with a foot tapping against the floor the whole, short way up. The whole while he imagined what he'd say. Apologies, perhaps?

No, he promised Trinity he'd learn from that mistake.

No apologies, so surely, he thought, it was appropriate to instead scold the man for being an idiot.

Maybe not that either.

His contemplation was cut short by the ding of the elevator, and the doors pulled open, leaving a long hallway full of nurses for him to weave through. Dennis stepped quietly through and greeted each stranger in silence as he closed in on the nurse station.

When he stopped at the desk, a woman with dark complexion looked up from her chart. "What can I help you with, sir?"

"Michael Robinavitch? He was moved down here today from the ICU. Is he taking visitors?"

She didn't need to look down at her log to know where the man's room was, it seemed. "Dr. Robinavitch is a little further down the hall in 18E. He's got a visitor already right now, but we allow two at a time, so you're fine as long the volume stays down."

"Thank you ma'am," he nodded, scurrying further down the hall.

What should he say? It freaked him out now that he was so close to speaking with the man he'd mentally established to have been dead already. What could he possibly say that'd be of any significance to the admittedly large list of unspoken truths between the two of them? What could Dennis Whitaker say that would matter to Michael Robinavitch?

Once again, he was interrupted from his thoughts as he stood in front of the door to Robby's room, because the door opened, and the woman that walked out was Dana Evans.

Her eyes widened just as quickly as his did, and words did not come out before she could close the gap between them, bear-hugging Dennis like a long-lost son. She nearly squeezed the life out of him, and he clung to her, feeling the tears poke behind his eyes, threatening to come out.

"Miss Dana?" He mumbled into her shoulder, nearly shaking now.

"What the hell are you doin' here, baby? How've you been?" She cooed, rubbing his back to sooth whatever shaking had started.

"What- what are-" he stammered, unable to get the words out correctly. "What're you doin' here? How are you here?"

She pulled back, but held tight to Dennis' biceps as she spoke: "Dunno, kid. I'm that dumbass's emergency contact," she jabbed her thumb at the closed door behind her, referring to Robby.

"What?"

"I'm as confused as you are. I thought the guy had forgotten all about me. Honestly, maybe he did. But he put my landline in his records at some point in the last ten years and," she snorted, "I didn't imagine he's been makin' any new friends to care for him, so... I took the first flight to Pittsburgh yesterday morning. Idiot's lucky my address ain't changed."

"You're still in Knoxville?" Dennis asked.

"Been there all this time. Been waitin' for you to come visit," she poked, but there was a kind of hurt in her eyes. "But y'never did."

"I..." he shook his head. "I'm so sorry. I went home. Back to Nebraska—right before you left Franklin."

She was already nodding, lips pursed. "I had a feelin' he ran you off for good. Well, 'for good,' and yet, here you are."

"It's a real long story," he fidgeted.

Finally, Dana offered a little smile, cupping his cheeks in her hands as she whispered, "Well, you better tell me after you go say hi to Dr. Dumbass, alright, kid? I bet he's been wantin' to see you."

Faced with the reality of having to walk through the door sent a wave of fear through Dennis, but he nodded, pulling away from Dana.

"I'll be back in a minute, I gotta piss like a racehorse."

When she barreled down the hall, Dennis took a deep breath in and held it in his lungs. He held it the whole way through the action: opening the door, stepping inside, shutting the door behind him, and walking until he saw his lover just beyond the corner.

The sight alone stole the breath he'd selfishly tried keeping in his lungs. And it drew silent tears from his eyes.

Robby's eyes were already on him, tracking as the boy stepped slowly into the room. A nurse nodded at bedside, but Dennis did not nod back, he just kept stepping forward, eyes locked on the man in the bed. Fresh, white bandages were secured tight around his head and IVs hooked into his arm. His entire left leg was propped on a platform above the bed and wrapped in a thick cast. His right foot, though wrapped, didn't look near as hurt. Up his forearms and biceps were loose coverings hiding road rash scrapes. He was pale, and his beard was more unkempt than he'd probably have liked.

"Visitor?" The nurse asked.

"...Yes sir," Dennis breathed out, eyes stuck on Robby. "Hey, Dr. Robby," he tried impersonally, wiping a tear with the back of his hand.

"He isn't speaking," the nurse spoke up softly. "He sustained a cerebral contusion and edema. We're lucky he pulled out of the coma so quickly, but speech seems to be lost for now."

There was a deep pit in Dennis' stomach.

"What-" he nearly choked up "-else is present?"

The way Dennis worded the question signaled to the nurse that he was likely a doctor, so he spoke plainly: "He had comminuted tibia and fibula fractures, the motorcycle crushed his left leg. There was extensive soft tissue damage with nerve injury, so our ICU doctors focused on external fixation before casting."

"When'll it heal?"

"It probably won't. Not perfectly," the nurse spoke innocently, but his words were like bells of doom, each one coming out like a hammer to the nail of Robby's coffin. "He'll likely have a limp in the long-term. He should consider a cane for balance."

"A cane-" Dennis reached his hand up, pressing the back of it into his mouth to stop the emotion from trying to spill out. "Will his speech return?"

He dreaded asking, but what else was there left to lose?

"That's difficult to say. Neurology estimates we'll know in the first few weeks if he has a real chance at re-establishing it, but beyond that, it's... in the air."

Robby looked at Dennis like he was the moon in the sky. He looked at him like everything was going to be okay. Or at least like he was trying to convince Dennis, with his eyes alone, that everything was going to be okay.

It did not feel, to Dennis, like anything was going to be okay.

A stifled sob betrayed him, and he sniffled while trying - almost frustratedly - to wipe away the tears which continued falling, compromising what little composure he'd mustered before walking into the room a moment ago.

Awkwardly, the nurse stepped away from the bed and passed Dennis, now standing between him and the door to the room. Quietly, the nurse offered, "I'll give you a moment with him."

When the door clicked closed, Dennis shuffled over to the corner, pulling a dinky plastic chair over to the side of the bed and plopping down in it. He held himself upright for only a moment before slumping forward, burying his eyes in Robby's wrist. The tears were pouring like a rainstorm now.

Every thought to be had about their relationship swarmed his noisy mind, buzzing incessantly as he tried clinging, even now, to the mangled man in the hospital bed. For several minutes longer than anticipated, he sat there, lurched over the bed, crying into Robby's wrist. And the man just let him. There was no protest, and the room itself was peaceful. It was quiet. Not that such a luxury comforted Dennis now.

When he had his fill of it, Dennis leaned back, sitting upright and looking at Robby with his own, red, puffy eyes. A single, nearly-dried tear trail ran down the man's cheek and into his beard.

"Can you hear me?" Dennis whispered with a clamped jaw and shaky voice.

Robby hummed an "Mm" and nodded.

As pain momentarily evolved into anger, Dennis grit his teeth and seethed, albeit quietly:

"You're a fate worse than death, Michael Robinavitch."

Oddly to Dennis, Robby nodded, almost like he expected to hear that. Like he expected to accept it.

"You're a selfish man," he continued to bite out. "And a hard-ass. And you're-" he choked "-a fucking hypocrite."

Dennis' breaths were heavy and frantic now, lips curled downward as he watched Robby process all his harshest words. The words he'd wanted to throw at the man for years. The words he didn't know still needed to be said.

"You'll take care of anyone but yourself," he judged, with all the love and concern in his heart.

So will you, Robby might've argued, had he been able to form words.

And it would've been true. Dennis was, as was always the case, something akin to Robby in spirit. Or rather, Robby saw much of himself in Dennis. Their unfaltering need to care for others, and their total inability to look inward and care for themselves. It always led to their worst choices. In a way, Robby - all these years - may have been trying inadvertently to cure himself by instead curing Dennis of such habits.

"I'd like nothing more than to hate you," Dennis continued, then paused. "...But love is complicated like that, isn't it?"

He took a beat to wipe more tears away, leaning back in his chair, peeling his eyes away from Robby as if to try one last time to dismiss the man once and for all. As if to give one last attempt at letting go of him.

But Robby wouldn't have that, even in his current state.

With what little strength he could muster, Robby slid his hand out over the edge of the bed, resting his index and middle finger on Dennis' kneecap. That contact, embarrassingly, forced all rational thought from Dennis, and he faced Robby once again, brows furrowed. His eyes were like wet pearls upon Robby's, and his chin quivered, teeth chattering.

"I don't..." he reached his own hand out, placing it over Robby's, admitting a truth he'd always been too afraid to speak: "I can't shake you."

Robby's lip twitched. He scanned Dennis' face.

Before the boy could speak any more, the door clicked open from beyond the corner, and he stood from the chair, quietly. Dana walked around that corner just as Robby pulled his hand back to rest on the bed. Seeing the two of them, Dana and Dennis, in the same room was an odd wave of nostalgia for Robby. An intense wave.

She stopped at the foot of the bed, hip cocking to one side as she crossed her arms.

"My Tennessee boys. Back in the same room again." She chuckled softly with a shake of her head before adding, "must be the second-coming of Jesus Christ himself."

"I was busy scolding him," Dennis spoke humorlessly.

"I think you deserve to sneak in a punch or two, as well, kid," she joked.

Looking over at Robby, who - of course - found it funny, Dennis mumbled, "I still might. Haven't decided yet."

The man in the bed probably greatly appreciated Dana's change of tone, although hearing such honest words from Dennis after having been running from them for a decade was nothing short of life-changingly reviving.

He just couldn't verbalize it anymore. At least not for now.

"It is kinda like a family reunion," Dennis admitted fondly after he and Dana bickered for a minute. "We're just missing Heather."

Dana snorted and joked: "She ain't missin' us, I'll tell you that much."

"How come?"

"Girl's making it big in Nashville. Almost out of med school already."

"You're in contact with her?" Dennis grinned, eyes lighting up.

Robby loved seeing the light return to his boy's eyes, even if it was only momentarily.

"'Course I am! I ain't Robby. I'm not an asshat. She's been working hard these past few years, making a name for herself at Meharry. Even takes the time to come over and visit Adam and the kids in Knoxville every couple of months or so."

"How're Lawrence and Vincent?"

"Law's in school now," Dana beamed. "Vinny's still at General Motors, I hear. They treat him real well, I guess."

They killed what could've been an hour, just catching up and reminiscing on everything, all while keeping Robby in the loop. Nurses floated in and out of the room for the duration, occasionally pushing pain meds, which gave Robby more of a chance to smile when Dana cracked her funnier jokes.

Luckily for him, though, he didn't need any pain meds to endure a smile every time he stole glances at Dennis. The evening sun beamed in from the hospital window behind him, casting a heavenly ray of warm light on his pale skin. He looked like home to Robby. Dennis was home to him, in a way.

He felt like the only safe place in the world for Robby.

 

 

 

 

Robby stayed in Presby's SDU for a little over a week more before being discharged under Dana's care. How he'd managed to do that?

He had cleverly begun to write on paper in lieu of speaking, although he was beginning to manage forming simple sounds, which was progress. And it was a fantastic sign that he might actually regain his ability to speak. Not now, certainly, but sometime in the future, if he worked very hard.

With his newfound skills at scribbling horribly onto little notepads or important documents, Robby practically begged Dana to get him out of the hospital under the guise that she'd care for him. Of course, he didn't expect that she'd actually take up the role of caretaker during his recovery period: but she did.

She'd already called Adam the day she landed in Pittsburgh, and now she'd sorted things out at the University of Tennessee's hospital, where she worked—settled effectively all of her vacation days for the year to care for Robby as much as she could.

He got to see the kind of nurse she had become over the decade. She was a skilled woman with a big heart. Or maybe she just had a soft spot for Robby and for Dennis. Either way, she helped Robby maneuver around his suddenly cramped apartment, and she helped administer his medications. She observed him and helped to undress him and bag the large cast on his left leg whenever he needed to bathe.

Now she felt less like a sister and more like a mother to him.

The first few days as Robby's caretaker were a kind of trial and error session. It turned out, caring for Dr. Robinavitch was about as frustrating as one would expect. Even as broken as he was, physically speaking, he still had the spirit of an independent son of a bitch in him. He wanted to do things himself—cooking, bathing, moving from the sofa to his bedroom, moving from his bed to the balcony to people-watch. 

"Don't you dare try and get off that couch yourself, crazy bastard," Dana would say, tossing her wooden spoon onto a paper plate next to the stove.

She'd also occasionally sneak in a "I'm havin' a glass of your good whiskey," which always made Robby squirm in protest, unable to get up from the couch.

He didn't actually care that she indulged a glass or two those first few nights, just liked keeping up appearances; he wasn't normal if he wasn't feigning agitation at his favorite nurse.

One night, about five days into their routine, there was a knock on Robby's front door.

When Dana opened it, cooking apron still secured around her, she greeted curtly, "What can I do for you, ma'am?"

"Hello! This is Dr. Robinavitch's place, yes?"

"Who's askin'?" Dana spoke, almost like a bouncer.

"I'm Dr. Samira Mohan," she smiled politely, balancing on one arm a big dish covered in foil. "We work together at Presby. I worked under him a long time ago at a clinic. It felt-" she gestured to the dish in her hands "-wrong not to at least leave a 'get-well' gift of some kind."

Dana glanced at the dish, then back up at Samira. "You worked in Franklin?"

The doctor's eyes lit up, and she nodded with a grin. "I did. You must've as well, if you know of it, yes?"

A fond little smirk crept onto Dana's face. "Sure did." She paused for a second, then decided - without consulting Robby - to offer: "Why don't you come in a while? I've got dinner on the stove anyway, we can..." she searched for the words, "mash our dishes together and have a proper meal, hm?"

Samira wouldn't decline such an offer, so she stepped into Robby's apartment, taking in the poorly decorated - or rather, undecorated - walls and allowed Dana to take the dish from her. They rounded the corner straight into the kitchen, and Dana sat the dish down on the kitchen island before heading just behind the sofa and ruffling Robby's hair.

She leaned down just a tad and said, "We got a friend for dinner tonight, Robby. You better be feelin' social."

"Mhm," he approved, hoping it was Dennis Whitaker.

But it was not Dennis' voice that said: "How've you been doing, Dr. Robinavitch?"

Still, Samira Mohan was a nice surprise. When she rounded to the front of the sofa and eyed him, concern and familiarity in equal measure glinted her eyes. She clasped her hands in front of her all shyly—a far cry from her demeanor at work, where she dominated.

"D-" he tried the consonant a few times, struggling greatly, but managing to mumble the word, "doctor," with a strained nod.

"Miss Mohan, help him to the table, will you?" Dana asked, pulling a pan out of the oven with her mitts on. "We're having beef meatloaf and boiled potatoes. The taters' don't come with butter, though, so I won't feel bad if you pass on 'em."

"I'm just grateful for the food," Samira assured, offering her arm in front of Robby.

He sat his book down on the coffee table—Dana had been making him read words aloud on random pages to help rehabilitate his speech. Samira held firm as he pulled up to his feet, keeping his weight on his right leg.

"Surely you've got crutches, Dr. Robinavitch? A wheelchair?" She strained, hoisting his arm over her shoulder for balance.

"He's got both, and he's too damn stubborn to use them," Dana snorted, pulling the pot of boiled potatoes off the stove.

Robby groaned, swatting dismissively at the woman.

"Gimme more of that attitude and I'll eat your share of dinner, big guy," she jokingly warned with a pointed finger.

Samira managed to navigate him to a seat at the end of his dining table, carefully sitting him down and using a stool under the table to prop his thick-casted left leg up. A minute later, Dana had served up the meatloaf on a big, white serving plate, dumping the little potatoes into a bowl and placing them at the center of the table. When she returned to the kitchen island and peeled back the foil on Samira's dish, an earthy, tangy aroma teased her nose.

"Ooh," she voiced her intrigue with excited eyes, "this smells delicious. What'd you make?"

"It's supposed to be sambar-" Samira adjusted Robby's shirt for him, then took her own seat at the table "-but this one's more of a... lentil soup. I hope you don't mind, I added cooked carrots and drumsticks. Tamarind for tanginess," she noted, "but the real fun though is the spices. My mother always put mustard seeds, curry leaves, and a just a little jaggery to bring it all together."

Dana smiled. She quite liked how personable Samira Mohan was. She'd scold Robby later - for the millionth time this week - for not having kept in contact over the years and telling her about the woman.

"Sounds delicious."

"It's more of a breakfast thing," Samira added awkwardly, as if it would make any difference to Dana or Robby.

When Dana took her seat at the table, she enjoyed the little break from serving Robby. Samira took up that job since she sat closer, helping to reach for each dish and serve food. Meatloaf slice and potatoes went on the plate, then a few spoons of sambar went into his bowl.

They ate in relative silence at first, only Robby couldn't.

Tremors riddled the man's hand as he tried lifting the spoon from the sambar bowl. So much so that by the time it had reached his mouth, the savory soup had all but splashed off the spoon, leaving little to enjoy.

"Robby," Dana mothered from across the table. "Let Samira help you."

Immediately, the man shook his head. "N-" he struggled, "no." 

That shame weighed upon him quite visibly in the dim dining room light. Samira tried not to stare, but it was odd to see the man she'd looked up to for so many years be reduced to such a form. It was human to ponder such a change in character, but it also squeezed her heart to see his utter disinterest in being helped.

So, he struggled through the rest of the meal, using all his strength to at least not make a mess as he ate, battling against the wretched tremors in his hands.

"He's always been a stubborn son of a bitch," Dana sighed.

Samira offered an amused half-smirk. "I've picked up on that over the years."

"So you worked with him in Franklin, hm?"

"Oh, yes! I was in and out of the clinic a few times as a patient. He and I connected at some point, and around the fall of '62 he reached out with an open position." She sipped sambar from her spoon, then wiped her mouth and continued: "It helped a lot. Gave me great experience to wrap up med school with."

"You didn't meet Dennis Whitaker, did you?" Dana asked passingly.

"I-" Samira chuckled, "did not remember meeting him, no. But funny enough, he ended up at Presby last month for an ED rotation. Said he remembered me. I felt awful."

"Don't sweat it," Dana sipped at her glass of water. "Kid couldn't have been there much longer before you got there. Robby made sure of that."

That comment should've tensed the air in the room, but it really didn't. Samira seemed to know, generally, what Dana meant. Clearly she'd gotten to know Robby's less charming side at some point, in some capacity.

"I'm sure he did," she glanced at the man, who pretended to pay no mind. "He almost ran him off at Presby, too, on Whitaker's first day."

"Ah," Dana cocked her head, one eyebrow raised as she stared. "Some things never change, huh?"

With slack eyelids, Robby offered a wholly unamused look at Dana.

 

 

Another week passed, and Dennis had pointedly avoided being in a room alone with Robby the whole while. He'd visit the man's depressing apartment under the guise of wanting to catch up with Dana Evans, which wasn't much of a guise anyway; he did so enjoy speaking with her.

But come end of week, she needed to head back to Tennessee, and Dennis could not bring himself to turn away from Michael Robinavitch.

"So you're going?" Trinity leaned against the fridge, watching as Dennis finished cramming what few belongings he had into his bag.

It was the same bag he'd stuffed his things into when she dragged him into her apartment for the first time back in July. Nearly two months ago now. But it felt like a lifetime had passed in those two months.

"Our friend's flight leaves in a few hours," Dennis spoke softly—detached.

"Dr. Robby's got heavy pockets, right?" She half-heartedly protested: "Can't he hire a caretaker?"

"He wouldn't do that if a gun was pointed to his head." Dennis spoke like he wished Robby would, and in truth: he did wish. He wished this unexplainable burden of love did not fall upon his shoulders. He wished Robby would make it easier for him and hire a stranger to care for him so he could leave this all behind once and for all.

But then he'd just worry whether the stranger knew Robby like he did.

Dennis gently added, "I'm hoping I'll only be there a few days."

That wording forced a scoff from Trinity. "You don't have to do this if it's not—"

"—I have to," he nodded, zipping up his bag. "And... I don't expect you to fully understand why," Dennis looked up, eyeing Trinity with fond, kind eyes. She was guarded as usual, arms crossed, body still leaned against the fridge to feign nonchalance. But her eyes told a different story.

Avoiding the word 'love,' Trinity made light of the elephant in the room by jabbing: "I know why, it's 'cause you're hung up on him."

Dennis chuckled.

"Fate worse than death," he recited, almost under his breath.

They both knew it was far, far more complicated than that. Trinity Santos was an excellent judge of character when she really allowed herself to see a person, and she'd seen many sides of Dennis this summer. She'd seen his smiles and his tears, his dreams and his worst fears come true. Trinity had seen enough to know that 'love' was a rudimentary way to describe his ties to Michael Robinavitch.

Those ties truly lay somewhere near identity. Somewhere around need. Somewhere in front of or behind purpose.

"Well," she sighed.

"Well," he returned.

Trinity pushed herself upright, arms still crossed, eyes still fixated on Dennis'.

"You better not lose yourself," she offered with a sort of finality.

Dennis did not immediately respond.

"Not that you much could, anyway," Trinity added, then paused, feeling a wave of sentimentality wash over her as she added: "You'll always be a Huckleberry."

A familiar little smile tugged at his lips.

 

 

"Phenytoin orally in the morning, afternoon, and at bedtime, and he'll need refills bi-weekly for a few months, per Dr. Lounds." Dana held up two bottles for Dennis to eyeball as he set his bag down on the sofa next to where Robby sat. She then shook the second bottle, saying, "Diazepam to help if he's havin' trouble stayin' still at night."

"Staying still?" Dennis couldn't help but glance at Robby, who focused intensely on the book in his lap.

"He's got some occasional muscle spasms that'll probably linger for..." she trailed off, but her eyes suggested a long time. "There's also-" she sauntered over to Robby's kitchen island, setting down the pill bottles and pointing at the medicine cabinet "-Dramamine and then a buncha' over-the-counters for pain."

Frantically, Dana moved over to the pot on the stove, cursing under her breath as she peered into it and found a burning mess of what might've once been edible. It was certainly not so, now.

"What's for dinner?" Dennis asked, meandering around the living room, soaking in how dreary it all felt.

"Failed attempt at sambar," she grumbled.

"...Sambar?"

"Well," Dana sighed, resting her hands on her hips and giving the pot an offensive glance, "mine was more like a mystery soup. Dammit— I'm sorry, kid. Wanted to have a dinner ready for you and Mr. Grumpy by the time I headed out."

"Hey," Dennis soothed, "It's all good. I'll... toss something together for him. You've bent over backward these past few days."

"Yeah, well," she shrugged. "...You do what you gotta do for family."

Family.

That word rang in Robby's ears like stinging guilt as he eavesdropped from the couch. It was guilt simply because she still considered him family after ten years of utter silence. Clearly Tennessee memories bound their souls more tightly than he'd imagined. More than he deserved.

"It's, uh," Dennis tried, a little awkwardly, "unfortunate we didn't get to catch up much."

"You've been busy with school. Nobody's holdin' that against you. Least of all anyone in this room," she chuckled. "How's it treatin' you?"

"I'm almost finished," he nodded. "Picked the internship at Presby, so that's lined up for next spring."

Those should've been excited words, but now they were just hollow.

"Yeah, well," Dana walked over to the main hallway toward the entrance of the apartment, slugging a cream-colored tote over her shoulder and grabbing her wheeled suitcase by the handle. "You'll survive it, kid. You always do."

 Hardly, he thought.

Dana wheeled her suitcase over to the living room, parking it right in front of Robby and cocking a hip as she looked down, giving him a fond, feisty look.

"You better behave yourself," she spoke light-heartedly at first. Then she squatted down, just in front of the sofa, looking up at Robby with eyes like hot daggers, lowering her voice so that Dennis could not hear from in the kitchen.

"I mean it, Michael Robinavitch. If you still feel the same about that sweet boy as you did in Franklin, you better start takin' this life a little more seriously. 'Cause it's the only one you've got with him."

Robby's jaw clenched, but he nodded. Rather than embarrass himself trying to get a word out, he offered what could've very well been the most sincere look into Dana's eyes that he'd ever granted. She - so very briefly - saw into his soul, just enough to know that he was thankful for all she'd done for him.

"I'll be seeing you around," she spoke at normal volume again, standing at full height again and grabbing her suitcase handle. As she walked back into the kitchen toward the front door of the apartment, she called back, "And you better not change that emergency contact!"

Dennis stifled a single, soft chuckle. Dana gestured with her hand for him to follow her, so he did. Out into the hallway, and then out the front door of the apartment, then down the stairs and out the door of the building, he followed her. The sun had long since begun to set in the sky, leaving them swamped by blue shadows cast upon the front wall of the building.

They walked down the concrete steps and onto the sidewalk, side by side as Dana's suitcase rattled with each crack in the ground. The street itself wasn't busy, but the commotion of Pittsburgh echoed through the world around them even from the furthest corners of the city.

Dana turned to face Dennis, looking at him in a way that she'd never done before. It almost startled him when she went in for a hug, without any warning. She held him there for a long minute before pulling back, wiping a single tear from her cheek as she swam in his eyes.

"You're a strong man, you know that?"

He swallowed. He needed to swallow the emotion that bubbled forth from those simple words. That little reminder.

"You listen to me, Dennis Whitaker," she said. "You're never gonna fight a tougher battle than Robby. And it's gonna be a long, long battle. But if ever there was a person on this hopeless planet that could finally win it, I'd bet on you."

She choked up a little, something Dennis had never seen from Dana. Something that forced more emotion into the lump stuck in his throat.

"If that fight ever gets to be too much," she nodded, "you call me, and I'll come runnin'. You hear me?"

"Yes ma'am," he swallowed hard.

She patted Dennis shoulder, taking in the sight of him one last time. "It's good to see how far you've come, kid."

Dennis nodded, and they shared one more fond look before Dana pulled away.

"Alright, go get him. He's gonna need dinner soon or he'll get cranky," she spoke casually, but Dennis could hear the sniffling of her nose. He could almost hear the tears leaving her eyes.

"Of course," he nodded, scurrying away, back up the concrete steps. Wiping a tear of his own, he could not stop himself from turning back and blurting: "Miss Dana?"

His voice echoed in the street. She turned back, stopping her suitcase.

Dennis opened his mouth, but words did not immediately come out. For a moment, he stood in pause, silenced by the lump in his throat. He certainly wanted to say more, but she had a plane to catch.

"It was real good to see you, too," he offered meekly and with a little rural twang, which she'd assumed he'd all but lost over the years.

Seeing that—hearing his timid soul from Franklin, Tennessee jump out for a split second forced a touch of a smile onto her lips. She offered one last nod.

Dennis didn't stay any longer, making his way back up to Robby's apartment with haste.

The place was dead quiet save for Robby's occasional page turn from the sofa. Dennis hadn't been alone in a room with Robby in a while, and he wished he could've kept it that way.

He meandered around for a while, refamiliarizing himself with the place, despite having just been toured around and given the rundown of Robby's daily routine by Dana. It wasn't an extensive routine. Robby bathed every other day thanks to the massive cast on his left leg and his general inability to stand upright. He ate twice a day—hated breakfast. He spent the majority of his time on the sofa, left leg propped up as he read and tried to sound out words.

That would be the gist of it all.

But for right now... Dennis needed to figure out dinner.

"I could... try a soup, or something," he spoke from the kitchen, unwilling to hang near Robby. "Or..." he looked around the kitchen, eyes landing on a little worn leatherbound book with tiny, loose pages sticking out. "I could..."

He walked over, picking up the book, which was no larger than his hand. He examined the front and the back, then flipped through the first few loose pages.

It was a recipe book.

"I could try, uh..." he trailed off, flipping through pages and pages of dishes that were - as he'd very confidently guess - beyond his skillset. "Christ," he groaned.

"Not," Robby spoke hoarsely from the sofa, immediately grabbing Dennis' attention. "Not," he struggled, "h-" and the words tried coming out, but nearly didn't. "Hungry."

Dennis rolled his eyes.

"No, you need to eat."

Robby let out a sound akin to protest.

"You need to eat dinner, Robby," the younger man bit out, agitation already festering in his throat.

"No," the older man tried more confidently.

"I won't let you make this difficult on day one—"

Before Dennis could finish, a page slipped out from the book, twirling in its descent until it landed like a snowflake onto the kitchen counter. It would've been an entirely innocent little piece of paper, had it not said atop the page in bold, pen-scribbled letters: Chicken Pot Pie - For Mr. Whitaker.

Dennis squinted, ignoring whatever noises Robby half-heartedly threw out from his spot on the sofa. Leaning down, Dennis examined the page closer. It was torn on one end, like it'd been ripped from a journal of some kind.

He ran his fingers over the handwriting. Little divots in the paper from the pen that had made its marks. It was a detailed recipe of the very chicken pot pie that Robby had made for Dennis so often in Franklin. The pot pie that he'd always passed off as casual. The pot pie that he swore was just 'a dish he'd made too much of to eat himself.'

It seemed Robby meant it when he said he'd saved the recipe after all these years. But it wasn't just saved, and it wasn't just a recipe, it seemed.

It was... always for Dennis alone.

The title - and he scanned it over and over again just to be sure his eyes weren't playing tricks - clearly stated: 'For Mr. Whitaker.' The date atop the page on the outer corner read September 1960.

They'd only just met by then.

The world beneath Dennis' feet seemed to thin out until it was nothing but clouds, and he fell through them so gently. It was a silent realization, but one that demanded him to reassess everything he'd ever known about his relationship with Robby. And it forced him to do so right there in the man's kitchen.

All this time, even since their very first encounters, Robby had been doing these things for Dennis. Exclusively for Dennis. It begged dangerous questions.

He wondered, what was it all for? 

He wondered, was this of pity or of love?

And he wondered, If the latter, Michael Robinavitch, then have you loved me all this time?

The answer didn't hide itself, either: when Dennis flipped the little page over, another full side of Robby's writing faced him. The entire page was scribbled into with his horrible handwriting, reading:

 

August 13, 1962

Dennis Whitaker, 

I wish I hadn't pushed you to the ground on Friday. Dana having walked out frightened me. I did not want her to see us. This hurts me deeply, and I shudder to think how it hurt you. My fears lead me to pushing you away, because I fear I'll hurt you beyond repair. I fear the world will hurt you and I. 

Of course, I've been no good at making these feelings clear to you, so now I'll write them down, ready to read them aloud to you next time you stay afterhours at the clinic. Next time we share a moment alone together, where I feel safest. When I've got you to myself, I will address the thought that would require a million words. The confession that would require all my life to make you truly understand. 

It's a confession that I could cut short simply by screaming aloud that I love you. 

But that would only be the very simplest beginning of it. 

What came next would be a lifetime together. Sharing a bed. Hiding away in the winter when it's cold, that I might find an excuse to warm you up by a fire with your favorite foods at the ready. We'd find a forest all to ourselves in the summer, yelling at the top of our lungs as we stick to one another under the hot sun laying on riverbanks. I'd make love to you in the fall when all else seems bleak amidst the decaying Tennessee tree-lines, the ones you always love to stare at when you've got idle time. I'd hold you in my arms as we soak in spring showers when yet another silent winter has passed us by.

I want you to watch me grow old this way. We'd do it again, every year, like real lovers do. We'd lay to rest only after plucking every cord of love in our bodies. After having grown our branches as far as they'd reach, together. This is the love I crave from you. It's the life I'd like to have. You are the cure to a sickness that has coursed in my heart for only as long as I haven't had you. 

You know the man I am, and I know the man you are. We save lives, none more desperately than our own. But each day, those lives wear down on us, and when you one day dive into the world of medicine, you'll begin to share that same darkness. I want to shield you from it. I want to be there for you when the days are long and the clouds are thick. I'd like to be the air in your lungs. I'd like to be the net to catch you, because I've lived this all before, and I want you to live it better.

I know you need me. I know you needed me. I've known since the day you woke up in my tub, flushed and overheated. I only know it because I needed you too. I need you too. 

I'm not sure I'd live so good a life anymore without you. The baggage I carry is only made lighter when you're with me. The night above me gives way only to your stars. The water only leaves my lungs when you kiss me. The chill under my skin only settles with your body on mine. You're the wind that shoves me away from the cliff's edge. You are what keeps me alive, and I did not know it, or accept it. Not soon enough.

I love you so desperately. I need you so terribly. 

Selfishly, I pray you afford me a fraction of this love I do not deserve in return.

 

Dennis stood paralyzed, hand gripping the edge of the kitchen counter to keep from keeling over. He trembled, teeth gritting against one another. There was no escape from the tears behind his eyes, so to save himself the embarrassment, he spoke to Robby without turning around:

"We'll-" he nearly choked "-figure out dinner in a minute. I need to use the restroom."

He slipped the little page back into the book with all the other pages, closing it and sticking it back in its corner. Then Dennis bolted for the bathroom without so much as glancing to see if Robby had turned around and seen him. He probably didn't.

Dennis locked the door, then stood leaning in front of the mirror above the sink, simply looking at himself.

He wasn't sure what kind of man he saw looking back at him, but it was difficult, given his vision was utterly distorted by the unceasing flow of decades-old tears.

 

 

Dennis gave himself a little longer to compose himself, then he splashed cold water in his face, braving the kitchen again and showing an immensely improved amount of patience to Robby. Through single-word responses, Robby managed to convey enough that Dennis was able to make a dinner they both enjoyed.

Having decided not to force the man off the sofa and to the dining table, Dennis brought dinner to him. They ate in the living room, pretending like old friends that there was no significance to sharing such a silence with one another.

But one stole glances at the other any chance they got.

"I'll get the bath running," Dennis said, having finished washing their plates off in the sink.

"No," Robby sighed like he was tired of struggling to move around. "T-" he stuttered a few times before getting out the word "tomorrow."

Dennis couldn't bring himself to argue that night, not after what he'd read. Not when finally - after over a decade - he truly knew what was going on in Michael Robinavitch's head.

"...Okay," he resigned sweetly in what was almost a whisper.

After having given his meds, Dennis fell asleep on the sofa that night, head in Robby's lap as the older man read from his book, occasionally trying to sound out words, newfound determination fueled by the company he now had.

He, too, fell asleep at some point, and they began their first full day together the next morning. It was a simple day, and Dennis - to Robby's amusement - stuck around him the whole day, never really leaving his sight for more than a moment.

In the afternoon, Dennis pulled 'Principles of Internal Medicine' from his bag, perhaps as a show of affection. Robby saw the book—recognized it as his own from a decade ago. Realized that Dennis had held onto it all this time. 

They did not address that, though. It just hung in the air like one sweet note in a melody.

When, once again, the sun had set over the windows of the glass sliding door leading out to the balcony from the living room, Dennis stretched, standing up from the couch.

"Alright, you need to bathe tonight."

Robby did not protest, only nodded.

They struggled into the bathroom together and Dennis undressed Robby until he was bare. They waited until the water ran hot, then Dennis sheathed the man's massive cast with plastic and helped him into the tub, leaving his left leg out and elevated as much as possible.

He washed Robby's hair first. Then washed his body as best he could. Robby, really, could've done some of that himself, but Dennis took charge. Dennis did it instead, for whatever reason, caring in full for the man.

As he scrubbed his shoulders, Robby let out a noise almost like a chuckle.

Robby tried, "F-" and stumbled the consonant a few times before succeeding "-funny."

Dennis softly cooed, "What's funny?" He continued to scrub, soothing Robby's skin with water he'd cup in his hands and pour over as he washed the suds away.

"This," Robby tapped the tub. "Was..." determined, he struggled the words out, "you."

Realizing immediately what Robby was referring to, Dennis stopped the ministrations of his soap-covered hand, brows furrowing. He swallowed, looking down at the tub, which looked so strikingly like the tub he'd met Robby for the first time in. 

The one in Franklin, that Robby had used to save Dennis from heat exhaustion. The very tub that set this whole ride in motion.

Everything had come full circle, and Robby had just pointed it out. He was right, too. Dennis now cared for him in the same way that Robby did all those years ago. Dennis felt it was finally his turn to be the one that saved the other.

And perhaps, in doing so, Dennis was saving himself, too.

"You saved my life that day, y'know?" He whispered, continuing his scrubbing idly. "Changed everything." Dennis allowed a smile, hardly visible, to creep onto the corner of his lips.

The only thing Robby said in response was, "Mine."

Dennis' brows knitted together. He wasn't really sure what Robby meant. The one-word responses were, for the most part, effectively communicated. This, however, was a little more difficult to interpret, as desperately as he wanted to.

Seeing the soft confusion on Dennis' face, Robby repeated more confidently:

"Mine."

"Yours?"

Robby pulled his hand out of the water with newfound strength, reaching up and cupping Dennis' cheek, swimming in his tired, familiar eyes. His thumb rubbed little crescents just over the younger man's pronounced eyebags, like he could wipe the decades-long fatigue of love from Dennis' skin.

"Mine."

Chapter End Notes

I want to (very seriously today) thank you all so immensely for reading along with this story.

It's been such an honor to feel like I'm in a safe space to share this thing piece of myself that I've been wanting to create for over a year now, and to have been able to do it in this way where so many of you resonate with it and relate to it and understand it... is one of the most beautiful gifts I've been given in recent years.

I do need to reiterate that I love these characters, and will continue to staunchly see them only as their characters. They live in my little bubble inside of 'The Overs'. <3

And with that said... The Overs is... well, almost over! One more chapter to go.

Until then, look forward to my official song for the fanfiction, which comes out Wednesday!

-

Post-chapter art sharing:

@moontilts beautifully drew a scene from chapter 13!

Chapter 14: The Overs

Chapter Summary

"The one who found a cure for Michael Robinavitch."

Chapter Notes

Chapter 14’s song is “The Overs” inspired by the fanfiction.

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Nashville, 1979

 

Michael Robinavitch, most of the time, didn't believe he deserved a life as kind as the one he now lived. He'd die before sitting down and spilling his innermost troubles to a professional, but he made up for that with fleeting social honesty among colleagues.

And old friends.

"It's a little strange to see you like this," he spoke especially honestly to Dr. Heather Collins, who sat across from him at the desk in her charming, cozy office full of stuffed bookshelves, vibrant potted plants, and earthy-brown walls.

The woman crossed her arms and snorted. "It's a little strange to see you at all."

"Writing letters was getting a bit tiring," Robby quipped.

"Dennis Whitaker doesn't seem to mind writing to me," she remarked. "And he's been doing it for years."

"Oh, I'm aware."

Dennis had taken Dana's emergency Pittsburgh visit as an opportunity to keep in contact with his Franklin family, but it took him years to convince Robby to do the same, for Dana and for Heather. Even then, Robby was a horrendous pen pal on his own.

Through scarcely exchanged letters, Dennis and Robby learned that Heather Collins made it through medical school and multiple years of residency, taking up the role of a clinical instructor and physician at Meharry Medical College in Nashville.

She'd become a formidable face in the medical landscape of Tennessee, focusing her efforts equally on doctoring and teaching the next generation of faces who shared stories like her own.

"You've been very busy," he wore a polite smile, fidgeting with his cane where it rested on the arm of his chair. "It's been... what? Twelve years now?"

"You knew where I was," she nodded. "Could've reached out anytime."

"I do apologize," he spoke with sincerity. Then, softly, he added: "I was busy in the trenches."

Heather kissed her teeth. She crossed one leg over the other, leaning back in her chair as she eyed the man. He had a cane now—she'd certainly bring that up. He looked a little weathered, older than the last time they'd seen one another. The man's voice also possessed a very subtle but constant shake now.

But most noticeably, there was a distinct lack of darkness behind his eyes.

"You've been stuck in those trenches for a long, long time. Longer than you like to admit," Heather reminded. "But," she shifted gears, adopting a more caring tone, "...I hope you've started finding your way out?"

He thought of Dennis Whitaker as he nodded, without skipping a beat. "Trying."

"Not without-" Heather gestured to Robby's cane "-picking up some wounds along the way, I see."

Robby looked down at the cane like he'd forgotten it was there, then looked back up at Heather. "It's just for a little help with balance," he disregarded. "I was in an accident a few years ago."

Unsurprised, Heather said, "Must've been serious."

Robby looked down, digging a nail into his right hand in attempt to stop the subtle tremors. "It set me back a bit, is all. Whitaker actually... he looked after me while I was recovering. Dana, too, for the first few weeks. Surely she told you?"

Heather shook her head. "Of course she did. I just liked to see if you'd admit as much to me face-to-face."

"Ah."

A fond smile threatened Heather's otherwise stoic expression. "How is Dennis Whitaker?"

"Doesn't he write you?"

Heather shrugged. "I think you rubbed off on him: he seldom opens up about his personal life. I almost wonder if he has one."

Robby knew. He just felt the need to act as though he didn't.

"He's alright, I imagine," Robby finally answered, guarded. "We c-" the word caught in his throat for a split second as a tremor shook through "-catch up sometimes. He still lives in Pittsburgh."

Heather found it almost amusing that Robby still - as it seemed - wanted to believe she was unaware of his fondness for that boy. As though she hadn't fallen in love with Robby herself at one point, knowing every secret the man's heart whispered—how it'd always thumped a little louder when he spoke of Dennis Whitaker.

Sensing her oddly perceptive reaction, Robby fidgeted for only a moment more before changing the subject entirely:

"I'm in Nashville because I," he sighed, rubbing the back of his head, "wanted to ask for your help."

"Ah, I see. Have you forgotten how to suture? Came all this way to ask me rather than embarrass yourself in front of your colleagues?" She joked with the kind of sarcasm Robby was used to dishing out. Only he would know she was joking.

And he did. He chuckled.

"My lunch is," Heather checked her watch, "twenty minutes more. I've got a class to teach at two-thirty."

Straight to the point to respect her time, Robby said, "As you know, ABMS rejected the request for Emergency Medicine to be recognized as a specialty in our field."

"Three years ago. In '76," Heather squinted.

Robby nodded, covering one hand over the other for a moment to still the tremor that shook through it.

"What? You'd like me to override that decision?" She asked facetiously.

"You've still got jokes, Heather Collins," Robby grinned. "I wish either of us had that power."

"It would simplify a lot, I'm sure," she agreed.

"But..." Robby's eyebrows raised like he had a proposition. "You're a very well-respected physician in Internal Medicine."

"And you're the renowned medical director of Presbyterian-University Hospital in Pittsburgh. Are we measuring titles, Robby?"

"Yours'll be fancier than mine before too much longer," he stated. "But— yes. More importantly, I'm recently part of a board of physicians looking to push to get Emergency Medicine approved, and our new angle," he nodded his head side to side anxiously, "needs us to involve ourselves with other departments."

"You aim to... attach yourselves," Collins surmised. "To coalesce."

"If we're partnered with Internal Medicine, among other boards that'll have us, the push for recognition would be much harder for ABMS to ignore."

"I should've known you didn't fly all the way down here just to see me," Heather teased. "Is my seat more interesting to you than our revivable friendship?"

"Oh, come now, doctor," he quipped. "I've never been terribly good at friendships."

"I'm acutely aware," she smiled fondly, though she did mean that. "But in all seriousness, Robby... I don't know. If they rejected the first proposition, what exactly could Emergency Medicine offer that would convince ABMS to rethink their decision?"

Robby did not immediately respond.

Waiting for his response, which still hadn't come, Heather added: "Not at all to dissent. I only— I imagine it's a waste of time and resources on your end to chase a dream like this any further if it isn't plausible by its second go-around."

"With all due respect to our colleagues alike, Dr. Collins," Robby frowned. "You and I know far better than most how much the country needs a department like this. America's in deep, deep shit. And we've seen that with our own eyes. We both know what the clinic wanted—needed to be."

"Franklin," she muttered as though the mere thought of it washed everything else away, if only momentarily.

Robby nodded.

After a pause, Heather raised her eyebrows. "Seems like a lifetime ago."

"By now, it w-" a tremor stopped him for a second "-was."

A light flashed behind Collins' eyes. She imagined what might've become of her, or her brother Lawrence, if not for the clinic all those years ago. Robby's quick thinking, the experience with trauma patients, the flexibility. Utter flexibility within a medical space.

At the time, it was a wholly unorthodox means of local medical practice, and even she knew that, with her previously limited view of the medical landscape. But what they'd managed to accomplish at the Robinavitch Health Center were miracles. They'd saved lives that hospitals wouldn't dare try to save. She'd witnessed Robby sprinting along decisions that most wouldn't dare tread.

That was the dream of Emergency Medicine. That was, Heather hoped, what Robby wanted to convince her to support.

And how could she not?

"I suppose we should want to leave the country a little better than we found it." Heather asked, "I assume you're not in Nashville for very long?"

"Just for the day," he offered a fond smile.

"Give..." she hesitated only momentarily before steeling herself. "Give me an address to send the letter to-" she raised a finger "-if I'm able to convince the board."

He didn't react visibly, but Robby's heart danced behind his ribs.

It was, of course, a treat to see Heather Collins after years of having little clue what she was up to, but equally important was her ability to persuade the Board of Internal Medicine. The fight against stress and overwhelm in Presby's ED had only gotten significantly worse over the last seven years, and though Robby hadn't worked directly on the floor of the ED in that time, he now managed it.

"P-" he struggled through a tremor, then played it off by clearing his throat "-pen?"

Heather pulled open the drawer on her side of the desk, taking out a shining Montblanc pen and handing it to Robby. She leaned back in her chair, arms folding as she watched him set his pocket-sized notebook on the desk and lean forward.

He wrote slow, and his right hand shook with tremors every few seconds, forcing a frown from him as he concentrated. It didn't help that he could feel Heather's observant eyes upon him. When he finished, Robby gently tore the page, sliding it over.

Heather peeled it off the desk, pinching it between her fingers and squinting at horrendous, nearly illegible handwriting.

Seeing her strain to read it, Robby swallowed. He felt a wave of insecurity bubble through his chest, like he hated being seen for his weaknesses. There was only person he'd become comfortable showing his faults to, and it was not Heather Collins.

"I can't imagine the work you had to put in to recover," she spoke cautiously, but tenderly.

Robby's jaw tensed. "...Today's just an off day. You should've seen me in the beginning. Couldn't even form full sentences for the first few months."

"That must be very difficult for a smart-aleck."

The man chuckled softly. "Oh, yes."

"How did you survive it?" Heather's tone was teasing, but there was a sincerity in her eyes like she really was curious.

Robby took a breath and said, "Finally learned to lean on people a little bit."

Heather paused for a beat, letting a genuine, warm smile onto her face.

She still had a lot of respect for Robby despite his many shortcomings, which she'd become intimately familiar with in the 1960s. Hearing - from his own mouth - that he'd finally begun to allow others to help him was a nice development. A step in the right direction.

A shame that it only took so many years and so many more mistakes to get there.

"Are you... quite happy in Pittsburgh?"

Robby looked up, unable to entirely suppress the light in his eyes. "I am," he nodded. "Very much so."

"Is he happy?" Heather asked perceptively.

Robby stared for a second. He didn't need to ask who she meant.

The tremors in his right hand picked up pace, then completely stopped for a moment, and his heart began to beat a little louder in his chest. "I..." he settled his eyes onto Heather's. Softly but guardedly, he said, "I certainly hope he is."

That was enough for Heather Collins.

She let her smile speak for the relief in her heart. She'd always carried a piece of Dennis Whitaker with her, even having not seen him for almost twenty years. He was the kindest soul on that side of Franklin, and she always wondered where he might end up.

"The one who found a cure for Michael Robinavitch," she mused.

Deflecting the swell of emotion, Robby spoke objectively and without conviction, "It might've been you, at one point."

"Sure," Heather pondered, smile still firm on her face. "I did love you. And I'm certain you loved me."

She paused, inhaling, then exhaling. In the sweetest, most familiar voice, she said:

"But the love you and I had was a dive in the pond. What you've got now... it's the very air you swim up for. Maybe it always was."

 

 

Robby took his lonely flight home to Pittsburgh, landing late in the night.

He tipped the taxi driver generously when they arrived at his apartment building. Plucking his glasses from his face and stuffing his journal back into his bag, Robby stepped out and into the unusually cold January night, leaning on his cane when necessary. He tugged his sherpa especially tight over his white button-down, holding tight to his day-bag as he hooked his black frames with his finger.

Inside the building, familiar, home-like heat enveloped him, and he took the elevator up to his floor, rubbing his forehead the whole way up. He was lucky the pounding in his skull was only a headache.

Migraines had become a pretty normal disruption in his life since the motorcycle crash.

When Robby made it into his apartment, he shut the door behind him quietly, locking it and slugging his bag and cane onto the floor. He left it there, lazily, as he limped into his kitchen, setting his reading glasses down and leaning himself into the island counter, eyeing the birthday card he'd yet to sign as it rested there.

Robby rubbed his palms over his face, up and down as he groaned his futile protest against the stress and the body-aches.

When he had his fill of grumbling, the man plucked a schmear and smoked salmon bagel from his fridge. It'd been sitting there since the prior morning, but Robby did not mind. He couldn't be bothered to cook anything, especially not so late.

After downing two Tylenol pills, Robby crumbled onto his couch, splaying his legs out, bagel in one hand as the other rubbed soothing circles into his aching left thigh. He hadn't even taken off his sherpa, just unbuckled the belt of his jeans, pulled his tucked dress shirt out, and loosened the tie at his collar.

His Sony Trinitron TV, in all its sharp, colorful glory, bored him half to death as he surfed channels.

"-With the coldest temps we've seen in almost a hundred years-" Robby changed the channel "-John N. Mitchell is officially on parole after serving nineteen months for his hand in the Watergate-" Robby changed the channel again "-Super Bowl Sunday is just two days away, folks-" he tried another channel "-Soviet Union testing new nuclear technology-" Robby tried one more channel "-Following, of course, the tragic Kent State Protest Shootings in Ohio, the-"

With a click of the remote, the TV blinked as it powered down. The news didn't need to tell him that the world was on fire; he saw it with his own eyes every day.

Robby finished his bagel in silence, eyes focusing on the red book sitting innocently on his coffee table just a few feet from him. Principles of Internal Medicine.

The book had gotten very old, unopened for years at this point. But still, it always sat there, like decoration. Or a reminder. It soothed him, in a way.

Wiping the last of the bagel from his lips with the back of his hand, Robby peeled himself off of the couch, hitching back over to the kitchen island and grabbing the pen which sat atop the little cream-colored card.

Robby scribbled a message and signed his name, just below where Dennis had done the same.

Quietly, Robby limped through his bedroom and into the bathroom, closing the door softly and stripping bare. Showering wasn't near as much a struggle now as it had been the first year after his crash. He'd even had the shower renovated into a walk-in with a fancy little bench, which he now habitually sat in while allowing scalding hot water to beat down over his body—it was great for his left leg.

He sat there for several minutes under the stream of water before actually washing his body and his hair. When he stepped out, Robby stopped in front of the big mirror just above the sink, examining himself.

Over the years, he'd developed a bit of a stomach, as could be expected from a man who, when possible, avoided walking and straining his leg. His beard was well kept, though not by his own doing. Small scars littered his chest under the dark hair there, like reminders of his own idiocy.

When Robby had his fill of self-inflicted judgment upon his appearance, he dried himself off from head to toe and brushed his teeth.

He then limped back out into the bedroom, stumbling in the darkness as he hugged the wall all the way to his dresser, quietly as he could. He opened the drawer and pulled out a pair of his favorite plaid boxers, slipping them on before finding his side of the bed.

Glimmering faintly under the moonlight pouring through the window was a gold cross necklace, laid out neatly just beside Robby's own silver Star of David atop his nightstand.

Robby peeled the covers back gently, just enough to slip under them as he shuffled himself into the bed as carefully as he could. It wasn't enough, though, and it almost never was, given his leg: the other warm body under those covers was already humming back to life.

Dennis shifted, skin ghosting against soft bedsheets as he mumbled:

"Is it time to get up?"

Robby whispered a gentle apology as he closed the space between them, spooning the younger man from behind, stomach flush against Dennis' backside.

"Not even close," Robby kissed behind the shell of Dennis' ear. "More sleep for you."

For a while, they remained still, basking in the sudden heat of such close contact. It starkly contrasted the recent weather in Pittsburgh. It had been a cold winter, which, of course, meant Robby ached more than usual; he'd enjoy any heat he could find.

After a moment longer, Dennis shifted himself in the bed, turning to face Robby, whose features were hardly visible save for the diffused, silvery light of the moon trickling in from the window and settling on his eyes.

Robby inched himself closer, adjusting his head on the pillow until he was close enough that Dennis could feel breath on skin. The younger man shimmied his hand from under the covers and ran his fingers over Robby's beard.

"How was Nashville?"

"Familiar."

"And Heather?" Dennis asked groggily, voice hoarse with sleep.

Robby stole a quick but greedy little kiss from his lover's lips. As he pulled away, he felt Dennis try pushing closer, chasing the kiss lazily. The man reached his hand up and tangled his fingers in Dennis' short, curly mullet. He rubbed soothing circles into scalp with his digits, drinking in the faint whistles of breath coming in and out of Dennis' nose.

"You'd have loved to see her. She's doing great. Even convinced her to write to ABMS. We'll have to send her a thank-you card, sometime, hm?"

Dennis leaned in and stole a kiss of his own this time, pulling back only to show Robby that he was smiling to hear that news.

"You should write somethin' sweet in Dana's birthday card," Dennis reminded.

"Did that before I showered," Robby gloated.

"And I only had to ask twice this time," Dennis bantered, voice still thick with sleep.

Robby stole one more kiss and said teasingly, "I'm getting g-" a little tremor "-good at this, aren't I?"

Dennis nuzzled closer, throwing his arm over Robby and asking, "How'd you do, today?"

"No migraine. Headache was manageable."

"Tremors?"

"Same old."

"And your leg?"

"Hey," Robby cooed soothingly. "I'm alright."

Dennis buried into the crook of Robby's neck. "Just checking."

"I've got my own doctor looking after me," Robby reminded, then trailed his hand down from Dennis' hair, rubbing the younger man's back and pressing kisses into the crown of his head. "Not going anywhere."

Dennis swallowed a lump trying to form in his throat. With a subtle little shake in his voice, he clutched at Robby's back, muttering almost guiltily into the hot skin of the man's neck: "One more time. Please."

Robby let out a chuckle as soft and quiet as Tennessee snow. It tickled the crown of Dennis' head. They clung impossibly close to one another, body heat melting them together under the covers like they'd never need to separate.

Once more, with conviction like man's first vow to God, Robby said,

"I'm not going anywhere, Dennis."

Chapter End Notes

What a journey this has been. I’ve been doing this thing for the last 3 winters now where I will spend the colder months of the year writing a story and sharing it alongside a song (or a handful of songs) because it’s just… my way of living and creating, I guess.

But with this story, it was the first time I tapped into something very, very personal to me, and it was the first time I’ve ever spent so much time and effort to make it the kind of story that I needed when I was growing into my own identity. The kind of story that I’d be proud to hand off to others as a way of saying, “get out there and live, despite it all.”

That experience alone (with all the music I discovered and the history I learned about) made this such a beautiful adventure, but it could never have been made any more beautiful than by sharing it with you guys.

You’ve been such an intuitive, loving, passionate community of readers to me, and I can’t ever thank you enough for that kindness. For spending the last three months with me, or spending any time at all on this story and making it to this point, I genuinely hope you know how grateful I am.

It’s a little more sad to me than usual that this fic is over now, but, I guess if the story has taught me anything as I wrote it, it’s that there is no singular, grand or dreadful finale. Life is a series of potentials and endings which always leave room for a new beginning. ‘The overs’ are as survivable as anything else we’ll experience, just a little more bitter before their sweetness.
